Foundations
What is calculus? (3 min 42 seconds)
[Instructor] So, what is calculus? Calculus is a branch of mathematics that studies change and, more specifically, continuous change. This branch of mathematics provides you methods to analyze and understand dynamic systems and relationships between quantities. By doing so, it allows you to find patterns between equations, such as with distance and velocity. Calculus was originally called infinitesimal calculus. It slowly began to show signs of development in ancient times in places such as Egypt, Greece, and China. Modern calculus was mainly developed by Isaac Newton and Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz, along with many other mathematicians. There are two main branches of calculus, differential calculus and integral calculus. Differential calculus focuses on rates of change and the slopes of curves with derivatives. Integral calculus focuses on accumulation and the area under curves with integrals. These two branches of calculus are connected together with the fundamental theorem of calculus. In this course, you will focus on three main topics of calculus. These include limits, derivatives, and integrals. Note that these three concepts build on each other with limits as the base, then derivatives, and finally putting it all together with integrals. There are more advanced topics you can explore within calculus that will not be reviewed in this course. These include series, sequences, and polar coordinates. The goal of this course is to provide you a foundation of the previous three topics mentioned, which were limits, derivatives, and integrals. Something to note is that, like with most other mathematical topics, calculus is based on mathematical proofs. A proof is a logical argument that demonstrates how a mathematical statement is true by using established knowledge and rules. This course will not cover proofs in detail, but note that they are sometimes mentioned to help you better understand how and why the various topics in this course work. Calculus is used throughout many industries and disciplines. It is frequently used in engineering, physics, healthcare, and social sciences. It is also heavily used in other branches of mathematics, such as probability. I will mention some specific applications throughout the course, along with providing some videos that focus on a whole application. When it comes to how calculus relates to data science in particular, note that data science is heavily based in mathematics in general. A lot of the advanced topics in data science tend to use calculus, such as more complex statistical problems. Calculus also provides a foundation for understanding and optimizing many of the machine learning algorithms data scientists use today. In this course, you can expect a thorough review of the three main topics, limits, derivatives, and integrals. I will provide you with definitions, explanations of how or why things work, various examples, and practical applications. Note that many of these topics you can dive deeper into or do more examples of on your own. The goal of this course, though, is to provide you as thorough of an overview as possible so you can take it from there to expand more on your own. Calculus can be challenging to understand at first, so the main suggestion I have for this course is to take your time and practice working through multiple examples, including examples outside of this course. Before you jump in to the three main calculus topics I mentioned prior, let's take a moment to review some of the common function types you will use throughout this course.

What is the function? (8 min 37 seconds )
- [Instructor] Let's begin by learning about functions. So, what is a function? A function is an equation where all values of x correspond to exactly one value of y via some rule. Note that sometimes y is referred to as f of x, but these are both the same thing when it comes to a function. So, why are functions important to calculus? Functions are used very frequently in many areas of mathematics, but especially in calculus. In order to use many of the techniques you will learn later on, such as limits and derivatives, you will need a function or functions to perform them on. A function is made of three main components. First, you have the domain of your function. This is the set of inputs for the function. Second, you have the range of your function. This is the set of outputs for the function. Finally, you have the rule for your function, which allows you to get the inputs, their corresponding outfits by some sort of rule that has been created. So, note that with this, often the domain is notated as x and the range is notated as y. Speaking of variables, note that sometimes x is referred to as the independent variable and then y is referred to as the dependent variable. This is because x can be chosen freely from a set of possibilities, hence from the domain of your function. And then, y depends on what the corresponding x value is, hence from the range of your function. When it comes to the rule for your function, there are two main things to keep in mind. First, you need to ensure your rule always returns only one output y for each input x. Second, you need to ensure the rule is unambiguous. This means for each value x, your rule must produce the same corresponding output y each time you run the function. So, let's say you have a function with a value of 2 for x and a value of 4 for y. If you run this function as many times as possible, it should always obtain a value of 4 for y no matter how many times you put in the value of 2 for x. But let's say if you have this function run another time where the value of y is now suddenly 6. This is not a valid rule, hence it can be a valid function. So, what does a function look like? Here are a few examples of some basic functions. Generally you will have the format of a function where you'll have y or again sometimes f of x on the left side, and then you'll have the equal sign. And then, on the right side you'll have your variable x and any sort of corresponding values or functions that are being taken with that variable x. So, for example, you have y equals cos, and then in parentheses x, which is cosine of x. Next you have y equals x to the 3rd or x cubed plus 5 multiplied by x squared plus 1. And finally you have y equals 5. Note that this last function is still a function even though you can't clearly see x because essentially it's zeroed out where it is just seen as a constant value of 5. I have mentioned a few times so far in this video that you can use f of x when writing a function. This is what is called function notation. You'll see throughout this course that I will often use the function notation f of x, but you can sometimes use other letters such as g of x or h of x. What this function notation is representing is the function and its corresponding output. So in this case, the function notation f of x is representing the function and its corresponding output. Let's look at a few function notation examples. So, let's say you have f of x equals x plus 1. You have your function notation f of x which in this case is put there instead of y, and then you have your equals sign then x + 1. Let's say you wanted to get the value where x is equal to 4, so you would have 4 plus 1, which in this case equals 5. A way to have function notation with this is you then have f of 4 where your value of x is replaced by the value you are putting into your function, which is 4 in this case. And again, this will equal 5. So sometimes you'll see f of 4 equals 5, which again is just another way of notating that your function with the value of 4 plugged in for x has a result of 5. You may be wondering what makes an equation not a function. Let's go through a few examples. For example, if you have x equals 1, this is not a function since it does not have a unique value y for each value of x. Next, you have y squared equals 4 minus x squared. In this case, if you put x equal to 0, you would then get values of y equal to plus or minus 2. Since there are two possible values for y for this one value of x, this means that it cannot be a function because you can't have two different possible values of y. When it comes to representing functions, there are various ways you can go about this. You can create tables, you can list out a set of points, you can create mapping diagrams, and you can visualize with graphs. The first three options usually just state each value of x and its corresponding value y versus the last one is where you actually visually graph it. So how do you graph a function? On a graph, you'll note that you have two lines that intersect each other perpendicularly. The line going up and down is the y-axis, and the line going across left to right is the x-axis. And here you have what are called quadrants. These are divided by these two axes. So you have your upper right quadrant, where in this case you have two points. In this case, let's say you wanted to plot the function y equals x squared where x equals 1, 1 squared equals 1, so then you would plot the point 1, 1. Next you would plug in x equals 2, which would get you the value of y equals 4, so you'd plug in the point 2, 4. Once you plot out all the points on the graph, then you can connect them together with a line. Note that how you connect the points though depends on what type of function you're working with, on if it is continuous or not over the particular domain and range you're looking at. So, if you plotted your function y equals x squared, this is what the graph would look like once it is all assembled. So you'll see it looks kind of like a big U, where it's going from the upper left and it goes down to where you have that point 0, 0, and then it goes back up in the same manner on the right side. The last thing I will mention is the vertical line test. So as you saw before, sometimes there are cases where an equation is not a function, and one way you could test this is with the vertical line test. So if you look at the graph on the left of the function, you have the function which is represented by the blue line. So it starts from the upper left, kind of goes down, has a little valley, and then goes straight back up pretty quickly. Then you'll see I have a red bar through it. So the vertical line test means that if this red bar, if you move it anywhere on the x-axis with your function, it should only touch that blue line of your graph of your function once. So if you moved it back and forth, it looks like it should only touch once, so you should be good to go. Now let's look at the graph on the right side with the equation where you have a U turned on its side, and so you have the blue line where it's coming through from the right over to the left and kind of loops back over to the right. Now you have your red line for your vertical line test, and you'll notice that it is touching two points of your line of your equation, meaning that it is not a function because it should only be touching one point on that line at all times. And with this one, it is touching two points. There are many types of functions out there that have many similarities and differences in how they're handled, formatted, and how they produce outputs. In the next video, you'll get an introduction to understanding the different types of functions that you'll come across to prepare you for the following videos where I will dive into deeper detail, reviewing some of the common ones you'll see throughout this course. Let's get started.

Types of functions (2 min 38 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned what a function is and why they are important for calculus. In the following videos, you will review some common types of functions and their corresponding properties. Note that many of these functions may look familiar from your previous mathematics education. The functions you'll explore in this chapter include algebraic, polynomial, rational, trigonometric, inverse, exponential, and logarithmic. Note that this does not encompass all types of functions, but it focuses on the main types that are key to better understanding calculus and that will be frequently used throughout this course. Here you could see some various and functions that include algebraic, polynomial, rational, trigonometric, inverse, exponential, and logarithmic functions. So you'll see you have F of X equals X cubed plus 4 multiplied by X plus 9. This is a mix of algebraic and polynomial. Next, you have F of X equal to E to the X plus 2 divided by X squared minus 5. This is a combination of exponential and rational, along with algebraic. After that, you have F of X equal to LN of X minus 6. This is a combination of logarithmic and algebraic. After that, you have F to the negative 1 of X equals X, which this is an inverse function. Finally, you have F of X equal to sine of X plus cosine of X, which is a combination of trigonometric and algebraic functions. Each of the following videos will be structured similarly, to give you a general overview of each type of function. And finally, a list of a few real-world applications of how the function type can be used. Functions are fundamental to calculus and many other areas of mathematics. They help you evaluate many mathematical scenarios, and it is important to understand how different types of functions operate. The goal of the following videos is to provide you with a basic understanding of each function type, so then you could take that information and expand upon it in your work and education, along with being able to better understand the following content in calculus where those functions are used. Let's start off by exploring polynomial and rational functions.

Polynomial and rational functions (8 min 21 seconds)
- [Instructor] First up are polynomial and rational functions. Before you get into those though, let's begin by understanding what algebraic functions are. Algebraic functions are defined by the root of a polynomial equation with one or more variables. These functions are made from algebraic operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, division, and exponentiation. Let's look at a few examples of functions that are algebraic and ones that are not algebraic. To the left, you have some algebraic functions of f of x equals the square root of x squared plus four, and then you have f of x equals x to the fifth minus three multiplied by x squared plus seven, and finally, you have f of x equals the cube root of x to the third plus two multiplied by x plus one. All three of these functions on the left are algebraic because they are found using those algebraic operations. To the right, you have some non-algebraic functions where you have f of x equals x to the x. This is not algebraic because you are taking the variable in the exponent. Next, you have f of x equals sine of x and f of x equals ln f x, which is the natural log of x. These two are imposing some sort of other function operation on them, such as a trigonometric or a logarithmic function, hence, they are not algebraic. There are various types of functions that are derived from algebraic functions, such as polynomial functions and rational functions. Let's begin exploring polynomial functions. These functions are in the form of an multiplied by x to the n plus an minus one multiplied by x to the n minus one plus all the way to plus a1 multiplied by x plus a0. Note that in this case, n must be greater than zero, and a of n must not equal zero. Let's take a look at the degrees of polynomials. So in this case, the degree of the polynomial is represented by the variable n. So if you have the degree of n equal to zero, then it is a constant function where it equals constant values such as seven or 29. If the degree n equals one, then it is a linear function. If the degree is n equals two, then it is a quadratic function. And finally, if the degree n equals three, it is a cubic function. Let's look at a few examples of polynomial functions. For f of x, you have x squared plus one, which this would be a quadratic function since it has a squared term. Next, you have f of x equals x to the fourth minus five multiplied by x to the third plus eight multiplied by x squared minus x. Finally, you have f of x equals seven, which is a constant function because the degree of n equals zero, and hence, it's like multiplying x to the zero, which just equals one. To the right, you will see what a graph may look like for a polynomial function, where oftentimes they are represented by various curves such as different U's, or sometimes they kind of come up from the left side and then up to the right or vice versa. Let's take a further look at the degrees of polynomials and what they can tell you. Something interesting is how the sine and degree of the leading coefficient of a polynomial function can determine the behavior of its graph. So in the first case, if you have an even degree and a positive leading coefficient, then both ends of the graph will rise toward infinity. Number two, if you have an even degree and negative leading coefficient, then both ends of the graph will fall towards negative infinity. Number three, if you have an odd degree and a positive leading coefficient, then the left end of the graph falls, which will be towards negative infinity and the right end of the graph rises, which will be towards positive infinity. And finally, number four, if you have an odd degree and negative leading coefficient, the left end of the graph will rise towards positive infinity, and the right end of the graph will fall towards negative infinity. There are many properties for polynomial functions, but let's review some of the key ones. First, you have the roots are values of x, where f of x equals zero. You'll hear roots mentioned many times throughout this course, so it's very important to know that you can find them using this method. Number two, a polynomial function with a degree of n can have up to n real roots. Number three, polynomial functions are continuous and differentiable for all real values of x. Number four, a polynomial function with a degree of n can have up to n minus one turning points. This means where the graph will change direction. Number five, polynomial functions do not have vertical, horizontal, or oblique asymptotes. And finally, number six, polynomials can sometimes exhibit symmetry if they contain all odd or all even powers of x. Let's pivot and take a look at rational functions. Rational functions are where you have a function f of x in the form of f of x equals g of x divided by h of x. In this form, g of x and h of x are both polynomial functions. And note that h of x must not equal zero because you cannot divide a fraction by zero and have it be a valid result. Hence, rational functions are basically a quotient of polynomial functions, making them extremely related to them. Let's take a look at what some rational functions may look like. First you have f of x equals three multiplied by x to the fifth minus two multiplied by x squared plus three, and then you'll divide all of that by x squared minus one. So this is a rational function because you have your two polynomial functions on the top and bottom portion of your fraction. Next, you have f of x equals x squared divided by x cubed minus two multiplied by x. Again, you have your two polynomial functions, even if you just have that single term of x squared on the top. And finally, you have f of x equals three divided by x minus four. Remember that even though this is just a constant value, it is still a polynomial function where you just imagine it's multiplied by x to the zero, meaning it is multiplied by one, and hence, why you leave that part out. If you look to the right, this is what a rational function may look like when it is plotted. So again, you have your blue line, and it has kind of some different curves going on, so you'll notice it has kind of a lot of different turning points in the process. Let's review some common properties for rational functions. First, the domain of a rational function is all real numbers except where the denominator h of x equals zero. These points are called undefined points or excluded values where the function f of x is undefined at that particular point. Number two, rational functions are continuous and differentiable for all real x values where the function is defined. So note that this does not work for the undefined values. Number three, vertical asymptotes appear where the denominator h of x equals zero, and the numerator g of x does not equal zero. Horizontal asymptotes occur when the degree of the numerator is less than or equal to the degree of the denominator. And finally, rational functions can sometimes have symmetry depending on how the negative values of the functions reflect. There are many real life applications for polynomial and rational functions. They can be used in economics to model revenue growth or marginal costs. In pharmacology, they can be used to model drug concentration over time. And finally, in engineering, they could be used to model electrical circuits. Polynomial and rational functions both provide a solid base for functions to work with. These functions will often be the simplest types of functions you will work with throughout this course, but note that they are still important to know and understand, even though they tend to be on the simpler side. I recommend taking time on your own to continue exploring polynomial and rational functions, especially if you still feel uncomfortable with what these functions are. Up next, let's explore inverse functions.

Inverse functions (7 min 17 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next up are inverse functions. Let's start off by exploring how inverse functions only work with what are called one-to-one functions. This is where you only get one output for each input of a function. So this means f of x1 must not equal f of x2 when x1 does not equal x2. One way you can test this is using the horizontal line test. This ensures each value of your function only intersects the horizontal line once for every y value. So similar to the vertical line test, where you use a vertical line to tell if an equation is a function or not, the horizontal line test then tells you if your function is a one-to-one function or not. So if you look at the left, you have the graph of the function y equals x cubed, which is just going up from the bottom left and then going back up on the bottom right after it levels out a little bit around 0,0. If you do the horizontal line test with this function and make it go up and down with that red line, it should only touch the line of the function once, meaning it is one-to-one. Now, if you look at the function on the right side, where you have the function of x squared, you'll then have the blue line representing the function where it is a U. But if you do the horizontal line test with the red line for this one, you'll note that it touches two points of the line for basically the whole function. This means that it does not pass the horizontal line test and that it is not one-to-one. Note that for this video, I will focus on showing you how to work with one-to-one functions when it comes to inverse functions. Note that there are some cases, though, where a function can be turned into a one-to-one function to find its inverse by restricting its domain values. So for example, with the horizontal line test, you saw the function f of x equals x squared did not pass it. But this is a function that you can turn into a one-to-one function to, hence, find its inverse. Now let's learn what inverse functions are. Inverse functions are when you have a function f of x and you want to do the reverse of that function. Inverse functions are often notated with f to the negative one of x, where you can think of it as mapping x to y with the function f of x and y to x with the inverse function. You can generally prove a function has the proper inverse function when you have f of f to the negative one of x equals x and when f to the negative one f of x equals x. This means you cancel out the functions to get just x for any value of x. Note that even though you notate an inverse function with a power of negative one, this does not mean the same thing as taking the original function to the power of negative one. So this means f to the negative one of x is not the same as one divided by f of x. Let's review some common properties for inverse functions. Number one: a function must be one-to-one in order to get the inverse of a function. Note that there are situations where you can convert a function into a one-to-one function in order to gather its inverse, so even if it might not be one-to-one initially, you can still find its inverse. Number two: the domain of the function is the range of the inverse function, and vice versa. Number three: it has a composition property where f of f of negative one of x equals x and f negative one of f of x equals x. Number four: they are reversible, meaning if f of x is continuous and differentiable, then so is the inverse function where it exists. And finally, the graphs of a function and its inverse are symmetric, meaning they create a mirror image across y equals x. So how do you find an inverse function? Generally, the process is you solve the equation y equals f of x for x. Once you do this, then you'll interchange the values of x and y in your result to get your inverse function. Let's look at this in an example. Let's say you have f of x equals x cubed. In this case, you'll have y equals f of x, which equals x cubed. So you could take that f of x term out. And then, if you take the cube root of both sides, you get the cube root of y equals x. Now, if you switch your variables, you'll get y equals the cube root of x, meaning f to the negative one of x equals the cube root of x. Let's look at another example. Let's say f of x equals five multiplied by x plus nine. So you'll have y equals f of x equals five multiplied by x plus nine. Getting rid of the f of x and subtracting both sides by nine, you get y minus nine equals five multiplied by x. Divide both sides by five to get y minus nine divided by five equals x. Now if you switch your variables, you get y equals x minus nine divided by five, meaning your inverse function f of the negative one of x equals x minus nine divided by five. If you look to the right, you'll see the graph of your function and your inverse function. So the blue line starting at the bottom left and going quickly up towards the right is your function, five multiplied by x plus nine. And then you have your inverse function, x minus nine divided by five, which is the red line starting a little bit higher on the left side and slowly going over to the right where even at x equals six, it is yet to get to the positive side of that. Like I mentioned before, if you drew a line where y equals x for this, you would then get that mirror image of these two different functions, since again, you have its function and its inverse. Let's look at one more example, but this time, let's have f of x equals one divided by x. So you'll have y equals f of x equals one divided by x. Getting rid of f of x and multiplying both sides by x, you get y multiplied by x equals one. Divide both sides by y to get x equals one divided by y. Switching your variables gives you y equals one divided by x, which means your inverse is f to the negative one of x equals one divided by x. Note there are some cases like this where the inverse function is the same as the original function. So in this case, f of x is the same as f to the negative one of x. There are many real-life applications for inverse functions. They can be used in pharmacology to estimate the time when a drug concentration reaches a desired level of effect. In kinematics, inverse functions are used to provide the time needed to reach a specific position for a function measured in time. And finally, in economics, they could be used in cost and demand functions to maximize profits. This is just scratching the surface of inverse functions due to their many nuances and various real-life applications. You will see inverse functions frequently used throughout trigonometric, exponential, and logarithmic functions throughout the course. I highly recommend taking time on your own to continue practicing how to find inverse functions. Up next: let's explore trigonometric functions.

Trigonometric functions (14 min 26 seconds) 
- [Instructor] Next, let's explore trigonometric functions. Before you explore these types of functions, let's take a moment to understand how to measure angles with radians and the unit circle. This graph is what is called a unit circle. This circle spans all four quadrants of a graph where it is in one unit in each direction, so it has a radius of 1, hence it is a unit circle. This circle is key to trigonometry, due to its ability to easily measure all the angles from 0 degrees to 360 degrees with trigonometric functions. So you'll see here you have your unit circle with your 1 radius, and it is measuring your angle with the theta sine, and you'll see that it is measuring it with what are called trigonometric functions. Many problems in trigonometry and calculus use radians instead of degrees to measure them, so it is very important to learn what these are. A radian is a standard unit, a measurement for angles using the unit circle where an angle is the arc length of the associated arc on the unit circle. This means it is a different method to measure angles instead of using degrees. This is a visualization of the unit circle with the different radians and their associated degrees. So for example, on the right side you have 0 degrees, which technically relates to both 0 and to pi. And then for 30 degrees you have pi divided by 6. 45 degrees is pi divided by 4, 60 degrees is pi divided by 3 and 90 degrees is pi divided by 2. If you jump all the way to 180 degrees, that's pi, 270 degrees is 3 pi divided by 2. And again, that then brings you back to that 0 degrees with 2 pi. I highly suggest taking a moment to review this unit circle or looking one up online to understand radians and their associated degrees. You'll notice that when measuring radians, you will start at the far right side and then move in a counterclockwise motion when measuring them. So again, that 0 or 2 pi starts on the very right and then you start to go up where on the Y axis where X equals 0, you then get to 25% of the way through of the circle, and then you get through 50% of it at the x axis where Y equals 0, and then 75% of the way through on the Y axis where X is equal to 0 but in the negative direction and then back around. Trigonometric functions give you the power to use angle measures in radians or degrees. These functions can help you find a point on any circle including the unit circle, along with help you measure the sides and angles of any triangle. Let's review the trigonometric functions you'll come across in this course. First you have what is called sine and it is notated by sine of x. Then you have cosine, which is notated by cosine of x, tangent, which is notated by tangent of x. And these are the first three main trigonometric functions. Next, you have the three reciprocals for the three main trigonometric functions. So you have cosecant, which is notated by csc of x, secant, which is notated by sec of x, and then cotangent, which is notated by cot x. So you'll note that those are shortened, much more kind of like with tangent. Finally, you have the inverse trigonometric functions, which are notated by arcsine, which is notated by arccsin of x, arccosine with arc cosine of x and arctangent, which is arctan of x. So these are how you'll come across most of these trigonometric functions. Note that the three inverse trigonometric functions can often be notated differently, where sometimes you will see them as, for example, with arcsine of x, you'll see sine and a -1 sine to x instead. Let's understand how these trigonometric functions interact with the unit circle. So here is the unit circle, and on the very inside you have your degrees. On the next level you have your radians, and then on the outside you have coordinates. So these coordinates are where they land in the graph. So in this case, your angle is often measured by theta, but sometimes it's measured by X in your equations and that is going to be what you'll be measuring with your trigonometric functions. So remember, your radius is going to be equaling 1 and these coordinates will come in play when it comes to working with the different sine, cosine, tangent and the other trigonometric functions. I highly suggest you take some time to study this unit circle with these different coordinates or look up a unit circle online or a table with these different coordinate points, since they will be very important for you to remember. Let's define the six main trigonometric functions using the unit circle you just looked at. So theta will be the angle you're measuring and you'll have your X and Y coordinates. So you'll have sine of theta equals y, cosine of theta equals X, tangent of theta equals y divided by X, cosecant of theta equals 1 divided by y, secant of theta equals 1 divided by X and cotangent of theta equals X divided by y. Note that if X is equal to 0, then both secant and tangent are undefined. And if y equals 0, then cosecant and cotangent are undefined. This is because you cannot have a fraction divided by 0. Let's look at what trigonometric functions look like graphically. On this graph you'll see two wave-like curves representing the sine and cosine functions. The sine function is represented by the red line that intersects the Y axis at 0, where X is equal to 0, and the blue line represents the cosine function where it intersects the Y axis at 1 where X is equal 0. So you'll note that it kind of looks like the same graph just shifted over slightly. And this is because again, these are very highly related functions to each other. So you'll notice that many of the trigonometric functions have a similar shape where again, they have what is called oscillating behavior with these waves going up and down. Trigonometric functions have many properties. So let's review a few of the main ones you should know. Let's start off with reciprocal identities. This shouldn't come as a surprise with how related trigonometric functions are to each other. So here you have sine of theta, which equals 1 divided by cosecant of theta, then cosine of theta equals 1 divided by secant of theta. Tangent of theta equals 1 divided by cotangent of theta, cosecant of theta equals 1 divided by sine of theta. Secant of theta equals 1 divided by cosine of theta and cotangent of theta equals 1 divided by tangent of theta. So note that it is common for you to switch between these different trigonometric functions using these reciprocal identities. Next, let's look up Pythagorean formulas. And this is due to the relation of trigonometric functions with triangles. First up you have sine squared of x plus cosine squared of x equals 1. This follows the Pythagorean theorem where you have the squared values of A squared plus B squared equals C squared. And since you have that radius equal to 1 for your unit circle, that is why these 2 squared equal 1. The other two formulas follow this where again, you can use those reciprocal identities to calculate this. So here you get 1 plus tangent X squared equals secant squared of X and 1 plus cotangent squared of X equals cosecant squared of X. Next, you have some sum and difference formulas where you can transform when you have multiple variables inside a sine or cosine function. So if you have sine of A plus B, you'll get this equal to sine of A multiplied by cosine of B plus cosine of A multiplied by sine of B. If you have sine of A minus B, you'll get sine of A multiplied by cosine of B minus cosine of A multiplied by sine of B. Now let's switch to cosine. So if you have cosine of A plus B, this equals cosine of A multiplied by cosine of B plus sine of A multiplied by sine of B. And finally, for cosine of A minus B, you'll get cosine of A multiplied by cosine of B minus sine of A multiplied by sine of B. Next up, let's look at some even-odd identities. Note that a trigonometric function is even if f of -X equals f of X and it is odd if f of -X equals -f of x. Let's start with sine of - theta, which equals - sine of theta. This means it is odd because it has a negative sign in front of sine of theta. Next is cosine of - theta which equals cosine of theta. This means it is an even function because the cosine of - theta is equal to cosine of theta, meaning there's no extra negative sign on the outside of cosine of theta. Next you have tangent of - theta, which equals - tangent of theta, meaning this one is odd. And then cosecant of - theta equals - cosecant of theta, meaning this one is odd as well. And then secant of - theta equals secant of theta, which is even. And finally, cotangent of - theta equals - cotangent of theta, which is odd. So you'll see four of them are odd and two of them are even. Let's wrap up properties by looking at some double angle formulas for sine and cosine. What these allow you to do is calculate the sine or cosine of an angle that is double the size of the angle you are currently looking at. To do so for sine you have sine of 2 multiplied by x and this will equal 2 multiplied by sine of 3 multiplied by cosine of x. For cosine, this will be cosine of 2 multiplied by x equals cosine squared of x minus sine squared of x. Let's begin by looking at an example of how to get all six trigonometric functions at the point of pi divided by 6. So here you have your radians of pi divided by 6. So if you look at the unit circle to the right, you'll see that this corresponds to the XY coordinate of X equal to square root of 3 divided by 2 and y equals 1/2. Using your definitions from earlier, this means sine of pi divided by 6 equals 1/2, and cosine of pi divided by 6 equals square root of 3 divided by 2. So with this unit circle, you'll see that all of the X values correspond to cosine and all of the Y values correspond to sine. Now you can get the remaining four by using these two values in order to get their results at pi divided by 6. So for tangent of pi divided by 6, this is equal to sine divided by cosine. So you have 1/2 divided by the square root of 3 divided by 2, which gets you a result of 1 divided by square root of 3. For cosecant of pi divided by 6, this is 1 divided by sine so it gets you 1 divided by 1/2, which equals 2. Next is secant of pi divided by 6, which equals 1 divided by the square root of 3 divided by 2, which equals 2 multiplied by the square root of 3 divided by 3. And finally, cotangent of pi divided by 6 equals square root of 3 divided by 2 divided by 1/2 equals square root of 3. Now let's look at an example where you were solving for theta. Let's say you have cosecant squared of theta plus 2 multiplied by cosecant of theta plus 1 equals 0. And let's say you're looking at where theta is in the region of 0 to 2 pi. Let's use substitution where X equals cosecant of theta, which gets you the equation of x squared plus 2 multiplied by x plus 1 equals 0. Factoring this gets you x plus 1 multiplied by x plus 1 equals 0. You can then combine this to be x plus 1 squared equals 0. If you take the square root of both sides, you'll get x plus 1 equals 0, hence x equals -1. Plugging in cosecant of theta equals 1 divided by sine of theta, you'll get 1 divided by sine of theta equals -1. This conversion to sine will then help you solve for theta since if it is equal to -1, you can rearrange this to just have it be sine of theta equals -1. So if you looked at your unit circle where sine of theta equals -1, that will correspond to 3 multiplied by pi, divided by 2 radians, which equals 270 degrees since this is at the point of 0 -1. There are many real life applications for trigonometric functions. They can be used in physics to model various physical phenomena such as modeling vibrations of strings and motion of pendulums. In astronomy, they can be used to measure the distances between stars, planets, and galaxies. In construction, they can be used to calculate the slopes of beams and roofs. And finally, in aviation, they can be used to determine flight paths to ensure there are safe takeoffs and landings. This is just scratching the surface of trigonometric functions due to their interaction with angles, various properties, and many real life applications. I highly recommend taking time on your own to ensure you are familiar with the different trigonometric functions, the unit circle, their various properties, and continuing to practice more problems. Let's wrap up this chapter by exploring exponential and logarithmic functions in the next video.

Exponential and logarithmic functions (14 min 45 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up with exponential and logarithmic functions. Let's start by exploring exponential functions, and then you will learn about logarithmic functions. The reason why you are learning about these two functions together is because they are very connected. Let's get started. Exponential functions are functions where you have a constant real number raised to a variable power. This is defined for real numbers. The form an exponential function takes is f of x equals b to the x. Note that this is for values of b greater than zero, and where b is not equal to one. In this function, b is referred to as the base, and x is referred to as the exponent. Let's explore why b needs to be greater than zero and not equal to one. If b equals zero or b equals one, then you will actually have a constant function instead of an exponential function. And if you have a negative b value, then it will provide inconsistent results. Let's look at an example of why negative numbers are not included in exponential functions. Let's say you have b equal to negative nine. So, using your exponential function format of f of x equals b to the x, you'll then substitute in your value of b equals negative nine two f of x equals negative nine to the x. Now, let's say you want to plug in the power x equals three, so if you take f of three, this will equal negative nine to the three, which equals 729. This works fine, but what if you take x equals 0.5? If you take f of 0.5, this is equal to negative nine to the 0.5, and this is the same as saying it is equal to the square root of negative nine. This is equal to three multiplied by i, where i is an imaginary number. This ends up giving you a result that contains complex numbers instead of real numbers, and that will not work for exponential functions. Here are a few examples of what exponential functions look like, along with a graph of an exponential function. So you have f of x equals five to the x, you have f of x equals three to the x, and you have f of x equals e to the two multiplied by x. Note that the e will be explained later on. And then, to the right, you'll see the graph of an exponential function, where you slowly have the graph coming from the left and then quickly going up on the right side. Something to note when it comes to dealing with powers is that a power function is not the same thing as an exponential function. So a power function is notated as f of x equals x to the b, where, in this case, you have your variable x as the base, and then your variable b as the exponent. This is because power functions have that variable raised to a fixed power. This is not the same as exponential functions, where you are raising a fixed value to a variable power. Let's review some common properties for exponential functions. First, the base of the function, b, must be greater than zero and must not equal one. If x is equal to zero, then the function will always equal one. Three, the domain is defined for all real numbers. Four, the range is defined for all positive real numbers. Next, if b is greater than zero but less than one, then f of x will approach zero as x approaches infinity, and f of x will approach infinity as x approaches negative infinity. Now, if b is greater than one, then f of x will approach infinity as x approaches infinity, and f of x will approach zero as x approaches negative infinity. There are various properties for exponents that are often referred to as the laws of exponents. These will help you simplify expressions that contain exponents with your exponential functions. So, first you have the law of product, which is b to the x multiplied by b to the y equals b to the x plus y. So essentially if you multiply two exponential functions, you can then just add their powers together. Next, you have law of quotient, so if you have b of x divided by b of y, this will equal b to the x minus y, so essentially the opposite of the law of product. Next, you have the law of power of a power, so if you have b to the x, and then that is to the y, this will then equal b to the x multiplied by y. Then you have law of power of a product, where you have a multiplied by b, and then both of those are to the x. This will equal a to the x multiplied by b to the x, where essentially you're distributing that x value between those two different values of a and b. Next, you have Law of power of a quotient, where you have a divided by b, and that is taken all to the x. This will equal a to the x divided by b to the x, again distributing that exponent. And finally, you have the Law of negative exponent, where you have b to the negative x equals one divided by b to the x. So note that, with this last one, sometimes I will notate throughout this course exponential functions in that b to the minus x format, and other times, I will notate it in the one over b to the x format. There's a special type of exponential function that appears in many real world applications. This is called the natural exponential function. This function is represented by f of x equals e to the x, where e is what is called Euler's number, and it's approximately to 2.7183. So this is similar to how Pi is a number that is often used in mathematics, Euler's number is another one that is great to know. Since e is greater than one, as x approaches infinity, so does the function f of x. Now that you have a general understanding of exponential functions, let's take a look at logarithmic functions. Logarithmic functions are functions where you have a variable that represents the exponent needed to produce a given number from a constant real base. This is defined for real numbers. Logarithmic functions take the form of y equals log base b of x, so you'll note that the b is slightly lower as an index next to that log, and that log stands for logarithm, and this will be for values of b greater than zero and not equal to one. Note that this is equivalent to x equals b to the y. You'll still have your base be notated as b. And if b is equal to zero, or b is equal to one, then it is a constant function. Note that negative b values also provide inconsistent results, hence why they are not used in these types of functions. So how are exponential functions and logarithmic functions related? Logarithmic functions are essentially the inverse of exponential functions. Since this is the case, then the following is true, where you have the log of base b of b to the x equals x, which equals b to the log base b of x. So, essentially, the exponential functions and the logarithmic functions cancel each other out. Let's look at some examples of logarithmic functions along with the graph. Here, you have f of x equals log base 10 of x. Then you have f of x equals ln of x, which, I will explain in a few slides what that ln means. And then you have f of x equals log base seven of x to the four divided by y. To the right, you'll see you have a logarithmic function. It looks similar in shape to your exponential function, but it's coming in from a different direction, where it's coming in from the bottom and going straight up, and then it finally ends up starting to curve over to the right and slowly decreases the amount that the function is going towards the positive direction of y. Let's review some common properties for logarithmic functions. First, the base, b, must be greater than zero, and b must not equal one. If x equals one, then the function will always equal zero. Three, the domain is defined for all positive real numbers. Four, the range is defined for all real numbers. So you'll notice the domain and range essentially flip, as far as what they're defined for with exponents and logarithms. Number five, logarithms are the inverse of exponential functions where you have log base b of b to the x equals x, which equals b to the log base b of x. Six, if b is greater than zero and less than one, then f of x will approach negative infinity as x approaches infinity, and f of x will approach infinity as x approaches zero. And finally, if b is greater than one, then f of x will approach infinity as x approaches infinity, and f of x will approach negative infinity as x approaches zero. Let's look at some key logarithmic properties that will help you simplify your logarithmic expressions as you work with them throughout this course. First, you have the same base rule, and this is log base b of b is equal to one, so essentially if you have the same base as the value inside of those parentheses, it'll then just equal one. Next, you have the product rule, where you have log base b of x multiplied by y equals log base b of x plus log base b of y. Next, you have the quotient rule, where you have log base b of x divided by y equals log base b of x minus log base b of y. So you note for the product and quotient rules, essentially it changes the operations from multiplication to addition where you separate them out, and then for quotients, it then changes to subtraction. So this is a really great way to separate out variables when dealing with logarithms. Next is the power rule, where if you have log base b of x to the r, this will equal r multiplied by log base b of x. This will be very important when it comes to working with logarithms and those exponential functions, and how you can move that exponent and treat it more as a constant multiple. Finally, you have the change of base rule. There are multiple variations of this rule, but a common version of it is log base a of x equals log base b of x divided by log base b of a. This will help you essentially change the base of the logarithm you are working with. Now let's explore natural logarithms, which are similar to natural exponential functions, where you are using that Euler's number. And note that these appear in many real world applications. So the natural logarithmic function is often notated by that ln of x. So ln of x is often notated as the natural log, or natural logarithm, of x. And this is equal to log base e of x, where essentially the log base e will then just merge to just be that ln. Again, this uses Euler's number, e, which is approximately equal to 2.7183. And since e to the x and the natural log of x are inverse functions, then the natural log of e to the x equals x, which equals e to the natural log of x, so again, they cancel each other out. One of the great things is you can use logarithmic functions and exponential functions to solve each other. Let's see this in action with an example. So let's say you have seven to the x equals five, and you want to solve for the value of x. So you'll take the natural logarithm of both sides of this equation. Note that you could use a different logarithm, but the natural logarithm tends to be one of the easiest ones to work with for these types of situations. So you have the natural log of seven to the x equals natural log of five. Using that power rule, you can then move the exponent out to have it be x multiplied by the natural log of seven equals natural log of five. Then divide both sides by that natural log of seven to get x equals natural log of five divided by natural log of seven, which is approximately equal to 0.827. So this is a great example of how you can use logarithms, and their properties, to solve an exponential function. Now let's go the other direction. So let's say you have the natural log of one divided by x equals 12. If you take both sides to the exponent of Euler's number, e, you'll have e to the natural log of one divided by x equals e to the 12, and this gets you one to the x equals e to the 12. Once you rearrange this, you get x equals one divided by e to the 12, which is approximately equal to 6.14 multiplied by 10 to the negative six, meaning it's a very, very small number. There are many real life applications for both exponential and logarithmic functions. In biology, exponential functions are used to model population growth and decay. In finance, they're used to model compound interest of how money grows over time. In seismology, the Richter scale uses a logarithmic function with the scale to model the magnitude of earthquakes. And finally, in acoustic metrology, logarithmic functions are used to model how loud a sound is in decibels. This should provide you a thorough introduction of both exponential and logarithmic functions, but know that there are many variations of them, as you saw with the natural versions. I highly recommend taking time on your own to continue practicing more problems with exponential and logarithmic functions and see how you can use their various properties to solve them. This wraps up your introduction to calculus and functions, but note that this is just the beginning. I suggest you take time on your own to further explore these common functions, along with some less common functions. In the next chapter, you'll learn all about the first key concept of calculus, limits.
