Day 02

Limits

Tangent lines, velocity, and area (3 min 24 seconds)
- Ready to dive into the first major topic of calculus? Then let's tackle limits. In this chapter, you will define one and two-sided limits, discover limit properties, and learn about infinite limits. Before moving on though, let's first explore the topics of tangent lines, velocity, and area. Let's start off by exploring what a tangent line is. A tangent line is a line that touches the curve of a function at a point and has the same slope of the function at that point. This tangent line helps represent the rate of change of that function at that particular point. This graph shows you what a tangent line looks like. So you have the blue curve, which is like an upside-down U, and then that red flat line on top is the tangent line because it is right up against that curve and it is equal to the slope at that particular point of the curve. Before calculus came around, it was difficult to determine the rate of change of a curve at a particular point due to many functions having complicated curves. This is where the topics of limits and derivatives come in handy to help you solve this problem. You will learn about this topic in greater detail in the derivatives chapter in this course. Next step is velocity. Velocity deals with distance and time. The average velocity of an object over a period of time is the distance the object traveled divided by the amount of time traveled. Now, if you want to find the velocity at a certain point, then you need to find the instantaneous velocity. Just like how it was difficult to determine the rate of change of a curve at a particular point, it was difficult before calculus to determine the instantaneous velocity of an object at a particular point. This is also due to functions having complicated curves. This is where the topics of limits and derivatives come in handy again to help you solve this problem to help you describe motion and rates of change for objects. Finally, let's take a look at area problems. There are various formulas out there to determine the area for traditional shapes, but what about non-traditional shapes caused by the curves of functions? This is where the topic of integrals becomes useful to help you find the exact area under a curve instead of using estimation methods such as summing rectangles. Integrals are perfect to use for a scenario like this one where you have the area between two curves. So here you have two parabolas, so a U looking curve and then an upside-down U looking curve. And let's say you wanted to find the shaded region where those curves intersect in the middle. That area would be a little difficult to determine if you did not have integrals. As you could tell from these three problems, the three major topics of calculus discussed in this course, which are limits, derivatives, and integrals, are critically needed to help solve these. Let's start off this calculus journey by exploring what a limit is in the next video.

What is a limit? (4 min 8 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you learned about tangent lines and velocity problems, you're ready to dive deep into the content of this chapter. Let's kick off with the first major topic of calculus, limits. Limits are used to define the value of a function f of x as the input approaches a defined point a. This is denoted with the following equation, where you have lim of x to a, which is the limit as x goes to a of f of x equals L. This means as your x is getting arbitrarily close to a, the value of the function f of x approaches L. This does not necessarily mean the function f of x needs to be defined at the point x equals a, just that as the function f of x approaches a, the value L exists. Note that this is not the precise definition of a limit, but that definition will be explained later on in this chapter. Let's break down the limit equation. So you had the equation lim of x to a of f of x equals L. The word lim represents the limit, which makes sense with it being an abbreviation of the word limit. Next, you have your variable x, and then you have the value you are approaching, which is a. Then you have your function f of x. And finally, your limit result L, which is any real number, or it can technically be positive or negative infinity. Let's look at what a limit can be like when you were looking at a graph. So let's say you have this curve and you see the red point at where x equals 2, you can then have the limit be as x is approaching 2. And so then you're gathering what that point equals on the y-axis as x is approaching 2. Note that the limit definition I gave you is technically for a two-sided limit. What this means is that you are calculating the limit as the function f of x approaches the value a from the left and the right side of the function. You'll learn about one-sided limits in the next video and see how they differ from two-sided limits. So why are limits so important? Limits provide a solid foundation for calculus, making them the essential building block needed to do basically any other concept in calculus. They are needed to define and understand topics such as derivatives and integrals. Now, there are times where the limit just does not work out. This is when the limit has no result, and you will then say, "This is when the limit does not exist." You can shorten this to DNE, usually capitalized, and note that this is frequently how you will see it notated in other resources. There are various reasons as to why the limit might not exist, and what it can mean if a limit does not exist, such as an asymptote or a jump in a function. This will be more thoroughly explained in the next chapter. There are various practical applications for limits. In physics, they are used in a variety of calculations, such as calculating instantaneous velocity, acceleration, and electric field strength. In engineering, they can be used to calculate stress and strain when building structures. Finally, in economics, they can be used to determine market equilibrium. This is just the beginning of limits. You'll continue to learn more about limits and see them in action throughout the rest of this course, including with derivatives and integrals. In the next video, let's explore what one-sided limits are.
One-sided limits (7 min)
- [Instructor] Now that you've learned what a limit is, let's take a step back and learn what a one-sided limit is. In this video, you'll learn what one-sided limits are and how they relate and differ from your regular two-sided limits. One-sided limits are what you calculate when you want to determine the limit of a function from just one direction. This will either be from the left or the right direction. In this case, you are only focused on the limit from that one direction and you don't care what it would be or not from the other direction. Let's take a look at the left limit first. Left limits are used to define the value that a function f of x approaches as the input approaches a defined point a from the left. This is denoted with the following equation where the limit as x approaches a from the left of f of x equals L. In this case, the left limit is denoted by the little minus sign above your value a. This means as your value x gets arbitrarily close to a, the value of your function f of x approaches L only where x is less than a. This does not necessarily mean that the function has to be defined at x equals a. What this does imply though is as the function approaches a from the left, the value of L for your limit exists. Note that the left limit does not care about what happens with values of x that are greater than a. Now let's switch gears and take a look at right limits. Right limits are used to define the value that a function f of x approaches as the input approaches a defined point a from the right. This is denoted with the following equation where the limit of x to a plus of f of x equals L. Like last time, L is the result of your limit. And then this time, the plus sign above the a is what denotes that it's a right limit versus the minus sign for the left limit. Similar to left limits, this means as your x is getting arbitrarily close to a, then the value of your function f of x approaches L only where x is greater than a. This does not necessarily mean that the function has to be defined at x equals a. What this does imply though is as the function approaches a from the right, the value L does exist. Note that the right limit does not care about what happens with values of x that are less than a. As you could tell, left and right limits are essentially opposites of each other, where you have one for each direction. Like I mentioned before, the main change in the function notation is the right hand limit has a plus sign and the left hand limit has a minus sign. And again, those will be above the values that you are taking the limit to. Be aware if these signs do show up in a limit problem that this is what they are indicating, that they are either a right or left limit. From what you have seen, one-sided limits are essentially a step down from a regular limit, also known as a two-sided limit. Since a regular limit must be defined in both directions versus just one direction, it holds a higher significance overall. Next, let's look at a more complete definition of a limit to see how one-sided and two-sided limits are related to each other. The limit of a function f of x as x approaches a defined point a exists if and only if, one, the left limit exists where you have the limit as x approaches a with that minus sign of f of x equals L1. Number two is the right limit exists where you have the limit as x approaches a with that plus sign and then of f of x, and this is equal to L2. And three, the left and right limits must equal each other. This means the limit as x approaches a from the left of f of x equals the limit as x approaches a from the right of f of x. This is also denoted by L1 equals L2. This more concrete definition of a limit further elaborates on the fact the left and right limits must exist in order for the overall limit to exist. This also ensures that these two limits must equal the same value in order for the limit to exist. Note that there are sometimes cases where the left and right limits do exist, but they don't equal each other. This causes the overall limit to not exist. Let's look at a quick example of this. Let's say you have the function f of x where it is equal to 2 for values of x less than 3, and it is equal to 5 for values of x greater than or equal to 3. Let's get the limit as x approaches 3 of your function f of x. Let's begin by getting the left limit of the function as x approaches 3 from the left. So, you will use your formula of limit as x approaches a from the left of f of x equals the limit as x approaches 3 from the left of 2. In this case, the limit of a constant value is just itself, so your limit equals 2 for your left limit. Now let's look at the right limit. Using your formula, you have the limit as x approaches a from the right of f of x equals the limit as x approaches 3 from the right of 5. Like last time, since it is a constant value, it just equals itself, which is 5 for your right limit. As you see, the limit as x approaches a from the left of f of x is not equal to the limit as x approaches a from the right of f of x. So even though the left and right limits do exist, they do not equal each other because 2 does not equal 5. This means the overall limit does not work and it is deemed as DNE, or does not exist. So you notate that as the limit as x approaches three of f of x equals DNE. Knowing about one-sided limits can help provide you a better overall understanding of how limits work. They can also be very useful for cases like the previous example where they can help you properly evaluate the limit of a function. Let's move on to some common limit properties.

Limit properties (7 min 10 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you are familiar with what a limit is, let's begin the process of learning how to calculate them. You'll start off by reviewing many of the common limit properties. I will list these limit properties in this video and then I'll show you how to calculate limits using these properties on the next chapter. The following limit properties I will show you include the following conditions. First, F of X and G of X are both functions that are approaching the value of X. Both A and C are real numbers that are used as constant values. N is a real number. And finally, L and M, both capitalized, are real numbers such that the limit as X goes to A of F of X equals capital L, and the limit as X goes to A of G of X is equal to capital M. Let's start with an easy one, the identity rule. The identity rule states if you're taking the limit of a function F of X equals X as X goes to A, then the limit is simply A. So as you can see here, you have the limit as X goes to A of X and you simply substitute in that value A for X, and this gets you a result of A. Let's look at another easy rule, the constant rule. This rule states if you're taking the limit of a constant value, then it will always be the constant itself. This makes sense since the limit of a number is always itself. So here you see you have the limit of X to A of the constant value C. And no matter what that value A is, the answer is always going to be C. Let's pivot to a little bit more complex rules starting with the sum rule. This rule states the limit of a sum is equal to the sum of its limits. This makes it easy to separate out functions and take their limits individually. So here you have the limit as X goes to A of F of X push GX is equal to the limit as X goes to A of F of X plus the limit as X goes to A of G of X. This is also equal to capital L plus capital M. If you remember before, L and M essentially represent the limits for F of X and G of X. Next, let's look at a similar rule, the difference rule. This rule states the limit of a difference is equal to the difference of its limits. Like with the sum rule, this rule makes it easy to separate out functions and take their limits individually. So you have the limit as X goes to A of F of X subtracted by G of X is equal to the limit as X goes to A of F of X minus the limit as X goes to A of G of X. And if you use this in the terms of just the limit results, you get capital L minus capital M. Now let's look at some multiplication beginning with the constant multiple rule. This rule states you can take a constant multiplied with a function outside of the limit. Then you can apply the constant multiple after the limit is taken. This is an easy way to simplify the process by having you just focus on getting the limit of the function itself and then dealing with the constant multiple later. So you have the limit as X goes to A of C multiplied by F of X, and that is going to be equal to C multiplied by the limit as X goes to A of F of X. You can also think of this as terms as C multiplied by capital L for the limit of F of X. Moving on with more multiplication, let's take a look at the product rule. The product rule states the limit of a product is equal to the product of its limits. Like with the sum difference rules, this makes it easy to separate out the functions to take their limits individually. So here you have the limit as X goes to A of F of X multiplied by G of X is equal to the limit as X goes to A of F of X multiplied by the limit as X goes to A of G of X. This is equal to their limit results of capital L multiplied by capital M. Next, let's take a look at the quotient rule, which is similar to the product rule where you can easily separate out the limit being taken. This rule states the limit of a quotient is equal to the quotient of its limits. Like I mentioned before, this rule allows you to separate out the functions to take their limits individually. The main caveat for this rule to keep in mind is the denominator must not equal zero. So here you have the limit of X goes to A of F of X divided by G of X is equal to the limit as X goes to A of F of X divided by the limit as X goes to A of G of X. You could think of this as terms as capital L divided by capital M, and again, remember this is for M not equal to zero, since if the denominator is equal to zero, then you cannot use this rule. Let's move on to the power rule. This rule functions similarly to the constant multiple rule. The power rule states if a function has a power N, then you can move the power outside of limit and apply it after the limit has been taken. This is another great way to simplify the process of taking a limit to just focus on the function itself. So here you have the limit as X goes to A of F of X to the N is equal to the limit as X goes to A of F of X, and then you'll take all of that to the N. You can also think of this as capital L to the N, where again, that's your limit result taken to that power N after the fact. Finally, let's take a look at the root rule. This is very similar to the power rule where this rule states if you have a function with a root N, you can move the root outside of the limit and then apply the root to the limit after it's been taken. This rule is essentially an extension of the power rule since roots are fractional powers. So here you have the limit as X goes to A of the root of N of F of X is equal to the root of N of the limit as X goes to A of F of X. You can also think of this as the root of N with your capital L representing your limit result. These limit properties can be used to help ease the process to calculate limits since they can become a bit tricky at times. Like I mentioned earlier, you will see these limit properties in action in a later video along with throughout the rest of the course. Now that she knows some basics about limits, let's pivot and take a look at a more detailed definition of exactly what a limit is by looking at the precise definition of a limit.

Precise definition of a limit (5 min 51 seconds)
- [Instructor] So far, you have learned what a limit is with a general definition, along with what one-sided limits are and how they reinforce that limit definition. Now let's take a look at a more technical definition of what a limit is. This more technical definition of a limit is sometimes called the precise definition of a limit. It is also sometimes referred to as the epsilon-delta definition of a limit. This is due to the epsilon and delta values both being used in this version of defining a limit. The precise definition of a limit states the limit of a function f of x as x approaches a is equal to L, so this is represented by the limit as x approaches a of f of x equals L. And this is for every epsilon greater than 0, then there exists a delta greater than 0, such that the absolute value of f of x minus L is less than epsilon whenever 0 is less than the absolute value of x minus a, which is less than delta. This definition is definitely more complex looking than the previous definition I showed you, so let's take a moment to break it down. The first part of this definition starts off with the typical limit definition, like you saw in the past. The epsilon greater than 0 is known as the universal quantifier. This represents the maximum allowable error difference between the function f of x and the limit L. The delta greater than 0 is called an existential quantifier. This represents how close x must be to a to ensure your function f of x stays within the error of the limit. Let's look at the two inequalities that are represented in the conditional statement for the precise definition of a limit. Your first equality is the absolute value of f of x minus L is less than epsilon. This inequality ensures the function values of f of x are close enough to the limit L. Your second inequality is 0 less than the absolute value of x minus a less than delta, and this inequality ensures x is close enough to a without it being exactly equal to a. There are few key items to take note of. First, it is important to note that there are technically an infinite amount of deltas that you can choose from. Generally though the smaller the delta, the higher the accuracy. You'll also want to note that the function needs to be defined around x equals a, but it does not necessarily have to be defined at exactly x equals a like it would with the regular limit definition. Let's see the precise definition of a limit in action. Let's use this definition to prove the limit as x approaches 2 of 3 multiplied by x plus 1 is equal to 7. First, you'll have your inequality, the absolute value of F of x minus L is less than epsilon. Now, you could substitute in your function f of x, which in this case is 3 multiplied by x plus 1. So you have the absolute value of 3 multiplied by x plus 1 minus 7, which is less than epsilon. You can simplify those two constant values to get the absolute value of 3 multiplied by x minus 6 is less than epsilon. Using the constant multiple rule, you can move out that 3 to then get 3 multiplied by the absolute value of x minus 2 is less than epsilon. Now, let's divide both sides by 3. Looking at your inequality, it looks like a good delta value to choose would be epsilon divided by 3. Now, let's bring in your second inequality, 0 less than the absolute value of x minus a less than delta. Plugging in your value of a, which in this case is 2, you'll get 0 less than the absolute value of x minus 2 less than delta. Now, you can multiply both sides by 3 since if you do that on both sides, it will essentially still be the same. So you have 3 multiplied by the absolute value of x minus 2, which is less than 3 multiplied by delta. You'll notice that 3 multiplied by delta is equal to epsilon because you had your delta equal to epsilon divided by 3. Since the absolute value of f of x minus 7 is less than epsilon, then this limit satisfies the precise definition of a limit using epsilon and delta of getting x arbitrarily close to the limit of 2. Why is this precise definition of a limit necessary with its complexity? First, the precise definition of a limit provides a mathematically rigorous method to understand how a limit works. This definition in turn is what is usually used when proving how limits work in the first place. It helps prove this by more precisely defining how close is close enough instead of simply stating it needs to be arbitrarily close when taking a limit. Now you should have a very solid understanding of what a limit is, not only with the regular definition, but with the precise definition. I suggest taking time on your own to further explore the definition of a limit. Again, whether it is the regular definition or the precise definition. Let's wrap up this chapter by taking a look at what infinite limits are.

Infinite limits (7 min 7 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this introduction of limits by discussing infinite limits. I will discuss a few different scenarios in this video regarding infinite limits. Let's begin with a definition. Infinite limits are what occurs when the limit of a function is equal to positive or and negative infinity. This can occur for both one-sided and two-sided limits. These limits show up on a regular basis, so it is important to be aware of them and understand what they can potentially be showing you. Let's look at two versions of this. First, the limit of a function f of x is equal to infinity. If f of x increases without bound as x approaches a, this is represented by the limit as x approaches a of f of x equals infinity. This means as your x is getting arbitrarily close to a, the value of the function f of x approaches positive infinity. Now let's look at the other version of this. The limit of a function f of x is equal to negative infinity if f of x decreases without bound as x approaches a This is represented by the limit as x approaches a of f of x equals negative infinity. This means as your x is getting arbitrarily close to a, the value of the function f of x approaches negative infinity. Remember, both of these definitions do not imply the function has to be defined at the point x equals a. What these definitions do say though is as the function f of x approaches a point a, the value of positive or negative infinity exists. You can define both of these scenarios as left or right limits by simply adding the plus or minus signs accordingly to indicate which direction it is going to indicate whether the right or the left limit is going to positive or negative infinity. So what does a limit equaling positive or negative infinity imply? If the left, right, or two-sided limit of a function equals positive or negative infinity, then you can assume there is a vertical asymptote for your function f of x at the point x equals a. On this graph, you can look at two different vertical asymptote for this function, so you'll see where the value of two is. You have these blue lines where it looks like an L and a flipped L on the right and left sides, and then an upside down U on the bottom of the graph. You'll notice where the red dotted line around x equals two and x equals negative two are two vertical asymptotes. This means that this function will never quite reach that value of two or negative two for x because it is either going to positive or negative infinity, which in this case, it's actually doing that simultaneously. Let's look at a few properties when dealing with multiple functions where one of the limits equals positive infinity. Let's assume you have the functions f of x and g of x where the limit as x approaches a of f of x equals infinity and the limit is x approaches a of g of x equals capital L. If this is the case, then you can assume the following statements. So number one, the limit as x approaches a of f of x plus g of x is equal to infinity. If capital L is greater than zero, which is your limit for your function g of x, then the limit is x approaches a of f of x multiplied by g of x is equal to infinity. If capital L less than zero, which is again your limit for g of x, then the limit is x approaches a of f of x multiplied by g of x is equal to negative infinity. And finally, the limit as x approaches a of f of x divided by g of x equals zero. Note that you can simply switch the sign of the result for all four these properties if you're working with the case where the limit is approaching negative infinity. Next, let's take a look at a related scenario dealing with infinity and limits. It is important to note that you can actually take the limit of x going to positive or negative infinity. This is where you would have a equal to positive or negative infinity in your limit showing that x approaches positive or negative infinity. In this scenario, you are essentially putting an infinitely large number in the place of x to evaluate the result. There are various outcomes you can get when taking the limit to infinity, whether it's positive or negative of a function. First, you can simply get a numerical value such as zero or 10, and this is common for rational functions. Option two is you can get positive or negative infinity, which is common for polynomial functions. Third, there may be oscillating behavior where it's oscillating between two different numbers, and this is common for trigonometric functions. Finally, it can have a limit that does not exist, which is represented by DNE, which is common for discontinuous functions. Before, you learn about vertical asymptotes when it came to infinite limits, but what about horizontal asymptotes? If the left, right, or two-sided limit of a function f of x is taken to positive or negative infinity and equals a constant value, capital L for the limit, then you can assume there is a horizontal asymptote that exists for function f of x at the limit capital L. In this graph, you can look at a horizontal asymptote. So you'll notice there is a vertical asymptote at x equals zero. But let's look at the case where y equals two. So you'll see these look like two Ls that are flipped, so you have the blue lines going down, and then you have that red dotted line going across the graph showing where the horizontal asymptote is. So this is showing that the function will never quite reach y equals two because it'll simply be going out in those two directions. Throughout the rest of the course, you'll frequently see infinite limits and limits taken to infinity. It is important to understand how to work with them and what their behavior represents, such as vertical and horizontal asymptotes. This wraps up the basics of limits and their key properties. Remember, limits are a key concept for calculus and help define the value that a function approaches as the input approaches a defined point. Limits will be the key focus throughout the rest of the course, even when they aren't obvious. In the next chapter, you will explore various methods to evaluate limits.

Day 03
Evaluating Limits 
Methods to calculate limits (2 min 2 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you will explore various methods to calculate limits. Remember, a limit helps define the value that a function approaches as the input approaches a defined point. Let's start off by reviewing the methods you're going to learn in this chapter. The methods you'll explore to calculate limits are direct substitution, using their properties, using factoring, working with limits for common functions, such as trigonometric functions, implementing the squeeze theorem, and determining when the limit does not exist. Note that this does not encompass all methods available to calculate limits, but it focuses on the common methods used in most limit scenarios. The method of direct substitution is fairly straightforward. This method is where you simply substitute the value a that x is approaching directly for x. For example, the limit as x approaches 3 for the function x squared would simply be 3 squared, which equals 9. I will frequently use this method throughout the rest of the course. Each of the following videos will be structured similarly to give you a general overview of each method for calculating limits. Each video will provide you with the important definitions of the method, any properties associated with the method, and multiple examples of how to calculate limits using the method. It is important to know how to not only understand limits, but how to calculate them in various scenarios as well. Limits are fundamental to calculus, so understanding how to calculate them will help you better understand other key concepts later on, such as derivatives and integrals. The goal of the following videos is to provide you with working knowledge on how to implement these various methods of calculating limits. You can take this knowledge and expand upon it in your work and education. Let's get started with calculating limits using their properties.

Calculate limits using properties, part 1 (9 min 50 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's start off by using the various limit properties to calculate limits. You will use the properties discussed in the previous chapter. Let's first refresh you on what those limit properties are. So here, you have the limit properties starting with the identity rule. So you have the limit as x goes to a of x is equal to the value of a. Next, you have the constant rule, which is the limit as x goes to a of c is equal to the constant itself, c. After that, you have the constant multiple rule. So you have the limit as x goes to a of c multiplied by f of x is equal to c multiplied by the limit as x goes to a of f of x, which is equal to c multiplied by capital L. Next, you have the sum rule, which is the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus g of x is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus the limit as x goes to a of g of x, which equals capital L plus capital M. Next is the difference rule. So you have the limit as x goes to a of f of x minus g of x is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x minus limit as x goes to a of g of x is equal to capital L minus capital M. After that, you have the product rule, so you have limit as x goes to a of f of x multiplied by g of x is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x multiplied by the limit as x goes to a of g of x, which is equal to capital L multiplied by capital M. Then, you have the quotient rule. So you have the limit as x goes to a of f of x divided by g of x is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x divided by the limit as x goes to a of g of x, which is equal to capital L divided by capital M. And remember, for this particular rule, you need to make sure capital M, which is the limit of your denominator, is not equal to zero. After that, you have the power rule, which is the limit as x goes to a of f of x to the n is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x, and then you'll take all of that to the n, which is equal to capital L to the n. Finally, you have the root rule, which is an extension of the power rule where you have the limit as x goes to a of root n of f of x is equal to root n of limit as x goes to a of f of x, which is equal to root n of capital L. Let's start off with an easy example by using the identity rule. Let's get the limit as x approaches five for the value x. The identity rule states that the limit of this function is simply going to be the value it's going to. So using that identity rule as the limit of x going to a of x is equal to a. So in this case, you'll have the limit as x goes to five of x is simply equal to five. Let's look at another easy example using the constant rule. Let's get the limit as x approaches 15 for the constant value of seven. The constant rule states the limit of a constant is always itself where you have the limit as x goes to a of c is equal to c. Using this rule, you have the limit as x goes 15 of seven is equal to seven. Note that the answer will always be seven regardless of where you take the limit of x to, including positive or negative infinity. Let's move on to some example where you have multiple functions involved. Let's start by using the sum rule to get the limit as x goes to five of six plus x. So the sum rule states that you can separate out these functions, six and x, take their limits, and then add up their results. So this means you have the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus g of x is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus the limit as x goes to a of g of x equals capital L plus capital M. So this means you could separate out your limit as limit x goes to five of six plus x is equal to the limit as x goes to five of six plus the limit as x goes to five of x. Let's solve these limits separately. So beginning with the limit as x goes to five of six using the constant rule that is going to equal six. Next, you have the limit as x goes to five of x. Using the identity rule, the solution for this one is equal to five. Now, let's add your two limit results, L plus M, which in this case is equal to six plus five, which equals 11. So this means using the sum rule, the limit as x goes to five of six plus x is equal to 11. Let's take a look at another sum rule example. Let's get the limit as x goes to two of x squared plus x plus four. Here, you have the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus g of x plus h of x, and you can equal this to the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus the limit as x goes to a of g of x plus the limit as x goes to a of h of x. This is a great example of how you can extend the sum rule to work for more than just two functions. So in this case, you have the limit as x goes to two of x squared plus x plus four equals the limit as x goes to two of x squared plus the limit as x goes to two of x plus the limit as x goes to two of four. So with the first one, you have the limit as x goes to two of x squared. You can use direct substitution to simply get two squared, which equals four. For the second limit, limit as x goes to two of x, this uses the identity rule. So the solution is simply the value you're going to, which is two. Finally, you have the limit as x goes two of four, and using the constant rule, the result is four. So the limit as x goes to two of x squared plus x plus four is equal to four plus two plus four, which equals 10. Let's look at a few similar examples, but this time, let's use the difference rule. Let's get the limit as x goes to two of x cubed minus five. In this case, you have the limit as x goes to a, so you'll use the difference rule limit as x goes to a of f of x minus g of x equals the limit as x goes to a of f of x minus the limit as x goes to a of g of x, which is equal to capital L minus capital M. So in this case, you had the limit as x goes to two of x cubed minus five equals the limit as x goes to two of x cubed minus the limit as x goes to two of five. So let's get the separate limits. So you have the limit as x goes to two of x cubed equals two cubed using direct substitution, which gives you a result of eight. And then, you have the limit as x goes to two of five, using the constant rule, is equal to five. Now, you have L minus M, which equals eight minus five, which equals three. So this means your final result as the limit as x goes to two of x cubed minus five is equal to three. Let's look at an example where you combine the sum and difference rules together. This time, let's get the limit as x goes two infinity of x to the fourth plus x minus seven. So in this case, you use the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus g of x minus h of x is equal to the limit as x goes to a of f of x plus the limit as x goes to a of g of x minus the limit as x goes to a of h of x. This means your limit as x goes to infinity for x to the fourth plus x minus five is equal to the limit as x goes to infinity of x to the fourth plus the limit as x goes to infinity of x minus the limit as x goes to infinity of seven. So let's get these three different limits. So you have the limit as x goes to infinity of x to the fourth, which is equal to infinity to the fourth using direct substitution, which equals infinity. Next, you have the limit as x goes to infinity of x, which using the identity rule is just equal to infinity. And finally, you have the limit as x goes to infinity of seven, which is equal to itself, which is seven. Calculate limits using properties, part 2 (8 min 59 seconds)

Remember what the quotient rule, the big theme is to make sure your denominator M is not equal to zero in order to use this rule. Now you'll do capital L divided by capital M for your two limit results, which is equal to four divided by five. This means your limit as x goes two of x squared divided by x plus three is equal to 4/5. Let's look at another example of the quotient rule. This time let's get the limit as x goes two infinity of 10 divided by x squared. So remember, your quotient rule, limit as x goes to A of F of x divided by G of x is equal to the limit as x goes to A of F of x divided by the limit as x goes to A of G of x, which is equal to capital L divided by capital M, for M, not equal to zero. So in this case, you'll have the limit as x goes to infinity of 10 divided by x squared is equal to the limit as x goes to infinity of 10 divided by the limit as x goes to infinity of x squared. Let's get these two limit results. So you have the limit as x goes two infinity of 10, and using that constant rule, it is equal 10 even though it is going all the way to infinity because it is a constant value. Next, you get the limit as x goes to infinity of x squared, which using direct substitution you get infinity squared, which is still equal to infinity. Now you have capital L divided by capital M, which in this case equals 10 divided by infinity, which simplifies to zero. This is because the fraction result that you get is divided by an infinitely large term, making it so small that it becomes zero. So this means your final result of the limit as x goes two infinity of 10 divided by x squared is equal to zero. Let's move on to the last set of rules that function similarly to the constant multiple rule where you'll be taking out powers and roots, starting with the power rule. Let's get the limit as x goes to four of x minus one cubed. So this is a great situation to use the power rule to make it a little bit easier to solve. So here you have the limit as x goes to A of F of x to the N is equal to the limit as x goes to A of F of x and take all of that to the N, which is equal to capital L to the N. So again, you're basically taking that power out and putting it in at the very end after you get your limit result. So here you have the limit as x goes to four of x minus one cubed is equal to the limit as x goes to four of x minus one, and then you'll cube that limit result. So the limit as x goes four of x minus one is equal to four minus one using direct substitution, which gets you three. So now you can add in your power of N, which in this case is three. So you get L to the N, which equals three to the three, which equals 27. This means the limit as x goes four of x minus one cubed is equal to 27. Let's wrap up by looking at an example of using the root rule. In this case, you'll have the limit as x goes two of the square root of x cubed minus two multiplied by x. This is a great example to use the root rule, since this would be a little bit more complicated to potentially solve otherwise. So here you have the limit as x goes to A of the root N of F of x is equal to the root N of the limit as x goes to A of F of x, which is equal to the root N of capital L for your limit result. Here you get the limit as x goes to two of the square root of x cubed minus two multiplied by x, which is equal to the square root of the limit of x goes to two of x cubed minus two multiplied by x. Let's get the limit of the interior portion of the root. So you get the limit as x goes two of x cubed minus two multiplied by x. Using substitution, you get two cubed minus two multiplied by two, which is equal to eight minus four, which equals four. Next, you'll use your root rule to the root N of L, which in this case is the square root of four, which equals two. So here you have the limit as x equals two of the square root of x cube minus two multiplied by x, which equals two for your final result. Excellent job working through these limit property examples. Calculate limits using factoring (7 min 7 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this video, you'll explore how to calculate limits Using factoring. This technique will be necessary for some polynomial and rational function limits, so it's very important to know. Let's begin by learning what factoring is. Factoring is a technique used to simplify expressions. The way it accomplishes this is by rewriting the expression to cancel out any common factors. This method is needed when you try using direct substitution, but you get an indeterminate form as the result. The goal is to use this method to change your expression into an expression you can properly calculate a limit for. So what is an indeterminate form? The following is a list of indeterminate forms you may come across. You might have zero divided by zero, zero multiplied by infinity. Infinity divided by infinity, infinity minus infinity, infinity to the zero, zero to the zero, or one to infinity. Note that zero divided by zero, infinity minus infinity, and infinity divided by infinity tend to be the most common forms you'll come across. Let's go over the steps of how to calculate limits using factoring. Step one, you will use direct substitution on an expression to identify if an indeterminate form exists and what that indeterminate form is. Step two, you'll factor the numerator and or denominator to provide common factors throughout the expression. In this case, you can use conjugates as needed. Step three, you'll replace the original expression with the factored version of the expression. Step four, you'll simplify the expression including canceling out any common factors. And step five, you'll use direct substitution to solve the simplified expression. Let's see this in action with a few examples. Let's look at the function F of X equals x squared minus four. Divide all that by X minus two. Let's get the limit as x goes to two of F of X, which is equal to the limit as x goes to two of x squared minus four divided by X minus two. Step one says that you should try and use direct substitution and see what happens. So in this case, you have two squared minus four divided by two minus two, which equals zero divided by zero. Since this is an indeterminate form, it may be a great case to use factoring to help you find the limit. Since your denominator is already simplified as much as possible, you'll want to factor your numerator and try to see if you can get at least one of its factors to match the denominator, which was X minus two. So let's factor X squared minus four, and this can factor into X minus two and x plus two. So note that when it comes to factoring, essentially the right side will then multiply through to get what you have on the left side. So you could kind of think of it as like reversing the process. Now you have the limit as X goes two of X minus two multiplied by x plus two divided by X minus two, and here you could cancel out the X minus two since they are a common factor on the top and bottom. And so now you are just getting the limit as X goes two of the expression of x plus two. Now you can use direct substitution to get your results. So you have the limit as X goes two of x plus two equals two plus two, which equals four. So this means your final result for your limit as X goes two of X squared minus four divided by X minus two is equal to four. This is a perfect example of how you went from an indeterminate form to an actual result of four for your limit. Let's look at another example where you have F of X equal to the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by X, and then outside of that square root you have minus x. This time you'll want to try and get the limit as X goes to infinity of F of X, which in this case is equal to the limit as X goes to infinity of the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by x and then minus x on the outside of that. If you use direct substitution to solve this, you get the square root of infinity squared plus four multiplied by infinity, and then outside of that you get minus infinity. This gets you an indeterminate result of infinity minus infinity, making it a great case for factoring. In this example, you'll want to use a conjugate in order to simplify your expression. So in this case, your conjugate for the expression of square root of x squared plus four multiplied by X minus x is then going to be multiplied by the conjugate divided by itself. So you have the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by X plus x and divide that by itself. Once you multiply this through, you get the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by X minus x multiplied by the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by X plus x, and then you'll divide all that by the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by x plus x. If you multiply the top together, you then get x squared plus four multiplied by x minus x squared, and then still divide that by the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by x plus x. Since the x squared cancel out on the top portion, you then get four multiplied by x divided by the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by x plus x. You can then divide X throughout all of your fraction to get four divided by the square root of one plus four divided by x plus one. Now you can use this new expression with direct substitution to get your limit. So let's get the limit as X goes to infinity of four divided by the square root of one plus four divided by x, and then plus one outside of that. So if you use this, you'll get four divided by the square root of one plus four divided by infinity plus one. In this case, your four divided by infinity will go to zero. Since the limit as X goes to infinity of one divided by X is zero due to it being such a tiny number at infinity. So this means you have four divided by the square root of one plus zero, and then plus one outside of that. This is equal to four divided by two, which is equal to two. This means your final result as the limit as x goes to infinity of the square root of x squared plus four multiplied by X minus x is equal to two. Factoring can be a bit complicated to wrap your head around at first, but it will get easier with practice. The keys are learning how to find those common factors along with understanding how to use conjugates to your advantage like you saw with the second example. Let's use this knowledge and the limit properties to learn how to calculate limits for polynomial and rational functions in the next video.

Calculate the limits of polynomial and rational functions (8 min 32 seconds)
- [Speaker] Now that you've learned how to calculate limits using their properties and factoring, let's put that together to learn how to calculate limits of some common functions. The rule for polynomials is the term what the highest degree determines the limit result. So if you have the limit as x goes to infinity of x squared plus x minus one, you would then look at that x squared term and infinity squared is just equal to infinity, so that would be your limit result. Let's look at an example of a polynomial limit. Let's switch over to rational limits. Rational functions in general tend to work similarly to polynomial functions where often, you just use the direct substitution method. One of the big differences though, is you need to be aware if the denominator of your rational function equals zero, since this may cause the limit to not exist. Now, what do you do if the limit goes to positive or negative infinity for rational limits? There are three main cases you should be aware of. First, if the numerator has a lower degree, then the denominator, then the limit will go to zero. When I'm talking about degree here, I'm talking about the degree of the polynomial on the top and bottom portion. So for example, if there's x squared on the top and x cubed on the bottom, then the top would have a degree of two, and the bottom would have a degree of three. And again, that would mean that the limit would go to zero. The second case is if the numerator and denominator have equal degrees, then the limit will be the ratio of the leading coefficients. So let's say you had two multiplied by x cubed, divided by five, multiplied by x cubed. In this case, then the limit would just be two divided by five. Third case is if the numerator has a higher degree than the denominator, then the limit will go to positive or negative infinity depending on the signs of the coefficients. So let's say you have x cubed divided by x squared. So in this case, the numerator would have the higher degree than the denominator. You would then have it go to positive infinity for your limit result. Let's take a look at an example of getting a rational limit. So let's say you have f of x equals three multiplied by x squared plus five divided by x minus two. Let's get the limit as x goes to three. So you have three multiplied by x squared plus five divided by x minus two. And let's substitute that value three and wherever you have your x, so here you have three multiplied by three squared plus five divided by three minus two. This simplifies to 27 plus five divided by one, which equals 32 for your final limit result. Now, what do you do in the case where you want to get a limit of a rational function such as f of x equals four multiplied by x squared minus six, multiplied by x minus 18 divided by x squared minus nine, and you come across one of those indeterminate forms I mentioned prior. This is where you can then use factoring like you learned in the previous video. So if you look at this particular example where you have, let's say the limit as x goes to three of four multiplied by x squared minus six multiplied by x minus 18, divide all that by x squared minus nine, and you plug in that value three where you have x, you get four multiplied by three squared minus six multiplied by three minus 18 divided by three squared minus nine, which is equal to zero divided by zero. So this is a great case where you can then simplify this limit expression using factoring by rewriting and canceling out any common factors in order to get your limit. So let's factor out your rational expression four multiplied by x squared minus six, multiplied by X minus 18 divided by x squared minus nine. In this case, you can factor out the denominator of x squared minus nine to simply be x minus three multiplied by x plus three. You can start with a denominator in this case since it's a little bit more straightforward factor, and it can help give you an idea of how to factor the numerator in order to find a common factor to cancel out. Now, let's factor the numerator. First, you'll adjust your numerator expression a little bit to be four multiplied by x squared minus six multiplied by X minus 18 to transform it to be four multiplied by x squared minus 12 multiplied by x plus six multiplied by x minus 18. Note that the right side is equal to the left side. It's just now you have minus 12 plus six for x instead of just minus six. Since this will allow you to factor and get the common factor that we are looking for. Let's separate out two different portions of your equation. So let's look at the first two terms, four multiplied by x squared minus 12 multiplied by x, which in this case, you can factor that to be four multiplied by x and then x minus three. And then the second two terms, you have six multiplied by x minus 18, which you can factor that to be six multiplied by x minus three. So if you put this together, you have four multiplied by x squared minus six multiplied by x minus 18 equals six, multiplied by x minus three, multiplied by four, multiplied by x, multiplied by X minus three. You can then combine this to be four multiplied by x plus six, and then multiply that by x minus three. This will make it easier when it comes to simplifying your rational function. So now it's finally factored out from four multiplied by x squared minus six, multiplied by x minus 18 divided by x minus nine, which is equal to four multiplied by x plus six multiplied by x minus three. Divide that by x minus three, multiplied by x plus three. You can then cancel out your common factor of x minus three to get you a new result of four multiplied by x plus six divided by x plus three four year function. So let's get the limit as x goes to three a four multiplied by x plus six divided by x plus three. Using direct substitution, you get four multiplied by three plus six divided by three plus three. This equals 12 plus six divided by six, which equals three. This means your finite limit result as x goes to three of four multiplied by x squared minus six, multiplied by x minus 18 divided by x squared minus nine is equal to three. The limits for these types of functions tend to be straightforward once you know how to use dark substitution, the limit rules, and factoring when it comes to rational functions, the main difficult portion is when you're using that factoring with the rational functions, and sometimes they're not quite as straightforward. Let's review some common exponential limits. Starting with your general exponential functions of A to the X, you have the limit as X goes to infinity of A to the X is equal to infinity if A is greater than one. If you have the limit as X goes to negative infinity of A to the X, this is equal to zero if A is greater than one. If you get the limit as X goes to infinity of A to the X, this will equal zero if A is between zero and one. And finally, if the limit as X goes to negative infinity of A to the X is equal to infinity, if A is between zero and one. Next, you have the limit as X goes to infinity of A to the X is equal to infinity. Then the limit as X goes to negative infinity of A to the X is equal to zero. Now, let's flip those exponent signs so you get the limit as X goes to infinity of A to the negative X is equal to zero, and then the limit as X goes to negative infinity of A to the negative X is equal to infinity. Calculate limits using the squeeze theorem (7 min 41 seconds)
- [Instructor] So far, you have learned about how to use a variety of techniques to calculate limits, such as using direct substitution, limit rules and factoring. What do you do though, if these methods don't work? This is when the Squeeze Theorem becomes useful. The Squeeze Theorem allows you to calculate the limit of a function by squeezing it between two other functions, hence the name, "Squeeze Theorem". The way it works is the two other functions have to have their limits equal to each other at the point being evaluated. This theorem is sometimes called the Sandwich Theorem, or Pinching Theorem. Let's look at a technical definition of this theorem. Let F of X, G of X, and H of X all be functions over an open interval between A and B. If F of X is less than or equal to G of X, which is less than or equal to H of X, and for A, less than or equal to C, less than or equal to B, such that the limit as X goes to C of F of X equals the limit as X goes to C of H of X, which equals the capital limit L, then you have the limit as X goes C of G of X is equal to that same limit result, L. This allows you to use the limit result from one of the other functions that are easier to calculate in order to get the limit of the more difficult function you are working with. So why is the Squeeze Theorem needed in the first place? The Squeeze Theorem can be proven very useful to calculate limits for situations where typical analytical techniques don't work, such as direct substitution. It is also helpful when taking limits of trigonometric functions, or when dealing with oscillatory terms. So how do you calculate a limit using the Squeeze Theorem? The steps to use the theorem are, step one, first, try solving the expression using a method such as direct substitution. If it can't be solved, then you can try using the Squeeze Theorem. Step two, bound the expression with an inequality. Step three, modify the inequality to gather the full function using algebra throughout the whole inequality. This may include adding, subtracting, multiplying, or dividing. Just make sure you do it through all three parts of the inequality. Step four, evaluate the left and right hand limits, which are found on the outside of your inequality. And finally, step five, apply the Squeeze Theorem if the limits equal each other in order to get your overall limit of the function you are looking at. This theorem can be a bit confusing at first to understand, so let's review a few examples on how to use this. Let's look at the limit as X goes to zero of X squared, multiplied by E, to the negative one divided by x squared. If you use direct substitution for this, you get the limit as X goes to zero of X squared, multiplied by E, to the negative one divided by X squared. This equals zero squared multiplied by E to the negative one divided by zero squared. Since you can't divide by zero, this is a great scenario when you could potentially use the Squeeze Theorem. Now that you've tried solving it but can't, you can now bound your expression, which in this case makes sense to focus on the term, E to the negative one divided by X squared, since that is the one that is giving you issues. Now, the lowest this expression can go is zero, and the highest it can go is one. So this gives you the inequality of zero, less than or equal to E to the negative one divided by X squared, which is less than or equal to one. Now let's modify this inequality to get your full function. So to do this, you need to multiply X squared throughout this inequality. So here, you have X squared multiplied by zero, is less than or equal to X squared multiplied by E to the negative one, divided by X squared, which is less than or equal to X squared multiplied by one. If you simplify this, you have left side just equal to zero, and you'll have less than or equal to X squared, multiplied by E to the negative one divided by X squared, which is the function you were originally trying to get the limit for. And then finally, the right side is less than or equal to just X squared. Let's evaluate the left and right hand limits as X goes to zero. So, as the limit as X goes to zero for zero, is just equal to zero since it is a constant term. Next, for the limit as X goes to zero of X squared, using direct substitution, you get zero squared, which equals zero. Since the limit as X goes to zero of zero is equal to the limit as X goes to zero of X squared, meaning zero equals zero, that means that you can apply the Squeeze Theorem. This is because the left and right hand limits equal each other, and so you can then use this as your final limit result. So you have the limit as X goes to zero of X squared multiplied by E to the negative one, divided by X squared is equal to zero. Let's look at another example where you can get a taste of using this, but this time with trigonometric functions. Let's get the limit as X goes to infinity of sine of one divided by X, and divide all that by X. Let's get the limit as X goes to infinity of sine of one divided by X, divided by X, using direct substitution. If you use this, you get the sine of one divided by infinity, divided by infinity. Since the sine function has oscillating behavior as X goes to infinity, this is a great scenario to use the Squeeze Theorem. Let's bound this expression in an inequality, and it makes sense to focus on the sine of one divided by X term, since this seems to be the one giving us the most problems at the moment. Now the lowest sine of X can go is negative one, and the highest it can go is one. You can apply the same to this expression as long as X does not equal zero. This is because it will oscillate between these two values. This gives you an inequality of negative one is less than or equal to sine of one divided by X, which is less than or equal to one. Now let's modify the inequality to get your full expression. So you have one divided by X, multiplied by negative one is less than or equal to one divided by X, multiplied by sine of one divided by X, and that is less than or equal to one divided by X, multiplied by one. Simplify this to get negative one divided by X is less than or equal to one divided by X, multiplied by sine of one divided by x, which is less than or equal to one divided by X. Let's evaluate your left and right hand limits as X goes to infinity. So the limit as X goes to infinity of negative one divided by X is equal to negative one divided by infinity, which goes to zero because it ends up being such a small number. Next, let's get the limit as X goes to infinity of one divided by X, which equals one divided by infinity, which also goes to zero since again, it is such a small number at that point. This means your limit as X goes to infinity of negative one divided by X is equal to the limit as X goes to infinity of one divided by X, meaning zero equals zero. So this means your final result of the limit as X goes to infinity of sine of one divided by X, divided by X, is equal to zero. This theorem can be a bit tricky to get used to at first, but as you saw with a few examples, it can be incredibly useful. I suggest taking time on your own to continue practice using this theorem. In the next video, you'll see this theorem in action as you learn how to calculate limits of common trigonometric functions.

When limits fail to exist (5 min 33 seconds)
- [Instructor] Time to wrap up this chapter and topic of limits. So far, I have discussed a variety of scenarios and methods to calculate limits, but what happens when you are unable to calculate a limit and get a valid answer? There are a variety of scenarios where you try to calculate limits but are unable to do so. These scenarios are when you say the limit does not exist or it is sometimes shortened to DNE. What this means is that there's no solution for the limit. What about when the limit equals positive or negative infinity? In previous examples you saw that sometimes limits equal positive or negative infinity, but are these truly valid limit solutions? Well, you can declare a limit equals positive or negative infinity and find use in interpreting that limit. Note that the limit technically does not exist since the result is not a real number. Let's review some of the common scenarios for when limits don't exist. Some of these scenarios you may have come across so far in these chapters. I'll explain each scenario and provide a quick example to solidify the reasoning behind it. The first scenario is when the left and right limits don't match. This was mentioned earlier when discussing one-sided limits. This is incredibly common when working with piecewise functions. For example, let's say you have the limit as X goes to zero, for the function, the absolute value of X divided by X. If you get the limit as X approaches the right, so the limit of X, the zero plus of the absolute value of X divided by X. This equals one. If you get the limit as X approaches from the left, you get the limit of X approaches zero minus is absolute value of X divided by X is equal to negative one. This means your right and left limits do not equal each other because one does not equal negative one in this case. So then you will state the limit as X goes to zero of absolute value of X divided by X is equal to DNE, as in the limit does not exist. The second scenario is when there is unbounded behavior. Like I mentioned earlier, this is when your limits are equal to positive or negative infinity. This type of scenario can indicate there might be a vertical asymptote in your function. For example, if you try and get the limit as X goes to zero of one divided by X squared, this is equal to positive infinity. This is because this function proceeds to get infinitely large the smaller and closer it gets to zero. Because the limit is infinity though, this technically means that the limit does not exist, so you could write it as the limit as X goes to zero of one divided by X squared equals DNE. The next scenario is oscillating behavior. This is when the function infinitely oscillates as it approaches a point. The point being approached can be positive or negative infinity in this scenario. Since the limit will not settle on one value, this will cause the limit to not exist, because again, there is no certain value that you could say it stays at. The most common example for this is trigonometric functions such as sine of X approaching infinity. Since this function oscillates between the values of negative one to one. So here if you take the limit as X goes to infinity of sine of X, and again, you know that sine of X goes between negative one and one, then you would say the limit as X approaches infinity for sine of x equals DNE, as in it does not exist. Another scenario is when the function is not defined at the point where you are trying to take the limit. Since the function is not defined at that point, then you'll be unable to get a limit at that point. For example, let's say you want to get the limit as X goes to negative two of the square root of X. Since you're unable to take the square root of negative numbers and get a real number result, the limit of this function does not exist, so you would put DNE. The final scenario is where part of a limit may not exist by these standards such as sine of X, but when you combine it with a function such as sine of X divided by X, then you can find the limit using a method like the squeeze theorem. If you are unable to do that though, then sometimes the limit will just still not exist, so again, you would say the limit does not exist. As you continue evaluating limits, you will come across scenarios where the limit does not exist. It is good to understand how to discern when a limit does not exist, why it does not exist, and what this might indicate such as a vertical asymptote. Also be aware there are sometimes cases where the limits may appear to not exist, but might actually be possible to calculate using methods such as the squeeze theorem. This wraps up learning how to evaluate limits. I showed you a variety of methods and scenarios to ensure you have a solid foundation since limits are so important for calculus. Remember, practice is what will help you continue to solidify the knowledge you are learning throughout this course. In the next chapter, you will explore the concept of continuity, which will heavily use limits throughout that chapter.
