Day 04
Continuity
What is continuity? (3 min 21 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you will explore the topic of continuity. Continuity is an important concept to know in order to better understand how limits work with functions, and later on, how to work with derivatives and integrals. Let's start off with understanding exactly what continuity is. Continuity is when you determine if a mathematical function is continuous or discontinuous. The easiest way to visualize this is imagine you are drawing a graph of a function with a pen on a piece of paper. If the pen never lifts as you are drawing, then the function is continuous. If the pen does lift at some point while you are drawing, then the function is discontinuous. Speaking of visualizing, let's take a look at a few examples of graphs of functions that are continuous and discontinuous. The function to the left, that is in blue, is a continuous function, since there are no obvious breaks in the function, and it is one smooth curve. The function that is in red is discontinuous because you'll notice it starts off with kind of a parabola function, and then there's a jump down at the value of x equals two to a different part of the function, and so you'll notice that the line is not fully connected. So again, if you had your pen, you would have to then lift it and then put it back down on the paper at x equals two in order to continue drawing the curve. So why is continuity so important? Knowing if a function is continuous will allow you to perform certain operations on the function at certain points or intervals. This includes being able to do derivatives. Continuity is also crucial for certain mathematical theorems to work such as the intermediate value theorem. Speaking of points and intervals, in the following two videos, you'll explore the definitions for having continuity at a point versus continuity on an interval. This is because there are some functions that only have continuity at certain points or within certain intervals. This is different than other functions that are continuous over their entire domain. As far as functions that are continuous over their entire domains, these include polynomial functions, rational functions, trigonometric functions, exponential functions, and logarithmic functions. This also includes inverse functions, but note that their original functions must be continuous and one-to-one, meaning each value in the domain matches a unique value in the range. You may notice this includes the majority of functions that you have explored so far in this course. This is because continuous functions tend to be the most relevant ones to work with when it comes to calculus. In summary, you have learned that continuity is how you measure whether a function is continuous or discontinuous. Over the following videos, you will expand upon this topic and dive into further detail with more specific definitions and examples. Let's start with exploring continuity at a point.

Continuity at a point (5 min 47 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's start off by exploring continuity at a point. This means you are looking to see if a function is continuous at a particular point, such as 0 or 20. Note that this will only be checking for a point in particular, and it does not mean that the function is automatically continuous at all points in its domain. Let's jump into a formal definition. A function f is continuous at a point x equals a where the three following conditions are met: First, the function f must be defined at x equals a. Second, the function must have a limit that exists as x approaches a. And third, the limit as x approaches a must have f of x equals f of a. To expand upon the last condition, note that the limit as x approaches a must work for the left limit, right limit, and the overall limit. If this is not the case, then the function is discontinuous at the point x equals a. Let's look at this in a few examples. Let's begin with a simple example where f of x equals x^3. Let's say you want to determine if this function is continuous at the point x equals 1. Let's first see if the function f of x equals x^3 is defined at x equals 1. You can do this by plugging the value in, so f of 1 equals 1^3, which equals 1. Since this checks out, you're ready to check the next condition. Since this checks out, you're ready to move to the next condition to check to calculate the limit for this function. Let's calculate the limit for your function. So you'll have the limit as x goes to 1 for x^3. In order to calculate the limit for x^3, you can substitute 1 into the function to find the limit. This gets you 1^3, which simplifies to just be 1. This proves that the limit does exist. Now, let's check for the third condition to see if f of x equals f of a, which in this case, from when you plugged it into the function versus when you plugged it into the limit, you do have f of x equals f of a, where in this case it has 1 equal to 1. Since all three conditions are met, you could state the function f of x equals x^3 is continuous at the point of x equals 1. In this particular case, you can note that x^3 is defined for the whole function, but it is good to see the process of how to do this. Let's take a look at another example. Let's say you have the function f of x equals x^2 minus x plus 6, dividing all of that by x minus 3. Let's see if this function is continuous at x equals 3. You'll begin by seeing if the function is defined at the point x equals 3. You could do this by plugging it into your function. So f of 3 equals 3^2 minus 3 plus 6, divided by 3 minus 3. This gets you a result of 12 divided by 0, which is unfortunately undefined because you cannot divide by 0. Since the function is not defined at f of a, then it is not continuous at the point x equals 3. Let's look at one more example, where this time, you have a function f of x equal to x, where x is less than 5, and x plus 2, where x is greater than or equal to 5. Like before, let's begin by seeing if the function is defined at the point x equals 5. You could do this by plugging it into your function, where f of 5 will equal 5 plus 2. Because you're working with the value 5, you'll be working with the second portion of the function x plus 2. This gets you a value of 7, meaning it is defined. Now, let's move on to the second condition. In this case, you'll need to calculate the left and right limits in order to get the limit for the function f of x. This is due to the way it is defined and the particular point you're working with. Let's begin by getting the left limit, so the limit as x approaches 5 from the left of x is equal 5. If you substitute in your value, which equals just 5. Now, looking at the right limit of the limit of x going to 5 from the right of x plus 2 equals 5 plus 2, which equals 7. So in this case, the left limit of your function limit as x goes to 5 from the left of f of x is not equal to the right limit, which is the limit as x goes to 5 from the right of f of x. This is because your values 5 and 7 do not equal each other. This is a case where you would state the limit as x goes to 5 of f of x equals DNE, which means it does not exist. Since this did not meet your second condition, you'll state that your function f of x is not continuous at the point x equals 5. So why is continuity at just one little point so important. As you saw, continuity at a point can be the difference between being able to calculate a limit or not at a certain point in a function. The same will go for when you calculate derivatives later on in this course. Finding continuity at a point is a simple concept, but it has a huge significance in order to perform many of the calculations throughout this course, including the limits you calculated earlier on. Next step, let's explore continuity on an interval.

Continuity on an interval (6 min 58 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you've learned about continuity at a point, let's expand upon this topic to learn about continuity on an interval. Continuity on an interval can be a bit more complex, so let's start with looking at the big picture. Let's bring back that graph drawing analogy. In this case, you'll imagine drawing the graph of a function with a pen on a piece of paper from start to finish, which will be from point A to point B. If you do not have to lift your pen from the paper at any point, then the function is continuous. If the pen is lifted from the paper at some point, then the function is discontinuous. Before I go into a formal definition of continuity on an interval, let's first take a moment to define continuity from the left and from the right. First, function f of x is continuous from the right at a point a if the limit as x goes to a and notated the plus sign for it being from the right of f of x equals f of a. Second, a function f of x is continuous from the left point a if the limit as x goes to a with a minus sign notating that it's going from the left of f of x equals f of a. Now let's look at a formal definition of continuity on an interval. A function f of x is continuous on an interval if it is continuous for every point in that interval. As you could tell, this continuity is derived from the previous continuity you learned at a point. It logically makes sense that you would want all the points defined within the interval you have in order to have it be continuous throughout the whole interval. Let's define this further for open and closed intervals. An open interval is defined as parentheses a to b for points a and b and this particular interval does not include its endpoints. A closed interval is defined as hard brackets a and b for points a and b where this interval does include its endpoint a and b. For example, you would have an open interval of parentheses five comma 10. This would not include your values of five and 10 in your interval. Next, looking at a closed interval, you have hard brackets five and 10. This will include your values of five and 10 within your interval. Now, let's take a look at continuity on open versus closed intervals. A function f of x is continuous on an open interval a and b if it is continuous for every point on that interval, hence it being the same as the regular definition of continuity on an interval. Next, a function f of x is continuous on a closed interval hard brackets a and b if it is continuous for every point on the interval, continuous from the right of a, and continuous from the left of b. You could do this by taking the right limit of a and the left limit of b and ensure they equal f of a and f of b respectively. Now you see the extra requirements to ensure the endpoints are properly defined for the closed interval version of this definition of continuity. Let's take a look at this in a few examples. Let's begin with a simple example of f of x equals x squared on the open interval of zero to three. Note that this is an open interval due to the parentheses used around it instead of the hard brackets. In order to determine if this is continuous on this interval, you would use the definition of continuity at a point for every single point on the interval. In this case, since you know the function f of x equals x squared is a polynomial function and polynomial functions are continuous for all real numbers, then you can determine that f of x equals x squared is continuous on the open interval zero to three. Let's expand upon the first example for f of x equals x squared, but this time let's do it for the closed interval of hard brackets zero to three. You already know it is continuous for the open interval zero to three as you saw in the previous example, but now you need to determine if it is continuous from the right of a and continuous from the left of b. You could do this by taking the right limit of a and the left limit of b. In order to ensure these bounds equal the values of the function, let's first calculate those function values. So for your first value of zero, you'll plug it in to get f of zero equals zero squared, which equals zero. For your other bound three, you can plug it in to get f of three equals three squared, which equals nine. Now, let's get your limits for the left and right. So getting the limit as x approaches zero from the right of x squared. In this case, you could just plug in the value to get zero squared, which equals zero. Next, the limit as x approaches three from the left of x squared, you can also plug this in to just get three squared, which equals nine. Since both of these limit results equal their function values accordingly, you can then state your function f of x equals x squared is continuous on the closed interval of zero to three. So why is continuity over an open or closed interval important in the first place? As you saw earlier, continuity at a point can be the difference between being able to calculate a limit or a derivative at a certain point in a function or not. Being able to do so on the interval of a function, especially the full interval, allows you to fully define functions as continuous and perform calculations on more than just one point at a time. In the bigger picture, having continuity at a point or an interval allows you to work with optimization problems, numerical approximations, and numerical methods in mathematics. In a practical aspect, it allows engineers and physicists to properly model natural phenomena, such as temperature, speed, and growth. Finding continuity over an interval will help you perform many of the calculations throughout this course along with help you better understand and define many of the continuous functions such, as polynomials. Now, let's take a moment to dive deeper into discontinuity and what it really means.

What is discontinuity? (8 min 1 seconds)
- [Instructor] Over the last few videos, you've heard the word discontinuous mentioned multiple times. In this video, you will focus on understanding exactly what that means. Discontinuity is when you have a function that is not continuous. Hence, when you are drawing that graph with your pen and paper, at some point when you are drawing it, the pen lifts from the paper. This causes the function to be discontinuous. As you have seen prior, there are many instances that will disqualify a function from being continuous, hence, making it discontinuous. A function is discontinuous if any of the following are true: first, if the function f of x is not defined at a point, such as f of a not being defined, next, if the limit of f of x does not exist. Another reason is if the limit of f of x does not equal f of a. And finally, if the right or left limits of f of x don't exist or don't equal each other. There are three main types of discontinuities you will come across. This includes: removable discontinuities, jump discontinuities, and infinite discontinuities. Note that these are the three main types you will explore in this course, but some discontinuities do not fit in these definitions. Let's look at these in more detail by starting with removable discontinuities. A removable discontinuity is when you have a hole in the graph of a function. This occurs when the limit at a for a function f of x exists, but the function is not defined at f of a. This graph shows you a removable discontinuity where you have a hole in it. So here you have a blue line, and then at the point of x equals one and y equals two, you'll see you have an open circle. This is often how a removable discontinuity is noted, where that function is not defined at that particular point. A jump discontinuity is when sections of your function do not properly meet in a finite manner. This means there will be a jump when you graph the function. This occurs when the left and right limits exist, but they do not equal each other, hence, creating the jump in the function. This is a graph of a jump discontinuity. So at the point x equals two, you'll see the blue line is going up towards y equals three, and then all of a sudden, it jumps down to y equals negative one. Hence why you see it is given the name jump discontinuity because it's like you are jumping from the top part of the function to the bottom part at that particular point. An infinite discontinuity is when sections of your function do not properly meet in an infinite manner. This means your function is usually divided by a vertical asymptote. This occurs when the left limit and/or right limits equal negative or positive infinity. This graph shows you an infinite discontinuity. So you'll see your function has two different lines where it starts from the left, and then suddenly goes down at x equals three. And then on the right side, you'll see it is really high at x equals three and quickly goes back down. So this is not a jump discontinuity because you cannot see finite points where you are jumping from one part of the function to the next part of the function at the same point. Instead, you have what is called the vertical asymptote at the x equals three mark, where it is essentially dividing the function, and each part of it is going up and down simultaneously to positive and negative infinity. Let's look at a few examples of discontinuities. For example one, let's look at the function f of x equals x squared minus x plus six divided by x minus three. And let's look at the discontinuity at the point x equals three. As you calculated before, your function is not defined at x equals three due to it being divided by zero when you have three minus three. Let's take the limit of the function to determine what type of discontinuity this is. If you take the limit as x approaches three of x squared minus x plus six divided by x minus three, you'll then get the limit as x approaches three of x minus three multiplied by x plus two by factoring the top part, and then divide by x minus three. Now, you can take out your x minus three on both parts to get limit as x approaches three of x plus two is equal to three plus two by substituting the value three in, and that equals five. So in this case, the function is not defined at x equals three, but its limit exists. This means you have a removable discontinuity. Let's look at another example. Let's say you have the function f of x equals x for values of x less than five, and for x plus two for values of x greater than or equal to five. And let's look at the point x equals five to understand the discontinuity here. Let's begin by taking the limit of your function as x approaches five. So in this case, since the value is five, you'll be using the part of the function that is x plus two. So you'll get the limit as x approaches five of x plus two, which equals five plus two, which equals seven. Now let's take a look at the right and left limits. With the right limit, you get the limit as x approaches five from the right of x plus two equals five plus two, which equals seven. Now let's look at the left limit. With this limit, you get the limit as x approaches five from the left of x, since you are now looking at values less than five, coming from the left direction. This then equals five. In this case, your left limits and right limits do not equal each other. This is then labeled a jump discontinuity due to the jump between the two sections of the function. Let's look at one last example where this time you'll look at the function f of x equals x plus five divided by x plus one. And let's look at the discontinuity of x equals negative one. Like before, if you plug in the value of negative one, you get negative one plus five divided by negative one plus one equals four divided by zero. Since it is divided by zero, this means that it is not defined at that particular point. So let's get some limits to determine what is going on here. Let's focus on the right and left limits. So starting with the right limit, you get the limit as x approaches negative one from the right of x plus five divided by x plus one is equal to infinity. And then if you look at the left limit, you have the limit as x approaches negative one from the left of x plus five divided by x plus one equals negative infinity. In this case, both the left and right limits are either negative or positive infinity, making this an infinite discontinuity. So why is discontinuity important? Discontinuity can be an indication of where a function is behaving abnormally or has a sudden shift, affecting how it can be used within calculations. In the real world, discontinuity can point to sudden changes in velocity, temperature, or stock prices. Understanding discontinuity is important to know not only how it will affect potential calculations you can perform, but also provide you a better understanding of the function you are working with. Let's expand upon continuity in the next two videos by exploring some theorems starting with the composite function theorem.

Composite function theorem (5 min 33 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the next two videos, you'll explore two theorems that work due to the help of continuity. Let's start off with one theorem that can help you calculate limits, the composite function theorem. Before I jump into this theorem though, let's first review what a composite function is. A composite function is created when you combine two or more functions where the output of one function is used as the input of another function. This can be simplified to just a function within another function. It is often denoted as the functions of f of x and g of x that form a composite function of f of g of x or g of f of x. You may have noticed you've already been working with composite functions a little bit throughout the course, but now you can officially put a name to it and fully understand it. Now, let's move on to the theorem. The composite function theorem states if a function f of x is continuous at x equals b and the limit as x goes to a of g of x equals b, then the limit as x goes to a of f of g of x is equal to f of the limit as x goes to a of g of x, which at the end is just equal to f of b. Essentially, this is stating that you can take the limit of a composite function by taking the limit of the inner function then applying the outer function to the result of the limit of the inner function. Why is the composite function theorem important in the first place? First, this theorem is helpful to simplify and speed up the process when performing calculations that contain composite functions. In particular, this theorem is incredibly useful when proving that trigonometric functions are continuous over their domains. Let's use this theorem with a few examples. Let's say, you have the composite function f of g of x equals e to the cosine of x. Note that these types of functions will often just be denoted as f of x, but in this case I want to make it very obvious that it is a composite function, so we can label the outer and inner functions accordingly. Let's say, you want to get the limit as x goes to 0 of your composite function f of g of x. This means you want to get the limit as x goes to 0 of e to the cosine of x. Since this would be a little bit tricky to do, you can then break it down into your two functions. So, in this case, your outer function is f of x equal to e of x, since that is the outermost function you're working with. Next is the inner function, which in this case is actually in the exponent of your outer function. So, this means that g of x equals cosine of x. Now, using the composite function theorem, let's get the limit of g of x, which is your inner function. So, the limit as x goes to 0 of cosine of x equals cosine of 0, which equals 1. Now, let's use that theorem to then apply the outer function f of x with the result of your limit of g of x. So, getting the function f with your limit as x approaches 0 of g of x in this case equals e to the one which simplifies to just e. Now, the theorem comes together to give you a final result of the limit as x approaches 0 of e to the cosine of x equals e. Let's look at another example. This time, let's look at the composite function, f of g of x equals sin of x squared. Let's get the limit as x approaches 0 of f of g of x, which equals the limit as x approaches 0 of sin of x squared for this problem. Let's gather your outer and inner functions. In this case, your outer function is your trigonometric function sin of x, so f of x equals sin of x. And then your inner function is what's inside of your sin function. So, this means g of x equals x squared. Now, use your composite function theorem to start by getting the limit of your inner function, so you have the limit as x approaches 0 of x squared equals 0 squared, which is equal to just 0. Now, let's apply the outer function f to this to get f of the limit as x approaches 0 of g of x equals sin of 0, which is just 0. Bringing this theorem together gets you a final result of the limit as x approaches 0 of sin of x squared equals 0. The composite function theorem is used in many practical applications. In economics, it is used to analyze how variables such as interest rates affect other variables like consumer spending. In biology, it is used in population growth rates calculations where it has to factor in variables such as food availability. In physics, it is used to calculate the velocity of an object undergoing accelerated motion. This will not be the last time you see composite functions, since they will be common throughout calculus and hence throughout the rest of the course. Knowing this theorem is incredibly helpful when calculating more complicated limits. Next, let's explore another theorem that works closely with continuity, the intermediate value theorem.

Intermediate value theorem (4 min 45 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this chapter with another theorem. In this video, I will show you the Intermediate Value Theorem and how to use it. Let's get started. The Intermediate Value Theorem states if a function f of x is continuous over a closed-bounded interval a to b and d is any number between f of a and f of b, then there exists a number c such that a less than c less than b and f of c equals d. Note that this theorem is sometimes shortened to just IVT. Let's look at this graphically to see the Intermediate Value Theorem in action. Let's look at the graph of this function, represented by the blue line, and this is a continuous function on this graph. Let's look at the values of x equals negative 2 and x equals 0. Since this function is continuous on a closed interval between negative 2 and 0, you can use the Intermediate Value Theorem to prove a root exists in that given interval. Using this theorem then guarantees that, if you check for a point, it will then have a corresponding value in that interval. So for example, if you had the point of f of x equals negative 1, you would then know that there's some value c that corresponds with that point between negative 2 and 0. There are a few things to keep in mind with the Intermediate Value Theorem. First, the value d does not have to be unique and may occur in multiple points in your function. Second, the theorem states that the value d exists between f of a and f of b, but it does not specify exactly what that value is. Note that this theorem heavily relies on the function being continuous since it will not always work with discontinuous functions due to holes and other undefined areas existing. Why is the Intermediate Value Theorem important? This theorem helps guarantee continuous functions on closed intervals will have a corresponding value for each of the values between the end points. This theorem also provides a base for many other theorems, concepts, and calculations. Let's review some of the applications of the Intermediate Value Theorem. First, it can be used to prove a point exists below or above a given line. Second, it can be used to find the existence of roots for functions, especially when it is difficult to algebraically do so. And finally, it is used when proving other theorems such as the Extreme Value Theorem and the Mean Value Theorem. And let's look at it on the interval between 1 and 2. Since the function is continuous with it being a polynomial and it is on a closed interval between 1 and 2, you can use the Intermediate Value Theorem to prove a root exists in that given interval. Let's start by getting the values of f of 1 and f of 2. So for f of 1, you get f of 1 equals 1 cubed minus 1 minus 2, which equals negative 2. For f of 2, you get 2 cubed minus 2 minus 2, which equals 4. Since f of 1 equals negative 2, which is less than 0, and f of 2 equals 4, which is greater than 0, plus f of x is continuous, then the Intermediate Value Theorem guarantees that f of x equals 0 at some point on your interval between 1 and 2. So again, note that that point will then be c. Since you can prove the function equals 0 at some point in this interval, then you can conclude at least one root exists between the values of 1 and 2 for your function. The Intermediate Value Theorem is used in many practical applications. In pharmacokinetics, this theorem can verify the concentration of a drug reaching therapeutic levels within a given timeframe. In economics, it can be used to show equilibrium points exist, such as a price that equalizes supply and demand. In physics, this theorem can be used to ensure feasible solutions exist when designing trajectories or optimizing structural components. The Intermediate Value Theorem is fairly straightforward once you understand the key properties of it. Even though this theorem is simple, it is the basis for many other mathematical concepts and applications. This wraps up continuity, along with a few corresponding theorems. Remember, in this course, you'll mainly work with continuous functions since many of the calculations will require that property. In the next chapter, you will dive into the world of derivatives.

Chapter Quiz (6 questions)
1.Why is the Intermediate Value Theorem important?
	It guarantees that continuous functions on open intervals are always increasing.
	It guarantees that continuous functions on closed intervals are differentiable.
	It guarantees that continuous functions on closed intervals have a maximum and minimum value.
	It guarantees continuous functions on closed and bounded intervals have a corresponding value for each of the values between endpoints

2.What does the Composite Function Theorem state?
	A composite function is created when you multiply two or more functions together.
	If a function f(x) is differentiable at x=b and the limit as x approaches a of g(x)=b, then the limit as x approaches a of f(g(x))=f(the limit as x approaches a of g(x))=f(a).
	If a function f(x) is continuous at x=b and the limit as x approaches a of g(x)=b, then the limit as x approaches a of f(g(x))=f(the limit as x approaches a of g(x))=f(b).
	A composite function is created when you combine two or more functions where the output of one function is used as the input of another function.

3.Which of the following describes an example of removable discontinuity?
	The function is not defined at any point in the interval.
	The left and right limits exist, but they do not equal each other.
	The function is not defined at a certain point, but the limit exists.
	The function has a vertical asymptote, leading to infinity.

4.What is the definition of continuity on a closed, bounded interval [a,b]?
	A function f(x) is continuous on a closed, bounded interval [a,b] if it is continuous for every point on that interval, it is continuous from the right of a, and it is continuous from the left of b`
	A function f(x) is continuous on a closed, bounded interval [a,b] if it only has jump discontinuities at a and b.
	A function f(x) is continuous on a closed, bounded interval [a,b] if it has removable discontinuities at a and b.
	A function f(x) is continuous on a closed, bounded interval [a,b] if it is continuous only at the endpoints a and b.

	The limit exists as x approaches a.
	The function is defined at x=a.
	The limit as x approaches a equals f(a).

6.Why is continuity important?
	It allows you to find the maximum and minimum values of a function.
	It guarantees that a function will always have an inverse.
	It allows you to perform basic arithmetic operations on functions, like addition and multiplication.
	Knowing if a function is continuous will allow you to perform certain operations on it at certain points or intervals, such as derivatives.

Day 05
Derivatives Introduction
What is a derivative? (4 min 52 seconds)
- Hi there. How are you feeling so far? You should be proud of yourself. Now, are you ready to expand upon your knowledge of limits? In this chapter, you will learn the second key concept of calculus, derivatives. You will learn what derivatives are, how to define the mus and limits and some key concepts about them. Let's start off by learning what a derivative is. A derivative represents the rate of change of a function with respect to a variable. It also represents the slope of a tangent line. What this does is tell you how quickly the output variable is changing compared to the input variable. The derivative of f of x with respect to x is defined by the following equation. So here you have the derivative f of x equal to the limit as h approaches zero of f of x plus h minus f of x divided by h. As you could tell from the definition, derivatives have multiple things they can represent. You'll learn in the next few videos how to further interpret derivatives with respect to rate of change and slopes of tangent lines. You can also tell from the definition how closely related derivatives are to limits. This shows how important limits are to calculus and how basically everything in calculus is derived from limits. This is why understanding limits is key to understanding more complex concepts such as derivatives and later on in the course, integrals. Now, let's take a moment to break down the limit equation used to define a derivative. So in this case, you have your value x and a value h, and so h is a point that is a very short distance away from your value x. You also have your function represented by f and parentheses. So if you break this down, you have f prime of x, which represents your derivative, and you have the limit as h approaches zero. And so on the top portion you have f of x plus h, which represents the value of your function f at a point a short distance away from x. Then you subtract that by your function f at the point of x. What this top portion represents is the change in your function f of x over the small distance between x and h. You then divide this difference by your value h to get the average rate of change of your function f over the interval h. So bringing this all together where you have your limit of h going to zero, what this means is that you are going to get as close to zero as possible, since the value of zero for h would not be helpful in this case. So overall, what this does is it tells you the rate of change of function f of x as x is changing. Derivatives can be represented in a variety of forms. The following are the three main forms you will see them displayed as in this course. So the first one is f, and then the little tick symbol above it is what we call a prime symbol. And so I will call this f prime of x. Below that is going to be y, and then the prime symbol. So, again, that will be notated as y prime. And then finally you have dy divided by dx. So again, that is the three forms that the derivative can be in. So notate that sometimes I will call it f prime of x. Sometimes I will call it the derivative of x. It just depends. So why do you need to know about derivatives in the first place? Derivatives are key because they provide you a precise method to measure change in various fields and applications. Derivatives can be used to describe curvature and slope, find optimal points, and formulate differential equations. There are a multitude of real life applications for derivatives, for example, they're used in many physics equations like velocity and acceleration. In machine learning, derivatives are used to optimize functions and improve algorithms. In finance, they can be used to calculate profit and loss along with evaluating changes in the stock market. In seismology, they can be used to determine the magnitude range of earthquakes. This is just the beginning of derivatives. You'll continue to learn more about derivatives and see them in action throughout the rest of this course, even with integrals. Up next, let's take a moment to learn how to interpret derivatives as rate of change.

Rate if change (4 min 12 seconds)
- [Instructor] There are two main ways you can define derivatives. First, you can define them as a rate of change, and second, you can define them as the slope of a tangent line. Let's take a moment to understand derivatives as a rate of change. Rate of change is how quickly a quantity changes in relation to another quantity. You can define the instantaneous rate of change of a function f of x at a value a with its derivative f prime of a. Let's take a deeper look into this. Let's say you have a function f of x equals x cubed minus 2 multiplied by x plus 4. In this function, you have values of f of 0 equals 4, f of 1 equals 3, and f of 2 equals 8. So this means at f of 0, 4, you have x equals 0 and y equals 4. Now you may notice this function has decreased at some point between the values of 0 and 1 and has increased at some point between the values of 1 and 2. Note that this does not mean that at f of 1, the function is clearly decreasing because it did at some point between the values of 0 and 1, and it does not mean that f of 1 is clearly increasing with the function because it did at some point between the values of 1 and 2. Clearly this is conflicting, which will be explained later on. You also can't clearly state at the point f of 2 that the function is increasing at 2 because it did at some point between the values of 1 and 2. The only way to know for sure if a function is increasing or decreasing at a specific point is by taking its derivative. The way you determine if it is increasing or decreasing is by the sign of the derivative. If the derivative is positive, then the function is increasing at that point. If the derivative is negative, then the function is decreasing at that point. And finally, if the derivative is equal to 0, then it is neither increasing nor decreasing. Let's continue on with the example for your function f of x equals x cubed minus 2 multiplied by x plus 4. I will show you later on how to compute derivatives, but note that the derivative for this function is f prime of x equals 3 multiplied by x squared minus 2. If you evaluate your derivative at those three values, you'll get f prime of 0 equals -2, f prime of 1 equals 1, and f prime of 2 equals 10. From your results, you can interpret the derivatives and the signs of them to determine that the function f of x is decreasing at the point 0 and the function f of x is increasing at the points 1 and 2. Note that there should also be at least one point between the values of 0 and 1 such that the derivatives is equal to 0. This is proven by the intermediate value theorem. Let's take a look at the graph of the function you've been exploring. So this is the graph of the function f of x equals x cubed minus 2 multiplied by x plus 4. So you'll notice the three points on there are the three points you've been exploring, f of 0, f of 1, and f of 2. From this, you could tell that these match up with the derivatives where the function is decreasing at f of 0 and is increasing at f of 1 and f of 2. There are various applications for rate of change. For example, acceleration is the rate of change for velocity. Next, velocity is the rate of change of position. Temperature gradient is the rate of change of temperature. And finally, marginal revenue is the rate of change of profit. Derivatives are key to fining the rate of change at a specific point since they are the main method to ensure you actually properly determine if the function is increasing or decreasing at that point. Next, let's take a look at how to calculate derivatives using limits.

Calculating derivatives using limits (8 min 2 seconds)
- [Instructor] Before you go further, let's take a moment to teach you how to calculate derivatives using limits. Generally, you won't use this method to calculate derivatives, but it is important to understand the connection of derivatives to limits and how to calculate them with this method before you move on to more advanced methods. You'll use the definition of a derivative in the form of a limit. If you remember, the limit definition of a derivative is f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of f of x plus h minus f of x divided by h. Once you plug in your function, you will then solve the equation using basic algebra to find your final solution. Let's go through a few examples using limits to calculate derivatives. Let's start off with the simple function of f of x equals x. So you have your formula f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of f of x plus h minus F of x divided by h. Now if you plug in your function x, you get f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of x plus h minus x divided by h. In this case, the x's cancel out, so f prime of x equals limit as h approaches zero of just h divided by h. You can then divide those to then get f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of one, which the limit of a constant value is itself, which is equal to one in this case. So the derivative of your function f of x equals x is equal to f prime of x equals one. Let's look at a slightly more complicated example. Let's say you have the function f of x equals x squared minus seven. So looking at your formula, f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of f of x plus h minus f of x divided by h. This time it'll be a little bit more complicated plugging this into your formula. So if you do, you get f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of x plus h squared, noting that you need to have the x plus h where you had x before, minus seven, and then you'll subtract that by x squared minus seven since that is your function. And finally, divide all of that by h. Once you expand the first term, you'll then get f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of x squared plus two multiplied by x multiplied by h plus h squared minus seven minus x squared minus seven, divide all of that by h. In this case your x squared and seven terms will cancel out. So this gets you f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of two multiplied by x multiplied by h plus h squared divided by h. Since each of these terms has an h in it, you can then take that out to get the f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of two multiplied by x plus h, and in this case, since h is going to zero, you'll have two multiplied by x plus zero, meaning your final derivative for the function f of x equals x squared minus seven is f prime of x equals two multiplied by x. Let's take a look at the graph of the result of your last derivative and its corresponding function. On this graph, you can see the parabola. In blue is your function, f of x equals x squared minus seven, and then the straight red line through it is your derivative of two multiplied by x. You'll be able to observe the relationship between the function and its derivative, such as how where the function is decreasing on the left side, the derivative is going to be a negative value. And then on the right side, where the function is increasing, the derivative is a positive value. This corresponds with having the slope of the tangent line being either positive or negative to reflect what is going on with the function itself. Let's take a look at a little bit more complicated example. Where this time, you'll get the derivative for the function f of x equals sine of x. Let's use your formula of f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of f of x plus h minus f of x divided by h. Plugging in your function sine of x, you get f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of sine of x plus h, making sure you have both x and h in there. Subtract that by your function sine of x and divide all that by h. You can use the sine addition formula of sine of x plus h equal to sine of x multiplied by cosine of h plus cosine of x multiplied by sine of h in order to simplify your derivative. So let's plug this in to get f prime of x equals the limit as h approaches zero of sine of x multiplied by cosine of h plus cosine of x, multiplied by sine of h, minus sine of x, and then make sure you divide all of that by h. Next, let's factor the sine and cosine terms to get f prime of x equals limit as h approaches zero of sine of x multiplied by cosine of h minus one plus cosine of x multiplied by sine of h. Divide all of that by h. Now let's split this up to get two different limits. So the limit as h to approaches zero of sine of x multiplied by cosine of h minus one and then the limit as h approaches zero of cosine of x multiplied by sine of h divided by h. To simplify solving this, you can use the facts of these following two limits where you have the limit of h approaches zero of sine of h divided by h is equal to one, and then the limit as h approaches zero of cosine of h minus one divided by h is equal to zero. Let's plug these in to get the limit as h approaches zero of sine of x multiplied by cosine of h minus one divided by h is equal to sine of x multiplied by zero, which in this case equals just zero. Next, let's get the limit as h approaches zero of cosine of x multiplied by sine of h divided by h, which is equal to cosine of x multiplied by one. This means your result is cosine of x. Let's plug this back into your derivative, f prime of x equal to cosine of x plus zero, which gets you a final result of the derivative of your function. f of x equals sine of x to be f prime of x equals cosine of x. Let's take a look at the graph of your function and its corresponding derivative. In this graph, you'll see the function sine of x and cosine of x, and notice how they have oscillating behavior where they are kind of like waves that go up and down as they go across the graph. You'll notice, like with the previous graph, how where one function is increasing, it will then have positive values in the other function and vice versa. If a function is decreasing, then the corresponding function will have negative values. In this graph, the function cosine of x is the one represented in blue where the value at x equals zero has a y value of one, and then your function sine of x is represented by the red line, which has a value of x equals zero corresponding with a value of y equals zero. Now that you know how to calculate derivatives using the limit definition, you'll be better prepared for the following videos when I use this limit definition to better explain some derivative concepts. It is also good to know how to compute derivatives this way and appreciate how they're made, even when you learn tricks later on to shorten your calculations. Up next, let's learn about the other way to define derivatives, which is going to be with tangent lines and slopes.

Tangent lines and slopes (6 min 36 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next, let's take a moment to understand derivatives as the slope of a tangent line. Before I explain this concept, though, let's take a moment to understand secant lines. A secant line is a line that intersects at least two distinct points of another line. You can use the slope of a secant line at a point a, f of a to estimate the rate of change where the output changes in relation to the input. You can obtain the slope of a secant line at points x, f of x and a, f of a with the equation f of x minus f of a, divided by x minus a. You can replace x in this equation with the values a plus h, where h is a value close to 0. This gets you the equation f of a plus h, minus f of a, divided by a plus h minus a. You can simplify the denominator to f of a plus h, minus f of a, divided by h. This should look familiar to your derivative definition that you saw earlier. In this graph, you can see a secant line, so you have the parabola that looks like a U with the blue line, and then you have a red line going through it. This is a secant line because it is touching the blue line in two distinct points. Now, let's look at how secant lines relate to tangent lines in a graphical sense. With this graph, you can see a blue line, which is kind of like a squiggle, and then a red line going through it. So you'll be able to tell it is a secant line since it is going through at least two points in that line that are distinct. The part I want you to focus on, though, is the part at x equals 0, where the red line is touching the blue line at y equals 1. This is what you will call the tangent line, so you'll see how you can get a tangent line and a secant line at the same time. Note that it is very common for your secant lines to slowly approach your tangent line as h gets closer to 0. Since the secant line approaches the tangent line, the tangent line can take on the same definition for the function where it has a slope of f of x minus f of a, divided by x minus a. You can then use the technique I showed you with the secant line slope to then simplify this to the definition of a derivative, where you have f of a plus h minus f of a divided by h. This means the slope of the line at point a is the rate of change of the function f of x at point a. So here is another example of a tangent line going through a graph. So, this is similar to the last one, where you have that blue kind of squiggle going through with your function, and then you have the red tangent line going through it. So again, it is technically secant line as well because it's going through multiple points, but it's a tangent line because it is representing the slope of the function at x equals 0, and in this case, y equals 1, where it is intersecting your function. Let's look at a few examples of finding a tangent line. Let's say you have a function f of x equals x squared and you want to find the tangent line at x equals 3. Let's apply your limit definition of the limit as x approaches 3 of f of x minus f of a, divided by x minus a. Plugging in your value of a equals 3, you'll get the limit as x approaches 3 of f of x minus f of 3, divided by x minus 3. Now, let's plug in your function x squared to get you the limit as x approaches 3 of x squared minus 3 squared since, again, you're applying your function to the f of 3 value and still divide by x minus 3. You can factor the top portion of this fraction to get the limit as x approaches 3 of x minus 3, multiplied by x plus 3, divided by x minus 3. Now, you can cancel out both x minus 3 terms to simply get the limit as x approaches 3 of x plus 3. Substituting in your value of 3 for x, you get the limit as x approaches 3 is 3 plus 3, which equals 6. Now you know the slope for your tangent line, where x is 3 is equal to 6. If you want to find the full equation for your tangent line, you can plug in your value x equals 3 into your function f of x equals x squared. So, if you plug this in, you get f of 3 equals 3 squared, which equals 9. This means the point your tangent line will be at is 3, 9 with a slope of 6. You can use the point-slope equation of a line, where you have y minus y1 equals m multiplied by x minus x1 to get y minus 9, equals 6 multiplied by x minus 3. This simplifies to y equals 6 multiplied by x minus 9 as the full equation for your tangent line where x equals 3. Let's look at this in a graph. Like you learned before, since the derivative you got was positive, it means that the function is increasing at the point x equals 3. Looking at the tangent line and the graph of your function, this is a parent. So you have your function, which is a parabola of x squared, where you have the blue line and it looks like a U, and then you have your red line, which is your tangent line, and then I have a blue point at x equals 3. So you'll see that this does match up to your point of 3, 9 that you found earlier, and you'll see that it has that pretty steep slope of 6. So, like I mentioned before, this is a parent that the function is increasing at that particular point on the graph. Finding the slope of a tangent line is not only helpful, but a useful way to interpret derivatives. It is a parent that, in a way, they are still measuring the rate of change since it is measuring the rate of change at that particular point for the function, which becomes very clear when it is graphed. Next, let's take a look at continuity with derivatives.

Continuity with derivatives (5 min 55 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next up, let's talk about continuity derivatives and how these two topics are related. You learned about continuity in the previous chapter, so let's take a moment to review what a continuous function is. Remember, a function f is continuous at a point x equals a where the following three conditions are true. One, the function f is defined at the point x equals a. Two, the function has a limit that exists as x approaches a. Three, the limit as x approaches a is f of x equals f of a. If this is not the case, then the function is discontinuous at that point. Also remember that a function f of x is continuous on an open interval a to b if it is continuous for all points within that interval. Now, let's take a look at what differentiable means. A function f of x is differentiable at a point x equals a if the derivative f prime of a exists. To expand upon that, a function f of x is differentiable on an interval a to b if the derivative f prime of a exists for every point on the interval. Now, let's see how these two topics are related. It is stated that if a function f of x is differentiable at a point x equals a, then that function f of x is continuous at that point x equals a. You can prove this by showing the limit as x approaches a of f of x equals f of a. I want to make it clear from this definition that this means differentiability implies continuity. This means a point of a function is only differentiable if it is continuous. Note that this does not work the other way around, meaning continuity does not automatically imply differentiability. For example, let's look at the function f of x equals absolute value of x, and you want to see if this function is continuous and differentiable at x equals 0. First, let's see if this function is continuous at this point. You could do this by taking the limit as x approaches 0 of the absolute value of x. In this case, you could plug in your value of 0 to get the absolute value of 0, which equals just 0. Since this is an appropriate finite result, this means that your function is continuous at this point. Next, let's see if this function is differentiable using limits. In this case, you'll have f prime of x equals the limit as x approaches h of f of a plus h minus f of a divided by h. Remember, this is the limit definition of your derivative. Let's plug in your value of 0 for a, which gets you f prime of x equals limit as x approaches 0 of f of 0 plus h minus f of 0 divided by h. Now, let's plug in your function, which in this case is going to be the absolute value signs, so you'll have f prime of x equals the limit as x approaches 0 of the absolute value of 0 plus h minus the absolute value 0 divided by h. In this case, you will need to evaluate the limit from both sides with the left hand limit and the right hand limit to ensure that they equal. For this particular function, if you have the absolute value of h, this is equal to h for values of h greater than or equal to 0. Now, if you have the absolute value of h for values of h less than 0, then it is equal to -h. Let's get those left and right hand limits. Getting the left hand limit, you get the limit as x approaches 0 from the left of the absolute value of h divided by h by simplifying the limit you saw in the previous slide, which equals the limit as x approaches 0 from the left of -h divided by h since you're going from that left side. And then this simplifies to the limit as x approaches 0 from the left is -1, which is just equal to -1 since it is a constant value. Now, let's get the right hand limit, which is the limit as x approaches 0 from the right of absolute value of h divided by h, which equals the limit as x approaches 0 from the right of h divided by h, which equals the limit as x approaches 0 from the right of 1, which again since it is a constant value equals 1. In this case, your left hand limit of limit of x approaches 0 from the left of absolute value of h divided by h is not equal to your right hand limit of the limit as x approaches 0 from the right of absolute value of h divided by h. This means f of x equals the absolute value of x is continuous, but not differentiable at the point x equals 0. Hence, this is a great example where you have a function that has continuity but not differentiability. In summary, you have learned that if a derivative exists at a certain point for a function, then the function must be continuous at that point. However, the reverse is not always the case if a function is continuous at a point, meaning it is also differentiable at that point, like you saw in the previous example. Next, you will explore how to compute with differentiation.

Computing with differentiation (2 min 37 seconds)
- [Instructor] So far, you have calculated derivatives using the limit definition of a derivative. This is a great method to know that will come in handy since you can always use this method to calculate derivatives, even if they end up being a bit tricky. As you may have noticed though, finding a derivative with this method often takes many steps that may sometimes be unnecessary. There are a variety of situations where using the limit definition method may not be needed when deriving. You will learn a variety of rules to speed up and simplify your derivative calculations, such as the power rule to automatically know how to deal with derivatives that contain a power. There are also cases such as trigonometric derivatives where it will be easiest to memorize the derivatives for these functions since they tend to be computationally expensive, as you saw in a previous example. So what is this term differentiation? Differentiation is the process of finding derivatives. Note that all the methods you will learn to solve derivatives in the following chapters using a variety of differentiation techniques will all stem from the limit definition of a derivative. So if you remember, the definition is f prime of x equals the limit as h goes to zero of f of x plus h subtracted by f of x. Divide all that by h. Note that all the differentiation techniques can also be proven using this limit definition of a derivative. In the following chapters, you'll begin by learning some key differentiation rules you can use to better understand how to work with derivatives and speed up the overall process. Next, you'll learn about some derivatives of some common function types, including some where I will recommend that you memorize them. Finally, you'll learn about higher order derivatives and more advanced differentiation techniques such as implicit and logarithmic differentiation. Throughout the rest of the course, you will seldom see the limit definition of derivatives used to understand and solve derivatives. Remember, it is still important to know every derivative can be solved using that technique, but I will show you how to find faster and easier methods to solve derivatives. This wraps up the basics of derivatives and how to compute them using the limit definition. Remember, derivatives are a key concept for calculus and help measure the rate of change. They will be the key focus for the following few chapters. In the next chapter, you'll explore some of the important differentiation rules.
