Day 06
Differentiation Rules
Differentiation Rules (1 min 42 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you'll explore many of the common rules for derivatives. Remember, a derivative represents the rate of change of a function. Let's start by going over the differentiation rules in this chapter. The differentiation rules you'll explore are constant/constant multiple, sum/difference, product/quotient, power, and chain. There are a few cases for the constant rule and the sum and difference rules will be explored together since they are similar to each other. In the following chapter, you will explore derivatives of common types of functions such as trigonometric and exponential functions that have their own rules. Each of the following videos will be structured similarly to give you a general overview of each differentiation rule. Each video will provide you with the definition of the rule, the reasoning of why it is needed, and multiple examples of how to calculate derivatives using the rule. It is important to know how to not only understand the key rules for differentiation, but also how to use them to calculate derivatives in various scenarios. Derivatives are fundamental to calculus, so understanding how to calculate them using the rules when needed will help you better understand other key concepts later on, such as integrals. The goal of the following videos is to provide you with working knowledge on how to implement these various differentiation rules. You can take this knowledge and expand upon it in your own work and education. Let's get started with exploring constant rules.

Constant rules (3 min 48 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's begin by exploring the constant rules for derivatives. There are two constant rules you will cover in this video: the constant rule and the constant multiple rule. Let's start with the constant rule. The constant rule states for any constant c where your function f of x equals c, then the derivative of that function f prime of xequals zero. This makes sense since the graph of any constant value has a slope of zero. Note that this is the same even if the constant c equals zero. Since the derivative is the slope of the tangent line at a particular point in a function, then, in this case, since the original slope is zero, the derivative will also equal zero. Let's look at an example of the constant rule. Let's say you have a function f of x equals 8. If you take the derivative of it, you can use the constant rule to get f prime of x equals zero. Next, let's look at the constant multiple rule. The constant multiple rule states for a function g of x equals c multiplied by a function f of x for any constant c, the derivative for your function g prime of x equals c multiplied by f prime of x. You will notice this is very similar to the constant multiple rule for limits. You might be a bit confused as to why the constant stays the same for the constant multiple rule when the previous constant rule made the constant go to zero. This is because of how limits work, since the limit of a constant is always itself. Hence, the derivative of a constant multiplied can be taken aside from the equation and put back in with the final result. This rule can simplify the process when deriving functions by allowing you to move that constant outside of the function you are deriving. Let's look an example of this rule in action. Let's say you have a function f of x equal to 3 multiplied by x. If you wish to take the derivative of this, you can start off by ignoring the constant 3, since it will stay the same. Now you just need to take the derivative of x, which, as you learned previously, is simply equal to 1. Now you can bring back in your constant 3, so you'll have 3 multiplied by your derivative of x, which is equal to 3 multiplied by 1. And now you have a final derivative of f prime of x equal 3. Let's look at one more example of this. Let's say you have a function f of x equal to x divided by 5. You might be worried at first, since you are dividing your function x by the constant 5, but this is actually the same as multiplying your function x by the fraction 1/5. Since you are multiplying it by 1/5, you can still use the constant multiple rule. So now you just need to simply derive your function X, which, like before, is equal to 1. Now you can bring your constant 1/5 back into the equation, so you have 1/5 multiplied by your derivative of X, which equals 1/5 multiplied by 1, and so now you have a final derivative of 1/5. This should give you a solid understanding of how to use the constant rule and constant multiple rule. These rules are simple, yet effective when it comes to knowing how to quickly and easily solve derivatives, especially when the functions within them become more complicated. Up next, let's explore sum and difference rules.

Sum and difference rules (5 min 41 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next are the sum and difference rules for derivatives. These rules are essentially one and the same, but they're sometimes referenced separately. It is important to know how both of these rules work, since they can be extremely helpful when solving derivatives. Let's start off by exploring the sum rule. The sum rule states the derivative of a sum of functions is the same as the sum of their derivatives. This is represented by the functions H of X equal to the function F of X plus the function G of X. Then you have the derivative of H of X equal to the derivative of F of X, added with the derivative of G of X. Next, let's explore the difference rule. The difference rule states the derivative of a difference of functions is the same as the difference of their derivatives. This is represented by the functions H of X equal to the function F of X, subtracted by the function G of X, where you have the derivative of H of X equal to the derivative of F of x, subtracted by the derivative of G of X. As you could tell, these rules are very similarly defined. Sometimes they're referenced together and represented by the following equations. So you have H of X equal to the function F of x plus or minus the function G of X, where the derivative of H of X is equal to the derivative of F of X added, or subtracted by the derivative of G of X. How do these rules work? When you take a derivative, you are finding the rate of change for a function. Since you have two separate functions when you add or subtract them from each other, you can handle the functions individually to find their rates of change. Once you find this for each function, you can combine the results in order to get your final result. Let's look at a few examples of these rules in action. Let's start off with an example for the sum rule. Let's say you have the function F of X equal to 45 plus X. Since you're adding these two functions together, you can separate them to calculate your derivatives. So again, using your rule F of X equal to G of X, plus H of X. In this case, G of X equals 45 and H of X equals X. As you saw from the previous video, the derivative of a constant is zero, so your G prime of X is equals zero. And as you know from previous knowledge, the derivative of X is one. So H prime of X equals one. So now you bring back your rule where you have F prime of X equal to G prime of X, plus H prime of X, and let's plug in those values you got. So your final derivative F prime of X equals zero plus one, which gives you a final derivative of one. Let's look at another example, but this time with the difference rule. Let's say you have the function F of X equal to six, multiplied by X, subtracted by two, multiplied by X. Again, you will separate these two functions to calculate their derivatives. So this time you have the rule F of X equal to G of X subtracted by H of X. So you have your function G of X equal to six multiplied by X and your function H of X equal to two multiplied by X. Using the constant multiple rule and the knowledge that the derivative of X is one, you can then separate these derivatives. So you have G prime of X equal to six multiplied by one, which is six, and then you have your derivative of H of X, which is two multiplied by one, which equals two. Bring this back together with your rule of F prime of X equals G prime of X minus H prime of X to get your derivative of F of X. So here, you have six minus two, which gets you a final derivative of four. Let's look at one more example where these rules are combined together. Let's say you have the function F of X equal to nine, multiplied by X, subtracted by X, added with two. Here, you'll have the function F of X equal to G of X, minus H of X plus J of X. So you have your function G of X, which is nine, multiplied by X, your function H of X, which is equal to X, and finally your function J of X, which is equal to two. So even though you have three functions this time with both addition and subtraction, the separation process is the same as before, where again, you'll separate them, calculate their derivatives individually, and then combine them together in the final result. So using the constant multiple rule and the knowledge that the derivative of X is one, you will then have G prime of X equal to nine multiplied by one, which gets you a derivative of nine. You'll have your derivative H prime of X equal to one, and your derivative of J prime of X equal to zero. So now we will combine all of these results together for F prime of X equal to G prime of X, minus H prime of X, plus J prime of X. This gets you a final derivative of F of X equal to nine minus one plus zero, which equals eight for your final result. This should give you a solid understanding of how to use the sum and difference rules with derivatives. These rules are super helpful to use to separate out a derivative to make it easier to calculate in smaller pieces. Up next, let's explore the power rule.

Power rule (4 min 52 seconds)
- [Instructor] You may have noticed that I have not shown you any derivative examples of functions with exponents other than 1 so far. This is because there is a whole rule of how to deal with exponents called the power rule. The power rule states for a function f of x equal x to the n with a constant value n, the derivative is f prime of x equal to n multiplied by x to the n minus 1. An easy way to think about this rule is you take the current exponent, multiply that with the function as a constant value, and then reduce the current x exponent by 1. You may notice that you have essentially been using this rule to get the derivative of x so far. Since x is technically x to the 1, you may notice that you have essentially been using this rule to get the derivative of x so far. So you have your function f of x equal to x, but this is actually the same as x to the 1. So when you get your derivative of f of x, you have f prime of x equal to your current exponent of 1, so you have 1 multiplied by x, and then you have it to the 1 minus 1. So this is equal to 1 multiplied by x to the zero, which is equal to 1 multiplied by 1 to get you a final derivative of 1 for your function of x. This rule can be proven by using multiple techniques such as mathematical induction and the binomial theorem. I suggest taking time on your own to explore these proofs if you wish to have a more thorough understanding of the why behind how this rule works. Long story short, as you take the limits of the function, everything cancels out accordingly to provide you with the power rule. Let's look at a few examples of the power rule in action. Let's get the derivative for the function f of x equal to 5 multiplied by x squared. Using the constant multiple rule, you can separate out the constant 5 to focus on just your function x squared for now. So if you get the derivative of the function of x squared, you will then bring your power of 2 down, so you have 2 multiplied by x, and that's going to be to the 2 minus 1. Since again, you're subtracting 1 from your current power. This gets you 2 multiplied by x to the 1, and now you could bring in your constant 5. So you have 5 multiplied by your derivative of your function, f of x, so this will be 5 multiplied by 2 multiplied by x, which gets you a final derivative of 10 multiplied by x. Let's look at one more example. Let's get the derivative for the function f of x equal to 3 multiplied by x to the fourth plus 7 times x to the tenth. Here you'll be using your sum rule, so you have f of x equal to g of x plus h of x. You have g of x equal to 3 multiplied by x to the fourth, and h of x equal to 7, multiplied by x to the tenth. Let's start by getting the derivative of g of x. For now, let's separate out the constants 3 and 7, and we'll come back to adding those back in later. So to get the derivative of g of x for x to the fourth, you will simply bring the exponent of 4 down, so 4 multiplied by x to the 4 minus 1. So your derivative of g of x is going to be 4 multiplied by x cubed. And then next you have h of x, so again, this is going to be the derivative of x to the tenth. Bring down that 10, so you have 10 multiplied by x, and now you have it to the 10 minus 1. So you have your derivative of h prime of x equal to 10 multiplied by x to the 9. Now let's bring this together with the constant multiple and the sum rules to get 3 multiplied by g prime of x plus 7 multiplied by h prime of x. This gets you the derivative of f of x equal to 3 multiplied by 4 multiplied by x to the 3, plus 7 multiplied by 10 multiplied by x to the ninth. When you simplify this, you get a final derivative result of f prime of x equal to 12 multiplied by x to the 3 plus 70 times x to the 9. The power rule is very important and will be heavily used in the following videos with a variety of functions. Remembering the easy trick of multiplying the exponent by the function, then subtracting 1 from the exponent will significantly speed up the process of calculating many derivatives. Up next, let's increase the complexity and learn about the product rule.

Product rule (5 min 54 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's take a look at the product rule. Note that this rule is similar to the quotient rule, but they will be discussed separately due to their complexity and importance. Let's start off with the definition. The product rule states the derivative of a product of functions only works if each of the functions are differentiable individually. If they are differentiable, then the product rule states the derivative of the functions h of X equals the function f of x multiplied by the function g of x. Then the derivative of h prime of x equals f prime of x multiplied by g of x, plus f of x multiplied by g prime of x. You may wonder why you don't just multiply the two functions together. For example, how you would with the sum and difference rules where you simply add or subtract them from each other. Let's look at an example as to why this does not work. Let's say you have the function f of x and it is equal to x squared multiplied by x cubed. If you've simply multiplied these together to get x to the fifth, then you'll get a derivative of f of x equals five multiplied by x to the fourth. Now, let's get the derivatives first and then multiply them together. So here you have your function g of x equal to x squared and your function h of x equal to x cubed. If you get the derivative of g of x, you get g prime of x equal to two multiplied by x. If you get the derivative of h of x, you get h prime of x equal to three multiplied by x squared. Now let's multiply these derivatives together. So you have g prime of x multiplied by h prime of x, which equals two multiplied by x, and you multiply that by three multiplied by x squared. If you simplify this, you get six multiplied by x cubed as your final result. As you could see, the derivative of a product of functions is not equal to the product of the derivatives of the functions. The product rule can be proven using multiple techniques such as mathematical induction and implicit differentiation. I suggest taking time on your own to explore these proofs if you wish to have a more thorough understanding of the why behind how this rule works. Long story short, as you take the limits of the two functions being multiplied together, everything simplifies to provide you with the product rule. Let's look at an example of the product rule in action. Let's get the derivative for the function f of x equal to x squared multiplied by three, multiplied by x plus two. So here, you have f of x equal to g of x multiplied by h of x, so you'll have g of x equal to x squared and h of x equal to three multiplied by x plus two. Let's begin by getting the derivatives of g of x and h of x. So you have g prime of x equal to two multiplied by x using the power rule, and then you have the derivative h prime of x equals three, since, again, the two is going to cancel up to zero, and the derivative of x is one, so it'll just be three multiplied by one, which leaves it to just three. Now let's bring in the product rule. So here, you have f prime of x equal to g prime of x, multiplied by h of x plus g of x multiplied by h prime of x. Let's plug in your different values for this. So you have f prime of x equal to two multiplied by x. Then you'll multiply that by three multiplied by x plus two, and then you'll add x squared multiplied by three. Let's simplify this a little bit to get six multiplied by x squared, plus four, multiplied by x, plus three, multiplied by x squared. Add these like terms together to get a final result of f prime of x equal to nine multiplied by x squared, plus four multiplied by x. Let's look at another example. Let's get the derivative of f of x equal to x cubed multiplied by two, multiplied by x minus one. And so again, here, you have your function f of x equal to g of x multiplied by h of x. So g of x is equal to x cubed and h of x is equal to two multiplied by x minus one. Let's get their individual derivatives. So you have g prime of x equal to three multiplied by x squared using the power rule, and then you have h prime of x equal to two, since again, the derivative of x is one and the derivative of negative one, which is a constant value, is zero. Bring back in your product rules. So you have f prime of x equal to g prime of x multiplied by h of x plus g of x multiplied by h prime of x. Plug in your values to get f prime of x equal to three multiplied by x squared, and you'll multiply that by two multiplied by x minus one, and then you'll add x cubed multiplied by two. This simplifies to six multiplied by x cubed minus three multiplied by x squared, plus two multiplied by x cubed. Simplify this more to get a final result of f prime of x equal to eight, multiplied by x cubed, minus three multiplied by x squared. The product rule is not quite as intuitive as the previous rules, so it is important to properly memorize this rule since it will be frequently utilized. Next, you'll learn about the quotient rule, where you'll see how it works similarly to the product rule, but it has its own twist.

Quotient rule (5 min 55 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next, let's explore the quotient rule. This rule operates similarly to the product rule, where you can't just simply divide or multiply functions together to get their combined derivative. Let's start off with the definition. The quotient rule states the derivative of a quotient of functions only works if each of the functions are differentiable individually. If they are differentiable, then the derivative for the function h of x, which is equal to the function f of x divided by the function of g of x is then equal to h prime of x, which equals f prime of x multiplied by g of x, subtracted by f of x, multiplied by g prime of x, divide all of that by g of x squared. The derivative for the quotient rule is similar to the product rule with the overall format, especially with that top part of the quotient rule. There are a few differences where with the product rule, you add the two sets of functions and derivatives together, versus the quotient rules, you subtract them from each other. You also need to divide everything by the second function squared with the quotient rule, versus the product rule, you don't need to do that. Like before, you may wonder why you don't just divide the two functions by each other. Let's look at an example as to why this does not work. Let's say you have the function f of x equal to x squared divided by x to the seventh. This is equal to one divided by x to the fifth, or x to the minus five. If you simply get the derivative of this, it is going to equal minus five divided by x to the sixth, or minus five multiplied by x to the minus sixth. Now let's get the derivatives first, and then divide them by each other. So, let's have g of x equal to x squared and h of x equal to x to the seventh. So using the power rule for both of these, you have g prime of x equal to two multiplied by x, and then h prime of x equal to seven multiplied by x to the sixth. Now, let's divide these two derivatives by each other, so you have g prime of x divided by h prime of x, which is equal to two multiplied by x, divided by seven, multiplied by x to the sixth. If you simplify this, you get two divided by seven multiplied by x to the fifth. As you could see, the derivative of the quotient of functions is not equal to the quotient of the derivatives of the functions. The quotient rule can be proven using multiple techniques, such as mathematical induction and logarithmic differentiation. I suggest taking time on your own to explore these proofs if you wish to have a more thorough understanding of the why behind how this rule works. Long story short, as you take the limits of the two functions being divided by each other, everything simplifies to provide you with the quotient rule. Let's look at a few examples using the quotient rule. First, let's get the derivative for the function f of x, which is equal to five multiplied by x squared, divided by four, multiplied by x plus three. So here you have f of x equal to g of x divided by h of x. In this case, your function g of x is equal to five multiplied by x squared, and your function, h of x is equal to four multiplied by x plus three. Using the rules you've learned so far, your derivative for g of x is g prime of x equal to 10 multiplied by X, and then your derivative for h of x, h prime of x is equal to four. Now let's bring in your quotient rule where you have f prime of x equal to g prime of x, multiplied by h of x, subtracted by g of x, multiplied by h prime of x, and you'll divide all of that by h of x squared. Let's plug in your values to get f prime of x equal to 10 multiplied by x, and then you multiply that by four, multiplied by x plus three, subtract that by four, multiplied by five, multiplied by x squared. You're going to divide all of that by four multiplied by x plus three squared. This then simplifies to a final result of 20 multiplied by x squared plus 30 multiplied by x, dividing all of that by four, multiplied by x plus three squared. Let's look at another example. Let's get the derivative for the function f of x equal to x plus one divided by x squared plus four multiplied by X. So here you have your function f of x equal to g of x divided by h of x. So you have your function g of x equal to x plus one, and your function h of x equal to x squared plus four multiplied by X. Using the rules you've learned so far, you have g prime of x equal to one and h prime of x equal to two multiplied by x plus four. Now let's use your quotient rule f prime of x equal to g prime of x, multiplied by h of x, minus g of x, multiplied by h prime of x, divided by h of x squared. Plug in your values to get one multiplied by x squared plus four times x. Subtract that by x plus one multiplied by two times x plus four. Divide all of this by x squared plus four times x, and square that whole thing. Once you simplify this, you get a final result of negative x squared minus two multiplied by x minus four. Divide all of that by x squared plus four times x, and again, that whole bottom portion is squared. The quotient rule is also not as intuitive, just like the product rule, so it is important to properly memorize the technique, since it will be frequently utilized in the following videos. In the next video, you'll wrap up this chapter by exploring the chain rule.

Chain rule (8 min 24 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this chapter by learning about the chain rule. The previous rules you learned will help with many functions you will get derivatives for but not for all them. This is where the chain rule comes in handy. There are a variety of functions, such as these, that will not work with the rules you currently learned. This is because they are functions with functions. Some of these functions you will learn more about in the following chapter. When you have a function within a function, it is sometimes called the composition of f and g. It can be represented by two different notations, so it can either be represented by parenthesis f and a little circle g of x or by f and then parenthesis g(x). Note that the composition of f of g of x is not always equal to the composition of g of f of x. So again, note that you can't necessarily flip these. Now that you understand what composite functions are, let's explore the chain rule. The chain rule states the derivative of a function within a function only works if each of the functions are differentiable individually. If they are differentiable, then the derivative for the functions h(x) equals f(g(x)) is then equal to h'(x) equals f'(g(x)) multiplied by g'(x). As you could see, the derivative for the chain rule can be more complicated to understand than the previous rules you explored. The first part is understanding what function is the outer one and which function is the inner one. The second part is understanding how to take the derivatives of the inner and outer functions since, sometimes, they could be a bit complicated. Let's begin by looking at a few examples for how to identify what the inner function is and what the outer function is. So these are some of the previous examples you saw. So you have f(x) equal to ln of x to the fifth plus x to the minus five. In this case, your inner function is going to be x to the five plus x to the minus five, and your outer function is going to be ln(u). So you'll note that's, oftentimes, the outer function uses u to, essentially, represent the variable. Next, you have f(x) equal to the square root of three multiplied by x squared plus eight. In this case, your inner function is three multiplied by x squared plus eight, and your outer function is the square root of u. Finally, you have f(x) equal to e to the x to the fifth minus four multiplied by x squared plus four. In this case, your inner function is x to the fifth minus four multiplied by x squared plus four, and then your outer function is simply e to the u. Now, let's take a moment to dissect the chain rule now that you've reviewed it and you've seen kind of how the inner and outer functions work. So first, you have your h'(x), which represents your whole function, is equal to the f' portion. So this portion is saying you want to get the derivative of the outer function. Now, the g(x) inside of it is where you will evaluate the derivative of the outer function at that specific point g(x), then you have your second portion multiplied by g'(x), which is simply just getting the derivative of the inner function and multiplying it by the first chunk. Let's look at why you need the chain rule in the first place. Let's say you have the function f(x) equal to the square root of four multiplied by x plus seven. Let's say you just use the power rule to solve this. If you do so, you'll get a result of f'(x) equal to one half multiplied by four multiplied by x plus seven to the minus 0.5. This is, unfortunately, not the correct result since the correct derivative is actually f'(x) equal to two multiplied by four multiplied by x plus seven to the negative 0.5. So you'll notice, instead of it being one half, it's actually two. So again, even though this is a minor change, note that for some of the other examples, it's a very major difference. The chain rule can be proven using the limit definition and the mean value theorem. I suggest taking time on your own to explore this proof if you wish to have a more thorough understanding of the why behind how the chain rule works. Long story short, as you take the limits of the two composite functions, you use the difference quotient and mean value theorem to simplify everything which gets you the chain rule. Let's look at a few examples using the chain rule. Let's get the derivative for your function f(x) equal to three multiplied by x plus two, and take all that to the fourth. So let's find the composite function so you have f(x) equals g(h(x)). So in this case, your outer function, g(x), is equal to u to the fourth. So again, u is going to represent that unknown variable, and then h(x) is going to be your inner function, which is going to be inside those parenthesis, so it'll be three multiplied by x plus two. Now, let's get the derivatives of those two individual functions for the chain rule. So g'(x) is equal to four multiplied by u cubed, and then h'(x) is equal to just three. Using your chain rule, so you have f'(x) equal to g'(h(x)) multiplied by h'(x), you'll plug in your values to get f'(x) equals four multiplied by three multiplied by x plus two cubed. So again, you'll see you used your h(x) and plugged it into where that u was in the derivative of your g'(x), and then you're going to multiply that by your derivative of h'(x), which is just three. Once you simplify this a little bit, you get a final result of f'(x) equals 12 multiplied by three multiplied by x plus two to the third. Let's take a look at another example. Let's get the derivative for the function f(x) equals two multiplied by x plus three. Divide all that by six minus x squared, and you're going to take all of that to the third. So again, you have composite functions f(x) equal to g(h(x)). So in this case, g(x) is going to equal u cubed, and then h(x) is going to equal two multiplied by x plus three divided by six minus x squared. So again, g(x) is your outer function, and h(x) is your inner function. So in this case, using the power rule, g'(x) is equal to three multiplied by u squared, and then using the quotient rule, h'(x) is equal to two multiplied by six minus x squared. Subtract that by two, multiplied by x plus three. Multiply that by negative two multiplied by x, and then you'll divide everything by six minus x squared and then square that. Now, let's use your chain rule, f'(x) equal to g'(h(x)) multiplied by h'(x) to get a result of f'(x) equals three multiplied by two multiplied by x plus three divided by six minus x squared squared, and then you multiply that by two multiplied by six minus x squared minus two multiplied by x plus three multiplied by negative two multiplied by x. Divide all that by six minus x squared, and then square that. Note that there is an extended version of the chain rule. So if you have a composition of three or more functions, then you simply keep applying the chain rule as many times as there are functions. For example, if you have three composite functions g(x) equal to h(f(g(x))), then your chain rule dictates the derivative g'(x) is equal to h'(f(g(x))) multiplied by f'(g(x)) multiplied by g'(x). So again, here, you're just using the chain rule twice. So if you need to do it four or five, six or more times, you just keep expanding that. The chain rule can be a complicated one, but it is very necessary to work with more advanced problems where there are multiple functions within functions. This will be very apparent when you begin working with trigonometric, logarithmic, and exponential functions. This wraps up your introduction to differentiation rules. These rules are all very important and will be frequently used throughout future videos to take derivatives. I suggest take your time on your own to make sure you are familiar with these rules and to continue practicing them with different functions. In the next chapter, you'll explore many of the common derivatives for various functions.

Chapter Quiz (9 questions)
1.What does the sum rule state about the derivative of the sum of functions?
	The derivative of a sum of functions is the product of their derivatives.
	The derivative of a sum of functions is the subtraction of their derivatives.
	The derivative of a sum of functions is the sum of their derivatives.
	The derivative of a sum of functions is the quotient of their derivatives.

2.What does the constant rule state for derivatives?
	The derivative of a product of functions is the product of their derivatives.
	The derivative of a function multiplied by a constant is the constant times the derivative of the function.
	The derivative of the sum of two functions is the sum of their derivatives.
	The derivative of a constant function is always 0.

3.What is the result of the integral of the function f(x)=6x^5 + 9x^2?
	30 * x^4+18 * x
	x^6 + 3 * x^3 + C
	(6 * x^6)/5 + (9 * x^3)/2
	6 * x^6 + 9 * x^3 + C

4.What does the sum rule for Integrals state?
	The integral of a sum of functions is the same as the product of their integrals.
	The integral of a sum of functions is equal to the sum of their derivatives.
	The integral of a sum of functions is equal to the constant multiplied by each function.
	The integral of a sum of functions is the same as the sum of their integrals.

5.What is the integral of the function f(x) = 7 over the interval [1,3]?
	0
	21
	7
	14

6.What does the chain rule for differentiation state?
	The derivative of a function within a function is the sum of the derivative of the inner function evaluated at the outer function and the derivative of the outer function.
	The derivative of a function within a function is the product of the derivative of the outer function evaluated at the inner function and the derivative of the inner function.
	The derivative of a function within a function is the product of the outer function and the inner function.
	The derivative of a function within a function is the sum of the outer function and the inner function.

7.What is the correct derivative formula for the quotient rule?
	(f'(x) * g(x) + f(x) * g'(x)) / (g(x))^2
	f'(x)/g(x)
	(f'(x) * g(x) - f(x) * g'(x)) / (g(x))^2
	f(x) * g'(x) + g(x) * f'(x)

8.Which of the following statements is a correct representation of the product rule for differentiation?
	The derivative of the product of two functions is given by h'(x) = f(x) * g(x).
	The derivative of the product of two functions is always equal to the product of their derivatives.
	The product rule only applies to functions that are not differentiable.
	The derivative of the product of two functions is given by h'(x) = f'(x) * g(x) + f(x) * g'(x).

9.What is the power rule for differentiation?
	The power rule is used only for functions without exponents.
	The power rule states that the derivative of x^n is simply n.
	The power rule states that the derivative of a function is equal to the original function multiplied by its exponent.
	The power rule states for a function f(x)=x^n with a constant n, the derivative f'(x)=n*x^(n-1).

Day 07
Common Derivatives
Polynomial and rational derivatives (4 min 59 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you'll explore many common derivatives. These derivatives will be drawn from many of the functions you learned about earlier in this course. Let's start off with derivatives for polynomial and rational functions. Both of these types of derivatives are fairly easy to work with once you are familiar with the differentiation rules you learned about previously. For polynomial functions, you just need to work with the constant, constant multiple, sum, difference, and power rules. For rational functions, you need to work with the quotient, constant, constant multiple, sum, difference, and power rules. You'll notice the main difference for the rational functions is just using the quotient rule as well. Let's look an example of a polynomial derivative. Let's get the derivative of the polynomial function, f of x equal to 2 multiplied by x to the 7th, plus 9 multiplied by x to the 3rd, minus 6 multiplied by x. You'll notice this polynomial function will require the constant, multiple, sum, difference, and power rules to solve. Let's look at each term individually. Let's get the derivative of 2 multiplied by x to the 7th. So, you can move the 2 to the outside so you have 2, and then you will bring down the power to get 2 multiplied by 7, multiplied by x to the 6th, which simplifies to 14 multiplied by x to the 6th. Next, let's get the derivative for 9 multiplied by x cubed. So again, you can move the 9 to the outside, and your derivative for x cubed is simply 3 multiplied by x squared, so you'll have 9 multiplied by 3, multiplied by x squared, which simplifies to 27 multiplied by x squared. Finally, you have the derivative for 6 multiplied by x, which is simply 6. Now, you will combine these terms by adding or subtracting them accordingly to get a final derivative of f prime of x equal to 14 multiplied by x to the 6th, plus 27 multiplied by x squared minus 6. Now, let's look at an example of a rational derivative. Let's get the derivative of the rational function f of x equals 2, multiplied by x plus 5, divide all of that by x minus 1. You will use the quotient rule to solve this, so you'll have f of x equal to g of x divided by h of x, which in this case, your g of x is equal to 2 multiplied by x plus 5, and h of x is equal to x minus 1. So if you get the derivative of g prime of x, this is just 2, and the derivative of h prime of x is equal to 1. Now you have a quotient rule formula, so you have f prime of x equal to g prime of x multiplied by h of x, minus g of x multiplied by h prime of x, divide all of that by h of x squared. This gets you f prime of x equal to 2 multiplied by x minus 1, then you'll subtract all that by 2 multiplied by x plus five, multiply that by 1. Then, you'll finally divide everything by x minus 1 squared. If you simplify this, you get a final result of f prime of x equal to -7 divided by x minus 1 squared. Let's look at another example of a rational derivative that's a little bit more complicated. Let's have f of x equal to x squared plus 3 multiplied by x, divide all that by x plus 1. So like last time, you'll use your quotient rule with f of x equal to g of x divided by h of x. In this case, your g of x is equal to x squared plus 3 multiplied by x, and your h of x is equal to x plus 1. In this case, g prime of x, using the power rule, is simply 2 multiplied by x plus 3, and then h prime of x is going to be a derivative of 1, just like last time. Now, let's bring up your quotient rule formula of f prime of x equal to g prime of x, multiplied by h of x minus g of x, multiplied by h prime of x, divide all that by h of x squared. Plug in your values to get f prime of x equal to 2 multiplied by x plus 3, multiply that chunk by x plus 1, and then you're going to subtract all of that by x squared plus 3 multiplied by x, and you're going to multiply all of that by 1. Then, you're finally going to divide everything by x plus 1 squared. Once you simplify this, you get a final result of f prime of x equal to x squared plus 2 multiplied by x plus 3, divide all of that by x plus 1 squared. The derivatives of polynomial and rational functions tend to be fairly straightforward once you know the key differentiation rules as you saw in the previous examples. I recommend taking time on your own to practice getting the derivatives of these types of functions. In the next video, I will show you how to work with trigonometric derivatives.

Trigonometric derivatives (5 min 26 seconds)
- [Instructor] Up next are trigonometric derivatives. Trigonometric functions are important through mathematics and calculus, but they can be complicated to understand. This is why I will take some time to review trigonometric derivatives in detail. Before I get into the trigonometric derivatives, let's first review a few key facts about trigonometric functions and their limits. First, the limit as theta approaches zero for sine of theta divided by theta is equal to one. Second, the limit as theta approaches zero of cosine of theta minus one, divided by theta is equal to zero. Both of these limits are gathered by using the Squeeze Theorem, which you learned about previously. Let's use these two limit facts about sine and cosine functions along with the fact that they're both oscillating functions to help gather their derivatives. You can imagine how these two functions interact with each other as they circulate the unit circle, where they tend to rise and fall in a consistent pattern that will be shown in their derivatives. These are the derivatives for the six trigonometric functions. So first, you have F prime of sine of X is equal to cosine of X. Then you have F prime of cosine of X is equal to negative sine of X. Then you have F prime of tangent of X, which is equal to secant squared of X. Then you have F prime of cosecant of X, which is equal to negative cosecant of X, multiplied by cotangent of X. Then you have F prime of secant of X is equal to secant of X multiplied by tangent of X. And finally, F prime of cotangent of X is equal to negative cosecant squared of X. Like I mentioned earlier, the different properties dealing with the sine and cosine functions are key to finding their derivatives. Once you know the derivatives for the sine and cosine functions, you can derive the other four trigonometric function derivatives from there. I highly recommend you memorize these six trigonometric derivatives, since that will be the easiest way to work with them in the long term. Let's look at a few examples of trigonometric derivatives in action. Let's start off by showing how to get the derivative for the tangent function with sine of X divided by cosine of X. So here, you have F of X equals sine of X divided by cosine of X, which as you know, is tangent of X. Using your Quotient Rule, you have F of X equal to G of X divided by H of X. In this case, your G of X is equal to sine of X, and H of X is equal to cosine of X. Let's get the derivatives of these. So you have G prime of X equal to cosine of X, and H prime of X equal to negative sine of X. Now you'll look at your Quotient Rule, so you have F prime of X equal to G prime of X, multiplied by H of X, subtract that by G of X, multiplied by H prime of X, divide all of that by H of X squared. Now let's use the Quotient Rule and the trigonometric derivatives you learned earlier for sine and cosine to plug in these values. So you have F prime of X equal to cosine of X, multiplied by cosine of X, subtracted by sign of X, multiplied by negative sine of X. Divide all that by cosine of X squared. If you simplify the top portion of this, you get F prime of X equal to cosine squared of X, plus sine squared of X is equal to cosine of X squared. Since cosine squared of X plus sine squared of X is equal to one due to the Pythagorean Theorem, this will then get you a result of F prime of X equal to one divided by cosine of X squared. And so here, you see the final result of F prime of X is equal to F prime of tangent of X, which is equal to secant squared of X. Let's look at another example, where this time you have the function F of X equal to five multiplied by secant of X, subtract that by eight, multiplied by cotangent of X, plus X squared. As you see here, you can use the sum and difference rules, the trigonometric derivatives you learned, and the Power Rule to solve this. Let's break this down into smaller pieces. So first, you have F prime of secant of X is equal to secant of X multiplied by tangent of X. Next, you have F prime of cotangent of X equal to negative cosecant squared of X. And finally, you have F prime of X squared is equal to two multiplied by X. Let's bring this back together along with those constant multiples you have to get F prime of X equal to five, multiplied by secant of X, multiplied by tangent of X, subtract that by eight, multiplied by negative cosecant squared of X, and add that with two multiplied by X. If you simplify that middle term, you'll get a final result of F prime of X equal to five, multiplied by secant of X, multiplied by tangent of X, plus eight multiplied by cosecant squared of X, plus two multiplied by X. Like I mentioned before, the best way to learn trigonometric derivatives is to memorize the derivatives for each of the six trigonometric functions. I recommend taking time on your own to practice getting the derivatives of these functions. I also suggest looking at the proofs of how to get these derivatives if you're interested in the behind the scenes of how they work. Up next, I will show you how to work with exponential derivatives.

Exponential derivatives (5 min 57 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next, let's take a look at exponential derivatives. These are derivatives that contain exponents where the variable itself is in the exponent, such as e to the x. This video will not cover functions such as x squared since you could use the power rule to solve those types of functions. Let's begin by looking at general exponential functions in the format of f of x equals a to the x, where a is a real number greater than zero, like I mentioned previously, you cannot use the power rule to solve this function because the variable is in the exponent itself instead of the base. If you wish to get the derivative of a function f of x equals a to the x, then you'll derive it in the following format, so you'll have f prime of x equals the natural log of a multiplied by a to the x. Essentially, this will simplify your function when it's derived to a constant natural logarithmic value multiplied by your original exponential function. Let's look at an example where f of x equals four to the x. Using the format you just looked at. You'll need to gather the natural log of a, which in this case, a equals four, from your function four to the x, and you'll need to multiply that by your original function, four to the x. This then simplifies to get f prime of x equals the natural log of four multiplied by four to the x. Now, let's look at a specific case for exponential derivatives, which is the natural exponential function. So this function is denoted by f of x equals e to the x. If you use the same rule as before, you'll get f prime of x equals the natural log of e multiplied by e to the x. Since the natural log of e equals one, then the derivative is multiplied by one. This means you could simplify your derivative to simply be f prime of x equals e to the x. Let's look at this in an example. Let's say you have the function f of x equals seven multiplied by e to the x. Using what you learned prior with the constant multiple rule, you can ignore the seven and just get the derivative of e to the x, which in this case is just itself, e to the x, where you have f prime of x equals seven multiplied by your derivative, which is e to the x. Now, let's look at a case where you have a function within your function. This will mean your function will be in the form of f of x equals a to the g of x, where g of x is another function. This time, the derivative of your function will be f prime of x equals the natural log of a multiplied by a to the g of x multiplied by g prime of x. Let's dissect this derivative result. So in this case, you still had the same pieces of the derivative like you did before with the natural log of a and your original function a to the g of x. You may be wondering though, why do you also need to multiply this derivative by g prime of x? You need to include this in your derivative result due to the chain rule. This is where you will multiply your outer function derivative, f prime of g of x by the derivative of your inner function, g prime of x. Let's use this in an example. Let's take a look at the function f of x equals two to the x squared plus three, where the x squared plus three is all in the exponent. Here, you'll get your natural log of a, which in this case, is just equal to the natural log of two, and then let's get the derivative of your exponent, which is the function g of x, which is just x squared plus three in this case. So g prime of x is then going to equal two multiplied by x because that plus three is going to cancel out. So let's plug this into your formula of f prime of x equals the natural log of a multiplied by a to the g of x multiplied by g prime of x to get a final derivative result of f prime of x equals the natural log of two multiplied by two to the x squared plus three multiplied by two multiplied by x. Now, let's look at the natural exponent case again, but this time, for having a function within a function. So this time, you have your function f of x equals e to the g of x, where g of x is some sort of other function. The derivative in this case is f prime of x equals e to the g of x multiplied by g prime of x. This is similar to the general exponential function within a function formula. But note that you don't need to add in the natural logarithm at the beginning since like before, since the natural log of e equals one, the derivative is multiplied by one and hence unnecessary to add. Let's take a look at one more example. This time, you have the function f of x equals e to the x cubed plus five multiplied by x, where the x cubed plus five multiplied by x is all in the exponent. This means your g of x equals x cubed plus five multiplied by x, and in this case, you'll want to get the derivative of it where you have g prime of x equals three multiplied by x squared plus five. Let's plug this into your formula of f prime of x equals e to the g of x multiplied by g prime of x. This gets you a final result of f prime of x equals e to the x cubed plus five multiplied by x, and then you'll multiply all of that by three, multiplied by x squared plus five. Knowing how to work with exponential functions, including those containing other functions, will be helpful when you work with more complex derivatives in the future. Up next, you will learn about logarithmic derivatives, where you'll see how they work similarly to exponential derivatives.

Logarithmic derivatives (6 min 53 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now let's explore logarithmic derivatives. These are derivatives that contain logarithms of variables or functions within the logarithms. You will notice how these derivatives function similarly to those of exponents, which makes sense with how they are related to each other. Let's begin by looking at general logarithmic functions in the format, f of x equals log base a of x where x is greater than zero, a is greater than zero, and a is not equal to one. Note that a is some sort of a real number here. As I mentioned previously, you cannot use any of the previous rules to solve this type of function because the variable is within a logarithm. If you wish to get the derivative of a function f of x equals log base a of x, it will then be in the format of f prime of x equals one divided by the natural log of a multiplied by x. Note that the natural log of a multiplied by x is all in the denominator of that fraction. Essentially, this will simplify your derivative to be the inverse of a constant natural logarithmic value multiplied by your original variable x that you found within your logarithmic function. The way you get this derivative is by using what is called the change of base formula. There are many variations of this formula, but the goal is to change the base of your logarithm from some value a to some value b. There are various versions of this formula, such as log base b of x divided by log base b of a, and then there's natural log of x over natural log of a, and finally, log of x over log of a. For these derivatives, you'll be using the change of base formula natural log of x over natural log of A. This is because natural algorithms tend to be the easiest to work with. Let's take a look at an example where f of x equals log base five of x. If you wish to get the derivative of this function, you can then plug in your value for the natural log of a, which in this case will be the natural log of five since that is the base of your natural algorithm, and then plug this into your derivative formula to get f prime of x equals one divided by the natural log of five multiplied by x. Now, let's take a look at the specific case for the natural logarithmic function, f of x equals natural log of x. If you use the same rule as before, you'll get f prime of x equals one divided by a natural log of e multiplied by x. Like you saw with the natural exponential function, since the natural log of e equals one in this case, you will have your derivative multiplied by one. This means you could simplify your derivative to just be f prime of x equals one divided by x. Let's look at this in an example. Let's say you had the function f of x equals nine multiplied by the natural log of x. And in this case, you can ignore the nine since it's a constant multiple that you can bring in after the fact. As you saw previously, the derivative of the natural log of x, it's just one divided by x, so your result of f prime of x equals nine divided by x for your derivative. Now, let's look at the case where you have a function within a function. For logarithms, this will have the function being f of x equals log base a of g of x. In this case, your derivative will be f prime of x equals g prime of x divided by the natural log of a multiplied by g of x. Let's dissect this derivative result. So you still have the same pieces of the derivative that you did before with the natural log of a and the interior part of your logarithm, which in this case is just g of x instead of x. You may be wondering why do you also need to multiply this by g prime of x, which ends up in the numerator of your derivative? Just like with exponential functions within functions, you need to include this in your result due to the chain rule where you multiply your outer function derivative, f prime of g of x by the derivative of your inner function, g prime of x. Let's look at this in action. Let's say you have the function f of x equals log base three of x squared plus 45. First, let's gather the natural logarithm of A, which in this case, is just the natural logarithm of three. In this case, your g of x is equal to x squared plus 45 since that is the interior part of the logarithmic function. Let's get g prime of x in order to find your full derivative, which in this case, is equal to just two multiplied by x because that plus 45 drops off. Let's plug this into your formula of f prime of x equals g prime of x over the natural a of a multiplied by g of x to get you a final derivative result of f prime of x equals two multiplied by x divided by the natural log of three multiplied by x squared plus 45. Finally, let's look at the natural logarithmic case where you have a function within a function. So in this case, you have the function f of x equals the natural log of g of x where the derivative is f prime of x equals g prime of x over g of x. Like before, this is similar to the more generalized logarithmic function, but you do not need to add in the natural log and the denominator since the natural log of e equals one, the derivative will be multiplied by one, meaning that you can just leave it out. Let's look at one more example. Let's have the function f of x equal the natural log of two multiplied by x to the fourth. In this case, your g of x is equal to two multiplied by x to the fourth, and let's get the derivative of that to get g prime of x equals eight multiplied by x cubed. Let's plug this into your formula f prime of x equals g prime of x divided by g of x. Plugging in your values gets you f prime of x equals eight, multiplied by x cubed, divided by two, multiplied by x to the fourth. You can then simplify this to get a final derivative result of f prime of x equals four divided by x. Knowing how to work with logarithmic derivatives, including those containing other functions, will help with more complex derivatives you'll explore in the future. It is also amazing how the change of base formula helps simplify the process of finding these types of derivatives. Next step, let's take a look at inverse derivatives.

Inverse derivatives (5 min 22 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's take a moment to look at derivatives for inverse functions. If you remember from the inverse functions video earlier on in this course, an inverse of a function is essentially the reverse of it. Knowing how to deal with these derivatives will be important in order to help you understand inverse trigonometric derivatives. Let's say you have an inverse function for the function f of x, and it is notated by f to the -1 of x. In order to get the derivative of that inverse function f to the -1 of x, you get f-1 prime of x is equal to 1 divided by f prime of f-1 of x. Note that this only works if your original function f of x is both invertible and differentiable. This formula can be a bit difficult to understand at first with everything feeling like it is flipping around. Essentially, it is saying that derivative of the inverse of your original function f of x is equal to 1 divided by the derivative of your original function f of x evaluated at the specific point of f to the -1 of x. Note that you can prove this formula by using inverse relationships and the chain rule. Let's take a look at a few examples to make more sense of how these derivatives work. Let's have your original function f of x equal to x cubed. The inverse of this function is f to the -1 of x equal to x to the 1/3, also known as the cube root of x. Again, this is because the inverse function essentially undoes what the original function is doing. Let's begin by finding the derivative of your original function f of x equal to x cubed. You can use the power rule to get f prime of x equal to 3 multiplied by x squared. Now you have your derivative formula, so you have f to the -1 prime of x equals 1 divided by f prime of f to the -1 of x, so you have f to the -1 prime of x equal to 1 divided by 3, multiplied by x to the 1/3 squared. If you simplify this, this then gets you a final result of f to the -1 prime of x equal to 1/3 multiplied by x to the -2/3. Let's take a look at another example. Let's say you have the function f of x equal to 2 divided by x plus 7, and you want to find the derivative of the inverse of that function. First, let's gather the inverse of the function itself. So let's change f of x to be y to make this easier to solve. So you'll have y equal to 2 divided by x plus 7. And then you will solve for x. So the way you do this is you move your x over and then you move your y over, and then you subtract 7 from both sides to get x equal to 2 divided by y minus 7 as your result. You can then convert the y back to an x and the x back to a y to keep things consistent in your calculations, where you'll have y equal to 2 divided by x minus 7 to get you an official inverse of f to the -1 of x equals 2 divided by x minus 7. Now that you have the inverse function, you could start by finding the derivative of your original function f of x equals 2 divided by x plus 7 using your quotient rule. Once you do this, you get f prime of x equal to -2 divided by x plus 7 squared. And then let's have your formula f to the -1 prime of x equals 1 divided by f prime of f-1 of x. And plug in your values to get f to the -1 prime of x equals 1 divided by -2 divided by 2 divided by x minus 7 plus 7, and you'll square all of that. Once you simplify that, you get f to the -1 prime of x equals 1 divided by -x squared divided by 2, which gets you a final derivative for your inverse function to be -2 divided by x squared. Note that you can also do inverse derivatives with Euler functions, so again, exponentials and logarithms with the natural log and e to the whatever. So in this case, you have your function f of x equal to e to the x. And as you learned prior, the inverse function of e to the x is simply ln of x, or again, your natural log of x. If you get the derivative of your original function e to the x, you get f prime of x equals e to the x since again, the derivative is itself. Now let's plug this into your formula of f to the -1 prime of x equals 1 divided by f prime of f to the -1 of x, and you get f-1 prime of x equals 1 divided by e to the ln of x. Since the e and the ln cancel out, you get a final result of f to the -1 prime of x equals 1 divided by x. Dealing with inverse derivatives can be a bit tricky at first, but they will get easier with more practice. It's also a good reminder to keep working on learning how to find or memorize inverse functions depending on the case. You'll expand upon what you learned in this video in the next video, where I explain inverse trigonometric derivatives.

Inverse trigonometric derivatives (6 min)
- [Instructor] Now that you learned about trigonometric derivatives and inverse derivatives, let's combine these two topics and learn about inverse trigonometric derivatives. Let's first review the notation for inverse trigonometric functions. If you remember from before, there are two different notations for inverse trigonometric functions. So here you have sine to the negative one of x is equal to arc sine of x. You have cosine to the negative one of x, which is equal to arc cosine of x. Then you have tangent to the negative one of x, which is equal to arc tangent of x. Then you have cosecant to the negative one of x, which is equal to arc cosecant of x. Then you have secant to the negative one of x, which is equal to arc secant of x. And then you'll have cotangent to the negative one of x, which is equal to arc cotangent of x. I will generally refer to these in the arc form such as arc sine or arctan or arc tangent, but note that I will sometimes mix the formats that I show these in equations. Something to know before I show you the six derivatives for the inverse trigonometric functions is that their derivatives are actually algebraic functions. This is different from many cases where the resulting function from the derivative is usually in the same function category as the original function. For example, the derivative of sine is cosine, and again, they're both trigonometric functions, but in this case, that's not the same. This is an excellent case to show that sometimes the derivative of a function does not have to be in the same category as the original function. Let's take a look at the derivatives for the six inverse trigonometric functions. So the way you will often notate a derivative is d over dx, and then you'll have in parentheses the either arc sine or the sine to the negative one of x, and again, for whichever trigonometric function you're looking at. So here we have the derivative for arc sine of x, which is equal to one divided by the square root of one minus x squared. Next, you have the derivative of arc cosine, which is equal to negative one divided by square root of one minus x squared. Then you have the derivative of arc tangent of x, which is equal to one divided by one plus x squared. Next, you have the derivative of arc cosecant of x, which is equal to negative one divided by the absolute value of x multiplied by the square root of x squared minus one. Then you have the derivative of arc secant of x, which is equal to one divided by the absolute value of x multiplied by x squared minus one. And then finally, you have the derivative of arc cotangent of x, which is equal to negative one divided by one plus x squared. You'll notice that these derivatives, like their counterparts of just regular trigonometric derivatives, often have very similar structures throughout since they are very related to each other. These six derivatives can be found and proven by using the inverse function theorem. These then simplify from using the chain rule to get the algebraic derivatives you saw previously. I highly recommend you memorize these six inverse trigonometric derivatives since that will be the easiest way to work with them in the future. Let's look at a few examples of inverse trigonometric derivatives in action. Let's start by showing how to get the derivative of f of x, where the function is equal to six multiplied by arc cotangent of x subtracted by three multiplied by arc cosine of x. Let's split this up. So to get the derivative of arc cotangent of x, it is simply negative one divided by one plus x squared, and then the derivative for arc cosine of x is equal to negative one divided by the square root of one minus x squared. Let's plug these into your derivative to get f prime of x equal to six multiplied by negative one divided by one plus x squared. Subtract that by three multiplied by negative one divided by square root of one minus x squared. If you simplify this, you get a final result of f prime of x equals negative six divided by one plus x squared, and then you'll add three divided by square root of one minus x squared. Let's look at another example where this time you have f of x equal to x squared multiplied by arc sine of x. In this case, you will use your product rule, so you have f of x equals g of x multiplied by h of x. In this case, g of x equals x squared, and h of x equals arc sine of x. If you get your derivatives, you have g prime of x equals two multiplied by x, and then you have h prime of x equals one divided by square root of one minus x squared. Now, let's use your product rule to get f prime of x equals g prime of x multiplied by h of x plus g of x multiplied by h prime of x. This gets you f prime of x equal to two multiplied by x, and then you multiply that by arc sine of x. Then you'll add that by x squared multiplied by one divided by square root of one minus x squared. If you simplify this, you get a final result of two multiplied by x multiplied by sine to the negative one of x plus x squared divided by the square root of one minus x squared. Like I mentioned before, the best way to learn and use inverse trigonometric derivatives is to memorize the derivatives for each of these six functions. I recommend taking time on your own to practice getting the derivatives of these function and making sure you have those six key ones memorized. I also suggest taking a look at the proofs of how to get these derivatives if you are interested in how they work. In the last video of this chapter, I will expand on deriving trigonometric functions by exploring how to work with hyperbolic derivatives.

Hyperbolic derivatives (6 min 13 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this derivatives chapter by exploring hyperbolic derivatives. Before I get into the derivatives, though, let's first introduce what hyperbolic functions are in the first place. Hyperbolic functions are similar to trigonometric functions, but they're defined using a hyperbola instead of a unit circle. These types of functions are not as common to work with, but it is good to know their derivatives in case they do come up in your future work. Let's first review what the six hyperbolic functions are and what they equal. First, you have sine h of x equal to e to the x minus e to the minus x divided by two. You'll notice the notation has an h on the end, such as, again, sine h instead of just sine to indicate that it is a hyperbolic function. You'll also notice that they have the natural exponent e to the x often used in their functions due to how they relate to exponential contexts. So let's review the other five hyperbolic functions. So you have cosine h of x equal to e to the x plus e to the minus x divided by two. Next, you have tangent h of x is equal to sine h of x divided by cosine h of x, which is e to the x minus e to the minus x divided by e to the x plus e to the minus x. Next, you have cosecant h of x, which is equal to one divided by sine h of x, which is equal to two divided by e to the x minus e to the minus x. Next, you have cosecant h of x, which is equal to one divided by cosine h of x, which is equal to two divided by e to the x plus e to the minus x. And finally, you have cotangent h of x, which is equal to cosine h of x divided by sine h of x, which equals e to the x plus e to the minus x divided by e to the x minus e to the minus x. There are a few hyperbolic function properties you should take note of before we proceed. First, you have f prime of e to the minus x is equal to negative e to the minus x. You'll remember this from earlier, but again, it's something good to keep in mind. Next, you have sine h of negative x is equal to negative sine h of x. Then you have cosine h of negative x is equal to cosine h of x. After that, you have cosine h squared of x minus sine h squared of x is equal to one, and, finally, you have one minus tangent h squared of x is equal to secant h squared of x. Now that you've explored what the hyperbolic functions are and a few of their key properties, now you can find their derivatives. Like with inverse trigonometric functions, hyperbolic functions will often be notated with d/dx for the derivatives, and then again, the function itself, for example, sine h of x will be on the inside. So you'll have the derivative of sine h of x is equal to cosine h of x, and then you have the derivative of cosine h of x is equal to sine h of x. Then you have the derivative of tangent h of x is equal to secant h squared of x. Then you have the derivative of cosecant h of x is equal to negative cosecant h of x multiplied by cotangent h of x. Then you have the derivative of cosecant h of x is equal to negative cosecant h of x multiplied by tangent h of x. Finally, you have the derivative of cotangent h of x is equal to cosecant h squared of x. You'll notice these derivatives look very similar to your typical trigonometric derivatives with a few minor changes, and also note that, again, for example, with the derivative of sine h of x equal to cosine h of x, you can then use those previous hyperbolic functions to get the full algebraic version of the function. These six hyperbolic derivatives are found using exponential and hyperbolic properties. I highly recommend you memorize these six hyperbolic derivatives, like with the other trigonometric function derivatives, since that tends to be the easiest way to work with them. Let's look at a few examples of hyperbolic derivatives. First, let's get the derivative for the function f of x equals tangent h of x minus cosine h of x. Like you saw before, the derivative for tangent h of x is equal to secant h squared of x, and the derivative for cosine h of x is equal to sine h of x. Subtract these from each other to get a final result of f prime of x equals secant h squared f x minus sine h of x. Let's look at a little bit more complicated example this time. Let's get the derivative for the function f of x equals four multiplied by x squared multiplied by sine h of x. This time you will want to use the product rule along with the hyperbolic derivative you learned prior. In this case, you'll have f of x equals g of x multiplied by h of x. In this case, your g of x is equal to four multiplied by x squared, and then your h of x is equal to sine h of x. In this case, your g prime of x is equal to eight multiplied by x using the power rule, and your h prime of x is equal to cosine h of x using the derivatives you learned prior. Now, let's combine this with your project rule of f prime of x equals g prime of x multiplied by h of x plus g of x multiplied by h prime of x. This gets you a result of f prime of x equals eight multiplied by x, multiply that by sine h of x, and then you'll add four multiplied by x squared multiplied by cosine h of x. Like I mentioned before, the best way to learn hyperbolic derivatives is to memorize the derivatives for each of these six hyperbolic functions. I recommend taking time on your own to practice getting the derivatives of these functions. Remember, these functions are not as common as the other ones, but they're good to know in case you do encounter them. This wraps up how to work with many common derivatives of various functions. As you saw, it is a mix of learning new techniques using differentiation rules and memorization. Remember, practice is what will help you continue to solidify the knowledge you are learning throughout this course. In the next chapter, you'll explore more complex derivatives such as higher order derivatives and other differentiation techniques.

	Differentiate f(x) normally and then take the negative reciprocal

	f'(x) = 1 / (a * x)
	f'(x) = ln(a) / x
	f'(x) = 1/(ln(a) * x)
	f'(x) = a * ln(x)

	f'(x)=ln(x)*e^x
	f'(x)=e^x
	f'(x)=e^x * ln(1)

	cos(x)
	sec^2(x)
	tan(x)
