Day 08
Complex Derivatives
Second-order derivatives (4 min 52 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you will explore more complex derivatives, including higher order derivatives and other differentiation techniques. The other differentiation techniques will include implicit and logarithmic differentiation. Let's start off by exploring higher order derivatives. In the last few chapters, you learned what a derivative is and how to calculate it. So you may be wondering, "What is a higher order derivative?" A higher order derivative is when you take the derivative of a function multiple times. So instead of just deriving a function once, you may derive that original function, then derive the result of that first derivative, and then you repeat that as many times as you need to. Let's start off with exploring second order derivatives. A second order derivative is when you take a derivative two times in succession. Essentially, it is just doing the same thing twice. This is often notated by f double prime x or f to the 2 x where the 2 has parentheses around it. The reason for the second notation is it can become cumbersome to count how many tick marks there are, especially as they grow in number with the higher the order of the derivative is. Second order derivatives have the same properties as regular derivatives do, and they function basically the same, just instead of doing it once, you do it twice. It is important to note that second order derivatives measure acceleration. Hence, this is why they are often referred to as acceleration. In fields such as physics, the derivative is often represented with respect to time, t as d squared of x divided by dt squared. Note, that again, this is another way to notate that second order derivative. Let's look at a few examples of how second order derivatives work. Let's first look at the function f of x equal to 3 multiplied by x cubed plus 2 multiplied by x plus 7. Let's start off by getting the first derivative, so you have f prime of x equal to 9 multiplied by x squared plus 2. Now you're going to get the derivative again, so this time you're getting the derivative of 9 multiplied by x squared plus 2. So again, using the rules that you learned previously, the second derivative f double prime x will equal 18 multiplied by x. Something you'll notice as you continue to take more and more derivatives is you sometimes slowly lose more information about the original function over time. In the last example, you ended up losing the last two pieces of the original function, 2 multiplied by x plus 7, because they got zeroed out with the derivatives. This is something to keep in mind for future content. Let's look at another example of a second order derivative. So this time you have f of x equal to 4 multiplied by x multiplied by e to the x. Let's get the first order derivatives, so you have f prime of x equal to 4 multiplied by e to the x. Add that with 4 multiplied by x multiplied by e to the x. Again, the way you get this is using the previous rules you learned, along with working with exponential functions, such as e to the x. Now, to get the second order derivative, you're going to derive the result you got in that first derivative. So f double prime of x is equal to 4 multiplied bye to the x plus 4 multiplied by x multiplied by e to the x plus 4 multiplied by e to the x. You can simplify this result to simply be e to the x and multiply that by 8 plus 4 multiplied by x. You'll notice that some examples like this one will solely multiply in how many terms they have as the order of the derivative increases. So as you can tell, it just gets longer and longer the more derivatives you take. Why would you need to take a second order derivative in the first place? Like mentioned earlier, these derivatives are important in physics to measure acceleration when designing rollercoasters to ensure they have enough acceleration to have a fun but safe ride. In economics, they're used to help companies determine whether increasing their production will increase their profit or not. In business, companies can use them to determine the optimal pricing for the products they are selling. Once you understand the concept of second order derivatives, any derivatives higher than that are fairly straightforward with the simple repetition of the process. I suggest taking a moment to feel comfortable with the concept of second order derivatives in order to feel confident as you continue to take more of them. Up next, you'll continue to expand upon this concept with third order derivatives.

Third-order derivatives (4 min 58 seconds)
- [Instructor] Once you have learned the concept of second-order derivatives, any derivatives with a higher order than that are fairly easy to learn. Essentially, you just keep repeating the same process as many times as needed. In this video, you'll explore third-order derivatives. A third-order derivative is when you take a derivative three times in succession. Essentially, it is doing the same thing three times in a row. It is often notated by f triple prime x or f to the 3 of x. And again, note that the 3 is usually in parentheses. Remember, the second notation is needed because it can become cumbersome to count how many tick marks there are, as you can tell already, even just with three of them. Like with second-order derivatives, third-order derivatives have the same properties as regular first-order derivatives do. It is important to note that third-order derivatives measure the rate of change of acceleration. These derivatives are often referred to as jerk or jolt. In fields such as physics, the derivative is often represented by d to the 3 of x divided by dt to the 3. And again, that is with respect to time, t. And like before, this is another way that you can notate a third-order derivative. Let's look at a few examples of how third-order derivatives work. Let's first look at the function f of x equal to 4 multiplied by x to the 5th plus 7 multiplied by x cubed plus 9 multiplied by x plus 1. Let's get the first derivative using the rules that you learned before. So you have f prime of x equal to 20 multiplied by x to the 4th plus 21 multiplied by x squared plus 9. Now let's get the second-order derivative. So you have f double prime of x equal to 80 multiplied by x to the 3 plus 42 multiplied by x. Finally, you'll get your third-order derivative. So again, one more time. We have f triple prime of x equal to 240 multiplied by x squared plus 42. Like you noticed before, the more derivatives you take, the more information you will tend to lose over time for many functions that you take derivatives of. In the previous example, you ended up losing the last two pieces of the original function 9 multiplied by x plus 1. This is because they got zeroed out with the derivatives you were taking. Again, something to keep in mind for future content. Let's look at another example, but this time, let's have f of x equal to 5 to the x. Let's get the first derivative f prime of x. And this equals to the natural log of 5 multiplied by five to the x. Remember, you get this from what you learned in the exponential derivatives video. Let's get the next derivative of this. So here you have f double prime of x equal to natural log of 5 multiplied by natural log of 5 multiplied by 5 to the x. You can simplify this to the natural log of 5 squared multiplied by 5 to the x. Reason why the natural log of 5 shows up twice is the first derivative you have the natural log of 5 multiplied by 5 to the x. You essentially treat that natural log of 5 as just a constant. And again, you basically just keep getting that derivative over and over. Let's do this one more time to get f triple prime of x. And this equals natural log of 5 cubed multiplied by 5 to the x. You'll notice that some examples like this one tend to look very similar with each derivative, where the first derivative just seems to slowly grow over time, or it keeps the main function intact throughout the different iterations. So for example, with this one, 5 to the x still stays consistent through all the derivatives, but your natural log of 5 just slowly grows in number. Why would you need to take a third-order derivative in the first place? Like I mentioned earlier, these derivatives are important in physics to measure the rate of change of acceleration. For example, when manufacturing, how fast or slow to move elevators up and down. They can be used in robotics to ensure robotic arms in an assembly line move in a smooth enough fashion in order to do their job correctly. They are also used in other fields, such as economics, to measure any changes in acceleration of economic growth or decline. In biology, they're also used to measure how rapidly population growth or decline is changing. Like I mentioned before, once you understand the concept of second-order derivatives, any derivatives higher than that are fairly straightforward with, again, the simple repetition of the process. Up next, you'll continue to expand upon this concept with fourth-order derivatives.

Fourth-order derivatives (4 min 32 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's continue with exploring higher-order derivatives by taking a look at fourth-order derivatives. Like I mentioned before, once you know how to do one derivative, then you know how to do higher-order derivatives, since it is just doing the same thing over and over. Let's get started by looking at a definition. A fourth-order derivative is when you take a derivative four times in succession. Essentially, it is doing the same thing four times in a row. It is often notated by F with four prime symbols of X, or F to the fourth of X. Again, know that the second notation with the F and the parentheses four of X is often used especially for fourth-order derivatives because four prime symbols is a lot to count. Like the second-order and third-order derivatives, fourth-order derivatives have the same properties as regular first-order derivatives do. It is important to note that fourth-order derivatives measure the rate of change of the jerk with respect to time. These derivatives are often referred to as snap or jounce. They're often used to measure curvature, smoothness, and stability. In fields such as physics, the derivative with respect to time T is often notated by D to the fourth of X divided by DT to the fourth. Again, like before, this is another way that you can notate your fourth-order derivative is this type of notation. Let's look at a few examples of how fourth-order derivatives work. Let's look at the function F of X equal to two multiplied by X to the sixth plus three multiplied by X to the fifth plus eight multiplied by X squared plus 10. So in this example, you're simply just going to take the derivative four different times. So let's start with F prime of X equals 12 multiplied by X to the fifth plus 15 multiplied by X to the fourth plus 16 multiplied by X. Next, your second-order derivative is F double prime X is equal to 60 multiplied by X to the fourth plus 60 multiplied by X to the third plus 16. Let's do it again with the third-order derivative. So you have F to the three of X is 240 multiplied by X to the third plus 180 multiplied by X squared. Finally, you get to your fourth-order derivative, so you have F to the fourth of X is equal to 720 multiplied by X squared plus 360 multiplied by X. Let's look at another example, but this time, let's look at the trigonometric function F of X equals sine of X. In this case, you'll have your first derivative, F prime of X equals cosine of X. Then you'll have F double prime X equal to negative sine of X. Now you have your third-order derivative, F to the three of X, which is equal to negative cosine X. Finally, you have your fourth-order derivative, F to the fourth X, which is equal sine of X. You'll notice in the previous example how the fourth derivative of your function F of X is actually equal to your original function. This is because some functions, such as the sine and cosine trigonometric functions, have cyclical patterns where they will slowly cycle through and repeat their derivatives. So essentially, no matter how many derivatives you take, you will only get one of the four results shown in the previous example for the derivatives of sine of X. Note that this process is basically the same for cosine of X, except it just starts in a different spot. So why would you need to take a fourth-order derivative in the first place? Like I mentioned earlier, these derivatives are important in physics to measure snap, for example, to help create smooth paths for airplanes, rockets, and cars. In civil engineering, the snap is often used when designing bridges to ensure they can withstand varying loads being on them without breaking. And finally, in audio and image processing, these types of derivatives are used in algorithms to help create smoother and cleaner signals or visuals. Now that you know how to do second, third, and fourth-order derivatives, you know how to do any derivatives higher than four as well. You just keep taking as many derivatives as you need. In the next video, I will pivot topics a bit to discuss a key differentiation technique called implicit differentiation.

Implicit differentiation (6 min 58 seconds)
 - [Instructor] So far, you have used standard differentiation techniques. There are some cases, though, where standard differentiation will not suffice. This is where implicit differentiation and logarithmic differentiation come in handy. Let's start off by exploring implicit differentiation. There are some cases where the equation you are taking a derivative of is not in the standard format y equals f of x. There are also sometimes cases where the dependent variable, such as y, is not isolated to one side of the equation. For example, if you have y equal to x squared plus four multiplied by y cubed. This makes it difficult to solve for your dependent variable y explicitly, and it frequently arises in equations for relations that define shapes or curves, like circles and hyperbolas. This is where implicit differentiation comes in handy. Implicit differentiation is the process where you are differentiating equations where the dependent variable y is not isolated on one side of the equation. In this process, you differentiate both sides of the equation with respect to x by treating y as a function of x. Note, the chain rule is heavily used in this process since y greatly depends on x. Let's review the steps to conduct implicit differentiation. The three steps are, step one, differentiate both sides of your equation implicitly with respect to x. You'll use the chain rule where you have your dependent variable to get d over dx multiplied by y is equal to dy over dx. Step two, you'll simplify the equation by collecting all the dy/dx terms on one side of the equation. And finally, step three, you'll solve for your derivative dy/dx by isolating it to find the derivative of y with respect to x. Let's take a look at this technique in action with a few examples. Let's look at the equation of a circle x squared plus y squared equals 25. As you could tell, y is not isolated on one side of this equation. Your first step is to differentiate both sides of the equation implicitly with respect to x. So the way you do this is you have d over dx multiplied by x squared plus d over dx multiplied by y squared is equal to d over dx multiplied by 25. Now you'll differentiate each piece of the equation, so the derivative of x squared with respect to x is two multiplied by x. Next, you have the derivative d of dx for y squared. And remember, this is actually with respect to x, so you'll need to use the chain rule to get a result of two multiplied by y multiplied by dy/dx. And finally, the derivative of 25, since it is a constant, is just zero. Now you're ready for step two. This time you will want to isolate dy/dx by first subtracting both sides of the equation by 2x. So this will get you two multiplied by y multiplied by dy/dx is equal to negative two multiplied by x. Then you'll divide both sides of the equation by 2y to get dy over dx equal to negative two multiplied by x, divided by two multiplied by y. Finally, you can simplify this a little bit more to simply get dy over dx is equal to negative x over y. Since you only have one dy/dx term, you have already accomplished step three, meaning your final result is dy over dx equals the negative x over y. Let's look at another example where this time you want to differentiate the equation x cubed plus y cubed equals sine of x plus sine of y. Your first step is to differentiate both sides of the equation implicitly with respect to x. So this gets you d over dx multiplied by x cubed plus d over dx multiplied by y cubed is equal to d over dx multiplied by sine of x plus d over dx multiplied by sine y. Now let's take the derivatives of the different parts of this equation. So for the first part, for d of dx x cubed, you simply use the power rule to get three multiplied by x squared. The next part is d over dx multiplied by y cubed, which again, this time you will use the chain rule and the power rule to get three multiplied by y squared multiplied by dy over dx. Next, you have your derivative of d of dx sine of x, which, as you know, is simply cosine of x. And then finally, you need to use the chain rule one more time for the derivative d of dx of sine y to get cosine of y multiplied by dy/dx. This gets you the final result of three multiplied by x squared plus three multiplied by y squared multiplied by dy/dx is equal to cosine of x plus cosine of y multiplied by dy/dx. With step two, you'll want to isolate dy/dx by putting all the dy/dx terms on the left side of the equation. So if you do this, you get three multiplied by y squared multiplied by dy/dx subtracted by cosine of y multiplied by dy/dx, and this is equal to cosine of x subtracted by three multiplied by x squared. With step three, you will solve for your derivative dy/dx by isolating it to find the derivative of y with respect to x. Since both of your terms on the left side had the dy/dx, you can then put them in parentheses like so, where you have dy/dx multiplied by three multiplied by y squared minus cosine of y. And again, that is still equal to cosine of x subtracted by three multiplied by x squared. You'll then divide both sides by your three multiplied by y squared minus cosine of y to get a final result of dy/dx equals cosine of x minus three multiplied by x squared. Divide all of that by three multiplied by y squared minus cosine of y. There are various applications for using implicit differentiation. In economics, it can be used to find derivatives in systems of equations with interdependent variables. In engineering, it can be used to analyze curves and surfaces where y is indirectly related to x. And finally, in physics, it can be used to model rates of change like shadow length. The implicit differentiation method helps open the possibilities to drive equations that are not in the standard format and saves you time in the process of taking those derivatives, I suggest practicing with more equations using implicit differentiation on your own. Up next, you'll learn about another differentiation technique, logarithmic differentiation.

Logarithmic differentiation (8 min 5 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up by exploring another differentiation technique. In this video, you will learn what logarithmic differentiation is, how it works with implicit differentiation, and how to calculate derivatives using this method. There are some cases where taking the derivative of a function is too complicated using the traditional techniques you have learned so far, for example, if the derivative is a product of multiple functions, a complex quotient, or a function with variable bases and exponents. This is when a technique like logarithmic differentiation can be useful. Logarithmic differentiation is the process when you simplify a derivative using logarithms. In this process, you take the natural logarithm of both sides of the function, and then apply implicit differentiation. This helps you utilize the properties of logarithms to help break down your function into simpler terms to solve. So what are the steps to conduct logarithmic differentiation? The four steps are step one, you take the natural logarithm of both sides of your equation. So let's say you have y equals f of x. You would then take the natural log to get ln of y equals ln of f of x. So again, the natural log on both sides. Step two is you simplify your now logarithmic expression using logarithmic properties. Step three, you differentiate both sides of the equation implicitly with respect to x. And finally, step four, you solve for your derivative by isolating it to one side of the equation. There are three common logarithmic properties you can use to simplify your equation. The first one is the natural log of a multiplied by b is equal to the natural log of a plus the natural log of b. Next, if you have the natural log of a divided by b, this is equal to the natural log of a minus the natural log of b. And finally, if you have the natural log of a to the n, where n is some sort of constant value, that is then equal to n multiplied by the natural log of a. Let's take a look at using logarithmic differentiation with a few examples. Let's say you have the function y equals x to the x. This is a case where the derivative is a function with variable bases and exponents. Your first step is to take the natural logarithm of both sides to get the natural log of y equals the natural log of x to the x. In this case, you can use that property of the natural log of a to the n equals n multiplied by the natural log of a. So this simplifies your equation to be the natural log of y equals x multiplied by the natural log of x in order to complete step two to simplify your equation. Now this looks like an equation that you can derive. So for step three, you'll want to derive this equation implicitly with respect to x. Using the chain rule since y is a function of x on the left side, you'll get d, dx multiplied by the natural log of y equals one divided by y multiplied by y prime. Using the product rule on the right side, you can get one multiplied by the natural log of x plus x multiplied by one divided by x, which is equal to the natural log of x plus one. Finally, let's isolate y prime. So currently, you have one over y multiplied by y prime equal to the natural log of x plus one. So you can then move the y by multiplying it on both sides to get y prime equal to y multiplied by the natural log of x plus one. If you remember from earlier, your function y was equal to x to the x, so you could substitute this in to get a final derivative of y prime equals x to the x multiplied by the natural log of x plus one. Let's look at another example where this time, you want to differentiate the function y equals x squared multiplied by the square root of x plus one. This is the case where the derivative is a product of multiple functions. So your first step is to take the natural logarithm of both sides to get the natural log of y equals the natural log of x squared multiplied by the square root of x plus one. Step two, you can use the first logarithmic property mentioned earlier, which is the natural log of a multiplied by b equals the natural log of a plus the natural log of b. So this simplifies your equation to be the natural log of y equals the natural log of x squared plus the natural log of the square root of x plus one. This equation is still a little complicated to solve, so let's use another logarithmic property. This time, let's use the property of natural log of a to the n equals n multiplied by the natural log of a. So you can use this with your equation to get the natural log of x squared plus the natural log of the square root of x plus one equals two multiplied by the natural log of x plus 1/2 multiplied by the natural log of x plus one. For step three, you will want to derive this equation implicitly with respect to x. Using the chain rule since y is a function of x on the left side, like last time, you get d over dx multiplied by the natural log of y equals one divided by y multiplied by y prime. So let's look at the right side of your equation where you have the derivative d, dx of two multiplied by the natural log of x plus d, dx multiplied by 1/2 multiplied by the natural log of x plus one. And so the first portion is simply going to be two multiplied by the derivative of natural log of x, which is just one over x. So this gets you two over x, and then your second portion is going to be the derivative of the natural log of x plus one, which is one divided by x plus one. And then using the chain rule, you'll then multiply that to get one divided by two multiplied by x plus one. So now you have the equation one divided by y multiplied by y prime equals two divided by x plus one divided by two multiplied by x plus one. If you isolate just y prime to the left side of the equation, you multiply both sides by y to get y prime equals two divided by x plus one divided by two multiplied by x plus one, and you multiply all of that by y. If you use the information you had initially where y is equal to x squared multiplied by the square root of x plus one, you can then plug that in to get y prime equals two divided by x plus one divided by two multiplied by x plus one, and then you'll multiply that by x squared multiplied by the square root of x plus one. You can simplify this a bit to get a final result of y prime equals two multiplied by x multiplied by the square root of x plus one plus x squared divided by two multiplied by the square root of x plus one. There are various applications for using logarithmic differentiation. In engineering, it can be used in rate of change problems. In physics, it can be used for growth models and power law relationships. In economics, it can be used in elasticity and demand functions that contain exponents. This derivation method seems a bit convoluted at first, but as you saw from the few examples, it has some great advantages to really simplify the derivation process once you take those natural logs. It is also great seeing how you can use the power of logarithms to your advantage when deriving functions. This wraps up complex derivatives. It is important to understand how to take higher-order derivatives along with using two more advanced integration techniques. These will all be very useful when working with more complex functions in the future. I suggest take your time on your own to continue exploring the techniques you learned. In the next chapter, I will introduce you to some common derivative applications.
