Day 09
Derivate Applications
Related rates (6 min 13 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you'll explore multiple applications for derivatives. This includes approximations, theorems, optimization problems, and more. Let's start off by exploring related rates. Suppose you have two related variables that both change with time. For example, how volume is related to the radius of a sphere as it expands. A related rates problem is where you know how one of the rates change at a given instant for a variable, and then you use this to find the rate the other variable is changing at that same instant. changing at that same instant. Let's read the steps to solve a related rates problem. Step one, you'll want to define your two variables. Step two, determine an equation Step three, using the chain rule you will take the derivative of both sides of the equation with respect to the independent variable. Step four, substitute all known values at that instant into the equation. And step five, solve for the unknown rate of change. Let's use this process with a few related rates problems. Let's begin by looking at a problem where you inflate a spherical sports ball at a rate of five inches cubed per second. Let's determine how fast the radius is increasing when the radius is at six inches. Let's start off by defining your variables. So in this case, you'll have your variables of radius, r, and volume, capital V. In this case, you know, dv/dt, which is the rate that the volume is inflating at. And you need to find dr/dt, as in how fast is that radius increasing. You can relate these two variables by using the sphere volume equation, V equal to four thirds multiplied by pi, multiplied by r cubed. Now for step three, you will use the chain rule and take the derivative on both sides of the equation, which will be with respect to t, for time. So this gives you dv/dt on the left side since it was just the variable V. And then on the right side, you'll have four thirds multiplied by pi, multiplied by three, multiplied by r squared, multiplied by dr/dt. So if you simplify this on the right, you get dv/dt equals four multiplied by pi, multiplied by r squared, multiplied by dr/dt. In this case, you know, dv/dt equals five inches cubed per second and r equals six. So let's plug these into your equation for dv/dt and r to get five equal to four multiplied by pi, multiplied by six squared, multiplied by dr/dt. This simplifies to five equal to 144 multiplied by pi, multiplied by dr/dt. This means dr/dt equals five divided by 144 multiplied by pi, which is approximately equal to 0.011. So what this represents is the rate of change for your radius at that particular time. Let's look at another related rates problem. This time you have an airplane flying over an airport at a constant elevation of 10,000 meters. There is a delivery hub and an air traffic control tower spaced 1,000 meters apart on the ground. The airplane is currently flying horizontally away from the air traffic control tower. If the plane is flying at a rate of 250 meters per second, then what is the rate the distance between the air traffic control tower and the airplane increasing when the plane passes over the delivery hub? So this is another great instance of using related rates to solve. First, let's define the two variables for your problem, which in this case you have the distance between the air traffic control tower and the delivery hub, and you can have this be x, and then you can have the distance between the air traffic control tower and the airplane, which in this case you could have it be y. So in this case, you know dx/dt, but you need to find dy/dt. Next, let's relate these two variables. And in this case, you can use Pythagorean's theorem to relate your variables x and y since this problem makes a triangle. So this gets you an equation such as y f of t squared equals x of t squared plus 10,000 squared. Now for step three, you will use the chain rule to take the derivative on both sides of the equation, which will be with respect to t for time. So you have your equation y of t squared equals x of t squared plus 10,000 squared, and this derives to dy/dt multiplied by y is equal to dx/dt multiplied by x. You technically have a two on both sides, but you can cancel those out. Now you could substitute in your known values, which would be dx/dt equal to 250 and x equal to 1,000. So let's plug these into your equation where you have y of t squared equals 1,000 squared plus 10,000 squared, which means y is approximately 10,050. Now that you have y, you can then plug this into your derivative where you have dy/dt multiplied by 10,050, which equals 250 multiplied by 1,000. Solve this for dy/dt to get dy/dt equal to 250, multiplied by 1,000, divided by 10,050. And this is approximately equal to 24.88. This means you have solved for your unknown rate of change for your distance increasing between the air traffic control tower and the airplane, which now you know is approximately 24.88. Related rates help you work with related quantities as they change over time. I highly recommend taking time on your own to continue practicing more problems with related rates. Up next, you'll explore linear approximations.

Linear approximations (6 min 37 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this video, you'll explore linear approximations. You'll see how these approximations are closely related with derivatives. Let's start with the definition. Linear approximations are when you use a tangent line to make an approximation of a value, a. For example, if you wish to approximate the value of the square root of 9.02, you can use this method to do so. This method is helpful when you are estimating values a that are close to a defined x. In order to do a linear approximation, you need to know the equation of a tangent line. You can find a tangent line where x is equal to a value a for a function of x. The tangent line is represented by capital L of x equals f of a plus f prime of a multiplied by x minus a. As you could tell, you'll need a derivative in order to complete this equation. Let's go through a few examples. Let's say you have the function f of x equals the square root of x, and let's say you'll choose the value a equals 16 to estimate the value of the square root of 16.2. Let's plug this into your equation, Capital L of x equals f of a plus f prime of a multiplied by x minus a, which gets you capital L of x equals F of 16 plus f prime of 16 multiplied by x minus 16. Once you solve this and plug in your different values, you'll need to get f of 16, which simply equals your function square root of x, which is square root of 16, which just equals four. Next, you'll need to get the derivative at 16. So first, to get f prime of x, you'll use the power rule to get one half multiplied by x to the negative one half. Plugging in your value of 16, you'll get f prime of 16 equals one half multiplied by 16 to the negative one half. Once you simplify this, you'll get f prime of 16 equals one divided by eight. So this gets you the linear approximation equation of capital L of x equals four plus one eighth multiplied by x minus 16, and so this will be representing your tangent line at a equals four. Now that you have your equation, let's estimate where x is equal to 16.2. Let's plug this into your equation to get capital L of 16.2 equals four plus one eighth multiplied by 16.2 minus 16. This means capital L of 16.2 is equal to 4.025. If you plug in the square root of 16.2 into a calculator, you would get approximately 4.0249, which is rounded and is super close to your linear approximation you just discovered. A fun fact is your calculator actually uses approximations, but it is just at a much higher degree to get a more accurate result with more decimal places. Something else to keep in mind is this type of approximation only works for values of a that are close to x. So let's try and estimate where x equals 25 with the equation you just found. So you'll have capital L of 25 equals four plus one eighth multiplied by 25 minus 16. Simplifying this, you get capital L of 25 equals 6.375. As you know, the square root of 25 is equal to five, so you could tell it is not a very good estimate. Because again, the value you picked for this is too far away. This is why you need to ensure you choose an x that is nearby your a in order to properly estimate it. Let's look at an example using a trigonometric function. Let's have f of x equal sine of x where you have a equal to pi divided by three, which is equal to 60 degrees. And let's say you want to estimate where sine of x has x at 58 degrees. 60 degrees should be close enough. So let's get your linear approximation equation going. So you'll have capital L of x equals f of a plus f prime of a multiplied by x minus a, which plugging in, your A equals pi divided by three. You'll get capital L of x equals f of pi divided by three plus f prime of pi divided by three, multiplied by x minus pi divided by three. So using your equation, you'll need to find f of pi divided by three, f prime of pi divided by three in order to solve this equation. So for f pi divided by three, this will equal sine of pi divided by three, which is equal to the square root of three divided by two. Now let's get the derivative of your function, which the derivative of sine is cosine. So f prime of x equals cosine of x. So if you plug in f prime of pi divided by three, you'll get that equal to cosine of pi divided by three, which means that f prime of pi divided by three is equal to one half. Plugging this gets your linear approximation equation of capital L of x equals square root three divided by two plus one half multiplied by x minus pi divided by three. Now that you have your equation, let's estimate where x is equal to 58 degrees. First, you will convert your degrees into radians, where you'll have x equal 58 multiplied by pi divided by 180. Plugging this into your equation, you get capital L of 58, multiplied by pi divided by 180 is equal to the square root of three divided by two plus one half multiplied by 58, multiplied by pi minus 180 minus pi divided by three. This means your linear approximation at 58 degrees where you have capital L of 58 multiplied by pi divided by 180 is equal to 0.848. Again, if you plug this into a calculator for 58 degrees, you'll get a result of 0.849, which is super close to your linear approximation. Linear approximations can be helpful to simplify more complex functions such as motion and force at a defined point, along with helping you estimate values. It is also a foundational concept utilized in more complex topics such as Taylor Series. Up next, let's explore differentials.

Differentials (4 min 12 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next up, let's explore differentials. Now, you may be wondering, aren't differentials the same things as derivatives? They actually are not the same thing, but they are closely related to derivatives and linear approximations. Differentials help you estimate the amount a function changes for a small change in input values. The differential represents the linear approximation of the change in the function based on the change in the input. This is different from a derivative that measures the rate of change of the function and gives you the tangent line slope. When it comes to notating differentials, you'll notice they are very similar to how you denote derivatives. If you remember, with derivatives, they sometimes take the form dy over dx as equal to f prime of x. If you separate out the dy and dx, these are what are called the differentials. So, dy is a differential and then dx is a differential. So, if you move this function around where you have y equal to f of x, then your differential dy is defined by the function dy equal to f prime of x dx. This means your function f of x has a differential dy that is a linear approximation of the change in your function based on the change of your input dx in x. Note that dy is a function of both x and dx. Plus, x needs to be any non-zero real number in order for this function to work. Let's start off by looking at how you get a differential in the first place. Let's say you have the function y equals sine of x. In order to get the differential, you need to use your function dy equal to f prime of x dx. As you calculated prior, the derivative of f prime of sine of x is equal to cosine of x. If you plug this into your formula, you then get dy equal to cosine of x dx. Notice that this function is similar to getting the derivative of sine of x where you have f prime of sine of x is equal to dy over dx, which is equal to cosine of x. But this time, you're defining the differential dy instead of the full derivative dy over dx. Let's expand upon this example. Let's say you have x equal to five and dx equal to 0.2 to represent your change. You can then plug these values into your equation dy equal to cosine of x dx to get dy equal to cosine of 5 multiplied by 0.2. This simplifies to approximately 0.1992 for your differential dy. Let's look at another example. Let's say your function is y equal to x cubed plus 4 multiplied by x. This time, you want to compute the change from x equals 6 to x equals 6.1. In this case, that means your dx or your difference is going to be equal to 0.1, since if you subtract 6.1 by 6, you get 0.1. The derivative of x cubed plus 4 multiplied by x is simply 3 multiplied by x squared, plus 4 using the power rule. And then, if you plug this into your equation, you get dy equal to 3 multiplied by x squared plus 4dx. And let's plug in your values of 6 and .1 to get dy equal to 3 multiplied by 6 squared plus 4, and multiply all of that by 0.1. This gets you a result for your differential dy equal to 32.8. The goal of this application is to show you a new perspective on understanding what goes on behind derivatives with their differentials. I suggest take your time on your own to continue exploring how linear approximations and differentials are related. Up next, let's explore some extreme values such as minimum and maximum values.

Extreme Values (9 min 12 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this video, you will explore extreme values, including minimum and maximum values. Before you dive into finding these values with derivatives, let's take a moment to learn some key facts about extreme values also known as extremum. Let's take a moment to look at a graph to visually see extreme values. If you look at the graph, you'll notice there are multiple peaks and valleys. These are what are called extreme values. so if you look around x equals negative two, you'll see there is a high point that is somewhere between the values of y equals three and four. That is going to be a maximum value because it is a peak. Then if you look at x equals approximately negative 0.5, you'll see there is a valley that is a little bit below y equals negative one. That is what is called a minimum value. So as you could tell with this graph, you have multiple peaks, which are different maximum values and multiple valleys, which are minimum values. You'll also note the function goes straight up around x equals two and straight down where x equals negative two, and so that is where you will start to get to your bigger minimum and maximum values. Now that you've visually seen some extreme values, let's formally define them. First, you have your local or relative extreme values. A function f of x has a local minimum value, where x equals c and f of x is greater than or equal to f of c for every x in an open interval. And then on the flip side, a function, f of x, has a local maximum value where x equals c and f of x is less than or equal to f of c for every x in an open interval. Note that this interval does not have to be the full interval for the entire function, that it can just be for part of the function f of x. Next up are absolute or also known as global extreme values. A function f of x has an absolute minimum value where x equals c and f of x is greater than or equal to f of c for every x in the full interval of the function f of x. On the flip side, a function, f of x, has an absolute maximum value where x equals c and f of x is less than or equal to f of c for every x in the full interval of the function f of x. Note that this must be for all values of the function f of x. Now that you've learned about local and absolute extreme values, let's look at a few key facts about these two. Note that all absolute extreme values are local extreme values, but this does not work the other way around because not all local extreme values are absolute extreme values. This is because the absolute extreme value is for the entire function, versus the local extreme values focus on a particular interval within the function. From this, it is clear that you can then have multiple local extreme values, but you can only have one absolute minimum and one absolute maximum value. Now that you've defined what extreme values are, let's take a look at the extreme value theorem. The extreme value theorem states that for a continuous function, f of x, on an interval that is closed and bounded from a to b, there is an absolute minimum and an absolute maximum for the function f of x. This theorem is fairly logical since it is stating you will always need to have an absolute lowest and an absolute highest value with your function. Next, let's define what a critical point is. Let the value c be a point on the domain of function f of x. The value c is a critical point for function f of x if f prime of c equals zero, or f prime of c is undefined. Now you could see where the derivatives start to become very useful. Critical points will be very important for when you start exploring the following theorem. Expanding upon critical points and the relation to extreme values, you get Fermat's theorem. Fermat's theorem states if your function f of x has a local extreme value at a value c and f of x is differentiable at c, then f prime of c equals zero. So what this is saying is if you have extreme values with a derivative of zero, then the tangent line is at the top or bottom of a curve. Since that tangent line will then be a horizontal line, it will then have a zero slope causing the derivative to be zero. A few quick notes regarding Fermat's theorem. One is that this theorem only works where the derivative is defined, so it does not include extreme values where the derivative is undefined. Also, note that this theorem does not work the other way around where a derivative of zero implies a local extreme value. This is because you can have a derivative of zero that does not have any sort of extreme value associated with it. Let's look at an example of that. Let's say you have the function f of x equals x cubed, which is represented by the graph of this blue line where it's going up from the bottom left and then kind of levels out around x equals zero and then it goes back up fairly quickly. At this case, at the point x equals zero, you would have a derivative of zero because it would have that horizontal line creating a zero slope. But as you could tell from this graph, there is no extreme value here since this happens right in the middle of where this function is essentially flattening out and then continuing to go up in the same direction. Let's find some local extreme values using derivatives. Let's say you have the function f of x equals 1/3 multiplied by x cubed minus seven halves multiplied by x squared plus six multiplied by x. If you piece this out, you can get a derivative of f prime of x equals x squared minus seven multiplied by x plus six. If you factor this, you'll get f prime of x equals x minus six and x minus one. This means that your derivative f prime of x will equal zero at the points x equals six and x equals one. This will then make both of these extreme values. Let's validate your findings in this graph. So this is the graph of your function f of x equals 1/3 multiplied by x cubed minus seven halves multiplied by x squared plus six multiplied by x represented by the blue line. So you notice the blue line is going up pretty quickly on the slightly negative x side, and then it has that little peak at that x equals one, so that is one of your values, and then it quickly goes back down to where x equals six where it has a valley, and then it proceeds to go up very rapidly. Let's look at another example of this. Let's say you have the function f of x equals four multiplied by x divided by one plus x squared. You can get the derivative of this function using the quotient rule. So you'll have f prime of x equals one plus x squared multiplied by four subtracted by four multiplied by x multiplied by two multiplied by x. This will all be divided by one plus x squared, and then you'll square that. If you simplify the top portion of your fraction, you'll get f prime of x equals four minus four multiplied by x squared divided by one plus x squared, and again, that is also squared. As you may notice, the bottom portion of this equation will always be positive with x squared, and the one to be added to it will ensure it will never equal zero. This leaves the top portion to dissect for when the derivative equals zero. So you could get rid of the denominator to focus on just the numerator where you have four minus four multiplied by x squared equals zero. So here you get four equals four multiplied by x squared. Divide both sides by four to get one equals x squared, so you have x equals plus or minus one. So this means your derivative, f prime of x, equals zero at the values of x equals one and x equals two. You can validate your findings again on a graph, so this is your graph of f of x equals four multiplied by x divided by one plus x squared where it's represented by the blue line. So you'll see it's coming in from the left, and at that point x equals negative one, you'll see it has a little valley, and then it goes up fairly quickly to where x equals one, where it has a bit of a peak, and then it starts to slowly decrease from there. So you could tell you did find the correct local minimum and local maximum values. There's a lot to explore when it comes extreme values, but this should give you a well-rounded introduction. It is great having a way to mathematically find these values instead of just visually trying to point them out on a graph. You will continue exploring more theorems by learning about the mean value theorem in the next video.

Mean value theorem (6 min 50 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's move on to another theorem, the mean value theorem. This theorem is a very important one for much of calculus and it is often used with many first and second derivative tests. Before I teach you about the mean value theorem though, let's first learn what Rolle's theorem is. Rolle's theorem begins with a function f of x needing to meet the following three conditions. First, f of x must be continuous on a closed, bounded interval a to b. Second, f of x must be differentiable on an open interval a to b, and finally, the function of a must equal the function of b. If all these conditions are true, then there exists a number C such that a less than c less than b and f prime of c is equal to zero, as in the derivative of c is equal to zero. In other words, a critical point exists for f of x within the interval of a to b. You may be wondering why you need to know about this theorem in the first place. What is proven in this theorem is a key part of what makes the mean value theorem work. When you review the definition of the mean value theorem, you'll notice a lot of similarities to Rolle's theorem. Speaking of similarities, you'll see how the intermediate value theorem is also used within Rolle's theorem and the mean value theorem. Now let's take a look at the definition of the mean value theorem. This theorem also begins with a function f of x needing to meet two conditions this time instead of three. So the first condition is f of x must be continuous on a closed, bounded interval from a to b. The second condition is f of x must be differentiable on an open interval from a to b. If both of these conditions are true, then they exists a number c such that a less than c less than b and f prime of c is equal to f of b minus f of a divided by b minus a. So this is a way that you can find that derivative of c. As you may have noticed, Rolle's theorem is essentially a special case of the mean value theorem with that extra third condition you added. In simpler terms, what the mean value theorem is stating is that somewhere on your interval, the slope equals the average slope of the function. You determine this by using the first derivative to get that average rate of change or slope. This makes sense if you think about it in a simple example of someone running. If their average speed is five miles per hour when they're running, then at some point, they will run exactly five miles per hour. Note that this theorem does not specify exactly what the value c is, simply that it exists in the first place. Also note that there may be more than one instance within the interval where the conditions are met. For example, back to the person running, they may have run five miles per hour in three, five, or 10 instances. Again, this theorem will not specify how many instances, just simply that at least one exists. Let's look at this in an example. Let's have the function f of x equal to 1/4 multiplied by x cubed plus one with the interval of x being between zero and two. Since this is a polynomial function, you know it is continuous for all real numbers and hence, on the interval of zero to two. The same is also true for polynomials being differentiable for all real numbers. So again, that is also true for the open interval from zero to two. This means there exists at least one value c in that interval of zero to two such that f prime of c equals f of two minus f of zero divided by two minus zero. Let's calculate this out. So getting your derivative, you have f prime of x equal to 3/4 multiplied by x squared by bringing that power down. Next, let's evaluate your original function at the points of zero and two. So f of zero is equal to 1/4, multiplied by zero cubed plus one, which equals one, and then f of two equals 1/4, multiplied by two cubed plus one, which equals three. Now you have your formula f prime of c equals f of two minus f of zero divided by two minus zero. Plug in your values to get f prime of c equals three minus one divided by two minus zero, which equals one. Now let's solve for c. So you can use that derivative f prime of x equals 3/4 multiplied by x squared by plugging in one for f prime of x and changing x to c. So here, you have 3/4 multiplied by c squared equals one. This simplifies to c squared equal to 4/3, and so this means c equals the square root of 4/3, which is approximately equal to 1.155. What this represents is where the slope equals the secant line, also known as the first derivative. There are two corollaries that are a consequence of the mean value theorem. A corollary is a theorem that is a simple result of a previous theorem. This term is used when a theorem can be easily derived from another theorem or is simply self-evident from its proof. You can think of it as a baby theorem. In this case, the following two corollaries can easily be derived from the mean value theorem. The first corollary deals with functions with a derivative of zero. This corollary states if f prime of x is equal to zero for all x in the interval of a to b, then f of x is constant on the interval of a to b. This makes sense since it means all the function values have the same slope equal to each other, and since it is a zero derivative, this means it all has a flat slope, meaning that it should be a constant value. The second corollary deals with constant differences, sometimes called the constant difference theorem. This theorem states if f prime of x and g prime of x for all of x in the interval of a to b, then f of x equals g of x plus C on the interval a to b with some sort of constant value c. This makes sense since the functions have the same slopes over the interval for them to be equal on that interval. Note that c could technically be zero or any other value to move the line of the graph either above or below each other. I suggest take your time on your own to further explore the mean value theorem along with its two corollaries. This theorem and its corollaries are used in various practical applications throughout calculus and help you perform many other operations in mathematics. Up next, let's take a look at the shape of a graph and how you can apply derivatives to it.

Shape of a graph (11 min 38 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this video, you'll explore how to use derivatives to gather information about the shape of a graph. Let's start by defining what increasing and decreasing means in relation to a function. Let's assume you have the values x1 and x2 on an interval I, where x1 is less than x2. So, if f of x1 is less than f of x2, then your function f of x is increasing on the interval I. If f of x1 is greater than f of x2, then your function f of x is decreasing on your interval I. This is fairly logical, where if both values are going up simultaneously, then it would be increasing, versus if one value is going up while the other's going down, that would make it decreasing. Let's use derivatives to expand upon the concept of increasing and decreasing of a function. So if you have f prime of x greater than 0 for every x on the interval I, then your function f of x is increasing on that interval I. Next, if f prime of x equals 0, for every x on the interval I, then your function f of x is constant on the interval I. And finally, if f prime of x is less than 0 for every x on the interval I, then f of x is decreasing on your interval I. Let's use this with an example. Let's say you have the function f of x equals x cubed minus 3, multiplied by x squared plus 1. If you get the first derivative of this, you'll get f prime of x equals 3, multiplied by x squared, minus 6 multiplied by x. Now, let's set f prime of x equals to 0 to solve for critical points. So if you have 0 equals 3, multiplied by x squared, minus 6 multiplied by x, you can then factor this to be 0 equals 3, multiplied by x, multiplied by x minus 2. This gets you two critical points of x equals 0 and x equals 2. Let's take a look at the graph of this function. So you have your function of f of x equals x cubed minus 3, multiplied by x squared plus 1, and you'll notice to the left of your y-axis where you have negative x values, it is in fact increasing where it is going up towards that y equals 1 point at x equals 0. Now, between the values of x equals 0 and 2, you'll notice that it does curve down for a little bit, and then once x is greater than 2, it does go back up in a fairly steep increase. Let's take a look at another application of first derivatives with the shapes of graphs. In this case, you'll be looking at the first derivative test. For this test, suppose you have the function f of x that has a critical point at x equals c. There are three cases, then, that you can come across. First, if f prime of x is greater than 0 to the left of x equals c and f prime of x is less than 0 to the right of x equals c, then x equals c is a relative maximum. Finally, if f prime of x is the same on both sides of x equals c, then x equals c is not a relative minimum nor a relative maximum. So note that when I mention same here, this means that it is either less than 0 or greater than 0. Let's look at this with an example. Let's say you had the function f of x equals x cubed minus 3, multiplied by x squared minus 9, multiplied by x minus 1. Let's get the first derivative, where you have f prime of x equals 3, multiplied by x squared, minus 6 multiplied by x minus 9. Now, let's set f prime of x equals to 0 to find your critical points, so you have 0 equals 3, multiplied by x squared, minus 6 multiplied by x minus 9, which factors to be 0 equals 3, multiplied by x minus 3, multiplied by x plus 1. This will get you values of x equals negative 1 and x equals 3. So you have f prime of x equals 3, multiplied by x squared, minus 6 multiplied by x minus 9. So in this case, where x is less than negative 1, f prime of x is greater than 0, meaning it is increasing. Between the values of negative 1 and 3 for x, then f prime of x is less than 0, meaning it is decreasing. And finally, for x greater than 3, f prime of x is greater than 0, meaning it is increasing. So now, if you look at the graph for this, you'll notice that for x less than negative 1, there is a very steep incline. And then between x equals negative 1 and 3, there is a very steep decline in the graph. And then again, for x greater than 0, there is a very steep inline. So this graph is much more compact with much more steeper increases and decreases than the previous example. Now, let's take a look at some second derivatives. Suppose you have a function f of x and you want to look at some concavity. So using the concavity test, you can then check to see if f double prime of x is greater than 0 for all x in an interval on I, then you can conclude f of x is concave up on the interval I. On the other side, if f double prime of x is less than 0 for all x in an interval I, then f of x is concave down on that interval I. Let's see this in an example. Let's look at the function f of x equals x to the fourth, minus 4 multiplied by x cubed. With this function, let's determine where it is concave up and where it is concave down. The first derivative of the function is f prime of x equals 4, multiplied by x cubed, minus 12 multiplied by x squared. The second derivative of the function is f double prime of x equals 12, multiplied by x squared, minus 24 multiplied by x. If you set f double prime of x equals to 0 and factor, you'll then have 0 equals 12 multiplied by x squared, minus 24 multiplied by x, which then factors to 0 equals 12, multiplied by x, multiplied by x minus 2. This gets you two critical points of x equals 0 and x equals 2. Now, if you plug in values for your second derivative, you'll have f double prime of x equals 12, multiplied by x squared, minus 24 multiplied by x. And for values of x less than 0, your second derivative f double prime of x will be greater than 0. This means it will be concave up. If you test this second derivative on the interval of 0 less than x and less than 2, then you'll have f double prime of x is less than 0, meaning it is concave down. Finally, if you look at values where x is greater than 2, then your second derivative, f double prime of x, will be greater than 0, meaning it is concave up. If you look at the second derivative graphed, you'll notice that you have a parabola. So it's that U shape, and this matches your concave-up, concave-down findings. So, you'll see for those values of x less than 0, it is a positive value on the y-axis; between the values of 0 and 2, it is a negative value on the y-axis; and for values greater than 2, it is, again, positive on that y-axis. So what if your function does not have the same concavity throughout it? If you have a point x equals c where the concavity changes on a graph of a continuous function, then it is called an inflection point. The way you often determine this is by using your second derivative test to see if it is positive or negative. So let's look at that same example where you had f double prime of x equals 12, multiplied by x squared, minus 24 multiplied by x. Let's go back to those 2 points where you had x equals 0 and x equals 3 for your critical points. So, at x equals 0, you were then changing from the concave up shape to the concave down shape. At x equals 3, you were changing from the concave down shape to the concave up shape. So this means that x equals 0 and x equals 3 are both inflection points. Lastly, let's take a look at the official second derivative test. Suppose you have a function f of x that has a critical point at x equals c where the following three cases can occur. First, if f double prime of c is greater than 0, then x equals c is a relative minimum value. Next, if f double prime of c is less than 0, then x equals c is a relative maximum value. And finally, if f double prime of c equals 0, then x equals c can be relative minimum, relative maximum, or neither. Note that the third option will require further analysis to determine which one it is. Let's go through one more example. Let's look at the function f of x equals 3, multiplied by x to the fifth, minus 5 multiplied by x cubed. Getting your first derivative, you have f prime of x equals 15, multiplied by x to the fourth, minus 15 multiplied by x squared. Next, you get your second derivative of f double prime of x equals 60, multiplied by x to the third, minus 30 multiplied by x. Use your first derivative equal to 0 in order to find your critical points, so you'll have f prime of x equals 0, which gets you 0, equals 15 multiplied by X to the fourth, minus 15 multiplied by x squared. And then 0 equals 15, multiplied by x squared, multiplied by x squared minus 1 once you factor it. This actually gets you three critical points of x equals negative 1, x equals 0, and x equals 1. So, let's look at this with your second derivative. So if you have your second derivative, f double prime of x equals 60, multiplied by x cubed, minus 30 multiplied by x. And let's first evaluate it at x equals negative 1. So you get f double prime of negative 1 equals 60, multiplied by negative 1 to the cube, minus 30 multiplied by negative 1, which equals negative 30. Since this is less than 0, it is a relative maximum. Next, you have x equals 0. So plugging that in, you get f double prime of 0 equals 60, multiplied by 0 cubed, minus 30 multiplied by 0, which equals 0. In this case, you would need further analysis since you aren't quite sure if it's a relative maximum, relative minimum, or neither. And finally, for x equals 1, you'll have f double prime of 1 equals 60, multiplied by 1 cubed, minus 30 multiplied by 1, which equals 30, which gets you the relative minimum. So if you take a look at the graph of this, you'll notice that that x equals negative 1 point. You do have that relative maximum with that little peak. At x equals 0, it's actually just a flat line at that point, and so it is neither a relative maximum nor a relative minimum. And finally, at x equals 1, you have a little valley, meaning that you do have a relative minimum at that point. There are various other examples you can explore to better understand the shapes of graphs. But, this should give you a general idea of how to use first and second derivatives to help you describe the shape of your graph without visually seeing it. In the next video, you will explore optimization problems.

Optimization problems (9 min 48 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's move on to another derivative application, optimization problems. These are a great way to expand upon the concept of extreme values. Let's get started. Optimization problems seek to optimize a function by either minimizing or maximizing a particular quantity. Frequently, these problems have certain constraints dictating how small or large the value can be. Derivatives are key for solving these problems since you can use them to identify critical points and hence find possible minimum or maximum values to solve the optimization problem. Let's review some general steps to solve optimization problems. I know there's a lot of steps, so bear with me. Step one, you will understand the problem by gathering any variables and constraints or ranges, along with drawing any sketches if needed. In step two, you'll identify the quantity that needs to be minimized or maximized and if its domain is restricted. Step three, you'll write an objective function with one or more variables for the quantity being minimized or maximized. Step four, you'll write out a constraint equation if it is needed. Step five, you'll substitute the constraint equation into the objective function to rewrite it in terms of a single variable. Step six, you will find the first derivative of the objective function and set it equal to 0 to find critical points. Step seven, you will take the second derivative of the function to determine if the points are a minimum, maximum, or neither. And finally, step eight, you'll evaluate any remaining variables and ensure the solution satisfies any of the given restraints to complete your optimization problem. Let's look at a few optimization problem examples. Let's say you have the parabola y = 12 - x squared, and it's represented by the following graph where you have your blue line, and it looks like a big upside down U that intersects x = 0 and y = 12. Let's say you want to draw a rectangle under this parabola with its base on the x-axis. So, you will want to determine the rectangle you can make that has the largest area. Since the rectangle is symmetric over the y-axis, you can represent the width by 2 multiplied by x and the height by y. So note that x is going to be half the width of the rectangle. In this case, your constraint is going to be your parabola. In this case, you're wanting to maximize the area of the rectangle. Hence, this is the quantity you want to focus on. In this case, your domain spans from -x to x, where you have x greater than or equal to 0 due to symmetry. The parabola intersects the x-axis at x = plus or minus square root of 12, so you can then create the inequality of 0 less than or equal to x less than or equal to 12. The formula for area is given by area equals width multiplied by height. And then if you substitute in your two variables where width equals 2 multiplied by x and height equals y, you'll then get A of x = 2 multiplied by x multiplied by y. And note that this is what you want to maximize. The formula for your constraint is given by the parabola y = 12 - 6 squared. So you have your area of capital A of x equals 2 multiplied by x multiplied by y. Substituting that value in for y, you'll get A of x = 2 multiplied by x multiplied by 12 - x squared. Simplifying this gets you capital A of x = 24 multiplied by x - 2 multiplied by x cubed. If you compute the first derivative of your area, you'll get A prime of x = 24 - 6 multiplied by x squared. Now, let's set A prime of x equal to 0 to find any critical points. So if you have A prime of x = 0, this will then equal 24 - 6 multiplied by x squared. If you move the 24 around, you'll then get 24 = 6 multiplied by x squared. Divide both sides by 6 to get 4 = x squared, take the square root to get x equals plus or minus 2. Now let's get the second derivative of A double prime of x, which will equal to -12 multiplied by x. If you evaluate this at the value x = 2, you'll get A double prime of 2 = -12 multiplied by 2, which is equal to -24. Since the second derivative is less than 0 since -24 is less than 0, then this means it is a maximum value at x = 2. So here you have x = 2 where your width will equal 2 multiplied by x, which in this case your width = 2 multiplied by 2, which is equal to 2. And then you have your height, which is equal to y, which is equal to 12 - x squared. You'll then get a height of 12 - 2 squared, which is equal to 8. This means the rectangle with the greatest area underneath the parabola has dimensions of a width equal to 4 and a height equal to 8. Great job. Let's look at another optimization problem. Let's say a company wants to build a box with a square base and a open top that holds up to 32 cubic feet of content. The goal of this is to minimize the surface area of the box to reduce the amount of money spent on the cardboard to make each box. So you can have the volume of each box equal to 32 feet cubed. And then you can have a variable x be the side length of the square base of a box, and h be the height of the box. In this case, you'll want to minimize the surface area of the box, hence this the quantity you'll focus on. In this case, your domain is x greater than 0 and h greater than 0 due to the dimensions needing to be some sort of a positive value so you have some sort of material to make your box width. The formula for your quantity being minimized, which is the surface area, will be the surface area equal to the base area plus the side areas. In this case, you'll have S of x is equal to x squared + 4 multiplied by x multiplied by h. Remember, it is an open top, meaning you only need to calculate one of the square areas. The formula for your constraint is simply the volume where you have volume equals base area multiplied by height, so you'll get the volume equals x squared multiplied by h, which equals 32. So if you rearrange this, you'll get h = 32 divided by x squared. So if you have your surface area formula of S of x = x squared + 4 multiplied by x multiplied by h, you'll then get S of x = x squared + 4 multiplied by x multiplied by 32 divided by x squared once you substitute that value of h in. Once you simplify this again, you get S of x = x squared + 128 divided by x. If you take the first derivative of this to find critical points, you'll have S prime of x = 2 multiplied by x - 128 divided by x squared. Let's equal S prime of x = 0 to get those critical points, which will get you S prime of x = 0, which equals 2 multiplied by x - 128 divided by x squared. If you rearrange this equation by doing some division and multiplication, you'll get 64 = x cubed, which if you take the cube root of that, you'll have x = 4. Since x = 4 is greater than 0, it does fit in your domain so you're good to start looking at the second derivative. So you'll have S double prime of x = 2 + 256 divided by x cubed. And again, let's look at where x = 4. So you'll get S double prime of 4 = 2 + 256 divided by 4 cubed, which is equal to 6. Since the second derivative is greater than 0, where 6 is greater than 0, this means it is a minimum value. This gets you a result of x = 4 to optimize your square box for your side length equal to 4 feet. So let's get the height where you have volume equal to x squared multiplied by h = 32. Rearranging this gets h = 32 divided by x squared. Plug in 4 to get h = 32 divided by 4 squared, which means h just equals 2 So now let's plug this in to your surface area formula of S of x = x squared + 4 multiplied by x multiplied by h, which gets you S of x = 4 squared + 4 multiplied by 4 multiplied by 2, which is equal to 48 feet squared for your box to minimize that surface area for that volume. This means your final box dimensions will be 4 feet by 4 feet by 2 feet. There are various practical ways you can use derivatives to work with optimization problems in the real world. In physics, you can work with problems to minimize energy and maximize efficiency. In economics, you can use them to maximize profit, minimize cost, and optimize resource allocation. And finally, in business, you can use them to maximize sales and minimize inventory costs. There's a lot to explore when it comes to optimization problems, such as the various ones I showed in the previous slide when it came to physics, economics, and business, but know that that is just the beginning. Next, let's take a look at L'Hopital's rule.

LHopitals rule (6 min 30 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's continue applying derivatives by exploring L'Hpital's rule. This rule is named after 17th century French mathematician Guillaume de L'Hpital, but note that this theorem was technically introduced by Swiss mathematician Johann Bernoulli first in 1694. This is why it is sometimes referred to as Bernoulli's rule. If you remember from when I discuss calculating limits using factoring, that sometimes you can get an indeterminate form. This can be the case with derivatives as well. Let's review the list of some indeterminate forms you may come across. You may come across zero divided by zero, zero multiplied by infinity, infinity divided by infinity, infinity minus infinity, infinity to the zero, zero to the zero, and finally, one to infinity. The reason why L'Hpital's rule is helpful is because you can use it to calculate limits of functions that yield indeterminate forms like the ones I listed previously. In order to calculate the limit though, you'll need to understand how to calculate derivatives. Let's learn exactly what L'Hpital's rule is. Let f of x and g of x be differentiable functions over an open interval containing the real constant value a. So if the limit as x approaches a of f of x equals the limit as x approaches a of g of x, and those limits equal either zero, positive, or negative infinity, and if the limit as x approaches a of the derivative of f of x divided by the derivative of g of x either exists or equals positive or negative infinity, then your limit as x approaches a of f of x over g of x is equal to the limit as x approaches a of f, prime of x divided by g prime of x. I know this rule has a lot going on, so I will break it down for you. First, you need to ensure the limits equals zero, positive or negative infinity for the functions f of x and g of x. Next, you need to make sure the derivatives of f of x divided by g of x exist, or equal positive or negative infinity. If either of those conditions are not there, then you cannot use this rule. If all of the above is true though, this rule allows you to take the limit of the derivatives divided, often converting the limit to something that can be much more easily evaluated in the process, versus evaluating the original limit. Let's go through some examples. First, let's evaluate the limit as x goes to zero of sine of x divided by X. Currently, this is in the indeterminate form of zero divided by zero. Since the limit for both of these functions individually is zero, and their limit of the derivatives would exist since the denominator would turn from x to one with the derivative, and the numerator would be cosine of x instead of sine of x, you would be good to go in order to use this rule. Let's bring this together so you have a prime of x equal to one for your denominator, and then for your numerator you'll have f prime of sine of x, which is equal to cosine of x. So if you bring this into your limit, so you have the limit of x to zero of your derivatives of cosine of x divided by one, this is the same as getting the limit as x goes to zero of cosine of x. In this case, you can use substitution to have it be cosine of zero, which is equal to one. Now using L'Hpital's rule, you can say that the limit as x goes to zero of sine of x divided by x is equal to the limit as x goes to zero of cosine of x, which equals one. This time, let's evaluate the limit as x goes to infinity of e to the x divided by four, multiplied by x squared. This is currently in the indeterminate form of infinity divided by infinity. Since the limit for both of these functions individually is infinity, and the derivatives of them would exist, or again, equal infinity, you should be good to go to use L'Hpital's rule. Let's break this down. So you have f prime of e to the x equals e to the x, since the derivative of e to the x is itself, then you have f prime of four multiplied by x squared equals eight multiplied by X using the power rule. So you have the limit as x goes to infinity of e to the x divided by four, multiplied by x squared is equal to the limit as x goes to infinity of e to the x divided by eight multiplied by x, but this is still unfortunately in that form of infinity divided by infinity. The great thing though is you can apply L'Hpital's rule again to get a more simplified form to make sure that you can properly solve this limit. So here you have f prime of e to the x is still equal to e to the x, and then f prime of eight multiplied by X is equal to eight. So now you're saying the limit as x approaches infinity of e to the x divided by eight multiplied by x is equal to the limit as x approaches infinity of e to the x divided by eight, which simply equals infinity. So you can bring that back to your original limit as x goes to infinity of e to the x divided by four, multiplied by x squared is equal to the limit as x approaches infinity of e to the x divided by eight, which equals infinity. In this case, the limit technically does not exist because it is to infinity, but it's good to know that infinity is the final result. This is a great case where you can apply L'Hpital's rule multiple times, and again, get some sort of an actual result. L'Hpital's rule is great for simplifying the process of finding limits that may be difficult to compute directly due to their indeterminate forms. This rule can help evaluate asymptotic behavior by determining growth rates of functions. It can also be used in proofs and evaluations for improper integrals and series when infinite limits exist. I suggest take your time on your own to continue exploring L'Hpital's rule. It can be incredibly useful in various cases like the ones mentioned throughout this video. Up next is Newton's method.

Newtons method (8 min 6 seconds)
- [Instructor] Last up, let's explore Newton's method. You'll see how you can use this method with derivatives to approximate solutions to functions. Let's get started. Newton's method is helpful to use when you have an equation you wish to solve for zero. This is helpful when there's no way to do so algebraically. You can use it to estimate equations like sine of X equals X or F of X equals zero equals X to the fifth, plus eight multiplied by X to the fourth, plus four, multiplied by X cubed, minus two multiplied by X minus seven. It's also great to estimates gray roots such as the square root of 38. Newton's method provides a way to approximate the solution for function F of X, where F of X equals zero. The initial estimate is referred to as X naught, and the following estimates are referred to as X one, X two, and so on. For as many iterations as you perform, Newton's method is an iterative process, meaning you can repeat it as many times as needed. Let's take a look at the formula for Newton's method. Newton's method states the approximation for function F of X equals zero is X of N plus one equals X of N, subtracted by F of XN divided by F prime of XN. Note that F prime of XN must not equal to zero in order for this formula to work. This equation is gathered from tangent lines, using that derivative. Remember, you can run this process as many times as you want, but usually, a few times usually produces a satisfactory result. When it comes to your initial approximation, a common method is to sketch a graph of the function to eyeball what the initial estimate should be. You can also find the solution for the function on an interval, and gather the midpoint of the interval to use as your initial approximation. Let's look at a few examples, using Newton's method. Let's look at the function, F of X equals X cube minus two multiplied by X minus five. And in this case, you want to find where F of X equals zero. If you look at the graph of the function represented by the blue line, it starts to go up, has a local maximum, then a local minimum, and then continues to rise. So in this case where F of X equals zero, it looks like it intersects somewhere around X equals two. So a good idea would be to have your X naught equal to two. Now let's use Newton's method of X of N plus one equals X of N minus F of XN, divided by F prime of XN where you have X naught equal to two. You'll need to find the first derivative of your equation for this. So you have F of X equals X cubed, minus two multiplied by X minus five. The first derivative of prime of X equals three multiplied by X squared minus two. Now let's plug in X naught into your equation to get X naught plus one equals X naught, minus F of X naught divided by F prime of X naught. This means you have X of one equals X naught, minus X naught cubed, minus two multiplied by X naught, minus five, divide all that by three, multiplied by X naught squared minus two. If you plug in your values, you'll get X of one equals two, multiplied by two cubed, minus two multiplied by two, minus five, divided by three, multiplied by two squared, minus two. This gets you a value of 2.1. Let's run this again to get X of two equals X of one minus X of one cubed minus two, multiplied by X of one, minus five, divided by three, multiplied by X one of squared, minus two, to then plug in your new value of X of one equals 2.1 to get you X of two, which will equal 2.1 subtracted by 2.1 cubed, minus two, multiplied by 2.1 minus five. Divide all that by three, multiplied by 2.1 squared, minus two. This gets you a value of approximately 2.0946. Note that you can continue to use this method and go to as many decimal places of accuracy as you wish, but let's just stop at two iterations for now. So you can say your approximation where F of X equals zero is at approximately X of two, which is equal to 2.0946. Let's look at an example where you will approximate a square root, so you'll have the function F of X equals X squared minus seven, where you'll have X equals square root of seven. This will then make the function equal zero. Let's have your initial approximation be X of zero equals three, and this makes sense because the square root of four is equal to two and the square root of nine is equal to three. Hence, it should lie between these two values. So you could, in this case, potentially pick two instead of three, but either one should be fine. Now, let's plug this into your formula. X of N plus one equals X of N minus F of XN, divided by F prime of XN where you have X naught equal to three. Let's get the derivative of your function, F of X equals X squared minus seven, where you have F prime of X equals two, multiplied by X. Plugging in your values, you get X of zero plus one, equals X of zero minus F of X zero, divided by F prime of X zero. This gets you X of one equals X of zero, minus X of zero squared minus seven, divided by two, multiplied by X of zero. Once you plug in your value of three, you'll get X of one equals three, minus three squared, minus seven divided by two multiplied by three. This gets you a value of approximately 2.667. If you run this again, you'll get X of two equals X of one minus x of one squared minus seven divided by two, multiplied by X of one. Plug in your value of X of one to get you X of two equals 2.667, minus 2.667 squared minus seven divided by two, multiplied by 2.667. This gets you a value of 2.6462. Note that like before, you can continue to use this method and run it as many times as you wish, for as many decimal places of accuracy that you want, but you can say your approximation where F of X equals zero is approximately X of two equal to 2.6462. Note that there are some cases though where Newton's method will not work. Let's review some of the reasons of why this method would be insufficient. First, if the derivative of F of X at F of XN equals zero at one or more of the points, then it will fail to properly converge. Another reason is if the function F of X has more than one root, then the different estimations may approximate different roots. Another reason is behavior such as oscillation, because it will cause the approximation to potentially never be able to successfully approach a root, due to that oscillating behavior. And finally, if there's a lack of a clear definition of the function, this can cause the approximations to get further and further away from the solution instead of closer to it. There are a variety of circumstances where Newton's method can be handy, such as solving trigonometric or algebra equations, along with finding square roots. I suggest taking time on your own to practice using this method, along with learning about other iterative processes. This wraps up your introduction to derivative applications. These applications help provide more context and reasoning as to why derivatives are so important and useful in the real world. I suggest take your time on your own to continue exploring the applications you learned, along with other applications. In the next chapter, I will introduce you to the third major topic of calculus, integrals.

	dr/dt = 5 / (144)
	dr/dt = 5 / (48)
	dr/dt = 5 / (24)
	dr/dt = 5 / (72)
