	randomly choosing a value
	using the second derivative of the function
	sketching a graph of the function
	using the derivative of the function

3.What is the primary purpose of L'Hopital's Rule?
	to simplify derivatives directly
	to calculate limits of functions that yield indeterminate forms
	to solve algebraic equations
	to find the antiderivatives of functions

4.Which of the following statements is a key step in solving optimization problems using derivatives?
	Determine the average rate of change of the function.
	Calculate the integral of the function.
	Find the intersection points with the x-axis.
	Identify the critical points by setting the first derivative equal to zero.

5.If f(x) = x^3 - 3*x^2 + 1, at which intervals is the function increasing and decreasing?
	The function is increasing at x = 0 and decreasing at x = 2.
	The function is increasing at x > 0 and decreasing at x <= 0.
	The function is increasing at 0 < x < 2, and decreasing at x < 0 and x > 2.
	The function is increasing at x < 0 and x > 2, and decreasing at 0 < x < 2.

6.What does Rolle's Theorem state if a function f(x) meets its conditions?
	The function f(x) has no points of inflection on the interval (a,b).
	There exists a number c such that a<c<b and f(c)=0.
	The function f(x) is strictly increasing on the interval (a,b).
	There exists a number c such that a<c<b and f(c)=(f(b)-f(a))/(b-a).

7.What does Fermat's Theorem state about local extreme values of a differentiable function?
	A function has a local minimum if f(x) > 0 for all x in its domain.
	All local extreme values must also be global extreme values.
	If a function f has a local extreme value at x = c and f is differentiable at c, then f'(c) = 0.
	The derivative of a function is always zero at its critical points.

8.Which of the following best describes what differentials help you estimate?
	The units used to measure the function's output.
	The slope of the tangent line at a particular point.
	The type of function used to plot a graph.
	The amount a function changes for a small change in input values.

9.When estimating values using linear approximations, what is the most important reason to choose an x that is close to a?
	It reduces possible errors in computation due to fewer significant figures needed.
	It ensures that the derivative value is smaller, making the tangent line slope less steep.
	It allows for easier calculations since values close to zero are simpler to work with.
	This ensures that the approximation is more accurate since the tangent line better represents the function near a.

Day 10
Integrals Introduction
Anti-derivatives (2 min 49 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you will learn the third key concept of calculus, which is integrals. You'll learn what integrals are, how to define them, the differences between definite and indefinite integrals, and finally, the fundamental theorem of calculus. Let's start off with learning what anti-derivatives are. If you remember from the previous chapters, a derivative represents the rate of change of a function with respect to a variable or the slope of a tangent line. Sometimes derivatives will take your function down by a dimension. For example, if you have f of x equals x squared, the derivative of that is f prime of x equal to 2 multiplied by x. So again, it's going down from that two dimension to the one dimension. So what is an anti-derivative then? Essentially an anti-derivative is the opposite of taking a derivative, hence the prefix anti in the word. The formal definition states, for a function f of x, the anti-derivative is capital F of x, such that the capital F prime of x is equal to f of x. You can think of it as going up a dimension instead of down a dimension like you would with a derivative. Let's look at an example of finding anti-derivatives. Let's say you have the function f of x equals 3 multiplied by x squared, and let's say you want to find the anti-derivative for it. If you remember from earlier examples, the derivative of g of x equal to x cubed is equal to g prime of x, equals to 3 multiplied by x squared. This means an anti-derivative for your function f of x equals 3 multiplied by x squared is equal to g of x, which is x cubed. Let's look at an example though, where you have the function h of x equal to x cubed plus 28. If you get the derivative of this function, it is also 3 multiplied by x squared. This means h of x equal to x cubed plus 28 is also an anti-derivative for the function f of x equals 3 multiplied by x squared. Why is this the case? Essentially, for a given function f of x, there are an infinite amount of anti-derivatives differing by a constant value. Note that you do not have this occur with derivatives since you are going down in dimension, and hence, you are typically taking out information versus adding information. Since you are going up in dimension instead of down, there may be information such as constants that you may not know. This is what causes the infinite possibilities. Understanding what an anti-derivative is is the first step to learning what integrals are and how to calculate them. As you saw, anti-derivatives, and hence integrals are extremely connected to derivatives. Up next, let's learn what an integral is.

What is an integral? (3 min 36 seconds)
- Great job. You have worked hard to learn about limits and derivatives. And now that you know what an antiderivative is, you will use that concept to learn what an integral is. You'll also see how integrals are closely related to antiderivatives in that they act like opposites. So let's get back to it. An integral represents the accumulation of quantities, such as the area under the curve. Let's define an integral in terms of antiderivatives. Then an integral of your function F of X would be defined as the integral of F of XDX equals capital F of X plus C. Let's break down the integral equation you saw above. So the first part, this elongated S-looking symbol S, is what is called the integral sign, and it stands for sum. Next, you have F of X, which is called the integrand. So again, that is the function you are integrating on. X is the integration variable. Next, you have DX, which is the differential of X. And then you have capital F of X, which again is your derivative of your function F of X. And then finally, you have C, which is your constant of integration. Let's review a little bit of terminology for integrals. The process of finding integrals is called integration or integrating. If you want to specify the variable you're integrating on, you can state that you're integrating F of X with respect to X. This is important when you start integrating when there are multiple variables involved in an equation. Bringing back the continuity concepts from earlier in this course, if your function F of X is continuous on a closed, bounded interval A to B, then F of X is also integrable on the interval A to B. Note that you can integrate some discontinuous functions, but they can sometimes be more difficult to integrate, require certain conditions to integrate, or they may need extra steps to solve. There are two types of integrals. First, indefinite integrals, and second, definite integrals. Indefinite integrals integrate over an entire function with no bounds. This is how the integral was formatted in the definition earlier. So it is an indefinite integral. A definite integral is when you integrate over part of a function bound between two points A and B on a closed bounded interval of A to B. You'll go into these two types of integrals in more detail in later videos. So why do you need to know about integrals in the first place? Integrals are a key concept to know because they provide you a precise way to measure areas, volumes, and other quantities in various fields and applications. They can also be used to find surface area, the average value of a function, arc length, and more. What are some common practical applications of integrals? In physics, integrals can be used to compute displacement, work, and energy. In engineering, they can be used to solve heat transfer, fluid flow, and material stress equations. And finally, in economics, they're used to model metrics like cost or revenue over time. This is just the beginning of integrals. You'll continue to learn more about integrals and see them in action throughout the rest of this course. Before you proceed with diving into integrals, let's take a moment to understand how to interpret integrals as the area under the curve.

Area under the curve (3 min 44 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you know what integrals are, let's take a closer look at a common interpretation of them, the area under the curve. This interpretation tends to be the most intuitive and straightforward to understand. Let's dive in. In order to look at this interpretation, you will have to use definite integrals since this interpretation does not work for indefinite integrals due to the potential for infinite area. Let's say you have a function f of x with an integral of the integral of a to b of f of xdx, over the interval that's closed and bounded a to b. The result of this integral represents the total area between the curve of f of x and the x-axis given by the bounds of a and b. Let's take a look at this graphically. Let's say you have the function f of x equals 2 multiplied by x plus 1, and you want to look at it with the bounds of x being between 1 and 3. In the graph, you'll see you have the line of the function, and then the shaded area is going to be between those values of x of 1 to 3. This shaded area is what you would find using the integral. Integrals can be useful to calculate areas that may not be easy to do with traditional algebra. This is especially true when equations get more complex. There are different types of areas that could be found using integrals. Positive area is area you gather of the function f of x that is above the x-axis. Negative area is area you gather of the function f of x that is below the x-axis. Net area combines positive and negative areas of your function f of x, and this can result in the net area being positive, negative, or even zero. The zero case happens if the positive and negative areas cancel each other out. Finally, the total area is the unsigned area of the function over the interval, and it is often represented by an absolute value sign. Generally, the area you will find when integrating is the net area. This is because integrals are tied to the accumulation of quantities, so this will give you the combination of the positive and negative areas. What this provides you with is the total change or net result over the interval you are integrating over. Just note that sometimes this will not always be helpful when trying to understand a function. Let's look at an example of the graph of the function f of x equals x cubed for the values of x between negative 2 and 2. In this case, you have the function denoted by the line, and then, again, those shaded areas are the areas between negative 2 and 2. This is a case where the net area is equal to zero since the positive and negative areas will cancel each other out. Having a result of zero is not very helpful in this case if you're trying to determine how much space is between the function f of x and the x-axis. This is a great example of needing to find the total area, instead. This should provide you a more thorough understanding of the concept of area under the curve when it comes to integrals. Remember, this interpretation only works in practice with definite integrals, but you can use the concept to mentally visualize indefinite integrals, as well. Up next, let's dive a bit deeper into area under the curve by learning about summation and how it relates and differs from integration.

Summation vs. integration (3 min 41 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned about how you can interpret integrals as the area under the curve. In order to get that area under the curve though, you will need to do some summation. Let's take a moment to understand summation, and how it is similar and different from integration. Summation is when you add up a discrete number of values, and it is usually denoted by the sigma symbol. For example, you may be adding up the values of 1 squared plus 2 squared, plus all the way to plus 7 squared, and so you can notate that with summation notation where you have sigma, so the sum of n equals 1, 2, 7, of x squared, which equals 1 squared plus 2 squared, plus all the way to plus 7 squared. When it comes to finding the area under a curve, one method you can use is a summation of rectangles underneath the curve. You can vary where you center the rectangle on the curve, such as to the left of the curve, the middle of the curve, or the right of the curve. Generally, the middle of the curve is the most desirable, and this is due to the midpoint rule. You can also vary how many rectangles you use to sum up the area such as three, five, 10 or more. It is important to note that adding up rectangles under the curve will not provide you with a 100% accurate result of the area under the curve. The error will go down with the more rectangles you have, since there will be a smaller margin between the rectangles and the curves. This is why summation is not necessarily the best method to use when finding the area under the curve. This usually causes summation to have some sort of an error correction term. This is where integration comes in handy. One way you can think of integration is that you are summing up an infinite amount of rectangles to find the area under the curve. This is what will provide you with the accuracy you desire that a summation of just discrete rectangles cannot. This means that there is no error correction term needed because it should be a 100% accurate result. Let's look at some key differences between summation and integration. For summation, you use the sigma symbol, versus with integration, you use the integral sign. With summation, you are summing up a discrete number of values, versus with integration, you are doing so with an infinite number of values. With summation, you have a lower accuracy, versus with integration, you have a 100% accuracy. With summation, you often need that error correction term due to the not 100% accuracy, versus with integration, you don't need an error correction term. And finally, with summation, you'll be finding the rectangular area, versus with integration, you'll be finding the exact area. Something to keep in mind though, with this relation is as the number of rectangles being summed increases to infinity, then the limit of the summation will approach the integral. This will also cause the values of the summation function to become the values within the integration function as the number of rectangles approaches infinity. The goal of this video is to provide you a different perspective on how to view integrals as an infinite summation, along with the benefits of using integrals over regular summation to find the area under the curve. In the next video, you will go into detail on what indefinite integrals are.

Indefinite integrals (3 min 35 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you have taken some time to understand some overarching concepts of integrals, let's dive into the two types of integrals. I'll start with reviewing indefinite integrals since they provide a base for definite integrals. Let's review exactly what an indefinite integral is. If you remember from earlier, indefinite integrals are very closely related to antiderivatives. An indefinite integral represents all antiderivatives of a function f of x. The way you calculate an indefinite integral is by finding the antiderivative. This is represented by the equation of the integral of f of xdx equals capital F of x plus C, where capital F of x is the antiderivative of your function f of x and C is the constant of integration. So, why does an indefinite integral represent all the antiderivatives of a function? If you remember from a previous video, I mentioned that there can be an infinite amount of antiderivatives for a function due to the ability to add any constant value to it. The way the indefinite integral represents all these possibilities is with the constant of integration C. So, what is this magical value C I keep mentioning? The value C represents a constant term that is added to your antiderivative, often called the constant of integration. This term is ambiguous and can represent any constant value you could possibly add to the core of your antiderivative. This is because you can never truly know what the constant value should be for your antiderivative due to the inability to gather that information with going up in dimension instead of going down, like you would with a derivative. So for example, let's say you have a function f of x equal to 2 multiplied by x. You can have an antiderivative capital F of x equal to x squared plus 7, or you could have an antiderivative of F of x equal to x squared plus 391. In this case, you can just use the constant value C to represent any of those numerical constant terms that can be added to it. So for example, it can represent the 7, the 391, or any other values. So this will get you capital F of x equal to x squared plus C. There are a few key properties for indefinite integrals. First, you will notice there are no bounds or limits specified. This means when you evaluate your integral, you will often get a function as the result versus a numerical answer. Second, indefinite integrals represent a general solution to a derivative where you reverse the process. Third, indefinite integrals include a constant value of integration C due to differentiation, eliminating constant values in the process since, again, you are going in the opposite direction. Finally, combining the two previous properties I just mentioned, when you solve an indefinite integral, your result will represent all the antiderivatives instead of just one numerical result. Indefinite integrals are useful when it comes to providing general solutions for practical problems in industries such as engineering and physics. They're also very prevalent when solving a branch of mathematics called differential equations. Now that you have a better understanding of indefinite integrals, you'll be better prepared with how to solve them past just finding their antiderivatives. In future videos, I will show you how to switch your mindset from deriving to integrating functions. Up next, let's explore definite integrals and see how they differ from indefinite integrals.

Definite integrals (7 min 59 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you have a better understanding of what indefinite integrals are, let's switch over and explore definite integrals. You'll see how indefinite integrals still provide a very necessary base when it comes to working with definite integrals. Let's start with the definition. Remember from earlier how definite integrals can be represented as the area under the curve. For a function f(x), this is represented by the equation of the integral sign of a to b of f(x)dx, which is equal to F(b) - F(a). So note that you have the anti-derivative of f(x) is represented by F(x), and this is evaluated on the close bounded interval a to b. The way you can calculate a definite integral is by finding the anti-derivative and then plugging in the boundary values accordingly to solve for it. Let's review a few key parts of the definition. First, one of the big differences from indefinite integrals when it comes to definite integrals is adding the bounds of a and b. The value A is what represents the lower limit of the integral, and the value of B is what represents the upper limit of the integral. You'll see they're often notated just the right of your integration sign with A on the bottom and B on the top part of it. Next, you'll notice that instead of just having the anti-derivative in the solution, you are actually evaluating the anti-derivative at points a and b. Since B is your upper limit, you'll calculate the value entered into your anti-derivative at that point and then subtract that by the lower limit A. So this gets you the F(b) minus F(a). Finally, you may be wondering, where did the constant of integration C go? Previously, you saw with indefinite integrals that this was always included in the integral result and was very important to include it for definite integrals. The constant value capital C actually cancels out in the definition. Let's say if you calculate the integral at just b, you would get F(b) + C. Then if you calculate the integral at just a, then it would be F(a) + C. And then if you combine these results and subtract them like you would with the previous definition, you would get F(b) + C and F(a) + C. And as you can see, your have C - C, which just equals F(v) - F(a). Let's take a look at a more technical definition of definite integrals. If you have a continuous function f(x), that is on a close bounded interval, a to b, you can divide the interval into N sub intervals with a width of delta X with each one having a point XI, then you can get the definite integral of F(x) from a to b. Where it is the integral of a to b of f(x)dx = the limit as N approaches infinity of the sum of Ii equals one to N of f(xi) multiplied by delta X. So note that the triangle is what represents an upper delta like you saw earlier with summation versus integration. Integration is when you're summing up an infinite number of rectangles to get the exact area under the curve. With this technical definition you just saw for definite integrals, it is stating that you're taking the limit to infinity, hence an infinite amount of sub intervals, and then gathering the sum of them. This will provide you with the exact area under the curve of the definite integral. There are a few different area types you can come across when it comes to definite integrals. When calculating a definite integral, you will be finding the net area. You can think of the positive area as being A1 and the negative area being A2. So the net area would be given by the formula of the integral of a to b of f(x)dx = A1 - A2. In contrast, if you got the total area, then the total area would be the integral of a to b of f(x)dx = A1 + A2. There are a few key properties for definite integrals. First definite integrals have specified upper and lower bounds a and b. Second, they represent the net area under the curve or the accumulation of a quantity. Third, your result will be a single numerical value if the integral exists. And finally, the numerical result is calculated by using the upper and lower bounds given. Another key property of definite integrals is what occurs when you reverse your bounds so you can actually interchange your bounds on any definite integral. If you do reverse the bounds, then you just need to include a negative sign to ensure that you are still getting the correct result. So let's say you have the integral of a to b of f(x)dx. If you reverse the bounds, you would then just need to add that negative sign to get negative integral of b to a of f(x)dx. So that way they still equal the same correct result, but sometimes it's easier to flip the bounds when evaluating them. Let's look at an example of a definite integral in action. Let's say you have the function f(x) = 2 multiplied by X with your known anti-derivative of F(x) = x2. And let's say you want to evaluate this from the values of X between one and three. If you look at the graph, you have the blue line, which represents your function, 2 X x, and then the shaded region between X values of one and three is where you are going to be integrating to get that total value. And in this case, this is going to be representing the net area under the curve, but in this case, it is also technically the total area under the curve because it is all positive. So let's set up your integral. So you'll have f(x) = 2 X x, F(x) = x2, and then X between one and three. So if you have the integral of a to b of f(x)dx, this will equal the integral of one to three of two X xdx. Once you integrate this using your anti-derivative, you get x2 and you'll evaluate that from one to three. So you'll note that when I want you to evaluate a definite integral, once you get the solution, it'll often be notated with a bar on the side of the result with the bottom bound being on the bottom and the top bound being on the top. So in this case, your value A is one and B is three, and so hence you see the bar and I'll notate it as one to three. So in this case, you'll be getting b2 - a2 for your result by plugging it into your integral result of x2. So this gets you three squared minus one squared equals eight as your final result. Definite integrals help provide specific solutions for various practical problems and industries such as engineering and physics. They're often used when computing areas, volumes and surface areas. You'll continue to see throughout the rest of this course various applications for integrals, along with a chapter dedicated to it near the end of the course that focuses on just integral applications. These applications will include a lot of definite integrals in them. Now you have a thorough understanding of what integrals are for both the indefinite and definite cases. Knowing these types of integrals will be very necessary when it comes to showing you a variety of integration rules and calculation techniques in the next chapter. To wrap up this chapter, let's explore an important theorem, the fundamental theorem of calculus.

Fundamental theorem of calculus (7 min 3 seconds)
- [Instructor] Everything you have learned so far has built up to what is called, the fundamental theorem of calculus. This theorem is often known as one of the most important and powerful theorems in calculus, so make sure to pay attention. Before I dive into this theorem, though, let's begin by bringing back the mean value theorem, but this time in relation to integrals. The mean value theorem for integrals states, if a function F of X is continuous over closed-bounded interval A to B, then there exists a number C, such that A less than C less than B, and F of C equals one divided by B minus A, multiplied by the integral of A to B of F of X DX. What this is stating is that there is a point C that equals the average of the function F of X. The reason it is good to know this theorem is because it is used when proving the fundamental theorem of calculus. Let's use the mean value theorem in a quick example. Let's look at the function F of X equals two plus three multiplied by X, where you are looking at the interval of X being between zero and five. Using your mean value theorem formula, you have F of C, equals one divided by B minus A, multiplied by the integral of A to B of F of X DX. Let's plug in your values. So you have F of C equals one divided by five minus zero, which is your bounds of zero to five, where zero is A and five is B, multiplied by the integral of zero to five of two plus three multiplied by X DX. Once you integrate this, you get one fifth multiplied by two, multiplied by X, plus three halves multiplied by X squared, evaluated from the values of zero to five. Let's plug in these values to get one fifth multiplied by two, multiplied by five, plus three halves multiplied by five squared, minus one fifth multiplied by two, multiplied by zero, plus three halves, multiplied by zero squared. Once you simplify this, you get F of C equals 9.5. Now you know your average value of your function between the values of X of zero to five is equal to 9.5. Now let's dive into the powerful theorem of the fundamental theorem of calculus. The fundamental theorem of calculus is what officially defines the relationship between differentiation and integration. This theorem allows you to officially calculate integrals using anti-derivatives, instead of having to only use Riemann sums and calculating areas with computationally expensive calculations. This theorem is split into two parts, so let's start by exploring part one. Part one of the fundamental the of calculus states, if a function F of X is continuous over a close bounded interval A to B, then the function capital F of X is defined by capital F of X equals the integral of A of the X of F of T DT. The function capital F of X is continuous over the closed interval A to B and differentiable over the open interval A to B. Note that the derivative capital F prime of X is equal to F of X over your interval A to B. Essentially, this part of the theorem is stating how you can connect integration with differentiation by using anti-derivatives, since they are an inverse process. Let's look at a quick example using part one of the fundamental theorem of calculus. So let's say you have the function capital F of X equals the integral of zero to X of two, multiplied by X to the fourth. Using the first part of the fundamental theorem of calculus, you know the anti-derivative is equal to the function within the integral; hence, your anti-derivative F prime of X equals two multiplied by X to the fourth. Now let's look at part two of this theorem. Part two of the fundamental theorem of calculus states, if a function F of X is continuous over an interval that is close and bounded from A to B, and capital F of X is any anti-derivative of F of X, then the integral of A to B of F of XDX is equal to capital F of B minus capital F of A. This part of the theorem expands upon the relationship between integrals and anti-derivatives by allowing you to evaluate definite integrals by evaluating the anti-derivative at the end points of the interval. As you could tell, this part of the theorem is focused on just definite integrals. Let's look at this with another example. Let's say you want to evaluate the integral of zero to two of three, multiplied by X squared DX. Using your knowledge of anti-derivatives, you know the anti-derivative of three multiplied by X squared is capital F of X equal to X cubed. Now, you can evaluate using the fundamental theorem of calculus part two, where you have the integral of zero to two of three multiplied by X squared DX is equal to capital F of two, minus capital F of zero. In this case, it'll be the X cube that you plug in at your boundary values to get two cubed minus zero cubed, which equals eight. Let's look at a few practical applications for the fundamental theorem of calculus. When it comes to calculus, this theorem is very necessary to easily calculate definite integrals, along with making it easy to find areas under curves. In physics, this theorem can be used to find displacement from velocity functions and total accumulation of quantities over time. And finally, in engineering, it could be used to calculate the total charge flowing through a circuit by integrating current over time. The fundamental the of calculus, truly, provides a more unified view of calculus by connecting integrals and derivatives. This theme allows you to properly continue with calculating integrals throughout the rest of this course. This wraps up your introduction to integrals and how to compute them, using anti-derivatives. Remember, integrals are a key concept calculus and help measure the area under the curve. They will be the key focus for the rest of the chapters in this course. In the next chapter, you'll explore some of the important integration rules.

	It states that the function F(x), defined by the integral from a to x of f(t) dt, is continuous on [a,b] and differentiable on (a,b), where F'(x) = f(x).
	It states that for a continuous function f(x) over [a,b], there exists a number c such that a<c<b and f(c) equals the average value of the function.
	It connects the derivative of the integral of a function to the average value of that function over the interval [a,b].
	It allows you to calculate the area under the curve of a continuous function in terms of the endpoints a and b.

3.What is a key difference between indefinite integrals and definite integrals?
	Definite integrals are always negative, while indefinite integrals are always positive.
	Indefinite integrals require the use of limits, whereas definite integrals do not.
	Definite integrals cannot be used to find the area under a curve, while indefinite integrals can be used to find the area under the curve.
	Definite integrals have specified bounds a and b, while indefinite integrals do not.

4.What does the constant of integration C represent in an indefinite integral?
	It represents the upper limit of the integral.
	It represents the average value of the function.
	It represents an arbitrary constant value added to the antiderivative.
	It represents the variable of integration.

	Summation requires limits or bounds for calculation, whereas integration does not use any limits or bounds.
	Summation always results in exact area under the curve, while integration provides an estimate using fewer values.
	Integration uses the sigma notation while summation uses the integral symbol.
	Summation adds discrete values with less than 100% accuracy for area under the curve, whereas integration sums an infinite number of values for exact results.

Day 11
Integration rules 
Integration rules (1 min 47 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you'll explore many of the common rules for integrals. Remember, an integral represents the accumulation of quantities, such as the area under the curve. Let's start off by reviewing the rules you are going to cover. The integration rules you will explore are the zero function, constant/constant multiple, sum/difference, power, and additivity. The sum and difference rules will be explored together since they are similar to each other. In the following chapter, you will explore integrals of common types of functions, such as trigonometric and logarithmic functions that tend to have their own nuances when calculating them. Each of the following videos will be structured similarly, to give you a general overview of each integration rule. Each video will provide you with the definition of the rule, the reasoning of why it is needed, and multiple examples of how to calculate integrals using the rule. It is important to know how to not only understand key rules for integration, but how to use them to calculate integrals in various scenarios, as well. Integrals are fundamental to calculus, so understanding how to calculate them using their rules when needed will help you better understand other key concepts later on, such as complex integrals. You can also use them to explore more advanced calculus and differential equations topics on your own. The goal of the following videos is to provide you with working knowledge on how to implement these various integration rules. You can take this knowledge and expand upon it in your work and education. Let's get started with exploring the zero function.

Zero function (1 min 37 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's start off with an easy integration rule. This is when you integrate a zero function or, simply put, integrate the value zero. Let's first review the derivative of the zero function. Let's say you have the function f of x equals zero. If you remember with derivatives, the derivative of this function would be zero, no matter how many times you took it. This comes from the derivative of a constant being zero, especially if it is already zero. This is not the same case with integrals. With integrals, you sometimes lack information about the function you are working with, since you are going in the opposite direction as you would with a derivative. One of the key pieces of information you may be missing is a constant value, such as 6 or 732, but there's no way for you to know exactly what the constant value should be. This is where the zero function rule comes in handy for integrals. If you have a function f of x equals zero, then the integral of it is the integral of 0dx and it simply equals C, which is any constant value. You can think of this in terms of the anti-derivative, where zero is the derivative of any constant value C. This rule is fairly straightforward, where, whenever you integrate just the value zero, then the result will always be C to represent an unknown constant value. This rule can be proven with the fundamental theorem of calculus, which you can explore on your own. Up next, let's explore constant rules.

Constant rules (5 min 29 seconds)
- [Instructor] Up next are Constant Rules for Integrals. There are two constant rules you will cover in this video, including the constant rule and the constant multiple rule. These will be formatted very similarly to their derivative counterparts. Let's start with a constant rule. The constant rule states for any constant C1 where you have your function f of x equal to C1, then the integral of C1 dx equals C1 multiplied by x plus C2. Note that C2 here is another constant that you add on to your result. This rule makes sense since the graph of any constant value has a slope of zero and an integral is gathering the area underneath that function. Since that accumulation is proportional to x, it creates a linear function as the result. Let's look at an example of this. Let's say you have a function f of x equal to seven. If you take the indefinite integral of it, you can use the constant rule to get the integral of 7dx equals seven multiplied by x plus C. Let's add some bounds to this to make it a definite integral where your bounds are one and three. So you'll simply plug in the values of one and three into seven multiplied by x plus C to get seven multiplied by three, minus seven multiplied by one. Note that the constant is left out here because it will just cancel out in the end. So this gets you a final result of 14. Next, let's take a look at the constant multiple rule. The constant multiple rule states, for function g of x equal to C, multiplied by a function f of x with any constant C, the integral is then the integral of g of x equals the integral of C, multiplied by f of x dx, and this is the same as C multiplied by the integral of f of x dx. Essentially, this rule works the same as it would for derivatives where you take out the constant value, perform the integral of your function, and then multiply your constant value with your integral result. You might be confused as to why the constant stays the same when the previous rule made the constant expand with an x. This is because when you are integrating just the constant function C, then you are focusing on integrating that function to find the area under the curve. This is different when you multiply a constant C because, in this case, the function being integrated on is f of x, where C just turns into a fixed multiplier that scales the result of the integral of f of x. Let's look at a few examples of the constant multiple rule. Let's say you have the function f of x equal to five multiplied by x. Now let's take the integral of this to get the integral of five multiplied by xdx. When taking the integral of this, you can simply move the five outside of your integral to multiply at a later point, so this turns it into five multiplied by the integral of xdx. Now you just need to take the integral of x, which you previously learned is equal to x squared divided by two. Now you bring back in your constant, so you have five multiplied by x squared, divided by two, and make sure you add in your constant C. If you simplify this a bit more, you get a final result of five multiplied by x squared divided by two, plus C. Let's add some bounds to make this a definite integral, where you'll have bounds between zero and two. You simply plug in these values with your function five multiplied by x squared divided by two plus C. So this gets you five multiplied by two squared divided by two, and subtract that with five multiplied by zero squared, divided by two. Again, remember, the C is left out because that concept of integration simply cancels out in the process, so it's not necessary to put it in there. This gets you a final result of 10. Let's look at one more example of this rule in action. Let's say you have a function f of x equal to x divided by four. You might be worried at first since you're dividing your function x by the constant four, but this is the same as multiplying x by the fraction 1/4. This means that you could still use the constant multiple rule, so if you take the integral of this separated out, you have the integral of 1/4 multiplied by xdx. Now, you can move that constant out, so you have 1/4 multiplied by the integral of xdx. Like you learned before, the integral of x is simply x squared divided by two, so you have 1/4 multiplied by x squared divided by two. Add C to that, and then you can simplify this to get a final result of x squared divided by eight plus C. This should give you a solid understanding of how to use the constant rule and constant multiple rule for integrals. These rules are simple yet effective when it comes to knowing how to quickly and easily solve integrals, especially when the functions within them become more complicated. It also helps that these rules function very similarly to the derivative rules you previously learned. In the next video, you'll explore the sum and difference rules.
Sum and difference rules (8 min 27 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next, let's explore the sum and difference rules for integrals. These rules are essentially one and the same, but they're sometimes referenced separately. It is important to know how both of these rules work since they can be extremely helpful when solving integrals. Note that these also function similarly to their derivative counterparts. Let's begin with the sum rule. The sum rule states the integral of a sum of functions is the same as the sum of their integrals. This is represented by the functions H of X equal to the function F of X, added, with the function G of X. The integral of the function H of X is equal to the integral of H of X DX, equals the integral of F of X DX, plus the integral of G of X DX. Next is the difference rule. The difference rule states the integral of a difference of functions is the same as the difference of their integrals. This is represented by the functions H of X is equal to the function of F of X minus the function of G of X, where you have the integrals be the integral of H of X DX, equals the integral of F of X DX, minus the integral of G of X DX. As you could tell, these rules are very similarly defined. Sometimes they're referenced together and represented by the following equations. So you have your function H of X equal to your function, F of X, plus or minus your function G of X, where the integral of H of X DX is equal to the integral of F of X DX plus or minus the integral of G of X DX. How do these rules work? When you take an integral, you're finding the area under the curve of a function. Since you have two separate functions when you add or subtract them from each other, you can handle the functions individually to find their areas under the curve. Once you find this for each function, you can combine the results in order to get your final result. Let's look at a few examples of these rules in action. Let's start off with an example for the sum rule where you have your function F of X equal to 17, plus two, multiplied by X. So here, you have your integral of F of X DX, equals the integral of G of X DX, plus the integral of H of X DX. In this case, your G of X is equal to 17 and your H of X is equal to two, multiplied by X. So this means your integral of 17 plus two multiplied by X DX is equal to the integral of 17 DX, plus the integral of two, multiplied by X DX. First, you have your integral of 17 DX, which using the constant rule, is simply going to be 17 multiplied by X plus C. Next, you have your integral of two multiplied by X DX, which using the constant multiple rule and the knowledge of the integral of X, you have a result of two multiplied by X squared divided by two, plus C, which if you simplify that, is simply X squared plus C. So now to get your integral of F of X DX, you simply add these two results together. So you'll add 17 multiplied by X plus C, and add that with X squared plus C. This gets you a final result of X squared plus 17 multiplied by X plus C. Note that the two constants of integration are simplified to just one C, because C represents any unknown constant. This means you can just have one C to represent whatever value they would take, combined. Let's look at another example. This time, with the difference rule. Let's look at the function F of X equals three, multiply by X, minus 15. Again, like last time, you'll separate these two functions to calculate their integrals. So you have the integral of F of X DX, equals the integral of G of X DX, minus the integral of H of X DX. In this case, you'll have G of X equal to three multiplied by X, and your function H of X equal to 15. So this means your integral three multiplied by X minus 15 DX is equal to the integral of three multiplied by X DX, subtracted by the integral of 15 DX. Let's first give the integral of three multiplied by X DX, using the constant multiple rule, and the knowledge of what the integral of X is, and this will get you a result of three multiplied by X squared divided by two plus C. Next, you'll get the integral of 15 DX, which is simply equal to 15 multiplied by X plus C, since again, you're using that constant rule. Now to get your integral of F of X DX, you'll subtract these two integral results to get three multiplied by X squared, divided by two plus C, and you'll subtract all that by 15 multiplied by X plus C. This gets you a final result of three multiplied by X squared, divided by two, subtracted by 15, multiplied by X plus C. Note that you still want to keep your constant of integration C in your final result, even if they technically subtracted out in the previous line, because again, you need to make sure that you account for the unknown constant value you might get when you are integrating a function. Let's add some bounds to this to make it a definite integral, where you can have bounds between two and six. So you'll plug in your bounds two and six into your function three, multiplied by X squared, divided by two, minus 15, multiplied by X plus C. So if you plug these in, you get three multiplied by six squared, divided by two minus 15 multiplied by six. Then you'll subtract all of that by three, multiplied by two squared, divided by two, and then you'll subtract 15 multiplied by two. If you calculate all this out, you get a final result of negative 12. Let's do one more example where you combine the sum and difference rules. Let's say you have the function F of X equal to eight, multiplied by X, subtracted by X, plus 40. Even though there are three functions this time with both addition and subtraction, you'll still separate these functions like you did before to calculate their integrals. So here, you have the integral of F of X DX equal to the integral of G of X DX, subtracted by the integral of H of X DX, added with the integral of J of X DX. In this case, your G of X is equal to eight, multiplied by X, H of X is equal to X, and J of X is equal to 40. So this means your integral of eight, multiplied by X, subtracted by X, plus 40 DX is equal to the integral of eight, multiplied by X DX, subtracted by the integral of X DX, added with the integral of 40 DX. Let's get the integral results for these three separate functions. So first, you have the integral of eight multiplied by X DX, which is equal to eight, multiplied by X squared divided by two plus C, and that simplifies to four multiplied by X squared plus C. Next, you have the integral of X DX, which is simply X squared divided by two plus C. Finally, you have the integral of 40 DX, which is equal to 40 multiplied by X plus C. So if you bring this together to get your integral of F of X DX, you get four multiplied by X squared plus C, minus X squared divided by two plus C, plus 40, multiplied by X plus C. If you simplify this, you get a final result of four multiplied by X squared, minus X squared, divided by two, plus 40 multiplied by X, plus C. Note that again, you want to make sure, you have your one constant of integration to account for any of the unknown values when integrating. This should give you a solid understanding of how to use the sum and difference rules when integrating. These rules are super helpful to use to separate out an integral, to make it easier to calculate in smaller pieces. Up next, let's explore the power rule.

Power rule (5min 50 seconds)
- [Instructor] You may have noticed I have not shown you any integral examples of functions with exponents other than one so far. This is because there is a whole rule of how to deal with exponents called the power rule, same as with derivatives. Let's begin with the definition. The power rule states for a function f of x equal to x to the n with any constant n, the integral for this function is going to be the integral of f of x dx is equal to the integral of x to the n dx and this is equal to one divided by n plus one multiplied by x to the n plus one, plus C. An easy way to think of this rule is that you take the current exponent and increase it by one, and then you divide your function with a new exponent as a constant value. Note that this rule does not apply for functions where you have n equal to minus one or where you have a to the x with a constant value, a not equal to zero, to the value of x. These two situations will be covered in later videos when you review how to work with polynomial, rational, and exponential integrals. You'll notice that you are already using this rule when taking the integral of x. So for example, you have your function f of x equal to x, and this is actually the same as x to the one. So if you take your integral of f of x dx, this equals the integral of x to the one dx. So if you go through this process, you have this equal to one divided by one plus one, and then you multiply that by x to the one plus one, and then you'll add C. As you saw before with your rule in this case, your n is equal to one, and so that's where you're adding it into the formula you saw before. If you simplify this, you get 1/2 multiplied by x squared plus C, which simplifies to x squared divided by two, plus C. As you may have realized, this is essentially doing the opposite of the power rule for derivatives. This rule can be proven using multiple techniques such as Riemann sums or the inverse relationship between derivatives and integrals. I suggest taking time on your own to explore these proofs if you wish to have a more thorough understanding of the why behind how this rule works. Long story short, when you use the inverse relationship technique, you simply take the integral of the power rule for the derivatives to get the power rule for integrals. Let's look at a few more examples of using the power rule. Here. You have the function f of x equal to eight multiplied by x to the third. In this case, let's first separate out the constant using the constant multiple rule. So you have the integral of f of x dx equals eight, multiplied by the integral of x cubed dx. With this result, you'll have eight multiplied by your result of your integral. So here, you have n equal to three, so you have one divided by three plus one multiplied by x to the three plus one. Then you'll add C to that. If you simplify the portion within the parentheses, you have eight multiplied by x to the fourth to divided by four plus C. Simplify this a little bit more to get a final result of two multiplied by x to the fourth plus C. Let's look at another example. Let's get the integral for f of x equal to six multiplied by x to the fifth plus nine multiplied by x squared. Remember, you can separate out the constant six and nine using the constant multiple rule, and then you can use the sum rule to separate out the functions x to the fifth and x squared. This means the integral of f of x dx is equal to six multiplied by the integral of x to the fifth dx plus nine multiplied by the integral of x squared dx. Let's first look at the integral six multiplied by the integral of x to the fifth dx, and this is equal to six multiplied by one, divided by five plus one multiplied by x to the five plus one. This is because your n equals five in this case, and again, make sure you add C to this. If you simplify this, you get six multiplied by x to the six divided by six plus C, which is simply x to the six plus C. Looking at your second integral, you have nine multiplied by the integral of x squared dx, which is equal to nine multiplied by one, divided by two plus one, multiplied by x to the two plus one. Remember, in this case, now your n equals five. Make sure you add C to that result. In this case, that equals nine multiplied by x cubed, divided by three plus C, which is equal to three multiplied by x cubed plus C. If you combine these results together for f of x, you get x to the six plus C, plus three multiplied by x cubed plus C to get a final result of x to the sixth plus three multiplied by x cubed plus C. Let's add some bounds to make this a definite integral where you are going between the values of one and four. So you'll plug in the values of one and four to your equation x to the six plus three multiplied by x cubed plus C. So here, you have four to the six, plus three, multiplied by four cubed, subtracted by one to the sixth, plus three, multiplied by one cubed, which gives you a total result of 4,284. The power rule is very important and will be heavily used in the following videos with a variety of functions. Remembering the easy trick of increasing the exponent by one and dividing the function by the new exponent will significantly speed up the process when calculating integrals. Let's wrap up this chapter by exploring additivity over integrals.

Additivity over intervals (6 min 35 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's conclude this chapter and explore a rule that pertains to just definite integrals. This is because this rule deals with the intervals that are integrated over. Let's look at the definition for the rule additivity over intervals. Additivity over intervals states for a definite integral with any real number c, you have the integral from a to b of your function f of x, dx, is equal to the integral from a to c of f of x, dx, plus the integral from c to b of f of x, dx. Note that c does not have to be between the values of a and b because this rule will work with any value. This rule is sometimes called the linearity of integration. There are a variety of ways to prove this rule, such as Riemann sums, the fundamental theorem of calculus, or using Lebesgue integration theory. I suggest taking time on your own to explore these proofs if you wish to have a more thorough understanding of the why behind how this rule works. One of the main uses for this rule is when you have adjacent intervals a and c, and c and b that you wish to integrate over. It can also be used to simplify evaluating definite integrals over complex intervals by breaking them into smaller pieces. Let's look at a few examples of this rule in action. Let's say you have the integral from 0 to 10 of the function three multiplied by x squared dx. Now let's say you want to piece up this interval and integrate it over the intervals of zero to five and 5 to 10. This means your c is going to equal five. Using the additivity over intervals rule, you now have the integral from zero to five of three multiplied by x squared dx plus the integral from 5 to 10 of three multiplied by x squared dx. Now let's solve the integral of three multiplied by x squared dx. Using the power rule, you get three multiplied by x cubed divided by three plus c, which if you simplify is just x cubed plus c. Now you can integrate this over the two intervals, and then add those two results together. So let's do the first interval for x cubed plus c between zero and five. So this gets you five cubed minus zero cubed, which equals 125. The next interval is x cubed plus c from 5 to 10, which gets you 10 cubed minus five cubed, which equals 875. If you add these two results together, 125 and 875, you get a total result of 1,000. Just to double check that this works, you can integrate this over the full interval for x cubed plus c from 0 to 10 to get 10 cubed minus zero cubed, which also equals 1,000. Now instead of having your c = 5, let's have c = 20 to have it be outside of the original interval to show that this rule still works. So here, you have c = 20 with interval 0 to 20 and 20 to 10. Note that again, even though typically you have that first value be less than the second value in that 20 to 10 case, in this case, you want to make sure that 20 is first since otherwise, this rule will not work. Let's start with your interval of 0 to 20 for x cubed plus c. So this gets you 20 cubed minus zero cubed, which equals 8,000. Next, you have your interval from 20 to 10 for x cubed plus c. So this gets you 10 cubed minus 20 cubed, which equals -7,000. If you add these two together, it is actually 8,000 - 7,000, which still gets you the correct result of 1,000. Let's look at a case where additivity over intervals is needed. Let's say you're integrating the piecewise function f of x, where you have x squared for where x is between zero and two and x cubed for where x is between two and four. If you wish to integrate f of x, dx, from zero to four, that would be difficult to do all at once because there are different parts of the function at different parts of the interval. Let's use the additivity over intervals rule to break this up accordingly. In this case, it makes sense to have c = 2 to properly split up your piecewise function to integrate. So you'll have your intervals of zero to two, and two to four. If you put this together with your new integrals, you get the integral from zero to two of x squared dx, and then you'll add that with the integral of two to four of x cubed dx. Let's get the first integral of zero to two x squared dx, which is equal to x cube divided by three plus c using the power rule. And then the next one is the integral of two to four of x cubed dx, which equals x to the fourth divided by four plus c, again using the power rule. Now let's evaluate these integrals at their bounds. So for x cubed divided by three plus c from zero to two, you get two cubed divided by three minus zero cubed divided by three, which equals 8/3. Next, you have x to the fourth divided by four plus c from two to four. So in this case, you get four to the fourth divided by four minus two to the fourth divided by four, which equals 60. If you add the results 8/3 + 60 together, you get a total result of 62 2/3. Additivity over intervals can help you better evaluate definite integrals in circumstances where the original interval is complex, or when the function being integrated becomes complicated with a specific interval. You'll see this rule come in handy when working with piecewise functions, discontinuities, changes in functions, and more. This wraps up your introduction to integration rules. These rules are all very important and will be frequently used throughout future videos to take integrals. I suggest taking time on your own to continue practicing using these rules with different functions. In the next chapter, you'll explore many common integration techniques.

	The value c must be greater than the value of b.
	The value of c must be less than the value of a.
	The value c must be between the values of a and b.
	The value c does not have to be between the values of a and b.

2.What is the integration rule for a zero function?
	The integral of a zero function is C, representing an unknown constant.
	The integral of a zero function is the x.
	The integral of a zero function is infinity.
