Day 12
Integration techniques
Integration techniques (2 min 1 second)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you'll explore many of the common integration techniques. Integrals tend to be more complex than limits and derivatives. You have learned what integrals are and many important integration rules, but there are still many scenarios where what you have learned so far will not be enough to help you successfully solve some integrals. The goal of learning the following integration techniques is to help you bridge that gap of knowledge and help you be able to properly learn integrals of some common functions later on. The integration techniques you will explore are: Integration by substitution. Integration by parts. Partial fraction decomposition. Integration by a trigonometric substitution. And improper integrals. Note that this does not compass all techniques available to calculate integrals, but it focuses on many of the common techniques used in a variety of integration scenarios. Each of the following videos will be structured similarly to give you a general overview of each integration technique. Each video will provide you with the definition of the technique, the reasoning of why it is needed, and multiple examples of how to calculate integrals using the technique. One thing I want to note is I will show you some integration techniques that will include integrals that I have not thoroughly reviewed how you find them yet. These may be integrals of trigonometric functions or exponential functions that you'll explore more in the following chapter as far as how you find these. Note that, generally, you can use the anti-derivative technique to find these integrals, so sometimes I will mention that, and other times, just assume that I will explain how you find those types of integrals in more detail in the common integrals chapter. The goal of the following videos is to provide you with working knowledge on how to implement these various integration techniques. You can then take this knowledge and expand upon it in your work and education. Let's get started with exploring integration by substitution.

Integration by substitution (7 min 13 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's dive into the integration by substitution technique. This technique can prove very useful when working with integrals of more complicated functions. Let's take a look. So far, you have been working with straightforward functions, but what do you do when you come across functions like these? For example, you may have three minus one divided by X, multiplied by cosine of three, multiplied by X, subtracted by the natural log of X. Next, you'll have six multiplied by X squared minus five, multiplied that by each the two multiplied by X cubed, minus five multiplied by X. And finally, you have 12 multiplied by X cubed, multiplied by the cube root of three, multiplied by X to the fourth plus 80. This is where the integration by substitution method can become useful, is to solve functions like these. Integration by substitution is when you substitute parts of an integral to make it easier to solve. After you solve the integral, you then plug those values back into the results, accordingly. This is sometimes called change of variables, due to the exchange of variables in the process of using this technique. Let's take a look at the technical definition for integration by substitution. Let's say you have two functions, F of X and G of X that are continuous over a range of values. If G of X is a differential function, then you can use U equal to G of X to solve for the integral of F of G of X multiplied by G prime of X DX is equal to F of U DU. In this case, you are substituting out the function GX for U, and its derivative G prime of X DX for DU. Let's look at the steps to perform integration by substitution. Step one, you will select an expression G of X from the integral, and set it equal to U such that G prime of X is also part of the integral. For step two, you'll calculate G prime of X. Step three, you'll substitute U equals G of X and DU equals G prime of X DX. Step four, evaluate the integral with respect to U. And five, plug in U equals GX into the result and evaluate bounds if it is a definite integral. Why is this method needed in the first place? The main purpose for this method is to use it when the integral is the result of a chain rule derivative. This method also works well when dealing with composite functions. Let's use this method with a few examples. Let's take a look at the integral of X multiplied by E to the X squared DX. By traditional methods, this integral would be difficult to solve. In this case, the X squared and the exponent of E is what is proving to cause issues. So let's use your formula of the integral of F of G of X multiplied by G prime of X DX, equals the integral of F of U DU to get U equal to X squared. And then if you derive this, you then get DU equals two multiplied by X DX. The constant of two is fine to have, since you can simply divide the integral by two to ensure you get the correct result. So you can adjust this a little bit to have it be one half multiplied by DU equals X DX. Let's substitute this into your original integral. So you have the integral of X multiplied by E to the X square DX, is equal to the integral of one half multiplied by E to the U DU. You will learn later on that the integral of E to the U is just going to be itself. So this gets you a result of one half multiplied by E to the U plus C. Now if you remember, you have your U equals X squared, so let's plug this back into your result to get a final result of one half multiplied by E to the X squared plus C. Let's take a look at another example. Let's solve the definite integral of zero to 0.5 of X, divided by the square root of one, minus four multiplied by X squared DX. This is a great case to use integration by substitution with your formula of the integral of F of G of X multiplied by G prime of X DX is equal to the integral of F of U DU. In this case, the inner portion of that square root, the one minus four, multiplied by X squared seems to be causing the most issues. So you can have U equal to one minus four multiplied by X squared, and if you drive this, you get DU equals negative eight multiplied by X DX. You can adjust this accordingly to have negative one eighths multiplied by DU is equal to X DX when substituting it into your integral. Now, let's substitute this into your integral to get the integral of zero to 0.5 of X over the square root of one minus four multiplied by X square DX is equal to the integral of zero to 0.5 of negative one eighths, multiplied by one divided by the square root of U DU. Using the power rule, you can solve this to get negative one eighths multiplied by one half, multiplied by the square root of U plus C. Combining those two fractions gets you negative one fourth, multiplied by the square root of U plus C. Now let's plug in your value of U equal to one minus four, multiplied by X squared, to get you negative one fourths multiplied by the square root of one minus four, multiplied by X squared plus C. Since this is a definite integral, let's evaluate it at your bounds of zero to 0.5, to get you negative one fourths multiplied by the square root of one minus four, multiplied by 0.5 squared, and then you'll subtract that by negative one fourths, multiplied by the square root of one minus four, multiplied by zero squared. Once you calculate this all out, you'll get a final result of 0.25. Let's review some practical applications for integration by substitution. In probability, this method can be used to calculate the expected value of a random variable. In physics, it can be used to solve fluid dynamics problems where there is a pipe with a non-uniform cross-section. And finally, in engineering, it can be used to find the stored energy in a capacitor. Integration by substitution can be a little difficult to use at first, but the main difficult part is just knowing how to pick the correct U. If you are having difficulty solving your integral after substituting your U and DU variables N, then it is usually a good indication you need to choose a different U. Let's move on to another integration technique, integration by parts.

Integration by parts (8 min 44 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned how to integrate using substitution. This technique is helpful in many situations, but what do you do if this technique does not work? This is where integration by parts can become helpful. In the previous video, you saw how you can use substitution to integrate the integral of x multiplied by e to the x squared dx. You would use your formula of the integral of f of g of x multiplied by g prime of x dx is equal to the integral of f of u du. In this case, you had u equal to x squared and du equal to two multiplied by x dx. This technique unfortunately does not work though if you have a similar case, such as the integral of x multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x dx. This is because there are no possible substitutions that would work. Integration by parts is similar to the derivative product rule due to this technique often being used in cases where two functions are being multiplied together. For integration by parts, if you have an integral with two functions, f of x, and g of x, that are both differentiable, then you can use the following formula to solve the integral. So here you have the integral of f of g prime of x dx is equal to f of x multiplied by g of x. Subtract that by the integral of f prime of x multiplied by g of x dx. The previous equation can be a bit much to remember, so you'll often see this other equation used instead, where you have the integral of udv equals u multiplied by v, subtracted by the integral of vdu. In this version, u is equal to f of x, v is equal to g of x, du is equal to f prime of x dx, and dv is equal to g prime of x dx. The definite integral version of this is very similar, where you just need to evaluate both parts between your bounds of a and b. So, here you have the integral of a to b of udv is equal to u multiplied by v, evaluated from a to b, subtracted by the integral of a to b of vdu. One of the most difficult aspects of integrating by parts is deciding what your u and dv variables are going to be. Worst case scenario, you can always go back to the start if your integral doesn't work, and try different variables for u and dv. Thankfully, there is a helpful acronym many mathematicians use called I-L-A-T-E, or sometimes arranged as L-I-A-T-E, depending on the source. What does this funny acronym, I-L-A-T-E, or L-I-A-T-E stand for? For the first version of this acronym, the order is denoted with I for inverse trigonometric functions, L for logarithmic functions, A for algebraic functions, T for trigonometric functions, and E for exponential functions. The way you use this acronym is you choose u by going from top to bottom on this list to determine which u will be best. Note that this technique does not work 100% of the time, such as if you're unable to integrate your choice of dv. Let's take a look at a few examples of the integration by parts technique in action. Let's go back to that example of the integral of x multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x dx. It looks like you can split this integral into two functions, x and e to the five multiplied by x. Using the I-L-A-T-E acronym, it looks like A, for algebraic, which in this case would be x comes before e, for exponential, which in this case is e to the five multiplied by x. So let's make u equal to x and dv equal to e to the five multiplied by x dx. Now that you have your u and dv variables, let's get your du and v variables. If you derive u equals x, you get du equals dx. And if you integrate dv equals e to the five multiplied by x dx, you get v equals one-fifth multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x. Now let's use your formula of the integral of udv equals u multiplied by v, subtracted by the integral of vdu. Let's plug in your values to get the integral of x multiplied by e to the five multiplied by xdx is equal to x multiplied by one-fifth, multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x for your u multiplied by v. And then you'll subtract that by the integral of one-fifth multiplied by e to the five multiplied by xdx. So again, this is your vdu portion. Once you solve this, you can simplify the first part of this to just get x divided by five multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x. And then for your integral, you get a result of negative 1 over 25 multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x plus C. Now let's look at a definite integral example where you want to solve the integral of zero to pi of x multiplied by cosine of x dx. You'll split your integral into your two functions of x and cosine of x. Using your I-L-A-T-E acronym, with A for algebraic, and T for trigonometric, you'll have your algebraic value be x, and your trigonometric value be cosine of x. Since A comes before T, it makes sense to have your u value equal x and your dv value equal cosine of x dx. Let's get the du and v variables next. So, if you derive u equals x, you simply get du equals dx, just like the previous example. For dv equals cosine of x dx, you'll integrate this to get v equal to sine of x. Note that you can use either the anti-derivative to find this, or you'll see an explanation later on when I review trigonometric integrals. Let's use your formula, the integral of udv equals u multiplied by v, subtracted by the integral of vdu. Let's plug in your values to get the integral of zero to pi of x multiplied by e to the five multiplied by x dx is equal to x multiplied by sine of x evaluated from zero to pi, subtracted by the integral of zero to pi of sine of x dx. Again, this is using those u, v, du, and dv values you calculated in the previous slide. The first portion of your equation stays the same, so you have x multiplied by sine of x, and then the integral of sine of x is equal to cosine of x. So you'll get plus cosine of x plus C, and let's evaluate all of that from zero to pi. This gets you pi multiplied by sine of pi, plus cosine of pi, subtract all that by zero multiplied by sine of zero plus cosine of zero. This equals negative one minus one, which gets you a final result of negative two. There are many practical applications for using the integration by parts method. In engineering, integration by parts is often used to calculate coefficients and Fourier series expansions. In physics, Hooke's law has integrals to calculate the work done by a spring. In finance, you can use this method to calculate the future value of investments with continuously compounded interest. And finally, in probability, you can use this when finding the expected value of a random variable when a complex integral is required to be solved. This should give you a solid understanding of how to use the integration by parts method in order to solve integrals. This technique is very useful when working with multiple functions, and you will see it arise in multiple cases in future videos, including when proving how certain integrals work. Up next, let's explore another method, partial fraction decomposition.

Partial fraction decomposition (9 min 6 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned about integration by parts In this video, you will use a similar concept where you'll piece up an integral into multiple parts and then proceed to integrate it. This time, though, you'll work with certain types of rational functions. The next technique you will learn is called partial fraction decomposition. This technique basically does what it states in its name, which is partition a fraction into multiple pieces to allow you to integrate on those. Officially, this technique is where you break down a rational function into simpler rational functions to integrate on. If you remember, a rational function is in the format of f of x equals g of x divided by h of x where this kind of looks like a fraction. For example, you may have u of x equals x cubed plus 1 over x squared plus 5 multiplied by x minus 7. This is a rational function, but it is pretty difficult to integrate on. You may have other rational functions, though, such as v of x equal to 1 divided by 2 multiplied by x plus 7 that are easy and straightforward to integrate on, especially once you learn some tricks in the next chapter for integrating rational functions. When you have cases like u of x, though, where it's not as straightforward, this is where this method can be very useful. Let's review these steps for partial fraction decomposition. I know that these steps look fairly long, and it is a bit lengthy, so stay with me as we go through these. Step 1, you'll want to check the degree of the polynomial in the numerator to ensure it is a lower degree than the polynomial in the denominator. If this is not the case, then you will need to perform polynomial long division first. For Step 2, you will factorize the denominator into as low a factors as possible. Linear factors are the most ideal, but note that you can have higher degree factors than that, such as quadratic factors. For Step 3, you'll separate your rational function by the denominator into multiple rational functions with constants A, B, and so on for linear factors, and A1 multiplied by x plus B1, A2 multiplied by x plus B2 and so on for quadratic factors. For Step 4, you'll want to multiply both sides of your equation with the denominator. Step 5, you'll expand the equations and solve for the constant values. Step 6, you will substitute the constant values back into the partial fractions you created, and Step 7, you will split up the result into different integrals and solve. Like I mentioned before, this process is a little bit more involved and lengthy, so let's go into it in detail with an example. Let's look at the integral of 3 multiplied by x divided by x squared subtracted by 3 multiplied by x minus 4 dx. First, you will check the degree of the polynomial in the numerator. In this case, the polynomial in the numerator has a degree of 1. Then you'll check the degree of the polynomial in the denominator, which, in this case, is 2, since you have that x squared term. This means the polynomial degree is one less in the numerator than the denominator, meaning you are good to proceed. Next, you'll factorize the denominator into as low a factors as possible. For this one, you have x squared minus 3 multiplied by x minus 4. This one fairly easily factors into x minus 4 and x plus 1. Now that you have your denominator factorized, you can have your equation 3 multiplied by x over x squared minus 3 multiplied by x minus 4 equal 3 multiplied by x divided by x minus 4 and x plus 1. Now let's separate your rational function by the denominator into multiple rational functions with different constants, which, in this case, will be capital A and capital B, so this means 3 multiplied by x over x minus 4 multiplied by x plus 1. This gets you capital A divided by x minus 4 plus capital B divided by x plus 1. Remember, in this case, your constants are capital A and capital B, since both of the denominators are linear factors. Now, if you multiply both sides of the equation by the denominator x minus 4 and x plus 1, you then get 3 multiplied by x equals capital A multiplied by x plus 1 plus capital B multiplied by x minus 4. Now let's solve for the constant values of capital A and capital B. You know from your factoring that x must equal -1 and 4. Let's start off with the case of x equals -1, so here you have 3 multiplied by x equals capital A multiplied by x plus 1 plus B multiplied by x minus 4, so plugging in x equals -1 gets you 3 multiplied by -1 equals capital A multiplied by -1 plus 1 plus B multiplied by -1 minus 4. In this case, your constant capital A cancels out, so you get -3 equals -5 multiplied by B, which means B equals 3/5. Now let's plug in x equals 4, and so let's put this into your equation of 3 multiplied by x equals capital A multiplied by x plus 1 plus B multiplied by x minus 4 to get you 3 multiplied by 4 equals capital A multiplied by 4 plus 1 plus B multiplied by 4 minus 4. This time, your constant capital B will cancel out to get you 12 equals 5 multiplied by A where A is then equal to 12 divided by 5. Now let's substitute the constant values back into the partial fractions, so you had capital A over x minus 4 plus capital B over x plus 1, and now you have 12 divided by 5 over x minus 4 plus 3 divided by 5 over x plus 1. Now you have a new integral for 3 multiplied by x over x squared minus 3 multiplied by x minus 4 dx, which will now be equal to the integral of 12 divided by 5 divided by x minus 4 dx plus the integral of 3 divided by 5 divided by x plus 1 dx. Let's solve each integral. In this case, again, you can get the antiderivative, and you will learn some tricks in a later video for rational integrals as far as how to easily solve these integrals. In this case, you have the integral of 12 divided by 5 divided by x minus 4 dx is equal to 12 divided by 5 multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of x minus 4. Next, you have the integral of 3 divided by 5 divided by x plus 1 dx is equal to 3/5 multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of x plus 1. This means your final result for the integral of 3 multiplied by x divided by x squared minus 3 multiplied by x minus 4 dx is equal to 12 divided by 5 multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of x minus 4 plus 3/5 multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of x plus 1 plus C. As you saw from the previous example, this technique tends to be very involved and very computationally intensive with many steps taken to get the final solution. This technique can definitely feel complicated at times, but it is very helpful when you can factor the denominator of the rational function with ease. Why do you need to know this complicated integration method in the first place? This method not only helps you simplify the integration process for some more complex rational functions; it also allows you to facilitate something called Laplace transforms along with simplify algebraic expressions and help algorithms run. For some practical applications in engineering, this technique is used to model dynamic systems, like vibrations, and analyze electrical circuits with transfer functions. In signal processing, it could be used to break down signals and filter frequency domains, and finally, in physics, it is commonly used in quantum mechanics and when calculating gravitational potentials. Like I mentioned before, this method can definitely be complicated at times to use and very computationally intensive. There are many resources available online you can review to learn more about this method and dive deeper into some more complex examples. This method will be mentioned some but used sparingly throughout the rest of the course due to its complexity. Up next, let's explore integration by trigonometric substitution.
Integration by trigonometric substitution (11 min 25 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned how to integrate using partial fraction decomposition. Now you'll review some special cases for a select few functions where you can use triganometric substitution. This technique is not as frequently used, but it is very helpful and powerful when it is necessary. Trigonometric substitution is a method used to simplify integrals involving certain square root functions. This method replaces part of the square root with a triganometric function, making it easier to solve or possible to solve in the first place. This is done by using substitution and triganometric identities. The following three equations are the main ones you will work with when it comes to trigonometric substitutions. So first you have the square root of A squared minus X squared, where X is a variable and A is a constant value. Next you have the square root of A squared plus X squared, and then finally you have the square root of X squared minus A squared. Note that these are not the only square root equations out there that you could use triganometric substitution, but these three are the main ones that you'll come across and will give you a good idea of how to work with this technique. So for each of these three cases, these are the triganometric substitutions you would make. So for the square root of A squared minus X squared, you would then have X equal to A multiplied by sine 0 theta and DX equal to A multiplied by cosine A theta D theta. Note that with this one, you'll often use the triganometric identity of one minus sine squared 0 theta equals cosine squared 0 theta, and then theta you will then have equal arc sine of X divided by A. The next one is the square root of A squared plus X squared, where you'll have X equals A multiplied by tangent 0 theta, DX equal to A multiplied by C squared 0 theta D theta. And then you'll use the triganometric rule of one minus tangent squared theta equals secant squared theta and you'll have theta equal to arc tangent of X divided by A. Finally, you have the square root of X squared minus A squared where you'll have X equal to A multiplied by secant 0 theta DX equal to A multiplied by secant 0 theta, multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. And then you'll use the rule of secant squared, theta minus one equals tangent squared theta, and you'll have this one have theta equal to arc secant of X divided by A. Note that you'll need to solve for theta to get your final result. Let's go through some examples using these different trigonometric substitutions. Let's begin with the integral of GX divided by the square root of four minus X squared. So in this case you have the format of the square root of A squared minus X squared where you will use X equals A multiplied by sine 0 theta and DX equal to A multiplied by cosine 0 theta D theta. In this case A is equal to two, because two squared equals four, so you have the square root of two squared minus X squared, where you'll have X equals two multiplied by sine 0 theta and DX equals two multiplied by cosine 0 theta D theta. Now let's plug these into your integral to get the integral of two multiplied by cosine 0 theta D theta divided by the square root of four minus two multiplied by sine 0 theta, and that portion is going to be squared. Once you square that portion, you get the integral of two multiplied by cosine 0 theta D theta divided by the square root of four minus four multiplied by sine squared theta. Using that triganometric rule, you have one minus sine squared theta equals cosine squared theta, which in this case will be four minus four multiplied by sine squared theta equals four multiplied by cosine squared theta. Substituting the sine gets you the integral of two multiplied by cosine of theta D theta, divided by the square root of four, multiplied by cosine squared theta. Now let's simplify this integral even more where you have the integral of two multiplied by cosine theta D theta divided by the square root of four, multiplied by cosine squared of theta to just be the integral of two multiplied by cosine A theta D theta divided by two multiplied by cosine A theta. Those two multiplied by cosine of theta terms will cancel out to just get you the integral of 1D theta, which simply equals theta plus C. In this case, since A equals two, you'll have your theta equal arc sine of X divided by A, so your theta equals arc sign of X divided by two, and since you're just substituting in that value of theta, you get a final result of arc sign of X divided by two plus C for your integral result. Let's look at another example. This time you'll have the integral of DX divided by the square root of X squared plus nine. And so this time it is in the form of the square root of A squared plus X squared where you'll have X equals A multiplied by tangent of theta and DX equal to A multiplied by secant squared of theta D theta. In this case your A equals three since three squared is nine, which gets you the square root of three squared plus X squared, where you'll have X equal three multiplied by tangent of theta and X equal to three, multiplied by secant squared of theta D theta. Plug this into your integral to get the integral of three, multiplied by secant squared of theta D theta divided by the square root of three, multiplied by tangent of theta squared plus nine. Once you take the squared of that tangent term, you'll get the integral of three, multiplied by secant squared theta D theta divided by the square root of nine, multiplied by tangent squared theta plus nine. And let's use that triganometric rule where you have one minus tangent squared theta equals secant squared theta, where in this case it'll be nine minus tangent squared theta equals nine multiplied by secant squared theta. Plugging this in gets you the integral of three multiplied by secant squared to theta D theta divided by the square root of nine multiplied by secant squared D theta. Let's simplify your integral three multiplied by secant squared theta D theta divided by the square root of nine, multiplied by secant squared theta. This gets you the integral of three multiplied by secant theta D theta divided by three, multiplied by secant of theta. Once you divide out that bottom term, you simply get the integral of secant theta D theta, which equals the natural log of the absolute value of secant of theta plus tangent of theta plus C. Note that you will learn more in the next chapter of how to get this type of integral, but again, you can always use the anti-derivative as well. So using your result of natural log of the absolute value of secant of theta plus tangent of theta plus C, you'll have A equals three, and this time you'll have theta equal arc tangent of X divided by A and theta equal to arc tangent of X divided by three once you plug in that value of A. Plugging this into your integral result, you get the natural log of the absolute value of secant of arc tangent X divided by three plus tangent of arc, tangent of X divided by three, and then make sure you add your plus C at the end. This gets you a final result of the natural log of the square root of X squared plus nine divided by three plus X divided by three, and then finally plus C. Finally, let's solve the integral of the square root of X squared minus four DX In this case, it's in the form of the square root of X squared minus A squared where you have X equal to A multiplied by secant theta DX equal to A multiplied by secant theta multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. In this case your A equals two since two squared equals four. So you have the square root of X squared minus two squared, where you'll have X equals two, multiplied by secant theta and DX equals two multiplied by secant of theta, multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. Plugging this into your integral, you get the integral of the square root of two multiplied by secant of theta squared minus four, and then you multiplied that by two multiplied by secant theta multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. Once you take that squared value, you'll get the integral of the square root of four, multiplied by secant squared of theta minus four multiplied by two, multiplied by secant of theta, multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. Using the trigonometric rules, secant squared theta minus one equals tangent squared theta, you'll get four multiplied by secant squared theta minus four, equals four multiplied by tangent squared theta. Plugging this in gets you the integral of four, multiplied by tangent squared of theta, multiplied by two, multiplied by secant theta, multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. This gets you the integral of two multiplied by tangent of theta, multiplied by two, multiplied by secant of theta multiplied by tangent of theta D theta. Once you multiply these terms through, you get the integral of four multiplied by tangent squared of theta, multiplied by secant of theta D theta. Now let's use another trigonometric rule where you have the integral of four multiplied by tangent squared of theta, multiplied by secant of theta D theta where you have tangent squared of theta equals secant squared theta minus one. So plugging this in you get the integral of four multiplied by secant and squared of theta minus one, multiplied by secant of theta D theta, which gets you the integral of four multiplied by secant cubed of theta minus four, multiplied by secant of theta D theta. Once you separate this out using the difference rule and solve this, you'll then get two, multiplied by secant of theta, multiplied by tangent of theta minus two, multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of secant of theta plus tangent of theta plus C. Since your A value equals two, you'll have data equal arc secant of X divided by A, which becomes theta equals arc secant of X divided by two. This gets you two multiplied by secant of arc secant of X divided by two, multiplied by tangent of arc secant of X divided by two. And then you have this go on a second line where you have this minus two multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of secant of arc secant of X divided by two, plus tangent of arc secant of X divided by two plus C. Once you solve this all out, you'll then get a final result of X multiplied by the square root of X squared minus four divided by two minus two multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of X plus the square root of X squared minus four divided by two, and that'll be for plus C at the end. Trigonometric substitutions can be useful in a variety of situations. In mathematics, they are used to calculate arc lengths, areas of irregular shapes, volumes and surface areas. In engineering, they can be used to calculate stress distributions and deflection curves and structures like beams. In astronomy, they're often used in orbital mechanics with some of Kepler's laws. And finally, in physics, they can be used to analyze passive objects and magnetic field equations. Trigonometric substitution tends to be one of the more complicated integration techniques, as you saw with a few examples, but it can be very useful in certain instances when working with certain types of square root functions. I highly recommend taking time on your own to further explore this concept. Let's wrap up this chapter by exploring improper integrals.

Improper integrals (7 min 8 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned how to integrate using trigonometric substitution. Let's wrap up integration techniques by exploring improper integrals. These are integrals that are going to include some special cases. First of all, what is an improper integral? An improper integral is where the integral has some sort of behavior causing it to go to infinity. This is usually because either infinity is in the integration bounds, such as the integral of zero to infinity of F(x)dx, or a function becomes infinite within a defined interval, such as the integral of zero to one of one divided by x, dx. This infinite behavior is what causes the integral to be improper in nature. As you may have noticed, this type of integral only applies to definite integrals due to the need for integration bounds. So how do you deal with these types of integrals? Let's take a look at these steps. When it comes to solving an improper integral, you first start with step one by identifying if the integral is improper due to the interval involving infinity or the function becoming infinite within the interval. Note that this can include positive or negative infinity, but I just state infinity to include both. Then, for step two, you replace the improper portion of the integral with the limit. Step three, find the anti-derivative of the integral to solve it. Step four, evaluate if the limit exists. Note that you will need to do this for both positive and negative infinity separately, if both of these are contained in the bounds of your integral. And finally, step five. If the limit exists, the integral converges and has a finite result. If the limit does not exist, then the interval diverges with an infinite result. Let's take a look at this in a few examples. Let's first look at the case where you have the integral of one to infinity of e to the negative x, dx. First, you'll identify the improperness of this integral, which in this case comes from having infinity in the top bound of the integral. This is where you'll need to replace this with a limit. For your limit, let's have the limit as b approaches infinity, and then have your integral be the integral of one to b, of e to the negative x, dx. So now you'll see your integral contains b as the top bound instead of infinity. Now, let's evaluate your integral of one to b of e to the negative x, dx, by finding the anti-derivative. In this case, your result will be negative e to the negative x plus C from the bounds of one to b. When it comes to evaluating your definite integral, the constant value of integration C will be cut out in the next steps. So plugging in your bounds of one to b, you get negative e to the minus b subtracted by negative e to the minus one. This simplifies to negative e to the minus b plus e to the minus one. Now let's evaluate your limit at the anti-derivative result that you got. So here you have the limit as b approaches infinity of negative e to the negative b plus e to the minus one. In this case, you can just plug infinity into that first term where you have negative e to the negative infinity, plus e to the minus one. Looking at that first term of negative e to negative infinity, this actually goes to zero. So you can simplify this to be zero plus e to the negative one, which is just e to the negative one. Since the limit converges, you now have a final result for your integral of one to infinity of e to the negative x, dx equals e to the negative one, which is approximately equal to 0.3679. Let's take a look at another example. Let's bring back the function you saw earlier of the integral of zero to one of one divided by x, dx. This time you can identify the improperness of this integral due to the function becoming infinite within this specific interval. This is because if you have one divided by zero, this is undefined. So in this case, you will need to replace your bottom bound with a limit. So now you have the limit as a approaches zero from the right of the integral of a to one of one divided by x, dx. Now, let's get the anti-derivative of your integral of a to one of one over x, dx, which in this case is equal to the natural log of the absolute value of x plus C, evaluated from a to one. Like with other definite integrals, that plus C term will drop. So plugging in your values of a to one, you get the natural log of the absolute value of one minus the natural logarithm of the absolute value of a. The natural logarithm value of one is equal to zero. So this gets you zero minus the natural log of the absolute value of a, which is just equal to the negative natural logarithm of the absolute value of a. Now, let's evaluate your limit for the anti-derivative result. So you have the limit as a approaches zero from the right of the negative natural logarithm of the absolute value of a. Since the natural logarithm of zero is not a valid value, this then goes to infinity. This means your limit diverges, where you have the integral of zero to one of one divided by x, dx equals infinity, which means that your integral diverges. Let's review some practical applications for improper integrals. In statistics, improper integrals are helpful in calculating probabilities with infinite ranges. In engineering, they can be used to determine an electrical field that has an infinite charge distribution. And finally, in physics, they can be used to solve fluid dynamics problems where there is an infinite flow. Improper integrals is only useful for a few certain types of cases, but they can arise more than you think with the infinite tendency of some functions and having infinite bounds be more common than you would think. It is also great seeing how you can directly use limits to your advantage when integrating. This wraps up integration techniques. These integration techniques vary greatly in their complexity and uses. You will commonly see them in action with calculus proofs and when working with some common function integrals. I suggest take your time on your own to continue exploring the techniques you learned in this chapter. In the next chapter, I will introduce you to some common integrals of various functions.

2.When is the integration by substitution method helpful to use?
	the function is an exponential function with a constant base

	The function is undefined only at a certain point.
	The function is trigonometric.
	The function goes to infinity within the bounds or the bounds extend to infinity.
	The integral includes irrational numbers.

	x = 2*tan
	x = 2*cos
	x = 2*sin
	x = 2*sec

	Check the degree of the polynomial in the numerator to ensure it is lower in degree than the denominator.
	Factorize the denominator into as low of factors as possible.
	Multiply both sides of your equation with the denominator.
	Separate the rational function by the denominator into multiple rational functions with constants for numerators.


Day 13
Common Integrals
Polynomial and rational integrals (6 min 55 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you will explore some common integrals. These integrals will draw from many of the functions you learned about earlier in the course. Let's start off with integrals for polynomial and irrational functions. Polynomial integrals are fairly straightforward to work with once you are familiar with integration rules. The integration rules you will use are the constant, constant multiple, sum, difference, and power rules. For rational functions, things get a bit more complicated past using the rules I just listed, so I will make sure to clearly review those types of functions and how to integrate them. Let's look at an example of a polynomial integral. Let's get the integral for the polynomial function f(x) equals 3 multiplied by x to the 6th plus 2 multiplied by x to the 4th, minus 10 multiplied by x to the 3rd, plus 5. You will notice this polynomial function will require the constant, constant multiple, sum, difference, and power rules to solve. So if you want to get the integral of f(x)dx, it'll simply be the integral of your function dx. Let's look at each term individually for your integral to solve this. So first, let's get the integral of 3 multiplied by x to the sixth dx. This one, you just use the constant multiple and the power rule to increase the power and divide by the new power to get 3/7 multiplied by x to the 7th plus C. Next, you have 2 multiplied by x to the 4th dx, which, like the previous one using that power rule and constant multiple rule, to get 2/5 multiplied by x to the 5th plus C. Next term is the integral of -10 multiplied by x to the 3rd dx, which is equal to -10 divided by 4, multiplied by x to the 4th plus C. And finally, you have the integral of 5dx, which this is just going to use that constant rule where you have the result of 5 multiplied by x plus C. Let's bring all your results together and reduce the constants of integration to just 1 to get a final result of your integral of f(x)dx equal to 3/7 multiplied by x to the 7th plus 2/5 multiplied by x to the 5th, subtracted by 5/2 multiplied by x to the 4th, plus 5 multiplied by x plus C. You'll notice that I did reduce the third term from 10/4 to 5/2 'cause again, usually you want to simplify it as much as possible. Now, let's look at a special case mentioned earlier of getting the integral of the function f(x) where you have x to the power of -1,, which is also equal to 1 divided by x. So in this case, you are not able to use the power rule like you typically would. So if you get the integral of f(x)dx, this is the integral of 1/x dx, and the result for this is actually going to be the natural log with absolute value signs around your x plus C. The reason why you can't use the power rule for this is if you used it, the power would actually zero out and it would not give you the correct result. Hence, why you need to memorize this particular situation. This may be a bit confusing at first as to why a natural logarithm seems to magically appear from a rational function. This is because the natural log of x helps represent the area under the curve for your function 1/x. The reason why you need an absolute value for x in your natural logarithm is because you cannot take the natural log of a negative number. So what this does is that it ensures that you can still get your full scale of x values, even if they do include negative values. Let's look at an example where your function is equal to f(x) equals to 2 multiplied by x to the -1, which is also equal to 2/x. If you get the integral of f(x)dx, this equals the integral of 2/x dx, and the result for this is going to be 2 multiplied by the natural log of x in absolute value signs plus C. You'll notice that you just used the natural log x result that you got previously from your 1/x integral, and this time you just view that 2 as a constant multiplier, and again, you just multiply it on the outside of your result. Let's look at a similar case where you have a function formatted where it's f(x) equals 1 divided by a * x + b, where a and b are constant values. In order to get the integral for this function of f(x)dx, this will be the integral of 1/a multiplied by x + b dx, and this is equal to 1 divided by a, multiply that by the natural log, and then absolute value signs of a * x + b, and again, after all that, you add your plus C for your constant of integration. This formula for this integral is proven by using substitution in order to get the appropriate constant multiplied by the natural logarithmic function you found prior. Let's look at this formula used in an example. So let's say you have the function f(x) equals 1 divided 2 * x + 10. And so the integral of f(x)dx is equal to the integral of 1 divided by a * x + b dx. So in this case, your a is going to be equal to 2 and your b is going to be equal to 10. So if you get your integral of your function, you have the integral of 1 divided by 2, multiplied by x + 10 dx is equal to your formula of 1 divided by a, multiplied by the natural log, absolute value signs of a * x + b, and again, add your plus C at the end. So in this case, you'll plug in your a equals 2 and your b equals 10 to get a final result of 1 divided by 2, multiply that by the natural log, and again, absolute value signs of 2 * x + 10, and again, add your plus C at the end. You may be wondering why all these natural logarithms keep showing up when integrating over rational functions. This is often due to the relationship between exponential growth and logarithms, inverse proportionality, and how logarithms help convert multiplication to addition. The integrals for polynomials can be fairly straightforward as you saw in that first example, but the rational function ones can take some practice to get used to because they change into those natural logarithms in the process of integrating them. I recommend taking time on your own to practice getting integrals of these types of functions to make sure you are familiar with them. In the next video, I will show you how to work with trigonometric integrals.

Trigonometric integrals (6 min 20 seconds)
- [Instructor] Up next are trigonometric integrals. Trigonometric integrals can be complicated to understand at first, like with trigonometric derivatives, my best advice is to memorize these integrals since that is usually the best option to learn how to solve them. These are the integrals for the six main trigonometric functions. So your integral of sine of x dx equals negative cosine of x plus capital C for your constant of integration. Next, you have the integral of cosine of x dx, which is equal to sine of x plus C. Then the integral of tangent of x dx is equal to the natural log of the absolute value of secant of x plus C. The integral of cosecant of x dx equals the natural log of the absolute value of cosecant of x minus cotangent of x plus C. The integral of secant of x dx equals the natural log of the absolute value of secant of x plus tangent of x plus C. And finally, the integral of cotangent of x dx equals the natural log of the absolute value of sine of x plus C. There are a few important combinations of trigonometric functions where it is helpful to memorize their integrals as well. Let's begin by looking at the case where you integrate some different combinations of sine and cosine. So you have the integral of sine squared of x dx is equal to x divided by two, minus sine two, multiplied by x divided by four plus C. Next, you have the integral of cosine squared of x dx equals x squared plus sine of two multiplied by x divided by four plus C. And finally, you have the integral of sine of x multiplied by cosine of x dx, which equals sine squared x divided by two plus C. So you'll see how these are all formatted very similarly. Another important combination to look at is different combinations of cosecant, secant, cotangent and tangent. These are some of the popular combinations you'll come across. The integral of cosecant squared x dx equals negative cotangent of x plus C. The integral of secant squared of x dx equals tangent of x plus C. The integral of secant of x multiplied by tangent of x dx is equal to secant of x plus C. And finally, the integral of cosecant of x multiplied by cotangent of x dx is equal to negative cosecant x plus C. Like with derivatives, the different properties dealing with the sine and cosine functions are key to finding their integrals. The reason why natural logarithms show up with other trigonometric function integrals is because they use ratios of sine and cosine, hence leading to the need of logarithms when integrating. Like I mentioned before, I highly recommend you memorize these different trigonometric integrals since that tends to be the easiest way to work with them. Let's look at a few examples of trigonometric integrals in action. Let's begin by getting the integral of the cotangent function where you have cotangent of x equals cosine of x divided by sine of x. For this one, you'll be using substitution where you'll have u equals sine of x and du equals cosine of x dx. So your integral of cotangent of x dx is equal to the integral of cosine of x divided by sine of x dx. Once you substitute in your u and du, you get the integral of one divided by u du. The integral of one divided by u is going to be a natural logarithm, so you'll get the natural log of the absolute value u plus C, and if you plug in your value of u equals sine of x, you get the natural log of the absolute value of sine of x plus C. Let's look at another example. This time, you have the integral of cosecant to the six of x multiplied by sine to the fifth of x dx. Note that you can rewrite sine to the fifth of x equal to sine squared of x squared multiplied by sine of x. Plugging this in, you get the integral of cosine to the sixth of x multiplied by sine squared of x squared multiplied by sine x dx. Let's look at a trigonometric rule where you have sine squared of x equals one minus cosine squared of x. Plugging this in gets you the integral of cosine to the six of x multiplied by one minus cosine squared of x and square all that, multiply that by sine of x dx. Using substitution, you get u equals cosine of x and du equals negative sine of x dx. Plugging this into your equation, you get the integral of u to the sixth multiplied by one minus u squared and then squared that, multiplied by negative du. Simplifying this, you get the integral of negative u to the sixth plus two multiplied by u to the eighth minus u to the 10th du. Once you solve this, you get negative u to the seventh divided by seven, plus 2/9 multiplied by u to the nine minus u to the 11 divided by 11 plus C. Substitute this in to get a final result of negative cosine to the seventh x divided by seven plus 2/9 multiplied by cosine to the ninth of x minus cosine to the 11th of x divided by 11 plus C. Let's do one more example with the integral of tangent to the fourth x multiplied by secant squared x dx. Let's use substitution where u equals tangent of x and du equals secant squared of x dx. So this means you're integral of tangent, the fourth of x multiplied by secant squared of x dx equals the integral of u to the fourth du. Solving this, you get u to the fifth divided by five plus C and plug it in your value of u equals tangent of x, you get tangent of x to the fifth divided by five plus C as your final result. Like I have mentioned a few times, memorization is key when it comes to working with trigonometric integrals. Once you have those memorized, then it's fairly straightforward as far as plug and play with solving different variations and combinations of them. I recommend taking time on your own to practice getting these integrals, and if you wish to look up the proofs of how these integrals work in the first place. Note that this is not the last you'll see of trigonometric functions or related functions in this chapter. Up next, I will show you how to work with exponential integrals.

Exponential Integrals (3 min 47 seconds)
- [Instructor] Next, let's take a look at exponential integrals. These are integrals that contain exponents where the variable itself is in the exponent, such as e to the x. This video will not cover functions where you have a simple power in the exponent, such as X to the fifth, since you could simply use the Power Rule to solve those types of functions. Let's begin by looking at general exponential functions in the format where you have f of x equals a to the x, where you have your constant value a, and it is greater than zero and not equal to one. Like I mentioned previously, you cannot use the Power Rule to solve this function because the variable is in the exponent instead of the base. If you wish to get the integral of a function in the format of f of x equals a to the x, where a is greater than zero and not equal to one, you will use this formula where you have the integral of f of x dx equals a to the x divided by the natural log of a plus C. Again, you'll note here that you don't need absolute value signs on the natural log because a is already supposed to be greater than zero. Essentially, this integral will simplify to your original exponential function divided by a constant natural logarithmic value. You'll notice that this is the opposite of how you derive an exponential function where you multiply your function a to the x by the natural log of x. Let's look at an example where you have your function f of x equals three to the x. So in this case, your a is equal to three. So let's get the integral of f of x dx, and so this will equal the integral of three to the x plus C. So if you use your formula that you had prior, you'll have the integral of f of x dx equals a to the x divided by the natural log of a plus C. And then this gets you a final result of integral of f of x dx equals three to the x divided by the natural log of three plus C. Since again, your A is equal to three here. Now let's look at the special case for the natural exponential function, f of x equals e to the x. If you use the same integration rule as before for your function f of x equals e the x, you'll get the integral of f of x dx equals a to the x divided by the natural log of a plus C, which in this case is going to be e to the x divided by the natural log of e plus C. Since this simplifies to e to the x divided by one plus C, that also simplifies to e to the x plus C. So you actually have a more simplified version where the integral of e to the x dx is simply equal to e to the x plus C. As you'll notice, this is the same as when you derive the function f of x equals e to the x, where again, the derivative is itself. So it's the same with integration, the integral is itself. Let's look at another example, but this time with that natural exponential function. So here you have your function, f of x equals four multiplied by e to the x. So here, remember you have integral of each of the x dx is simply equal to each of the x plus C. So again, itself just adding that constant of integration. So if you have your integral of f of x dx equal to the integral of four multiplied by e to the x dx, your final result is simply going to be four multiplied by e to the x plus C. Since your four is just going to be using that constant multiple rule and just being applied to the result at the end. Knowing how to work with exponential functions, including those containing other functions, will help you with more complex integrals you will work with in the future. Next, you will learn about logarithmic integrals, where you'll see how they continue to produce logarithms, like how exponential integrals like to produce exponents.

Logarithmic integrals (4 min 44 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now let's explore logarithmic integrals. You will explore how to integrate functions, such as log 10 X and natural log of X. You will notice how these integrals work similarly to those of exponents with how they produce logarithms and their results, like how exponential functions produce exponents in their results. Let's begin by looking at a general logarithmic function in the format F of X equals log base A of X, where X is greater than zero and A is greater than zero and not equal to one. Note that you can't use any of the previous integration rules to properly solve this type of function because the variable is within the logarithm. If you wish to integrate the function F of X equals log base A of X where X is greater than zero and A is greater than zero and not equal to one, then you'll use the following formula. So here you have the integral of F of XDX equals one divided by the natural log of A plus X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X, and then add your plus C at the end. You can technically simplify this integral a little bit by having the integral of F of XDX equal to X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X divided by the natural log of A plus C. Essentially, in this integral, you just need to take the original A and X values from your logarithmic functions and plug them into the new function result. The way you get this integral is by using the change of base formula just like you did with derivatives. Remember, the goal of this formula is to change the base of your logarithm from A to B. As you could tell from the integral definition I just showed you, it uses the natural log of X divided by the natural log of A version of the proof, since the natural logarithm is usually the easiest to work with. Let's look at an example where F of X equals log base seven of X. And so in this case, your A is equal to seven. So using your formula, you have integral of F of XDX equals X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X, divide all that by the natural log of A plus C. So here you have your function integral of F of XDX equals the integral of log base seven of XDX, and that equals X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X. Divide all that by the natural log of A, which in this case is seven, and add C at the end with your plus C. Now let's look at the specific case for the natural logarithmic function, F of X equals the natural log of X. The formula to get the integral for F of X equals natural log of X is equal to the integral of F of XDX equals X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X plus C. The way you get this result is by using integration by parts. Let's look at an example using the natural logarithmic function. Here, you have F of X equals three multiplied by the natural log of X. So in this case, you could use the constant multiple rule, move the three out, and use your formula of the integral of F of XDX equals X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X plus C. And so this gets you a final result of the integral of F of XDX equals three multiplied by X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X plus C. Let's expand upon this example a little bit by adding some bounds to evaluate the integral. Let's say you want to evaluate the integral from the bounds between five and 10. So here you have your result, three multiplied by X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X, adding C, and again, you want to evaluate it from five to 10. So let's plug these numbers in. So this gets you three multiplied by 10 multiplied by the natural log of 10 minus 10. Subtract all that by three multiplied by five multiplied by the natural log of five minus five. This simplifies to 30 multiplied by the natural log of 10 minus 15 multiplied by the natural log of five minus 15, which is approximately equal to 29.936. Knowing how to work with logarithmic functions will help with more complex integrals you'll explore in the future. It is also amazing how the change of base formula and integration by parts helps simplify the process of finding these types of integrals. I suggest taking time on your own to review the different proofs for both of these integrals if you wish to learn more about them. Next up, let's take a look at inverse function integrals.

Inverse integrals (8 min 18 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's take a moment to look at integrals for inverse functions. Remember, inverse functions are essentially doing the reverse of the original function. Knowing how to deal with these integrals will be important in order to help you understand inverse trigonometric integrals. Let's say you have an inverse function of F to the negative one of X for your original function, F of X. In order to get the integral of that inverse function, F to the negative one of X for F of X, you have the integral of F to the negative one of X DX is equal to X multiplied by F to the negative one of X subtracted by the integral of F of F to the negative one of X DX plus C. Note that this only works if your original function F of X is invertible and integrable. This formula can be a bit difficult to understand at first with so many different pieces. Essentially, it is saying the integral of the inverse of your function F of X, is equal to X multiplied by your inverse function, subtracted by the integral of your function where it equals your inverse function. You can prove this formula by using integration by parts or by substitution. Let's look at a few examples to make more sense of how this formula works. So let's say you have the original function F of X equals X squared. And so, you know how to get this type of inverse function where it is simply F to the negative one of X is equal to X to the one half, or in this case that's the same as the square root of X. Remember, this is because the inverse function essentially undoes what the original function is doing. Let's begin by finding the integral of your original function F of X equals X squared. So you have the integral of F of X DX equals integral of X squared DX And using the power rule, this simply equals X to the third divided by three plus C. Now let's put this together with your inverse function formula. So you have the integral of F to the negative one of X DX is equal to X multiplied by F to the negative one of X subtracted by the integral of F of F to the negative one of X DX plus C. So this gets you X multiplied by X to the one half. Subtract that by X to the one half to the third divided by three plus C. Once you simplify this, you get a result of two thirds multiplied by X to the three halves plus C. Now let's prove the inverse function formula for integrals works by using the power rule to simply get the integral of the inverse function X to the one half. So, here, we're getting the integral of your inverse function, which is the integral of X to the one half DX. Using the power rule, you get X to the three halves and you'll divide that by three halves, which simplifies to two thirds multiplied by X to the three halves plus C, which again is the same result as you got prior. Let's take a look at another example. Let's say you have a function F of X equals five divided by X plus four, and you want to find the integral of the inverse of this function. First, let's find the inverse of the function itself. So the way you could do this is you could have Y equal to five divided by X plus four, and then you will solve for X. The way you simplify this is multiplying both sides by X plus four, dividing both sides by Y and then subtracting by four to get x equal to five divided by Y minus four. And then you can switch the Y and X to make things consistent, as far as the variables you're calculating on, to get you the inverse F to the negative one of X equals five divided by X minus four. Now that you have the inverse function, you can start by finding the integral of your original function F of X equals five divided by X plus four. Here, you will want to use the formula of the integral of one divided by A multiplied by X plus B DX equals one divided by A multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of A multiplied by X plus B plus C. So in this case, your A is equal to one and your B is equal to four, 'cause again, that five is seen more as a constant multiple. So the integral of F of X DX equals the integral of five divided by X plus four DX, which using your formula, is now equal to five multiplied by one, divided by one. Multiply that by the natural log, and then absolute values of one multiplied by X plus four. And again, add all that with plus C at the end. Once you simplify this a little bit, you get five multiplied by the natural log of absolute value of X plus four plus C. Now let's bring back in your inverse function integral to get the integral of F to the negative one of X DX equals X multiplied by the inverse function. So F to the negative one of X. Subtract that by the integral of F to the F to the negative one of X DX plus C. Now let's plug in your values. So you have X multiplied by five, divided by X minus four. Then you'll subtract five multiplied by the natural log. And again, absolute value signs. And then you'll have five divided by X minus four and then plus four. Again, still in that absolute value sign. And then, finally, you add your plus C at the end. If you simplify this a little bit, you'll get five minus four multiplied by X minus five, multiplied by the natural log of five divided by X plus C. And as you know with properties of logarithms, when you have things dividing in them, you can simply separate them out to be subtracting. Once you simplify this more, and again, multiply that minus five through the two versions of your natural log, you get a final result of five minus four multiplied by X minus five, multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of five plus five multiplied by the natural log absolute value of X plus C. Note that since C is an unknown constant and only the value of zero can integrate to a constant, then you can create what is called a new arbitrary constant, C prime, that equals your constant terms all added together. So in this case, you have C prime equal to five minus five multiplied by the natural log of five plus C. So this can simplify your final result to be the integral of your inverse function F to the negative one of X DX is equal to negative four multiplied by X plus five multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of X plus C prime, 'cause again, C prime is going to equal all those other constant value terms added together. The reason why this is great to have is this is the result you would get using other integration techniques. And again, the constant value can represent any value, so at the end of the day, you can essentially lump them all together. Let's look at one more example that applies to the exponential function F of X equals E to the X. As you've learned prior, the inverse of this function is the natural log of X, and then as you saw in a previous video, the integral of F of X DX is equal to the integral of E to the X DX, which is going to equal E to the X plus C. Let's plug this into your inverse integral formula. So you have the integral of F to the negative one of X DX is equal to X multiplied by F to the negative one of X. Subtract that by the integral of F of F to the negative one of X DX plus C. And this equals X multiplied by the natural log of X minus E to the natural log of X plus C. And as you know, E and natural log cancel each other out. So this simplifies to X multiplied by the natural log of X minus X plus C, which, as you saw, from the logarithmic integral video, this is the correct result for the natural log of X integral. Dealing with inverse derivatives can be a bit tricky at first, but they'll get easier as you continue to practice working on them. It is also a good reminder to keep working on learning either how to find or memorize inverse functions depending on the case. You'll expand what you learned in this video, and the trigonometric integral video you saw prior, in the next video, where I explain inverse trigonometric integrals.

Inverse trigonometric integrals (6 min 34 seconds)
- [Instructor] Now that you have learned about trigonometric integrals and inverse integrals, let's combine these two concepts together to learn about inverse trigonometric integrals. Note that these are for functions, such as cosine to the negative one of x, also known as arccosine, and they're not the same as, for example, one over cosine of x. These are the integrals for the six inverse trigonometric functions. So first, you have the integral of arcsine of x dx, which is equal to x multiplied by arcsine of x plus the square root of one minus x squared plus C. Next you have the integral of arccosine of x dx, which is equal to x multiplied by arccosine of x, subtract that by the square root of one minus x squared plus C. After that, you have the integral of arctangent of x dx, which is equal to x multiplied by arctangent of x, subtract that by 1/2 multiplied by the natural log of the absolute value of one plus x squared plus C. Next, you have the arccosecant of x dx, which is equal to x multiplied by arccosecant of x plus the natural log and the absolute value sign of x plus the square root of x squared minus one plus C. Then, you have the integral of arcsecant of x dx, which is equal to x multiplied by arcsecant of x, subtract that by the natural log of the absolute value sign of x plus the square root of x squared minus one plus C. And then finally, you have the integral of arccotangent of x dx, which is equal to x multiplied by the arccotangent of x plus 1/2 multiplied by the natural log absolute value signs of one plus x squared, and finally, plus C. As you can see, these integrals are very closely related to each other, just like their derivatives are. And again, this is due to the relationship of these types of functions. If you remember the derivatives of the six inverse trigonometric functions, note that you can get these inverse trigonometric functions by taking the integral of these values. So here are the integrals that have the six inverse trigonometric functions as their results. So if you take the integral of one divided by the square root of one minus x squared dx, this is equal to arcsine of x plus C. Next, you have the integral of negative one divided by the square root of one minus x squared dx, which is equal to the arccosine of x plus C. Next, you have the integral of one divided by one plus x squared dx, which is equal to arctangent of x plus C. After that, you have the integral of negative one divided by the absolute value of x multiplied by the square root of x squared minus one dx is equal to arccosecant of x plus C. Then, you have the integral of one divided by the absolute value of x multiplied by the square root of x squared minus one dx is equal to arcsecant of x plus C. And then finally, you have the integral of negative one divided by one plus x squared dx is equal to the arccotangent of x plus C. These integrals can be found and proven by implicit differentiation and techniques such as integration by parts. I highly recommend you memorize these six inverse trigonometric integrals, along with the integrals I just showed you that have the inverse trigonometric functions as their results, because that tends to be the easiest way to work with them. Let's look at a few examples of inverse trigonometric integrals in action. Let's start by showing how to get the integral for the function f of x equals five multiplied by arctangent of x plus arccosine of x. So here the integral of f of x dx is equal to the integral of five multiplied by arctangent of x plus arccosine of x dx. If you remember from what I showed you prior, the integral of arctangent of x dx is equal to x multiplied by arctangent of x, subtract that by 1/2 multiplied by the natural log of absolute value of one plus x squared plus C. And then, the integral of arccosine of x dx is equal to x multiplied by arccosine of x, subtract that by the square root of one minus x squared plus C. Now, you combine these two results together to get the integral of f of x dx is equal to five multiplied by a big chunk, which is x multiplied by arctangent of x minus 1/2 multiplied by the natural log of absolute value of one plus x squared. Now you move outside of those parentheses to get plus x multiplied by arccosine of x minus the square root of one minus x squared, and then finally, plus C. Once you simplify this, you get a final result of five multiplied by x multiplied by arctangent of x, subtract that by 5/2 multiplied by natural log of absolute value of one plus x squared, and then add x multiplied by arccosine of x, and then subtract that by the square root of one minus x squared, and finally, plus C. Let's look at another example where you have the function f of x equals one divided by one plus x squared, subtract that by one divided by the square root of one minus x squared. So if you remember from prior, the integral of one divided by one plus x squared dx is equal to arctangent of x plus C. For the next piece, you have the integral of one divided by the square root of one minus x squared dx, which is equal to arcsine of x plus C. If you combine these two results together and subtract them, you get a final result for your integral of f of x dx is equal to arctangent of x minus arcsine of x plus C. Like I mentioned before, the best way to learn inverse trigonometric integrals is to memorize the integrals for each of the six functions along with when those six functions are the results of integrals. I recommend taking time on your own to continue practicing how to get these integrals of these functions and to take a look at the proofs of how to get these integrals in the first place if you are interested in how they work. In the last video for this chapter, I will expand on integrating trigonometric functions by showing you how to work with hyperbolic integrals.

Hyperbolic integrals (5 min 1 second)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this chapter by exploring hyperbolic integrals. Remember, hyperbolic functions are similar to trigonometric functions, but they are defined using a hyperbola instead of a unit circle. These types of functions are not as common to work with as the other ones you explore in this course, but it is good to know their integrals in case they do come up in your future work. The following are the integrals for the six hyperbolic functions. So you have the integral of sine H of XDX is equal to cosine H of X plus C. Then you have the integral of cosine H of XDX is equal to sine H of X plus C. Then you have the integral of tangent H of XDX is equal to the natural log of the absolute value of cosine H of X plus C. Then you have the integral of cosecant H of XDX is equal to the natural log of tangent H of X divided by two, and again, those are an absolute value signs, plus C. After that, you have the integral of secant H of XDX is equal to arc tangent of sine H of X plus C, and then finally you have the integral of cotangent H of XDX is equal to the natural log of absolute value of sine of H of X plus C. You'll notice these six integrals look very similar to your typical trigonometric integrals with a few minor changes in the process. These six hyperbolic integrals are found using exponential and hyperbolic properties. Like with the other trigonometric function integrals I highly recommend you memorize these six hyperbolic integrals since that tends to be the easiest way to work with them. Let's look at a few examples of hyperbolic integrals in action. Let's start off by showing how to get the integral for the function F of X equals sine H of X minus cotangent H of X. As you saw before, the integral of sine H of XDX is equal to cosine H of X plus C, and the integral of cotangent H of XDX is equal to the natural log of sine H of X plus C. If you combine these together, you get your integral of F of XDX is equal to the integral of sine H of X minus cotangent H of XDX is equal to cosine H of X minus the natural log of absolute value of sine H of X plus C. Let's look at another example that's a little bit more complicated. Let's look at the function F of X equals secant H of X multiplied by tangent H of X. If you remember from a video I showed you in a prior chapter, the function secant H of X is equal to one divided by cosine H of X and tangent H of X is equal to sign H of X divided by cosine H of X. Again, this is similar to their counterpart trigonometric functions. So here if you get the integral of F of XDX, which is equal to the integral of secant H of X multiplied by the tangent H of XDX, this is the same as saying you want to get the integral of one divided by cosine H of X multiplied by sine H of X divided by cosine H of XDX, which is equal to the integral of sine H of X divided by cosine H squared of XDX. Let's use the substitution method to get this integral. So in this case, you'll have U equal to cosine H of X and DU equal to sine H of XDX. So in this case, that means your function is equal to the integral of one divided by U squared DU. Using the power rule, the result for this is simply negative one divided by U plus C. So if you sub in U equals cosine H of X, you get a result of negative one divided by cosine H of X plus C, which again, as you saw prior, is equal to negative secant H of X plus C for your final result. Note that there are many scenarios like the last example that contain different combinations of hyperbolic functions. I will not review those in this course since there are dozens of them, but I highly recommend taking time on your own to look them up and explore them. Like I mentioned before, the best way to learn hyperbolic integrals is to memorize the integrals for each of the six functions. I recommend taking time on your own to continue practicing getting integrals for these types of functions, along with making sure you have those six integrals memorized. Remember, these functions are not as common as the other ones you have worked with throughout the rest of this course, but they're good to know in case you do encounter them in the future. This wraps up how to work with many common integrals for various functions. As you saw throughout the chapter, it is a mix of learning new techniques, using integration rules and memorization. Remember, practice is what will help continue to solidify the knowledge you are learning throughout this course.
