Chapter quiz (7 questions)
1.What is the integral of the exponential function f(x)=a^x where a>0 and not equal to 1?
	(a^x)/ln(x)+C
	a*x^ln(a)+C
	(a^x)/ln(a)+C
	a^x*ln(a)+C
2.What is the general integral of the cotangent function, which is represented as cos(x)/sin(x)?
	sin(x) + C
	cos(x) + C
	tan(x) + C
	ln|sin(x)| + C
3.What is the correct integral of the function f(x) = x^-1?
	1 + C
	ln|x| + C
	x^0 + C
	-1/x + C
4.What is the integral of coth(x)?
	cosh(x)
	sinh(x)
	ln|sinh(x)|
	sech(x)
5.What is the integral of the function f(x)=1/(1+x^2)?
	tan^-1(x)+C
	cos^-1(x)+C
	cot^-1(x)+C
	sin^-1(x)+C
6.Which of the following condition(s) are required to calculate an inverse integral?
	The function must be differentiable.
	The function must be integrable.
	The function must be invertible and integrable.
	The function must be invertible and differentiable.
7.What is the integral of f(x)=log7(x)?
	x*log(x) - x + C
	ln(x*ln(7)) - x + C
	ln(x)/ln(7) + C
	(x*ln(x)-x)/ln(7) + C

Day 14
Complex integrals
Numerical integration (10 min 6 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this chapter, you will explore some complex integrals and integration techniques. You will first review some techniques you can use when the previous ones you learned are not a good option. Then you will explore double and triple integrals. Let's start off with numerical integration. Numerical integration is a class of methods to calculate the integral of a function by providing an approximate value. These techniques are great to use when it is difficult or impossible to find the integral using the previous techniques you learned. You will explore three numerical integration techniques. These include the midpoint, trapezoidal, and Simpson's rules. Let's begin by looking at the midpoint rule. The midpoint rule allows you to use a Riemann sum with subintervals of equal width and midpoints to estimate an integral. The formula is represented by capital MN equals the sum of i=1 to N of f of mi multiplied by upper delta of X. So the upper delta is the little triangle. Your upper delta X is equal to b minus a divided n, where you have your function f of x on the interval a,b with the number of subintervals equaling n. And finally, you have the midpoint of each subinterval, which is represented by m of i. Let's see the midpoint rule in an example. Let's look at the interval of 0,1 of e to the negative x squared multiplied by cosine of xdx. In this case. you have the interval between 0 and 1, and let's divide it into four subintervals. If you do this, you get four sub intervals of zero to 0.25, 0.25 to 0.5, 0.5 to 0.7.5, and 0.75 to 1. If you get the midpoints for each of these subintervals, which is the middle point in between each of them, you'll then get m of 1 equals 0.125, m of 2 equals 0.375m, m of 3 equals 0.625, and finally, m of 4 equals 0.875. Let's use your formula, capital M of n equals the sum of i equals 1 to N of f of mi multiplied by your upper delta X. Let's calculate your upper delta X first to get b minus a divided by n, which is equal to 1 minus 0 for your interval divided by four, which is the number of subintervals you have. This gets you 1/4. Now let's plug in your values. This gets you capital M of n equals 1/4 multiplied by your four midpoints evaluated at those different parts of the function. So you have f of 0.125 plus f of 0.375 plus f of 0.625 plus f of 0.875. Let's plug in your function. f of X equals 8 to the negative x squared multiplied by cosine of x. This gets you capital M of n equals 1/4 multiplied by e to the negative 0.125 squared multiplied by cosine of 0.125 plus e to the negative 0.375 squared multiplied by cosine of 0.375. And then on the next line, you'll have plus e to the negative 0.625 squared multiplied by cosine of 0.625 plus e to the negative 0.875 squared multiplied by cosine of 0.875. Once you calculate this out and round it to four decimal places, you'll get capital M of n equals 1/4 multiplied by 0.9760 plus 0.8149 plus 0.5441 plus 0.3617. Once you combine this and multiply it by 1/4, you then get the integral of 0,1 of e to the negative X squared multiplied by cosine of xdx is approximately equal to 0.6742. Next step up the trapezoidal rule. This rule is very closely related to the midpoint rule. The trapezoidal rule allows you to gather an approximate value of an integral by summing trapezoids instead of rectangles. The formula of this rule is given by capital T of n equals 1/2 multiplied by upper delta of x multiplied by f of x0 plus 2 multiplied by f of x1 plus 2 multiplied by f of x2 plus all the way to plus 2 multiplied by f of XN minus 1 plus f of xn. Your upper delta X is still given by b minus a divided by n, and you'll still have your function f of x on your interval of a,b, with your number of subintervals equal to n. And in this case, you have what are called partition points, represented by the xi. Let's use the trapezoidal rule to compute the interval of 0,1 of A to the negative x squared dx. In this case, you still have your interval of 0,1, and let's use four subintervals again, This time you'll have your X of 0 equals 0, x of 1 equals 0.25, x of 2 equals 0.5, x of 3 equals 0.75, and x of 4 equals 1. Note that this time you start out with that X0 term, and so that is going to be the very start of your interval. So even though there are four subintervals, you'll be actually getting five partition points. So let's use your equation, capital T of n equals 1/2 multiplied by upper delta of x multiplied by f of x0 plus 2 multiplied by f of x1 plus 2 multiplied by f of x2 plus all the way to plus 2 multiplied by f of Xn minus 1 plus f of xn. So your upper delta X is still equal to b minus a divided by N, which is equal to 1 minus 0 divided by 4, which equals 1/4 Let's plug this into your equation to get capital T of n equals 1/2 multiplied by 1/4 multiplied by f of 0 plus 2 multiplied by F of 0.25 plus 2 multiplied by f of 0.5 plus 2 multiplied by t of 0.75 plus f of 1. Let's plug in your function. f of x equals e to the negative x squared. This time, you'll get capital T of n equals 1/8 multiplied by e to the negative zero squared plus 2 multiplied by e to the negative 0.25 squared plus 2 multiplied by e to the negative 0.5 squared plus 2 multiplied by e to the negative 0.75 squared plus e to the negative one squared. Once you solve these different exponents and simplify them by rounding to four decimal places, you'll then get capital T of n equals 1/8 multiplied by one plus 1.8788 plus 1.5576 plus 1.1396 plus 0.3679. So this means your integral of 0,1 of e to the negative x squared dx is approximately equal to 0.7430. The last technique you'll explore is Simpson's rule. This rule is named after British mathematician Thomas Simpson. It is similar to the trapezoidal rule, but this time, it will have improved accuracy. This rule estimates an integral by summing parabolic segments instead of straight line approximations. In this course, you'll use a version of this rule called Simpson's 1/3 rule. This rule is given by the formula capital S of n equals b minus a divided by x multiplied by f of a plus 4 multiplied by f of a plus b divided by 2 plus f of b. So you have your function f of x, and your interval from a,b. Let's use this in an example. Let's get the integral of 0 to pi of sin of x squared dx. Note that the squared part is within the sine function. Let's look at this on the interval of 0,pi, so using your formula, you have capital S of n equals b minus a divided by X multiplied by f of a plus 4 multiplied by f of a plus b divided by 2 plus f of b. In this case, your a is 0 and your b is pi. So let's solve the outer fraction to get b minus a divided by 6 equals pi minus zero divided by 6, which just equals PI divided by 6. Let's plug in your bounds to get capital S of n equals pi of 6 multiplied by f of 0 plus 4 multiplied by f of 0 plus pi divided by 2 plus f of pi. Plugging in your function, you then get capital S of n equals pi divided by 6 multiplied by sine of 0 plus 4 multiplied by sine of pi squared divided by 4, since you need to square both the top and bottom of that fraction, and then finally plus sine of pi squared. Once you solve this, you then get the integral of 0,pi of sine of x squared dx is approximately equal to 1.1266. Note that for any of the examples, you can round it to whatever decimal place makes the most sense to you. But again, just note that the fewer decimal points you have as you're going along, the more likely you are to have some sort of rounding error. This should give you a good idea of how you can estimate integrals in cases where it is extremely difficult or impossible to solve them using traditional analytical techniques. Up next, let's review what complex functions are and how to integrate them.

Complex function integrals (6 min 12 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's start off by exploring complex function integrals. Note that this video, and the two following theorem videos, will all be highly connected. Let's start with learning what complex functions are. A complex function involves complex numbers as inputs and outputs. These types of functions operate on what is called a complex plane. This plane represents complex numbers plotted, using a Cartesian coordinate system. This complex plane contains the input and output values with both the real and imaginary parts. A complex integral integrates a complex function F of C, along a path capital C in a complex plane. This is different than a typical integral where you would integrate along a real line. This path is called the contour, hence why these integrals are often referred to as contour integrals. There are two common ways to notate a contour integral, or again, a complex function integral. The first format is where you'll have the integral, and then you'll have a circle in the middle of the integration sign, and then you'll have your capital C for your complex plane, represented on the bottom right of that integral sign. And then you'll have that for F of ZDZ. The second format that you'll often see is the integral with just upper gamma in the bottom right of it, and then you'll have F of Z, DZ. You'll use the first notation in this course, but note that again, these are the two main formats you'll see when looking at other resources. There are various methods you can use to compute these types of integrals, and it starts off if they are contour integrals over open or close curves. Contour integrals over open curves take the integral over a finite curve, connecting two points in a complex playing capital C. Note that this will cause the complex function integral to have slightly different notation. So for example, you have the integral of Z one to Z two of F of Z DZ. Contour integrals over close curves take the integral over a closed path, also known as a loop in a complex plane capital C. So note the two different notations depending on if it's an open curve versus a closed curve that you are integrating over. Let's first take a look at the case where you have an open curve. If the contour capital C can be parameterized as Z of T for A less than or equal to T, less than or equal to B, then you can compute the integral with the following formula. So you have the integral of C of F of Z, DZ, equals the integral of A to B of F of C of T of DZ DT, DT. Note that this is the easier of the two types of integrals calculate between the open and closed curves. Let's look at an example of this. Let's evaluate the contour integral along the straight line path of zero to I plus one of Z squared DZ. So in this case, you'll use your formula integral C of F of Z DZ is equal to the integral of A to B of F of Z of T, DZ DT, DT. In this case, your Z of T is equal to T multiplied by one plus I for values of T between zero and one. This is what happens when you parameterize your contour. Now let's get the derivative of this. So DZ is simply equal to I plus one of DT. Let's plug this in using your formula to get the integral of zero to one of T multiplied by one plus I, and square all of that, and then multiply that by one plus I DT. This simplifies to the integral of zero to one of T squared, multiplied by one plus I, and cube that set, DT. And in this case, one plus I cubed, is equal to negative two plus two, multiplied by I. So you can then move this outside your integral to get negative two plus two, multiplied by I, multiplied by the integral of zero to one of T squared DT. This gets you negative two plus two multiplied by I, multiplied by T cubed divided by three, evaluated from zero to one. Once you evaluate this, you get negative two plus two, multiplied by I, plus one cubed, divided by three, subtracted by negative two plus two multiplied by I, multiplied by zero cubed divided by three. This gets you a result of negative two thirds plus two thirds multiplied by I. If you are integrating a complex function integral over a close curve, then there are two main cases to consider. If your complex function F of Z is homomorphic, meaning there are no singularities inside of your complex plane, capital C, then you will use Cauchy's theorem with Cauchy's integral formula to solve it. If your complex function F of Z is neuromorphic, which means it's homomorphic except for isolated singularities inside the complex plane, capital C, then you'll use the residue theorem to solve your contour integral. There are various practical applications for complex function integrals. In probability, they're used in complex valued probability distributions. In physics, they're used in electromagnetism and fluid dynamics. And in engineering, they're used in stability analysis and control systems and signal processing. This should give you an idea of what complex function integrals are, and some methods on how to solve them. You'll focus on using Cauchy's theorem and the residue theorem in the following two videos to solve contour integrals over closed curves. Let's start by exploring Cauchy's Theorem.

Cauchys theorem (5 min 39 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned that there are two main methods when solving contour integrals with closed curves. In this video, you'll focus on using Cauchy's Theorem. Let's first review what this theorem is. This is what Cauchy's Theorem states. Let f of z be a complex function that is holomorphic, meaning it is complex differentiable and it has no singularities in a simple connected domain, capital D. If capital C is a simple closed contour lying entirely within D, then you have the complex function integral of f of z dz equals zero. This means the contour integral of f of z around any closed curve, capital C in a simply connected domain is equal to zero. So how does this theorem work? A simply connected domain is where any close curve can be continuously deformed to a point without leaving the domain. If a function is holomorphic in a domain, then any closed loop integral within that domain must vanish. This is similar to the fundamental theorem of calculus where the integral of a derivative over a closed integral cancels out. Cauchy's Theorem is what creates Cauchy's integral formula to allow you to calculate contour integrals on closed curves. So if you have a holomorphic complex function, f of z over a closed contour, capital C, enclosing z naught, then you have the following formula. f of z naught equals one divided by two, multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, and then you'll multiply that by the contour integral of f of z divided by z minus z naught dz. Let's use Cauchy's integral formula in a few examples. Let's say you want to solve the contour integral of e to z divided by z minus one dz. In this case, capital C is the circle, absolute value of z equal to two. You can check that the function f of z equals e to the z is holomorphic everywhere. Remember, this means that it is complex differentiable and it has no singularities. The denominator z minus one suggests that you have a simple poll at z equals one. Since z equals one is inside the contour of absolute value of z equals two, you can then use the following formula. So you have your formula f of z naught equals one divided by two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by your contour integral of f of z divided by z minus z naught dz. You can then rearrange this formula to get the contour interval of f of z over z minus z naught dz is equal to two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by f of z naught. Now let's plug in your values to get the contour integral of e to z over z minus one dz is equal to two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by e to the one. This is because your z naught in this case is one. So this gets you a final result of two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by e. Let's look at another example. Let's solve the contour integral z squared plus three multiplied by z plus two divided by z minus two dz. This time, capital C is the circle absolute value of z equal to three. You could check on your own that f of z equals z squared plus three multiplied by z plus two is holomorphic. Your denominator z minus two implies that there is a single poll at z equals two. So you could check that z equals two is inside the absolute value of z equals three. So let's use your formula f of z naught equals one divided by two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i. Multiply that by the contour integral of f of z divided by z minus z naught dz. Rearranging your formula again, you get the contour integral of f of z divided by z minus z naught dz is equal to two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by f of z naught. In this case your z naught equals two. So let's find what f of two should be. So in this case, f of two is equal to z squared plus three multiplied by z plus two, which plugging in your value of z naught gets you two squared plus three multiplied by two plus two. So now you know that f of two equals 12. Plugging this into your contour integral, you get the contour integral of z squared plus three multiplied by z plus two divided by z minus two is equal to two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by 12, which gets you a final result of 24 multiplied by pi, multiplied by i. As you could tell, once you understand the terminology and how to work with complex functions, this theorem is fairly easy and straightforward to use, especially with the integral formula that corresponds with it. The main part is ensuring you check if the complex function is holomorphic before proceeding. In the next video, you'll review the other method to solve contour integrals with closed curves, the Residue Theorem.

Residue theorem (6 min 51 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned how to use Cauchy's theorem to solve contour integrals with closed curves. But what happens if your complex function contains singularities? This is where the residue theorem shines. Let's first review what this theorem is. This is what the residue theorem states. Let f of z be a function that is meromorphic, meaning it is holomorphic except for isolated singularities, and this is going to be on a simple closed contour C in the complex plane. Suppose f of z has a finite number of isolated singularities notated by z1, z2, all the way to zn, and this will be inside of C. Then the contour integral is given by the contour integral of f of z over z minus z nought dz is equal to two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by the sum of k equals one to n of the residues of (f,zk). Let's review the contour integral for f of z with the residue theorem. So looking at your formula that you just reviewed, you first have the singularities of z1, z2 to zn, and then you have your residue, which is represented by the R and then es and (f,zk), so that'll be the residue f of zk, and this represents the coefficient one divided by z minus zk in the Laurent series expansion of f of z around zk. So how does this theorem work? Instead of directly computing the contour integral, you're going to find the residues at the singularities and multiply it by two, multiplied by pi, multiplied by i. This generalizes Cauchy's theorem, where the non-zero contributions come from the singularities inside of C. So how do you find these elusive residues? The residue at a singularity zK is found by, number one, if f of z has a simple pole zk, then the the residue is the residue of f,ck equals the limit as x approaches zk of z minus zk multiplied by f of z. Number two, if f of z has a pole of order m at zk, then the residue is given by the residue of f of zk equals one, divided by m minus one factorial, multiplied by the limit as x approaches zk with the derivative of d to the m minus one over d multiplied by z to the m minus one, and this will be for z minus ck, taken to the m, multiplied by f of z. Number three, if f of z is expanded as a Laurent series formatted as f of z equals the sum of n equals negative infinity to infinity of a of n multiplied by z minus zk to the n, then the residue at zk is a minus one. And note that this is not a minus one, but a of minus one. Let's use this in a few examples. Let's solve the contour integral of one divided by z minus two dz. In this case, you have a closed contour enclosing z equals two with a simple pole. So using the first option, you'll have the residue of f of zk equals limit as x approaches zk of z minus zk multiplied by f of z. Plugging in your values, you get the residue of f of two equals the limit as x approaches two of z minus two, and then you'll multiply by your numerator, which is your function, which is just one, so in this case, it equals one. So using your theorem, you get the contour integral of f of z divided by z minus z nought dz is equal to two, multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by the sum of k equals one to n of the residue of f and zk. Let's plug in your values to get the contour integral of one divided by z minus two dz equals two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i, multiplied by one, which just gets you two multiplied by pi, multiplied by i. Let's look at another example, and let's solve the contour integral of z plus two divided by z minus one to the third dz. In this case, you have a higher order pole of z equals one of order three. So using the second option to find the residue, you have the residue of f of zk equals one divided by m minus one factorial multiplied by the limit as x approaches zk of d to the m minus one over d, multiplied by z to the m minus one, and that'll be for z minus z of k to the m, multiplied by f of z. Plugging in your values, you get the residue of f one equals one divided by three minus one factorial multiplied by the limit as x approaches one of d to the three minus one divided by d of z to the three minus one, and you'll multiply that by z to the minus one cubed, multiplied by z plus two, divided by z minus one cubed. Simplifying this, you get the residue of (f,1) equals one half multiplied by the limit as x approaches one of d squared over dz squared, multiplied by z plus two since you're z minus one cubed cancels out. So in this case, let's find the second derivative of this. So your first derivative is d dz of z plus two, which equals one, and then if you take the second derivative of that, you get d squared over dc squared of one, which equals zero. So your residue f of one equals one half multiplied by the limit as x approaches one of zero, which equals one half multiplied by zero, which gets you just zero as your result. So the final result for your contour integral of z plus two over z minus one cubed dz is equal to zero. Note that you could technically use the third option of the Laurent series expansion on this example as well to get that same result. It is great seeing how you can use the residue theorem to solve contour integrals that contain singularities with closed curves. Double integrals (8 min 36 seconds)
- [Instructor] In the last few chapters, you learn what an integral is, and how to calculate it. But you may be wondering, what is a higher order integral? Let's dive into a definition of exactly what higher order integrals are. A higher order integral is when you take the integral of a function, multiple times. So instead of just integrating a function once, you may integrate that original function, then integrate the result of that first integral, and then repeat that as many times as needed. Let's start off by exploring double integrals. A double integral is when you take an integral two times in succession. Essentially, it is doing the same thing twice. It is notated by two integral signs next to each other. One thing to note is usually when you do a double integral, you'll usually have two different variables, you are integrating on, such as X and Y within your integral. So the way this is notated is usually, you have your function F of X comma Y instead of just F of X, and then the format for the double integral is, two integral signs right next to each other, and then you have F of XY, and then you'll have DX and DY since again, you're integrating both on X and Y in the process. Double integrals generally have the same properties as regular single integrals do, and they generally function the same. Essentially, instead of just integrating once, you're just going to be integrating twice. Note that this is a bit of a simplification for this course to teach you the general idea of how to work with double integrals, but they do tend to be a bit more complicated when defining their properties. Also, you generally tend to take definite double integrals instead of indefinite double integrals, since the indefinite ones can get a bit complicated. One important theorem I will mention before I proceed is Fubini's theorem. Fubini's theorem states if your function F of X comma Y is continuous over a rectangular region R, defined by A less than or equal to X, less than or equal to B and C less than or equal to Y, less than or equal to D, then your double integral of R of F of X comma Y, DX DA is going to be equal to the integral of A to B, Integral C to D of F of X comma Y, DY DX, is equal to the integral of C to D, integral A to B of F of X comma Y, DX DY. Essentially, this means you could break down the double integral into two single integrals to solve and evaluate to simplify your calculations. You'll also note that you can switch the order, and it doesn't necessarily matter as long as your variables are matching your bounds. So you may be wondering, what exactly does a double integral measure in the first place? Double integrals usually measure volume under a surface area of a region, or the average value of a function over a plane region. They can also be used in rectangular regions, other general plane regions, and with polar coordinates in a more circular context. For this video, I will focus on a simple example in a rectangular region. Let's look at an example of how double integrals work. So let's say you have the function F of X comma Y, Y equals four, multiplied by X to the seventh, plus eight multiplied by Y cubed. And let's say you have your bounds of X between zero and two and Y between one and three. So here, you have your double integral of F of X comma Y, DX DY, is equal to the integral of one to three, integral zero to two of four, multiplied by X to the seventh, plus eight multiplied by Y cubed, DX, DY. And so you'll notice the outer integral of one to three has your DY, and your inner integral of zero to two has DX. So again, make sure that your bounds and your DX DY are matching. Note that you should be able to integrate this function with respect to X or Y first. In this case, we will do with respect to X first. Remember, you can use Fubini's theorem to separate out these integrals to make them easier to calculate, and again, switch the order if needed. Let's begin by taking the integral with respect to X and separating the integral, using the sum rule. So let's get the integral from zero to two of four, multiplied by X to the seventh DX. So this gets you a result of four multiplied by one eighth, multiplied by X to the eighth, which simplifies to X to the eighth divided by two. If you evaluate this from zero to two, you get two to the eighth divided by two, minus zero to the eighth divided by two, which equals 256 divided by two, which is equal to 128. Next comes a little bit trickier part. So here, you're integrating from zero to two of eight, multiplied by Y cubed DX. Now again, this time, you're integrating with respect to X, so in a way, you kind of ignore that eight Y cubed, and then you just act like you're integrating one DX. So your result for this is going to be eight multiplied by Y cubed, multiplied by X. And again, you're still going to evaluate that from zero to two. So here, you have eight multiplied by Y cubed, multiplied by two. Subtract all that by eight, multiplied by Y cubed, multiplied by zero to get your result of 16 multiplied by Y cubed. So your first integral from zero to two of four multiplied by X to the seventh plus eight multiplied by Y cubed DX is equal to 128 plus 16, multiplied by Y cubed. This is how your results should be, since you should have all your X variables integrated out and evaluated so that you're just left in this case with your Y variable to integrate on. Now let's evaluate the next part of the integral with respect to DY. So here, you have the integral from one to three of 128 plus 16, multiplied by Y cubed DY. You can separate this out again, using the sum rule. So you have the integral of one to three of 128 DY, which is equal to 128 multiplied by Y, evaluated from one to three, which is 128 multiplied by three, minus 128 multiplied by one, which equals 256. Next, let's evaluate the integral of one to three of 16 multiplied by Y cubed DY, which is equal to 16 multiplied by one fourth multiplied by Y to the fourth, using the power rule. Simplify this to get four multiplied by Y to the fourth. Evaluate it from one to three. So this gets you four, multiplied by three to the fourth. Subtract that by a four, multiplied by one to the fourth, which equals 320. So this means your integral between one to three of 128 plus 16, multiplied by Y cubed DY, is equal to 256 plus 320, which gives you a result of 576. So this means the final result for your double integral of one to three integral is zero to two of four multiplied by X to the seventh plus eight multiplied by Y cubed DX DY is equal to 576. You may be wondering why you need to do a double integral in the first place. Like I mentioned earlier, double integrals are very helpful when measuring volume under a surface area of a region, or the average value of a function. In physics, double integrals are used for a variety of calculations such as center of mass and moments of inertia. In probability, they are used in joint probability density functions. And finally, in engineering, they're used in wave functions along with image and signal processing. Once you understand the concept of double integrals, any integrals with the higher order are fairly straightforward with simple repetition. This is similar to higher order derivatives where again, you basically just keep doing the same thing over and over. Note that this course just scratches the surface of double integrals, since they often take many weeks of content in a regular advanced university calculus course. Feel free to continue exploring double integrals more on your own, though. Up next, you'll continue to expand upon this concept by exploring triple integrals.

Triple integrals (7 min 53 seconds)
- [Trainer] Once you learn the concept of double integrals, any integrals at the higher order are fairly straightforward to learn. Essentially, you just keep repeating the same process as many times as needed. In this video, you will explore triple integrals. A triple integral is when you take an integral three times in succession. Essentially, it is just doing the same thing three times in a row. It is notated by three integral signs next to each other. One thing to note is usually when you do a triple integral, you have three different variables you are integrating on such as X, Y, and Z within your integral. So then your format for your triple integral is you have three integral signs, and then you have F of X, Y, Z, and then you have DXDYDZ. To keep things simple for this course, you can assume the properties for single and double integrals apply to triple integrals. Triple integrals bring you to three dimensions. They can be used in a variety of ways, such as measuring volume, force, or mass in a 3D region, along with finding the average value over three variables. Triple integrals can also be used in general 3D regions, cylindrical or spherical regions, and with plane projections. For this video, I will focus on a simple example to show you the process of working through a triple integral. Let's solve the triple integral for the function F of X, Y, Z equals six multiplied by X to the fifth plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by Z. In this case, you'll have X be between zero and one, Y between zero and two, and Z between zero and three. So your triple integral of F of X, Y, Z, DXDYDZ is equal to the integral of zero to three, integral zero to two, integral zero to one, six multiplied by X to the fifth plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by ZDXDYDZ. Again, note with Fubini's theorem that you should be able to separate out these integrals to integrate them. And again, you can put them in whatever order you want, just like as you see here, making sure that your X, Y, and Z bounds are matching appropriately with the DX, DY, and DZ. Let's begin by taking the integral with respect to X, and you can separate this integral out using the sum rule. So here you have the integral of zero to one of six multiplied by X to the fifth DX, which is equal to six multiplied by one sixth multiplied by X to the sixth using the power rule, which simplifies to just X to the sixth between zero and one. So if you evaluate this, you get one to the sixth minus zero to the sixth, which equals one. Next, you have your integral from zero to one of four multiplied by Y cubed DX, which equals four multiplied by Y cubed, multiplied by X from zero to one. Like with double integrals, again, when you're integrating on other variables, essentially you just act like you're integrating on one. Once you evaluate this, you get four multiplied by Y cubed multiplied by one. Subtract that by four multiplied by Y cubed multiplied by zero, and that gets you a result of four multiplied by Y cubed. Finally, you have the integral of zero to one of two multiplied by ZDX. Again, basically like you're integrating on the value of one. This gets you a result of two multiplied by Z multiplied by X, evaluated from zero to one, which is two multiplied by Z multiplied by one minus two multiplied by Z multiplied by zero, which equals two multiplied by Z. So this means your first integral from zero to one of six multiplied by X to the fifth plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by ZDX is equal to one plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by Z. Now let's evaluate the integral with respect to Y. So here you get zero to two of one plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by ZDY. Remember, you could separate this out using the sum rule. So first, your integral from zero to two of 1DY is simply equal to Y. Evaluate that from zero to two equals two minus zero, which is simply two. Next, you have the integral from zero to two of four multiplied by Y cubed DY, which equals four multiplied by one divided by four, multiplied by Y to the fourth, which equals Y to the fourth, evaluated from zero to two. This equals two to the fourth minus zero to the fourth, which equals 16. And then finally, you have your integral from zero to two of two multiplied by ZDY, which again, it's like integrating on one. So you have a result of two multiplied by Z multiplied by Y, evaluated from zero to two, which is two multiplied by Z multiplied by two, subtract that by two, multiplied by Z multiplied by zero, which equals four multiplied by Z. This means the result of your second integral of zero to two of one plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by ZDY is equal to two plus 16 plus four multiplied by Z, which is equal to 18 plus four multiplied by Z. Finally, let's evaluate your last integral with respect to Z. So you have the integral of zero to three of 18 plus four multiplied by ZDZ. Let's separate this out again using the sum rule. So you have the integral of zero to three of 18DZ is equal to 18 multiplied by Z, evaluated from zero to three, which is 18 multiplied by three minus 18 multiplied by zero, which equals 54. Next, you have the integral of zero to three of four multiplied by ZDZ, which equals four multiplied by one half, multiplied by Z squared, which equals two multiplied by Z squared, evaluated from zero to three. And this equals two multiplied by three squared minus two multiplied by zero squared, which equals 18. So your integral of zero to three of 18 plus four multiplied by ZDZ is equal to 54 plus 18, which gives you a result of 72. This means your final result for your triple integral of zero to three, zero to two, and zero to one of six multiplied by X to the fifth plus four multiplied by Y cubed plus two multiplied by ZDXDYDZ is equal to 72. So why would you need to take a triple integral in the first place? Like I mentioned earlier, these integrals are important to measure volume, force, and mass in a 3D region, along with finding the average value over three variables. They can be used in engineering to calculate electric and magnetic field intensities. They can also be used to model heat distribution in a 3D object in physics. Like I mentioned before, once you understand the concept of double integrals, any integrals such as triple integrals that are a higher order are fairly straightforward with simple repetition. Note that this course just scratches the surface of triple integrals, since again, like with double integrals, they often take up many weeks of content in a regular advanced calculus university course. But as always, you are welcome to continue exploring this concept more on your own. This wraps up your introduction to complex integrals, but note that this is just the beginning. I suggest you take time on your own to continue exploring these topics, since again, they're often taught in an advanced calculus university course and usually have a whole course dedicated to them and various other advanced concepts.
