1.What is the purpose of using triple integrals in calculus?
	to differentiate complex functions in higher dimensions
	to measure volume, force, or mass in a three-dimensional region and average value over 3 variables
	to integrate functions of two variables
	to solve single-variable equations

2.What does Fubini's Theorem allow you to do with double integrals?
	Evaluate double integrals without integration techniques
	Break down a double integral into two single integrals
	Calculate indefinite double integrals more easily
	Use only one variable to evaluate double integrals

3.When using the Residue Theorem, what do you compute to find the contour integral?
	The value of the function at the midpoint of the contour.
	The value of the function at the boundaries of the contour.
	The sum of residues at singularities inside the contour.
	The derivative of the function along the contour path.

4.What does Cauchys Theorem tell you about contour integrals in simply connected domains?
	The contour integral can only be zero if there is a singularity.
	The contour integral is zero only if the path is a straight line.
	The contour integral is always non-zero if the function is holomorphic.
	The contour integral is zero if the function is holomorphic.
5.What is a contour integral?
	an integral that doesn't involve a path or curve
	an integral along a straight line on the real number line
	an integral along a path in the complex plane
	an integral over the entire complex plane

6.What is the primary purpose of numerical integration techniques?
	to determine the precise analytical solution of the integral of a function
	to solve differential equations directly using algebraic methods
	to calculate the integral of a function by providing an approximate value
	to simplify complex functions into linear equations for easier integration

Day 15
Integral applications
Area between curves (6 min 47 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this course by exploring multiple applications for integrals. Not only can you use integrals to find the area under the curve, but there are various other applications in a wide variety of industries. The applications you will explore are area between curves, volume, average value of a function, arc length, surface area, force, work, and energy, masses and moments, and probability. Let's start off with finding the area between curves. So far, you have learned how to compute integrals to find the area beneath the curve of a function. This will usually be between the function and the X-axis. But what do you do if there are two functions created in an area? This is where you can expand upon your knowledge of integrals to find the area between curves. So for example in this graph, you have a blue line representing one function, which is a parabola. So it looks like a U. And then you have a red line representing another function, which is a straight line through Y equals four. So, you are looking at the shaded blue area where it is going to be the area between these two curves. You can calculate the area between curves by integrating over the difference between the two functions that make up the curve boundaries. You can do this in terms of X or Y, and often you will have designated bounds you will integrate over. Let's look at the formulas for the area between curves. Let F of X and G of X be continuous functions, such that F of X is greater than or equal to G of X over the close bounded interval A to B. The area for the given region between the two functions and its bounds is given by capital A equals the integral of A to B of F of X minus G of X DX. Now let's look at another version of this formula. Let F of Y and G of Y be continuous functions, such that F of Y is greater than or equal to G of Y over the closed bounded interval A to B. The area for the given region is capital A equals the integral of A to B of F of Y minus G of Y DY. In the two formulas you saw in the previous slide, the first one is when you were integrating the upper curve subtracted by the lower curve. So this is done in terms of X. The second one is when you're integrating the right curve subtracted by the left curve, and this is done in terms of Y. Sometimes you may need to find the intersection points of the two functions to integrate over. In this case, you will equal F of X equals G of X, or F of Y equals G of Y and solve to get X or Y accordingly to gather bounds for your integral. Let's start with finding the area between the functions of F of X equals the square root of X and G of X equals X squared between the values of zero and one. You can look at them graphically, where you have the graph of F of X equals the square root of X notated by the blue line that starts at X equals zero and slightly goes up into the right. And then you have G of X equals X squared, which is represented by the red line. So it's a parabola, making a U-shape. And what you want to find is the area between these two curves between zero and one. So that'll get you the shaded region between those two curves. In this case, you'll integrate capital A equals the integral of A to B of F of X minus G of X DX. This will be getting the upper function subtracted by the lower function. Substituting in your functions and balance, you get the integral of zero to one of the square root of X minus X squared DX. Once you solve this integral, you'll get two-thirds multiplied by X to the three-halves minus one-third multiplied by X cubed plus C evaluated from zero to one. Once you plug in your bounds, your constant of integration C will drop. So then you'll have two-thirds multiplied by one to the three-halves minus one-third multiplied by one cubed, and then you'll subtract all of that by two-thirds multiplied by zero to the three-halves minus one-third multiplied by zero cubed. This will then equal two-thirds minus one-third minus zero, which equals one-third. So, that will be the area between those two curves for your functions F of X equals the square root of X and G of X equals X squared between the values of zero and one. Onto another example, but this time let's find the area with functions of Y. So in this case, you'll have F of Y equals E to the Y and G of Y equals one plus Y squared. This will also be between the bounds of zero and one, but this time it is in terms of Y. So it'll be Y equals zero to Y equals one. In this graph, you'll notice you have your two curves of F of Y equals E to the Y, which is represented by the blue curve starting way from the bottom and then coming up. And then you also have one plus Y squared, which is the red curve. And so it's a parabola turned on its side, and again is going out to the right. And so the area you want to find between these two curves is represented by that little shaded region between the values of Y equals zero and Y equals one. In this case, you'll integrate from the right function subtracted by the left function. So this gets you capital A equals the integral of A to B of F of Y minus G of Y, D of Y. Plugging in your values gets you the integral of zero to one of E to the Y minus one plus Y squared DY. Once you integrate this, you'll get E to the Y minus Y minus one-third multiplied by Y cubed plus C evaluated from zero to one. Once you evaluate this by plugging in your values, your C will drop, which gets you each the one minus one minus one-third multiplied by one cubed. Then you'll subtract all of that by E to the zero, minus zero minus one-third multiplied by zero cubed. This then simplifies to E minus four-thirds minus one, which is equal to E minus seven-thirds. Knowing how to find the area between curves, whether they are stacked vertically or horizontally, can be very useful. I recommend taking time on your own to practice more problems with area between curves. Up next, you will explore using integrals to find volume.

Volume (10 min 1second)
- [Instructor] In the previous video, you learned how you can find the area of a shape, using integrals. But what if you want to go from two dimensions to three dimensions? In this video, you'll explore four common methods used to find the volume of various shapes using integrals. When working with volume methods, there are two main types you'll come across. First, you have methods that focus on rotating the area on the X axis or Y axis to determine the volume. These include the disk, washer, and shell, also known as cylinder, methods. The other type of method focuses on taking slices of the area and slowly building upon itself from start to end. This will be the focus for the cross-sectional area method. First up is the disk method. The disk method focuses on rotating a solid object around either the X axis or Y axis. The formula is capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of A to B of R squared DX. So to get your volume, you will need a radius notated by R. Sometimes the radius is equal to the function itself, and other times, you may need to adjust it if it is not on the X axis directly. You can adjust this formula to have it be for function of Y and DY in relation to the Y axis instead, if you need to. Let's look at an example of this method in action. Let's find the volume of a solid with a curve of Y equals the square root of X, and let's get this revolving around the X axis from the points of X equals zero to X equals four. In this case, your radius is just going to be your function where R equals the square root of X. Let's plug this into your formula of capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of A to B of R squared DX. This gets you capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of zero to four of the square root of X squared DX. Note that that just simplifies to X, so once you solve it, you get capital V equals pi, multiplied by X squared divided by two, evaluated from zero to four. Once you plug in your bounds, you get capital V equals pi multiplied by four squared, divided by two minus pi, multiplied by zero squared divided by two. This gets you a final volume of capital V equals eight, multiplied by a pi. Next is the washer method. This method will be similar to the disk method where you rotate a solid object around either the X axis or Y axis, but this time, you'll have a hollow part in the middle of it. The formula you use for this one is capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of A to B of capital R squared, minus R squared DX. So in this time, instead of just having one radius, you have two radii, so you have your outer radius of capital R and your inner radius R. Note that sometimes, the radii are equal to the functions themselves, and other times, you may need to adjust them if they're not on the X axis directly. And like before, you can adjust this formula to be for function of Y and DY in relation to the Y axis, if needed. Let's look at another example. This time, let's find the volume of a hollowed out solid with an outer region of Y equals four, and an inner region of Y equals X squared from the values of X equals zero, 2X equals two. In this case, you'll have your outer radius, capital R equal to four, and your inner radius R equal to X squared. So let's plug this into your formula of capital V equals pi multiplied by the integral of A to B of capital R squared, minus R squared DX, which gets you capital V equals pi multiplied by the integral of zero to two of four squared minus X squared squared DX. Once you take the squares of both of those values, you then get capital V equals pi multiplied by the integral of zero to two, of 16 minus X to the fourth DX. Once you solve this, you get capital V equals pi multiplied by 16 multiplied by X, minus X to the fifth divided by five. And let's evaluate this for your bounds of zero to two. This gets you capital V equals pi multiplied by 16, multiplied by two, minus two to the fifth divided by five, minus 16 multiplied by zero, minus zero to the fifth divided by five. This gets you a final volume of capital V equals 128 divided by five, multiplied by pi. Next step is the shell or cylinder method. This method is another rotating method where this time, it focuses on summing up cylindrical shells parallel to the axis of rotation. The formula for this method is given by capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of A to B of two multiplied by R, multiplied by hdx, where you have your radius R and your height H. Note that you can hollow out the cylinder, like you would with the washer method by simply subtracting the inner radius in your formula. Note that the radius is the horizontal distance from the Y axis. Sometimes the height will just be the function itself, or you may need to adjust it if it is not on the X axis directly. You can also adjust this formula, like always, to be for a function of Y and DY in relation to the Y axis, if needed. Let's look at this with an example where you have Y equals X squared for the values of between X equals zero and X equals one. In this case, you'll be revolving this region around the Y axis for these X values. So in this case, your radius R will equal X because you're going from that value of zero to one and rotating it around that Y axis, and your height will be your function, so H will be X squared. Let's use your formula, capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of A to B of two, multiplied by R multiplied by hdx, which gets you capital V equals pi multiplied by zero to one integral of two, multiplied by X, multiplied by X squared DX, which simplifies to capital V equals pi, multiplied by the integral of zero to one of two, multiplied by X cubed DX, which solved, gets you capital V equals pi, multiplied by X to the fourth divided by two, evaluated from zero to one. Plugging in your bounds, you get capital V equals pi multiplied by one to the fourth, divided by two, minus pi multiplied by zero to the fourth divided by two. This gets you a final volume, capital V equals pi divided by two. The last volume method you will learn is a bit different from the previous three methods. The cross-sectional area method is when you cut your shape into little slices, and then add them all up together. This is great to use for very odd shapes. The formula you will use is capital V equals the integral of A to B of capital A of X DX. In this case, your cross section area at the value X is going to be capital A of X. Note that sometimes the area is equal to the function itself and other times, you may need to adjust it if it is not on the X axis, like I mentioned before. And like always, you can adjust this formula to be for function of Y and DY in relation to the Y axis, if needed. Let's go through one more example, using this last method for volume. Let's find the volume of a solid pyramid that has a square base with a side size of four and a height of four. Since the side length of the square base decreases linearly with height, you can have the function F of Y equals four minus Y. This gives you a side length of four minus Y and a height of Y. Now, let's put this to the test by finding your volume where you have F of Y equals four minus y, and you can take the square of your function to get the square cross section. So you'll get F of Y squared equals four minus Y, and then you'll square all of that. And this will be evaluated on the bounds of Y equals zero to Y equals four, since your height is four. Now let's plug this into your formula, capital V equals the integral of A to B of capital AY DY. Note that I adjusted this formula to be in terms of Y and DY, due to the function you are using being F of Y. Plugging in your function and your bounds, you get capital V equals the integral of zero to four of four minus Y, and then you'll square all of that, DY. Once you multiply this out, you get capital V equals the integral of zero to four of Y squared, minus eight, multiplied by Y, plus 16 DY. Once you solve this, you get capital V equals Y cubed, divided by three minus four, multiplied by Y squared, plus 16, multiplied by Y, evaluated from zero to four. Plugging in your balance, you get capital V equals four cubed divided by three minus four multiplied by four squared, plus 16 multiplied by four. Then you'll subtract all of that by zero cubed, divided by three minus four, multiplied by zero squared, plus 16, multiplied by zero. Once you solve that, you get a final volume of capital V equals 64, divided by three. These are not all the methods you will come across to find the volume of shapes, but they are a few of the common ones. Next up, let's take a look at how you can use integrals to find the average value of a function.

Average value of a function (6 min 57 seconds)
- [Instructor] Onto another integral application. You should be familiar with the concept of finding the average of a set of finite values where you add up all the values and divide them by the size of the group of the values. But how do you find the average value when it comes to a function? You saw earlier how you can use derivatives to find minimum and maximum values of a function. Now, you can use integrals to find the average value of a function. This is similar to the process of finding the average of a set of values where you integrate a function f of x and divide it by the size of the interval of a to b. Let's take a look at the formula you can use to find the average value of a function. If you have a continuous function f of x on an interval of a to b that's closed and bounded, then you can find the average value of this function with the formula of favg, for f average, is equal to one divided by b minus a multiplied by the integral of a to b of f of x dx. This application is best understood in action, so let's work through some examples. Let's begin by finding the average value of the function f of x equals x squared over the interval one to five. If you look at the graph of this function, you'll see that it's the parabola of x squared, and you'll be looking at those values of x between one and five. This is denoted by the blue line for the full function, and then the red portion of the line is the portion you're looking at in particular. When looking at this graph, you may be tempted to just use the midpoint, which in this case is equal to three with the corresponding value of nine. But note that since this is not a straight line, this is not an accurate method to gather the average value of a function. Let's set up your integral in order to find the average value of the function f of x equals x squared for the values of x between one and five. You have your formula, f average equals one divided by b minus a multiplied by the integral of a to b of f of x dx. Plugging in your values, you get f average equals one divided by five minus one multiplied by the integral of one to five of x squared dx. Using the power rule, you then get 1/4 multiplied by x cubed divided by three, and you can simplify this to be x cubed divided by 12. And let's evaluate this for your bounds of one to five. Once you do this, you get five cubed divided by 12 minus one cubed divided by 12, which equals 31 cubed, and that approximately equals 10.33. As you'll notice, this is not equal to that midpoint of three with a corresponding value of nine because the average value of the function of f of x equals x squared for the values between of x of one to five is equal to 10.33 approximately. Let's take a look at another example. This time, let's look at the trigonometric function f of x equals sine of x for the values of x between zero and pi. So you'll see the graph of the sine function and it's oscillating behavior, and you'll note that the function is notated by the blue line and the red portion of the line between the values of zero and pi is the portion you're looking at in particular. For these types of functions, it could be tough to tell from the graph exactly what the average value should be due to the oscillating behavior. Let's set up your integral for this function. So you have f of x equals sine of x for your interval of x between zero and pi, and you have your formula f average equals one divided by b minus a multiplied by the integral of a to b of f of x dx. Plugging in your values, you get f of average equals one divided by pi minus zero multiplied by the integral of zero to pi of sine of x dx. Once you solve this integral, you get one divided by pi multiplied by negative cosine of x between zero and pi, which you can then combine these terms to get negative cosine of x divided by pi. And let's evaluate these for your bounds of zero to pi. Plugging in these values, you get negative cosine of pi divided by pi minus negative cosine of zero divided by pi. This gets you a final result of two over pi for the average value of your function sine of x between the bounds of zero and pi. Let's look at one more example. Let's look at the function f of x equals three multiplied by e to the x subtracted by two multiplied by x cube for the values of x between zero and four. This function looks a little funky, and you'll note that the function is notated by the blue line and the red portion of the lines between x of zero and four is where you are interested in finding the average value. As you could tell from this graph, it would be a little hard to eyeball exactly where the average value should be for this portion of the function especially since it includes some increasing and decreasing parts of the function. Let's plug this into your formula. So you have f of x equals three multiplied by e to the x minus two multiplied by x cubed for the values of x between zero and four. You have your formula f average equals one divided by b minus a multiplied by the integral of a to b of f of x dx. Plugging in your values, you get f average equals one divided by four minus zero multiplied by the integral of zero to four of three multiplied by e to the x subtracted by two multiplied by x cubed dx. Using the various rules you learned, you will then get an integral result of 1/4 multiplied by three multiplied by e to the x minus 2/4 multiplied by x to the fourth. And if you simplify this, you then get 3/4 multiplied by e to the x minus 1/4 multiplied by x to the fourth and evaluated from your bounds of zero to four. Plugging in your bounds, you get 3/4 multiplied by e to the fourth minus 1/4 multiplied by four to the fourth minus 3/4 multiplied by e to the zero minus 1/4 multiplied by zero to the fourth. This equals 3/4 multiplied by e to the fourth minus 131 divided by four, which is approximately equal to 8.1985. Knowing how to find the average value of a function is a simple concept, but it could be incredibly useful. Integrals are helpful for situations where this may be difficult to do without the assistance of computer software. Up next, you'll explore using integrals to find arc length.

Arc length (5 min 56 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's dive into another integral application. You are able to discover how to find the area beneath the curve or between two curves, but what about determining the length of the curve itself? This is what you'll explore in this video. When you want to find the length of a curve between two points, this is what you call arc length. You can think of arc length as the path you would take along the function itself. This can be incredibly useful to use when finding the distance of projectiles over non-linear distances. When calculating arc length, there are two main formulas you can use depending on if it is a function of x or y. If you have a function f(x) over a closed bounded interval, a to b, the arc length is given by the integral of a to b of the square root of one plus the derivative of f(x) squared dx. If you have a function g(y) over closed bounded intervals, c to d, the arc length is given by the integral of c to d of the square root of one plus the derivative of g(y) squared dy. Let's use these arc length formulas in a few examples. Let's begin by finding the arc length of y equals x over the interval of zero to one. In this case, your derivative of yy' is simply equal to one. Let's use your formula, integral of a to b of the square root of one plus f'(x) squared dx, and so plugging this in, you get the integral of zero to one of the square root of one plus one squared dx, and so this equals the integral of zero to one of the square root of two dx, and once you evaluate this, you get the square root of two multiplied by x evaluated from zero to one, which equals the square root of two multiplied by one minus the square root of two multiplied by zero, which simply equals the square root of two. With this particular example, you could actually prove it using the Pythagorean theorem since, again, it makes a nice little triangle. Let's look at in another example where you have the function y equals the natural log of secant(x) over the interval of zero to pi divided by four. In this case, your derivative y' is equal to secant(x) multiplied by tangent(x) divided by secant(x). You can cancel at the secant(x) to simply get a result of tangent(x) for your derivative. So let's plug this into your function of integral of a to b square root of one plus f'(x) square dx to get the integral of zero to pi divided by four with the square root of one plus tangent(x) squared dx, which is equal to integral of zero to pi divided by four of the square root of secant squared of x dx, which equals the natural log of the absolute value of secant(x) plus tangent(x) evaluated from zero to pi divided by four. Now, let's evaluate this so you get the natural log of the absolute value of secant of pi divided by four plus tangent of pi divided by four. Subtract that by the natural log of secant(0) plus tangent(0), again, absolute values for that one, and then this equals the natural log of the absolute value of square root of two plus one, which is approximately equal to 0.881. Let's look at one more example. This time, let's say you have the function x equals to one third multiplied by y to the three halves over the interval of zero to four. In this case, your derivative x' is equal to one half multiplied by y to the one half. This time, you'll use the formula integral of c to d of the square root of one plus g'(y) squared dy. This gets you the integral of zero to four of the square root of one plus one half multiplied by y to the one half, and you'll square that and dy to get you the integral of zero to four of the square root of one plus one fourth multiplied by y dy. With this integral, you'll want to use substitution to solve it. Let's have u equal to one plus one fourth multiplied by y, and this means du is equal to one fourth dy. You can then simplify this to have dy equal to four multiplied by du. Plug this into your integral to get the integral of zero to four of the square root of u multiplied by four du. This gets you a result of four multiplied by two thirds multiplied by u to the three halves which is equal to eight thirds multiplied by u to the three halves. Now. let's plug in your value of u equal to one plus one fourth multiplied by y to get you a result of eight thirds multiplied by one plus one fourth multiplied by y to the three halves, and evaluate that from zero to four. This gets you eight thirds multiplied by one plus one fourth multiplied by four. Put that to the three halves. Subtract that by eight thirds multiplied by one plus one fourth multiplied by zero, taking that to the three halves, and this simplifies to get you a result of 16 multiplied by the square root of two minus eight divided by three. Knowing how to find arc length is incredibly useful for various situations, especially when the curve length is not linear. Just be cautious knowing that some of the integrals from this formula can be very difficult to solve by hand and may require a computer or other device to estimate the solution. Up next, let's look at using integrals to compute surface area.

Surface area (7 min 31 seconds)
- [Instructor] So far, you learned how to determine the volume of a shape and the arc length of a curve, but what happens when you combine these two concepts together? You can then calculate the surface area of a shape. In this video, you'll learn how to calculate the surface area of a shape by using the revolution technique. This will combine the revolution technique with the arc length formula you learned about in the previous video. This will get you the total surface area of the shape you calculate. Note, this is not supposed to be confused with the washer method since the thickness when the finding surface area is supposed to be infinitesimally small. Let's take a look at those surface area formulas. Like for arc length, there are two versions depending on if it is a function of x or y. So if you have a function f(x) over a closed bounded interval, a to b, you then get a formula of the integral of a to b of two multiplied by pi multiplied by f(x) multiplied by the square root of one plus f'(x) squared dx. If you have a function g(y) over a closed bounded interval, c to d, you then get a formula with the integral c to d of two multiplied by pi multiplied by g(y) multiplied by the square root of one plus g'(y) squared dy. Let's use these formulas with some examples. Let's start off by getting the surface area of a cone, which you can use a linear line in order to get that surface area. Let's have the line be f(x) equal to x over the values between zero and three. In this case, your derivative f'(x) is simply equal to one. Let's plug this into your formula of integral a to b of two multiplied by pi multiplied by f(x) multiplied by the square root of one plus f'(x) squared dx. Once you plug in these values, you get the integral of zero to three for your interval of two multiplied by pi multiplied by x multiplied by the square root of one plus one squared dx. Once you simplify this and integrate this, you get a result of two multiplied by pi multiplied by square root of two multiplied by x squared divided by two. Simplify this a little bit more to get pi multiplied by square root of two multiplied by x squared. So let's evaluate this from zero to three. So you have pi multiplied by square root of two multiplied by three squared minus pi multiplied by square root of two multiplied by zero squared, which gets you a final result of nine multiplied by pi multiplied by square root of two for the surface area of your cone between the values of zero and three. Let's look at another example, but this time, let's get the surface area of a sphere. This time, you have the function f(x) equals the square root of nine minus x squared over the interval of negative three to three. This is because your radius, in this case, is equal to three. Your derivative in this problem is equal to f'(x) equal to negative x divided by the square root of nine minus x squared, and let's plug this into your formula of integral a to b of two multiplied by pi multiplied by the square root of one plus f'(x) squared dx. This gives you the integral over your interval negative three to three of two multiplied by pi multiplied by nine minus x squared multiplied by one plus negative x divided by square root of nine minus x squared, and then square that and, finally, dx. You can simplify this integral a little bit to get the integral of negative three to three of two multiplied by pi multiplied by nine minus x squared multiplied by three divided by nine minus x squared dx. Now, you can cancel out those two terms to just get the integral of negative three to three of six multiplied by pi dx, which gets you a result of six multiplied by pi multiplied by x evaluated from negative three to three. Evaluating this gets you six multiplied by pi multiplied by three minus six multiplied by pi multiplied by negative three, which gets you a final result of 36 multiplied by pi for the surface area of your sphere with a radius of three. Let's look at one more example where you have the function x equal to one half multiplied by y squared over the interval of zero to two. In this case, your x' is equal to y. Since you're working with a function of g(y) this time, you will then have the integral of cd of two multiplied by pi multiplied by g(y), multiplied by the square root of one plus g' of y squared dy. Let's plug in your values to get the integral of zero to two of two multiplied by pi multiplied by one half multiplied by y squared multiplied by the square root of one plus y squared dy. You can then simplify this to be the integral of zero to two of pi multiplied by y squared plus the square root of one plus y squared dy. This particular example requires you to (indistinct). So let's have u equal to one plus y squared and du equal to two ydy. If you solve the first equation for y squared, you get y squared equal to u minus one, and if you solve the second one, you get ydy equal to one half du. Let's plug this into your equation to get the integral of zero to two of one half multiplied by pi multiplied by u minus one multiplied by the square root of udu. This then simplifies to the integral of zero to two of one half multiplied by pi multiplied by u to the three halves minus u to the one half du. Integrate this to get one half multiplied by pi multiplied by two fifths multiplied by u to the five halves minus two thirds multiplied by u to the three halves. Let's plug in your values to then get a result of one half multiplied by pi multiplied by two fifths multiplied by one plus y squared to the five halves minus two thirds multiplied by one plus y squared to the three halves, then you'll evaluate this from zero to two. This gets you one half multiplied by pi multiplied by two fifths multiplied by one plus two squared to the five halves minus two thirds multiplied by one plus two squared to the three halves, and then you'll subtract all of that by one half multiplied by pi multiplied by two fifths multiplied by one plus zero squared to the five halves minus two thirds multiplied by one plus zero squared to the three halves. This simplifies to 50 multiplied by pi multiplied by the square root of five plus two multiplied by pi divided by 15, which is approximately equal to 23.835 for the surface area of this function. It is great seeing how you can utilize previous applications to find the surface area of objects, especially if they're not a normal shape. Like with arc length, note that some of the integrals from this formula can be very difficult to solve by hand and may require a computer to estimate the solution. Up next, let's work with force, work, and energy.

Force, work, and energy (6 min 16 seconds)
- [Instructor] In this video, you'll explore some practical applications in the realm of physics. In particular, you'll focus on exploring how to use integrals with force, work, and energy. Let's begin by exploring force. Force is when there is a push or pull on an object that causes the object to change velocity. This is measured in newtons, which is abbreviated to a capital N. The general formula for force is F equals m multiplied by a, where you have force equals mass multiplied by acceleration. Let's look at an example of using integrals with force. Let's say you have a 10 meter rope with a density of five newtons per meter. For this example, you want to find the total force required to pull the entire rope straight upward, assuming the current position is having it lie completely flat on the ground. In this case, you can integrate over the weight of the five newtons per meter for the 10 meters of the distance, so you'll have capital F equals the integral of zero to 10 of five dx. Getting your integral for this is five multiplied by x evaluated from zero to 10. Plug in your bounds to get five multiplied by 10 minus five multiplied by zero, which gets you a final result of 50 newtons required to do this. Now let's expand upon the topic of force by taking a look at the concept of work. So when you apply force to an object, the amount of energy it takes to move the object is known as work. Sometimes it is also known as the amount of energy transferred. This is measured in joules, which is abbreviated as a capital J. The general formula for work is capital W equals capital F multiplied by d. So this is work equals force multiplied by displacement. Note that you can get force if needed using that previous force formula I showed you. Let's put these integrals to work. Let's say you have the function f of x equals five multiplied by x squared newtons that acts on an object to move it from x equals zero meters to x equals three meters. In order to calculate the work, you can simply integrate the force equation over those bounds. So in this case, your work, capital W, is equal to the integral of a to b of f of x dx. Let's plug in your values to get capital W equals the integral of zero to three of five multiplied by x squared dx. Getting the result for your integral is 5/3 multiplied by x cubed, and let's evaluate it from the bounds of zero to three. This gets you 5/3 multiplied by three cubed minus 5/3 multiplied by zero cubed. This gets you a final result of 45 joules. Lastly, let's expand upon force and work by taking a look at energy. Energy is the capacity to do work or cause change. Energy can be in various forms such as potential, kinetic, and thermal. Energy is also measured in joules, which, if you remember, is abbreviated as capital J. Energy actually has various formulas depending on what type of energy you are looking at. Let's look at a few common formulas you may come across. For kinetic energy, you have capital KE equals 1/2 multiplied by m, multiplied by v squared. In this case, your kinetic energy is 1/2 multiplied by mass, multiplied by velocity squared. Next, looking at potential energy with gravity, you get the formula of capital PE equals m multiplied by g multiplied by h. This is your potential energy equals mass multiplied by acceleration due to gravity, multiplied by height. And finally, you have potential energy with springs, which gets you capital PE equals 1/2 multiplied by k, multiplied by x squared. This time, it's 1/2 multiplied by the spring constant, multiplied by the displacement of the spring squared. Let's look at using integrals to find the potential energy of a spring. In this case, you'll use Hooke's Laws for springs where the force is equal to capital F equals negative k multiplied by x. In this case, k is your spring constant and x is the displacement. You can then integrate this to get the potential energy of the spring. Let's have a spring with a spring constant of k equals 20 newtons per meter. This spring is then going to be stretched from x equals zero meters to x equals 0.4 meters. Let's plug this into your potential energy equation and integrate it to get capital PE equals the integral of a to b of negative k multiplied by x dx. Plugging in your values gets you capital PE equals the integral of zero to 0.4 of negative 20 multiplied by x dx. Once you integrate this, you get a result of negative 20 multiplied by 1/2, multiplied by x squared, which simplifies to negative 10, multiplied by x squared. Let's evaluate this for your bounds zero to 0.4 to get negative 10 multiplied by 0.4 squared, subtracted by negative 10, multiplied by zero squared. This gets you a final result of negative 1.6 joules of potential energy. Force, work, and energy are all great practical physics applications when it comes to using integrals. In the next video, you'll explore two other practical applications of physics: masses and moments.

Masses and moments (10 min 1 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's continue exploring more practical applications in the realm of physics. In this video, you'll focus on Masses and Moments. Let's begin by looking at some definitions. Mass is the total amount of matter in an object or region. This can be calculated in various dimensions, such as one dimension for length, two dimensions for area, and three dimensions for volume. For each of these three dimensions, you have the following formulas. So for the one dimension with length, you have m equals the integral of a to b of p of (x)dx. Next, for the two-dimension area, you have m equals the integral of your region, R of p of (x,y)dA. Finally, for your three-dimensional area of volume, you have m equals the integral of V of p of (x,y,z)dV. Next are moments. A moment measures the distribution of mass relative to a certain axis. You can have a moment in relation to the x-axis or the y-axis. If you have a moment for the x-axis, it is given by the formula M of x equals the integral of R of y, multiplied by p of (x,y)dA, which is equal to p multiplied by the integral of a to b of one half multiplied by r of x squared minus g of x squared, and that's for dx. If you get it in relation to the y-axis, it's M of y equals the integral of your region R of x multiplied by p of (x,y)dA. This is equal to p multiplied by the integral of a to b of x multiplied by f of x minus g of (x)dx. So note that these formulas are similar, but they do have some important differences to make sure you are aware of. Both of these concepts come together to form center of mass. The center of mass is the point where a region is perfectly balanced if suspended at a point. For a two-dimensional object, you can gather the center of mass coordinates by calculating xcm equals M of y divided by m and y of cm equals M of x divided by m. So in these two formulas, you have your moments, M of x and M of y, your axes x and y, and finally, your mass. So as you could tell, both the mass and moment calculations are all coming together to get your center of mass calculations. Let's dive into some examples. Let's find the mass of a rod with variable density. This rod has a length of four meters and a density of p of x equals x squared and kilograms per meter. To find its mass, you will need to integrate your density function, so in this case, you have a equals zero and b equals four for your bounds to account for your length. So you have your formula m equals the integral of a to b of p of (x)dx, where you have m equals the integral of zero to four of x squared dx, which solves to be m equals x cubed divided by three, evaluated from zero to four, which gets you m equals four cubed divided by three minus zero cubed divided by three. This gets you a final mass of 64 divided by three kilograms. Let's move on to another example, but this time let's find the moments. Let's say you have a region bounded by y equals x cubed and y equals the square root of x from the values of x of zero to one. In the graph, you will see you have your two lines of your two functions, so x cubed is represented by the blue line, which is going up from the bottom left, kind of levels out at x equals zero, and then proceeds to increase. Then you have your function square root of x, which starts at the 0.00 and starts to go up fairly quickly and then kind of levels out over time. So you'll notice that these two intersect at x equals zero and x equals one, hence why it makes sense to look at this region. For this problem, you will assume there is a density of one, meaning it has uniform density in this region, making it easier to calculate. Let's plug these into your two moment formulas. Starting with your moment with the x-axis, you have your functions y equals x cubed, y equals the square root of x, with x being between zero and one, and your density being p equals one. Let's plug this into your M of x formula to get you the integral of the region R of y, multiplied by p of (x,y)dA, which is equal to p multiplied by the integral of a to b of one half, multiplied by f of x squared minus g of x squared dx. This gets you M of x equals one multiplied by the integral of zero to one of one half, multiplied by the square root of x squared, minus x cubed squared dx. This simplifies to be M of x equals the integral of zero to one of one half, multiplied by x minus one half multiplied by x to the sixth dx. Once you solve this, you can then get M of x equals one half multiplied by one half, multiplied by x squared, minus one seventh, multiplied by x to the seventh with your bounds being zero to one. Plugging in your bounds, you get M of x equals one half multiplied by one half, multiplied by one squared minus one seven multiplied by one to the seventh, and then you'll subtract that by one half multiplied by one half, multiplied by zero squared minus one seventh, multiplied by zero to the seventh. This gets you M of x equal to five divided by 28. Now let's get your moment with relation to the y-axis. So remember, you have your functions y equals x cubed, y equals square root of x between the values of x of zero to one, where you have your density p equals one. So using your formula, you have M of y equals the integral of your region R of x multiplied by P of (x,y)dA equals p multiplied by the integral of a to b of x multiplied by f of x minus g of (x)dx. This gets you M of y equals one multiplied by the integral of zero to one of x multiplied by the square root of x minus x cubed dx. Once you multiply that x variable through, you'll get M of y equals the integral of zero to one of x to the three halves minus x to the fourth dx. Once you solve this, you get M of y equals two fifths multiplied by x to the five halves minus one fifth multiplied by x to the fifth, and this will be evaluated from your bounds of zero to one. This gets you M of y equals two fifths multiplied by one to five halves minus one fifth multiplied by one to the fifth. Then you'll subtract all that by two-fifths multiplied by zero to the five halves minus one fifth multiplied by a zero to the fifth. This gets you a final result of M of y equals one fifth. Let's continue on with the previous example, but this time let's expand upon it to find the center of mass. So remember, you have your functions, y equals x cubed, y equals square root of x under the region of x of zero to one with your density p equals one. Then you have your two center mass equations, which gets you xcm equals M of y divided m, and then ycm equals M of x divided by m. Bringing back your moment calculation results, you have M of x equals five divided by 28, and M of y equals one fifth. In this case, you will need to find the mass, and you can do so by calculating the mass by finding the area of the region. So in this case, you have A equals the integral of a to b of f of x minus g of (x)dx. Plugging in your values, you get A equals the integral of zero to one of the square root of x minus x cubed dx, which evaluated gets you A equals two thirds multiplied by x to the three halves, minus x to the fourth divided by four, evaluated from zero to one. Plugging in these values gets you A equals two thirds multiplied by one to the three halves, minus one to the fourth divided by four, and then you'll subtract two thirds multiplied by zero to the three halves minus zero to the fourth divided by four. This gets you a total area of A equals five divided by 12. Now let's plug this all together with your different variables and formulas where you have xcm equals M of y divided by m, ycm equals M of x divided by m, where M of x equals five divided by 28, M of y equals one fifth, and then your mass, since it is the same as your area in this case, is m equals five divided by 12. So to get your center of mass, you get xcm equals one fifth divided by five twelfths. This solves to be 12 divided by 25. Now to get your center of mass, ycm, it'll equal five divided by 28, divided by five, divided by 12, which gets you 12 divided by 28, which simplifies to just be three divided by seven. This means the coordinates for the center of mass for your region is 12 divided by 25 and three divided by seven. This means this is where on the graph you can perfectly balance this region when it has uniform density. Understanding masses, moments, and center of mass are all great practical applications when it comes to physics. Let's wrap up this chapter by looking at how you can use integrals with probability.

Probability (9 min 45 seconds)
- [Instructor] Let's wrap up this chapter by exploring how you can use integrals with probability. Probability at a basic level usually does not need any sort of calculus in order to perform calculations. But once you get to more advanced calculations, calculus becomes an integral part of it. Let's start off by exploring how you can use integrals with probability density functions. A probability density function describes the likelihood a random variable capital X takes on a particular value. This is also known as A PDF. The formula to find the probability that capital X is between two values, A and B, is given by P of A less than or equal to capital X, less than or equal to B, equals the integral of A to B of FX of X DX. Note that FX of X is the probability density function of your random variable capital X. Let's look at this in an example for an exponential distribution. Let's say you have a PDF of FX of X equals one fifth multiplied by E to the negative X fifths for X greater than or equal to zero. Let's find the probability that capital X is between the values of zero and three. Plugging this into your formula of P of A, less than or equal to capital X, less than or equal to B, equals the integral of A to B of FX of X DX, this gets you the probability P of zero less than or equal to capital X, less than or equal to three, which equals the integral of zero to three of one fifths multiplied by E to the negative X fifths DX. In this particular case, you'll use substitution where you'll have U equal to X fifths and DU equal to one fifth DX. Substituting this into your integral, you get the integral of zero to three of E to the negative UDU. This gets you negative E to the negative U, which if you plug in your value of U equals X fifths, you then get negative E to the negative X fifths, and let's evaluate that from zero to three. Plugging in your values, you get negative E to the negative three fifths subtracted by negative E to the negative zero fifths. This means your probability that your random variable X lies between the values of zero and three is equal to negative E to the negative three fifths, plus one, which is approximately equal to 0.4512. Next, let's take a look at expected value. The expected value for random variable capital X is essentially the average value of a distribution. This is similar to finding the mean in statistics. The formula to find the expected value of capital X is E capital X equals the integral of negative infinity to infinity of X multiplied by FX of XDX, where FX of X is the PDF of your random variable, capital X. Let's use this in an example with the uniform distribution. Your uniform distribution PDF is represented by FX of X equals one, and let's focus on getting the expectation between the values of zero and one. In this case, you have your expectation E of capital X equals the integral of negative infinity to infinity of X multiplied by FX of XDX, which in this case is E of capital X equals the integral of zero to one of X multiplied by one DX. Once you integrate this, you get one half multiplied by X squared, and you can evaluate that from zero to one, which gets you one half multiplied by one squared minus one half multiplied by zero squared. This gets you a final expectation E of capital X equals one half. Next up is variance and standard deviation. Variance describes the spread of your random variable X, such as how spread out the distribution is around the expected value. This is given by the formula var of capital X equals E of capital X squared minus E of capital X, and then you'll square that on the outside. In this case, E of capital X squared is gathered by the integral of negative infinity to infinity of X squared multiplied by FX of XDX. The standard deviation is simply the square root of the variance, and it is represented by the symbol sigma. So you have sigma equals the square root of var of capital X. Let's continue using your uniform distribution for this next example. So you'll still have FX of X equals one, and you want to look at the values between zero and one. As you calculated previously, your expectation E of capital X equals one half. Now look at your formula of var of capital X equals E of capital X squared minus E of capital X, and then you'll square all of that. Let's gather E of capital X squared, which is equal to negative infinity to infinity of X squared multiplied by FX of XDX, which plugging in your values, you get E of capital X squared equals the integral of zero to one of X squared multiplied by one DX. This integral gets you a result of one third multiplied by X cubed, and let's evaluate it from your bounds of zero to one. This gets you one third multiplied by one cubed minus one third multiplied by zero cubed, which gets you a final result of one third. Now, let's calculate your variance, so you have var of capital X equals E of capital X squared minus E of capital X, and then you'll square all of that. This gets you var of capital X equals one third minus one half squared. This means var of X equals 1 over 12. Now let's get your standard deviation, which if you remember, is sigma equal to the square root of var of capital X. In this case, sigma is equal to the square root of one over 12, which is approximately equal to 0.289. Finally, let's wrap up with one more distribution function. This time, let's take a look at the cumulative distribution function. The cumulative distribution function gives the probability that a random variable capital X takes on a value less than or equal to a value little X. This is also known as the CDF. You can gather the probability that capital X is less than or equal to a value of little X by using the formula of capital FX of X equals P of capital X less than or equal to little X, which equals the integral of negative infinity to X of FX of TDT. Let's use the previous exponential distribution PDF of FX of X equals one fifth multiplied by E to the negative X fifths for values of X greater than or equal to zero. This time, let's find the probability that your random variable capital X is less than or equal to the value of five. You'll use the same process as before by evaluating the integral of that PDF, but note that your bounds will be a little different. So if you have your formula capital FX of X equals P of capital X less than or equal to little X equals the integral of negative infinity two X of FX of TDT, you then get capital FX of X equals P of capital X less than or equal to five, which equals the integral of zero to five of one fifths multiplied by E to the negative X fifths DX. Like before, you will use substitution where you have U equals X fifths and DU equals one fifth DX to get the integral of zero to five of E to the minus UDU, which, as you saw before, is negative E to the minus U. Plugging in your value of U equals X fifths gets you negative E to the negative X fifths, evaluated from zero to five, which is negative E to the negative five divided by five minus negative E to the negative zero fifths. This then simplifies to negative E to the minus one plus one. This means your probability your random variable X is less than or equal to five equals negative E to the negative one plus one, which is approximately equal to 0.6321. Note that there are various other distributions you can work with using these formulas, such as the normal and gamma distributions. You can also apply integrals in other areas of probability, such as dealing with total probability, moments, and Bayes' theorem. To learn more about probability, I suggest checking out my LinkedIn learning course, Probability Foundations for Data Science. This wraps up building your foundation of calculus. 	50N
	75N

	Revolution technique
	Disk method
	Cross-section method
	Washer method
3.What is arc length useful for?
	determining the slope of a curve at a given point
	calculating the area under a curve
	finding the maximum value of a function over an interval
	finding the distance traveled along a curve, such as projectile motion
4.What is the average value of f(x) = x^2 over the interval [1, 5]?
	9
	12
	31/3
	15
5.What is one of the four common methods used to find the volume of various shapes using integrals?
	Disk Method
	Rectangle Method
	Sphere Method
	Triangle Method
6.How do you calculate the area between two curves?
	by integrating the product of the two functions over the given interval
	by integrating the difference between the two functions over the given interval
	by finding the intersection points and integrating each function separately
	by integrating the sum of the two functions over the given interval

7.What does the expected value of a random variable represent?
	The most frequently occurring value in a distribution.
	The value at which half the data points lie below and half above.
	The long-term average value of a distribution.
	The maximum possible value in a distribution.
8.What does the concept of the center of mass represent?
	It is the midpoint of an object's total volume.
	It is the point where the object experiences the greatest force.
	It is the point where a region would balance perfectly if suspended.
	It is the location where the mass of an object is concentrated.
